What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
In Tip 10 we discussed how to improve the collection ofverbal re¡sponses from our students using Cold Call. 
But there is an issue with Cold Call. You only get to hear the responses of a small number of students -usually one -for each question you ask. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Roughly what proportion of your questioning involves asking one or two students for the answer? 

ò 
What are some issues with this? 


What's the tip? 
Author Doug Lemov has a concept called the 'participation ratio' -in other words, how many students are participating in class through discussion, writing, answering questions, etc. What we ide¡ally want is mass participation. We want to engineer situations where most -if not all -our students answer a question, and we can call upon as many ofthose responses as we need. 
Here are eight reasons why. 
1. Mass participation provides an incentive for all students to think 
Cold Call is designed to make all students think. The idea is that because students do not know who will be called upon to answer, they all should be thinking hard in case it is them. But I am not con¡vinced most classrooms work like that. 
I think students use their intuition for probability to reason: 'With 30 of us in the class, the chance of sir or miss asking me is fairly low, so I will take my chances and not bother thinking about this one. And if the worst comes to the worst, I will start thinking if I get picked.' With Cold Call, the only student you know for certain has been thinking is the one called upon to answer the question. 
It is much more difficult to opt out of thinking when all students have to show their answers. Anyone showing a blank mini-white¡board or not holding up an ABCD card will be the first student who is questioned, so we can determine if this is due to a lack of under¡standing or a lack of effort. When this is done regularly, students realise that they may as well put the effort in initially, as not doing so will only be more hassle later. With mass participation, more stu¡dents think, so more students learn. 
2. Mass participation improves our checks for understanding 
Ifwe only hear responses from one or two students, and those students get the question correct, we may assume everyone else understands and so crack on. Likewise, if those one or two students struggle, we may assume this is a whole-class issue and hence pause the lesson to intervene. In either scenario, we may be wrong in our assumption and hence not respond appropriately to the needs of the majority of the class. By removing the biased sam¡ple, mass participation improves the validity and reliability of our checks for understanding. 
3. Mass participation helps us validate and celebrate the introverts I learned this during my conversation with English teacher, Jamie Thom. It is clear that a hands-up policy favours the more outgoing, confident students. But the flip side of that should not be under¡rated. Mass participation, particularly the non-verbal kind (mini¡whiteboards, ABCD cards) allows introverted students to shine. Jamie made the point that he is regularly blown away by the quality 
ofwriting his quieter students produce. This could be celebrated via a Warm Call or a quiet word of praise from teacher to student. 
Of course, as teachers, we have a duty to help students develop skills such as clear communication that will serve them well in the wider world. And that will come in time. But we have a greater re¡sponsibility to enable all our students to shine. 
4. Mass participation shows that everyone's answer is valued 
If you ask a question and then choose one or two students to answer it, there is no doubt that you value the answers of those students. But what about all the answers that you have not seen or heard? 
Imagine Emma has thought really hard about her answer, but then you pick Heena to answer it. Emma never gets to show you the fruits of her labour. From Emma's perspective, was there any point in thinking hard about the answer? 
But with mass participation, Emma gets to share her answer. And even if Emma's answer is not discussed this time, the fact that she has participated along with the rest of her classmates conveys the message that Emma has not wasted her efforts. 
5. With mass participation you can choose which answers to focus on This is a big one. If you only get the responses of one or two stu¡dents, then all you have to work with are those one or two answers. The chances are there are some really interesting answers among the 28 or so you have not seen. These may be examples ofkey mis¡conceptions that need dealing with, or different ways of solving the problem that you had not considered and are worth sharing. If you 
elicit more responses, then you can select the most useful ones to share. 
6. With mass participation you can bring students into the conversation 
The more responses you have, the better the discussion that follows. If all students have answered a question, they have some¡thing to discuss with the person next to them. If students have answered on mini-whiteboards, you can grab interesting responses and stick them under the visualiser, hold them up for the whole class to see, or simply record a selection on the board for further discussion. What's the same, what's different, which is the best an¡swer and why? 
Student responses are an incredibly valuable resource, and the more you have at your disposal, the more effective that resource can be. 
7. Mass participation keeps our teaching honest and responsive I have seen less experienced teachers (and I know I did this myself many times) ask one student the answer, and when they get it 
wrong ask another student, and if they get it correct then move on, assuming the first student must not have been listening properly. 
If we see all the responses of our students, then it becomes much harder to justify doing this. In the face of overwhelming evidence, there is no hiding place. If we can see that the vast majority of our students do not understand, we have to respond. 
8. With mass participation you can still target individual pupils 
One of the benefits of a technique like Cold Call is that you can direct questions to individual pupils. You might, for example, want to ask a certain child a question see if they have been listening, or to boost their confidence, or to challenge them, or because they usually struggle. Cold Call helps students feel seen. But you can still Cold Call following the use of a tool of mass participation (although technically it is a Warm Call ifyou've seen their response); it is just that you get all the added advantages alongside. 
So, should teachers ever use Cold Call? 
There is a danger that you read this tip and think 'right, no more Cold Call for me then'. But that is not my intention. Cold Call is a key part of a teacher's questioning toolkit for these six reasons: 
1. 
Cold Call is super speedy. 

2. 
Cold Call creates a good classroom dynamic, bouncing from stu¡dent to student. 

3. 
Longer explanations are well suited to oral responses, more so than mini-whiteboards or ABCD cards. 

4. 
Responding orally at length is a precursor to improving written communication. 

5. 
Cold Call is great to check students are listening and therefore keep everyone engaged: 'Leo, what did Jack just say?' 

6. 
Warm Calling following mass participation is really useful ¡'Aneesa, I see you think the answer is B, tell us why' or 'Tom, I can see you have written an answer of 17 on your mini-white¡board, please can you tell us how you got it?' 


You'll see in Tip 37 how Cold Calling 'switchers' is a key part of my approach to responsive teaching, and in Tip 66 how Cold Call can play a key role when varying the means of participation in the 'We Do' phase of an example. 
Ifyou can create the kind of classroom culture where students are thinking hard about the answer regardless ofwhether they believe they will be called upon, and students are confident enough to tell you that they don't understand the correct answer of the student you selected, then Cold Call works well. 
But, as a general rule, I think most teachers (myself very much included) could benefit from increasing the proportion ofthe questions they ask that elicit mass partici¡pation from their students. And exactly how to do that will be the focus of the rest of this chapter. 
The challenge -revisited! But first, I want to return to the task I set you at the start of this chapter -to write down the various means of participation you use in five key phases of a lesson. If you have not already done this, it is worthwhile taking the time to do so. 
With your lists in front ofyou, I would like you to place a label next to each phase of the lesson based on their relative participation ratio: 
ò 
H for high -you are confident that most if not all students are participating at that moment. 

ò 
M for medium -you estimate about half your students are participating. 

ò 
L for low-you can only be sure that a few ofyour students are participating. 


To help you decide, here is a good question to ask yourself: how easy would it be for a student to hide their lack of effort or under¡standing in this phase ofthe lesson, and you not find out about it? If it is difficult, then the participation ratio is high. If, however, a stu¡dent could sit there, not misbehaving, quietly nodding along, seem¡ing from a distance to be thinking hard and writing relevant things down, but you don't actually get a sense ofwhat they understand -and these students are many in number -then the participation ratio is lower. 
Here are a few thoughts from the classrooms I have taught in and visited. 
Retrieval Do Now/starter 
Here it can look like lots of students are participating as they busily write things down in their books. But what are they busily writ¡ing down? Is it the answer to the questions? If so, great. Or is it copying down the date, title, lesson objectives and the questions themselves? If so, they are not participating in thinking about the questions. 
Prerequisite knowledge check 
If there is one of these five lesson phases that requires a high participation ratio, it is this one. If we make assumptions about pre¡requisite knowledge based on the responses of one or two students, then we risk everything falling down when we attempt to build the new idea upon shaky foundations. 
Explanation/worked example 
This is the phase of the lesson where I see the lowest participation ratio. It tends to be dominated by teacher exposition, hands-up or Cold Call. If that is the case, how are we to know what our students have understood? 
Practice 
Often practice takes place in books, which have a high participation ratio so long as students are writing down things relevant to the question. But how do we then get a sense of their effort or under¡standing without calling upon every single child or collecting in the books? We will consider this more in Tip 12. 
Plenary/Exit Tickets 
A rushed plenary as students are packing away gives about as low a participation ratio as you can get. Exit Tickets taken seriously are on the other end of the spectrum. We will discuss Exit Tickets further in Tip 29. 
Look at your list. Put a big star next to any of these lesson phases with a low or medium participation ratio. As we discuss tools of mass participation throughout the rest of this chapter, consider whether one or more ofthese tools could help boost the participa¡tion ratio for that phase ofyour lesson. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider whether you need to increase the participation ratio for the questions you ask your students at specific phases of your lessons.

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip11

OVER TO YOU 
1.

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3. 
When are you going to try this idea for the first time? Please give the exact date and time. 

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
Big claim alert: based on the hundreds of lessons I have been lucky to watch over the last few years, I know of no other strategy that leads to a quicker improvement in the quality ofteaching and learn¡ing than boosting the participation ratio. 
As we discussed in Tip 11, our students are immediately more en¡gaged and thinking harder, and we are more knowledgeable about their understanding and hence more responsive to their needs. Dylan Wiliam, author and teacher educator, refers to the means by which we could elicit responses from a large number of our stu¡dents as tools ofmass participation. Tools of mass participation are as close to a magic bullet as exists in education. 
The rest of this chapter will be dedicated to making these tools as effective as possible, starting with the most powerful tool of all: mini-whiteboards. 
I am aware that as I write a section about mini-whiteboards, many primary colleagues will be shaking their heads thinking 'well, it's about time you caught on to them, mate'. I also know of many sec¡ondary colleagues who have been using mini-whiteboards for years. Essentially then, I am writing this chapter for me and anyone like me -the mini-whiteboard sceptics, dabblers or those arriving late to the party. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How regularly do you use mini-whiteboards in your teaching? 

ò 
What are some of the challenges you face? 


What's the tip? 
These days, mini-whiteboards are my favourite tool of mass partici¡pation. They are more shareable than exercise books and more flex¡ible than ABCD cards, allowing students to show their answers to open-response and multi-step exam questions, as well as to sketch any shape, graph or diagram the teacher may throw at them. There is clearly also something about the temporary nature of students' answers (a simple wipe and they are gone) compared to committing on paper that encourages otherwise reluctant students to write down an answer. 
What follows are 22 ideas that have improved my use of mini¡whiteboards in the classroom. In addition, I would once again rec¡ommend watching my conversation with Adam Boxer about mini¡whiteboards that is linked to in the resources. Adam goes deep! 
1. Plan how students will access the mini-whiteboards 
How your students will access their mini-whiteboards is easy to overlook as we focus our attention on the questions we will ask stu¡dents and the routines surrounding how they will respond. But ifwe can establish, practise and perfect a routine for accessing the mini¡whiteboards, precious time and precious attention are saved. 
Here are some questions to consider: 
1. 
Where will you keep your mini-whiteboards? 

2. 
Will you hand out the mini-whiteboards when they are needed, or hand them out at the start ofthe lesson? 

3. 
How will you hand out the mini-whiteboards: put them on students' desks before the lesson, hand them out at the door, put them at the end of rows, or assign the job ofhanding out the mini-whiteboards to one or two students? 

4. 
Does the answer to the previous question change ifyou teach in a different room than the lesson before? 

5. 
Will you hand out the pens and dusters at the same time? 

6. 
Do you want students to check their pen is working right away? What do they do ifit isn't? 

7. 
Where do students put the mini-whiteboards, pens and dusters during the lesson when they are not using them? 

8. 
How will you collect the equipment at the end? 


Different situations -rooms, classes, days of the week -may lead to different responses to these questions. But they are worth consider¡ing in advance. 
Answers to these questions are the single biggest barrier to mini¡whiteboard use I hear. 'Mini-whiteboards are just too much hassle.' I used to think exactly the same. All I can say is don't dismiss them until you have considered each of the tips below and ideas like the learner-generated examples (Tip 25) that work so much better with mini-whiteboards. Only then decide if finding ways to overcome the logistical challenges are worth it. 
For what it is worth, I am a fan ofplastic folders, each ofwhich contains a mini-whiteboard, pen and duster. These are placed in boxes by the door and students pick one up as they go past. The mini-whiteboards then stay in the folders on the desks until I tell students to get them out. Students tidy up themselves at the end and place the folders back in the box on the way out of the lesson. 
2. Make use ofboth sides ofthe mini-whiteboard 
It is possible to write on both sides of most mini-whiteboards, and this improves their versatility massively. Now you can ask students a complex, multistep procedure. Students can do their working out on one side, and then write their final answer nice and big on the other. Or if you ask students to vote for the answer to a diagnostic question, they can write their answer nice and big on one side, and make some notes on their reasoning on the other. 
When students show you their boards, you ask them to show the big answer side first. This allows you to clearly see a spread of the answers. If you then choose a child to question further, you can ask to see the other side of their board, and share this with the rest of the class if relevant. This simple idea prevents you from needing laser eyesight to pick out the final answer amidst a board full of working out. Limiting yourself to one answer per check for under¡standing also helps with this. 
Writing on both sides also helps the student. It can be daunting to explain your answer to the rest of the class or to your partner. But if students have their working out or reasoning written out, it acts as a support to help improve their verbal explanations. This may be particularly important for your quieter, less confident students. 
3. Circulate the room If you set a question for students to respond to on mini-whiteboards that is likely to take more than a minute to answer, it is a great op¡portunity to circulate the room. This provides an extra incentive for 
students to engage in the task, and also allows you to get a sense of understanding before you ask students to reveal their boards. 
Students tend to write bigger on mini-whiteboards than they would do in their exercise books. Therefore, you will probably be able to see three times the number of answers as you circulate than ifyou had to pause and crane your neck to read the tinier handwriting in their books. You may pick up on some misunderstanding at this stage and be able to start thinking about how you are going to respond. 
Adam Boxer suggests looking at the answers ofyour stronger stu¡dents first while circulating as they are likely to have made progress quicker and thus will have some content that you can either feed¡back on or use as you plan the next step. 
4. Hover your boards face down when you have your answer 
I learned this strategy from maths teacher, Dani Quinn. Previously I would say to students 'show me you are ready by putting your pens down'. Seems harmless enough. But there are three problems: 
1. 
It can be hard to see which students have put their pens down, especially at the back. 

2. 
If students put the pens down on top of their mini-whiteboards, then those dastardly things have a tendency to roll off onto the floor. 

3. 
It is all too tempting to take a cheeky glance at your neighbour's board ifit is sitting there invitingly. 


So now I ask students to hover their boards face down to show me they are ready. This solved all the above problems, and also saves about a second versus picking the mini-whiteboard up from the table when it is time for students to reveal their answers. And those seconds add up. 
One modification I sometimes make is to say 'hover your boards when I tell you'. The reason is it can be quite anxiety-inducing for a student who is working on a problem to be surrounded by peers excitedly hovering their boards ready to show the answer. In these instances -and it will be determined by the nature of the question or my knowledge of the class -I will leave a short gap, say 10 sec¡onds, between asking the question and giving the instruction 'okay, now hover your boards when you have your answer'. 
5. Start from the back You know when someone tells you something that is so obvious, and yet you have failed to realise it might be a good idea for the last 
18 years? Well, that happened to me with a tip via teacher, Matt Swain. 
When I was ready to see my students' responses, I would say 'three, two, one, show me your boards', and be immediately hit by a tidal wave of information. It would be hard to know where to start look¡ing, and with potentially 30+ boards ofinformation to observe, it was inevitable that some would get missed. 
But because ofMatt, that is no longer an issue. I now say 'okay, back row, show me your boards in three, two, one'. I can then process a smaller volume ofinformation, without the added issue ofhaving to peer over one board to see another. 
I can then thank the back row and say 'okay, middle row, your turn, show me your boards in three, two, one', and so on. 
Of course, this technique works with any desk configuration, from groups to the classic horseshoe, starting on the left of the room where needed. It is powerful because I am able to process the stu¡dents' responses in smaller chunks and so am less likely to miss anything, and the students towards the front are unable to see their classmate's boards (without making it obvious and turning around), so there is no added incentive to change their answer. 
6. Look at the answers ofyour weakest students first 
This is another idea I got from Adam Boxer. In an ideal world, you would be able to process all your students' responses. But even with the technique of starting from the back, this is difficult. Adam suggests that you direct your attention towards the boards ofyour weakest students (while, of course, seeming like you are looking at all responses). This should provide an accurate baseline as to where understanding in the class currently lies. 
7. Ifyou see a blank board, ask that student A blank board could be a signal of a lack of effort or a lack of understanding, and it is important to determine which. I always question students with a blank board first. A common response is 'I don't know'. In Tip 36, we discuss techniques to deal with such a response, but the key point is that a blank board accompanied by 'I don't know' is not the end of the conversation. Once students learn that a blank board will lead to further questioning, the proportion of those responses that are down to a lack of effort will decrease, as it is more hassle responding to the teacher than writing an answer down on their board. This improves the reliability ofyour 
check for understanding and promotes a classroom culture ofhigh expectations. 
8. Share student responses with the rest ofthe class 
As well as mini-whiteboards being great because you can see the responses of all your students, their portability and the fact that students tend to write bigger on them than they would in books makes them ideal to use for sharing individual student responses with the rest of the class. These could be examples ofmisconcep¡tions that you want to highlight and discuss, or correct work that you want other students to compare their work against. Holding the mini-whiteboard up is sufficient in smaller classes, but nothing beats getting students' work up on the big screen. In Tip 40 we will discuss my favourite piece of technology for doing this, as well as the pedagogy of sharing student answers. 
9. Discuss answers without attaching them to a student 
Mini-whiteboards are great for sharing students' responses, but sometimes that is not appropriate. Let's say you have asked stu¡dents to write the value of 3 + (-4) on their mini-whiteboards. You can see from David's board that he thinks the answer is -7. This is definitely an answer you want to discuss with the whole class, both to help David and to ensure all students have an opportunity to think hard about it. Now, if David is a confident student or you have established a culture of error (Tip 32), then it might be fine to say: 'David, I see you have gone for -7, please can you tell us why?' But I am sure we can all think of students for whom that would not be a good experience. In these cases, having observed David's answer and asked students to put their boards down, we can simply say: 'Okay, now one possible answer to this question is -7... ' and continue the discussion from there without needing to attach that answer to a student. 
10. Showcase excellent mini-whiteboard use 
Even though you might have told your students exactly how to use their mini-whiteboards in any given situation -write big, show your working out, circle your answer, etc. -it will be no surprise that this will not have registered with all students, especially in the early days of developing students' proficiency in mini-whiteboard use. 
A good way to overcome this is to showcase excellent mini-white¡board use. As you see your students' responses to a question, be on the lookout for examples ofboards looking just the way you hoped. After you have discussed the actual responses, share these exam¡ples of excellence with the rest of the class, again either by holding them up or showcasing them on the big screen: 
'Okay everyone, just before we move on, I want you all to 
take a look at this. Zach's mini-whiteboard is perfect. His 
writing is big and clear, he has split his board into two as 
I asked, and he has circled his answers. Well done, Zach! 
Now, the next time I ask you to show me your boards, I 
want to see everyone up to the same standard.' 
11. Have a departmental mini-whiteboard policy 
When Dani Quinn was head of maths at Michaela Community School, mini-whiteboards played a key role in her department's classroom practice. Dani and her team invested a great deal of time and energy thinking about all the tiny details ofwhiteboard practice that could be tweaked and improved, teaching these explicitly to the students and practising them regularly until they became routine. 
Below are some ofmy favourite guidelines from the handbook that every student was given. 
ò 
We place the boards right on top of our work so we don't waste time. 

ò 
As soon as we take the pen out, we put the lid at the top, so it doesn't roll away. 

ò 
We keep the boards secret-close to our bodies -and answer on our own, without looking at others. This is so that the teacher knows who they need to help. If you copy an answer from some¡one else, or even look at theirs to check, you won't get the help you need to get it right. Everyone needs a little extra help some¡times. No one will ever shame you or blame you for getting an answer wrong. 

ò 
We don't start writing until the teacher says 'Go!' We write in large, clear writing so the teacher can clearly see our answers. 

ò 
When we have written our answer, we gently hover our mini¡whiteboards face down above the desk. This helps the teacher see when people are ready to show their answers. 

ò 
We hold boards above our heads in unison when the teacher says 'three, two, one, show!' We use both hands to hold the board, so it doesn't wobble. 

ò 
Even ifwe have not finished our answer, we hold it up with ev¡eryone else. If you don't know how to start, put a question mark. 

ò 
We wait to be told to put our whiteboards down. 


Notice the detail-I would never think to include guidance on something apparently as trivial as what to do with the pen lid, and yet how much time has been lost to a rogue pen lid tumbling from a desk and bouncing out of sight around a chair leg? Also, notice the justifications provided to students for each of the guidelines, so stu¡dents know why we are asking them to do this. 
Having a policy that every teacher sticks to, and one that is detailed enough to provide a clear set of guidelines to follow, allows consis¡tency and good practice to spread across the department. This is especially important if teachers share classes or in the transition to a new teacher. 
12. Use mini-whiteboards as much as possible 
This might be the most obvious thing you have ever heard, but the more students use mini-whiteboards, the better they get. Actually, let me clarify that-the more students use mini-whiteboards using the expectations you have established and practised, the better they get. After all, practice doesn't make perfect, practice makes permanent. 
The first time students use mini-whiteboards, or if they only use them once in a blue moon (when the teacher is being observed, for example), students find it hard to resist the temptation to doodle things on the board not related to the content of the lesson -a smi¡ley face ifyou are lucky, another part ofthe body ifyou are not. 
Once students realise that mini-whiteboards are just another tool they use in lessons in the same way they use their exercise books, the novelty soon wears off. 
13. Use mini-whiteboards to enhance paired work 
We will discuss paired work explicitly in Tip 15. But here it is worth considering how mini-whiteboards can make paired work more pro¡ductive. Asking students to write down an answer independently on their mini-whiteboards before a paired discussion gives a focus to that discussion. It also stops students from having to remember everything in their working memories so they can better attend to what their partner is saying. I also find that mini-whiteboards work better than writing in books in this regard because writing tends to be larger and there is less of a requirement to be neat. Students can swap boards, rub out, annotate over and edit in ways they would either not be able to or not be prepared to in their exercise books. Similarly, ifyou choose to engage in a paired discussion without that period of independent contemplation, the mini-whiteboard can be a great medium for collaboration, with students taking turns to write down their thoughts, editing and annotating each other's ideas, and so on. 
14. Use mini-whiteboards to enhance book work when circulating 
Most lessons contain some form ofwriting in exercise books. That makes a lot of sense if it is an extended answer that would not fit onto a mini-whiteboard or it is during a period of extended inde¡pendent practice where students are all working at different paces. But the issue with book work is that it can be very difficult to check for understanding in the lesson. Circulating the room is good to do, but students write small in books, or you have to ask them to move their arms or turn back a page. 
But mini-whiteboards can help. Let's imagine students are working through a set of practice questions in their books. Each question requires working out. Students can do all of this in their books, but then copy their final answers to each question on their mini-white¡boards. Then, when circulating the room with your model answers in hand, you have a quick way of seeing if students are on the right track without needing to disturb them. And if you spot a wrong answer, you can ask the student to turn to the working in their book and investigate further. 
15. Use mini-whiteboards to enhance book work when checking for whole-class understanding 
Mini-whiteboards can support book work in another way. Imagine students are working through a set of 10 questions in their exercise books. Everyone is working at a different pace, but you want to do a quick check for understanding with a high participation ratio. You can stop the class and say: 
'Can everyone just quickly jot down their final answer 
to question three -not your working, just your answer ¡
nice and big on your mini-whiteboards ... Hover face down 
when you are ready ... Okay, three, two, one, show me your 
boards.' 
You now have a snapshot of whole-class understanding, putting you in a great position to respond. You could Warm Call a few stu¡dents with different answers and ask them to explain their working. You could choose to go through the answer yourself and then set an immediate follow-up question for students to do on their mini¡whiteboards. Or you could let the students crack on. 
This book-whiteboard combo works really well during a retrieval Do Now. Imagine you have put four questions on the board for students to be getting on with as they enter the room. Everyone is arriving at different times and working at different speeds, so re¡sponding in books makes the most sense. But then, when you are ready to go through the answers, asking students to quickly copy their final answer to question one on their mini-whiteboards and then show you when ready allows you to quickly assess whole-class understanding, rather than the usual 'what did you get, Ben?' that 
usually happens. Students then rub out, copy their answer for ques¡tion two, and so on. 
16. Ifyou can, get A3 mini-whiteboards 
You will not regret it. Obviously, these allow your students to write more on them, or write bigger and clearer. In maths, the A3 mini¡whiteboards work especially well at A-level where the questions typically demand longer-form written responses. 
While we are at it, you can also get mini-whiteboards with axes drawn on them for graph work, and different colours to support vis¡ually impaired students. 
17. Use mini-whiteboards before books for less confident students 
Have you taught any students who only want to write correct an¡swers down in their exercise books? This is, ofcourse, not ideal, and in the long term we would hope to create a classroom culture where mistakes are seen as a normal part ofthe learning process, and cor¡recting a mistake in your book is perfectly fine. 
But a short-term fix might be to allow students to work on mini¡whiteboards. Here mistakes are less permanent. Then, when they have checked their answer with their partner or you, they can copy it up nice and neat into their books. 
You would not want all students doing this, of course. But it might just be an important step in increasing the written participation of less confident students. 
18. Take mini-whiteboards as seriously as you take exercise books. 
If you are going to go for it with mini-whiteboards, you don't want anything holding you back.Just as you would expect to provide every student with an exercise book, you should also expect to provide every student with an intact mini-whiteboard, a pen that works, and a decent duster or cloth. And ifwhiteboards break, pens run out, or dusters get lost, then their replacement should be as seamless as when a student fills up or loses their exercise book. And do this throughout the year -so many times I have seen depart¡ments go big on mini-whiteboards in September, but by February there are so few pens, and the boards are in such a state of disre¡pair, that they are unusable. 
This might require convincing the person who controls the budget to set some aside for these provisions, but ifyou can demonstrate the power of mini-whiteboards (together with the potential saving on photocopying), then they are nothing short of an educational bargain. 
19. Train your pupils to not press too hard with whiteboard pens 
A quick one here from Chris Such, but a potential time and money saver. Mini-whiteboard pens can be ruined quickly if students press too hard on them. Before you know it, the nib has been flattened to the point of uselessness. Training students not to press as hard saves the nibs, ensuring the pen keeps a fine point and hence lasts much longer. Modelling and showcasing excellence are once again the key to this becoming part ofyour students' routines. 
20. Keep a box containing the lids of any pens you throw away 
Forget the pedagogy. Now we are into the stuff that really matters. This is from primary teacher, Emma Turner. How many times do those pesky pen lids go missing? And when they do, that nib is going to dry out pretty quickly. By keeping lids from discarded pens in a box, you never need to worry about this again! 
21. There's using mini-whiteboards, and then there's using mini-whiteboards 
I have visited classrooms where students regularly respond toques¡tions on mini-whiteboards, but they never have the opportunity to show their answers to the teacher. Instead, the teacher embarks upon a series of one-to-one questions -'Jamal, what did you put for this question? Lucy, did you put the same?' In such instances, the mini-whiteboard is being used as a substitute for an exercise book. As discussed above, the mini-whiteboard is a good substitute as students seem more willing to have a go at a question when they know they can rub out the answer, and they tend to write bigger on mini-whiteboards, making it easier for the teacher to see their work as they circulate the room. But to not then ask students to all show their answers at the same time is to miss the biggest reason to use mini-whiteboards: to check all students' effort and understanding. With all students' answers held high, the teacher can ensure every student is participating and thinking, get an accurate sense of where students are at, choose interesting answers to focus on, and instigate a paired discussion. To keep mini-whiteboards rooted to desks is like buying a MacBook Pro1 and using it as a tea tray. 
22. How to respond ifSLT want evidence in books Teachers often tell me that they would like to use mini-whiteboards more, but the problem is there is not enough evidence of work in students' books to satisfy the senior leadership team (SLT). There 
are a few responses to this, many not suitable for printing in a book like this. Here are three that might not get you fired: 
1. 
'Please tell me what exercise books are used for.' It may be different in other subjects, but I am not at all convinced that students learn much in maths by looking back in their exercise books. Possibly they look back at worked examples when re¡vising or practising. But answers to practice questions? I don't think so. What matters there is how much students understand in the moment, and effective mini-whiteboard use can help with that through increased participation, better checks for under¡standing, and facilitating more productive collaboration. It is interesting to note that many of the highest performing schools I visit are not overly fussed about what goes in books and what doesn't. The key-of course -is selecting the best medium to support the thinking you want students to do. 

2. 
'Please show me the evidence.' Can SLT find any evidence to support the view that there needs to be a certain amount ofwork in exercise books? I'll tell you now, they cannot. 

3. 
'Please take part in one of my lessons.' This is the best one. Invite them to take part in one ofyour lessons where you will be using mini-whiteboards. Not just visit your lesson, but take part. When they see how much good, hard thinking is required with a skilled use of mini-whiteboards, with no opportunity to opt out, you might find they are more supportive. This works especially well if they are not a maths specialist, so they can see there is no hiding place like there can be in a lesson dominated by book work, where student work remains rooted to their desks. 


Oh, and my ranting about copying things down into books returns in Tip 65. You have been warned...

For any phase that you have identified as having a low or medium participation ratio, could increasing and improving your use of mini-whiteboards be a solution?

What's the problem? 
One potential issue with using mini-whiteboards as a tool of mass participation is that it is not immediately obvious ifthere is a con¡sensus among your students in terms of the answer they have cho¡sen, or a split in opinion. 
This problem can be reduced by training students to write their an¡swers big and clear, and to start from the back, but you still have the problem that when using mini-whiteboards with the same white background and black ink, it can be hard to distinguish and deci¡pher visual data in the few seconds we usually allocate for this. 
Added to this is the fact that mini-whiteboards need to be written on. Students will get quicker at this over time, but seconds tick away as we wait for all our students to have written their responses. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What alternatives to mini-whiteboards do you use to elicit mass participation? 

ò
What are some ofthe challenges you face? 


What's the tip? 
Step forward voting systems. There are lots of different types ofvoting systems: 
ò 
Fingers 

ò 
Thumbs 

ò 
True/false cards 

ò 
Red, amber, and green cards 

ò 
ABCD cards 

ò 
Electronic voting systems. 


None of these has the same levels ofversatility as mini-white¡boards. Indeed, they are only suited to responding to questions with a finite number ofpossible responses. But if, like me, a significant proportion ofyour questioning is multiple choice, then such voting systems can become a powerful tool. 
Here are five ideas to help mass participation via voting systems be as effective as possible. 
1. Ensure different student responses are easy to distinguish 

Have a look at the two images above. The one on the left shows stu¡dents responding to a diagnostic question using mini-whiteboards, whereas the one on the right shows students responding using ABCD cards. Notice how quickly you can see there is a convergence in the class on the right, whereas you need to strain your eyes to get a sense of the distribution of answers in the class on the left. 
The only reason you can observe the consensus in the class on the right so quickly is because those ABCD cards are different colours. So, it is worth splashing out and using the colour photocopier, and even going a bit 2005 and whipping out the laminator to produce a class set ofABCD cards that are easily distinguishable and durable. 
Students indicating true with a thumb up or false with a thumb down is pretty easy to distinguish. But personally, I find it hard to distinguish between two fingers for a Band three fingers for a C in a large class. Students also have a tendency to go for 'B-and-a-half', revealing two fully protruding fingers and one curled but poised to spring to life should the owner see other students who have gone for a C. It is a bit harder to change your answers with cards. 
So, if I ask a true/false question, I tend to use thumbs. But if there are more than two options, I find ABCD cards the way to go. 
2. Avoid the tactical delay A classic issue when you encourage mass participation is the 'tactical delay'. This is the few seconds between the instruction to 
show your answers and when certain students actually reveal their answers. 
The tactical delay is a particular problem when using cards or thumbs (versus mini-whiteboards) for a few reasons: 
1. 
With thumbs and cards, the choices are visually distinct, so it is all too easy to notice the answers of those students to the side of you. 

2. 
The coloured back of the card reveals your choice in a way the back of a mini-whiteboard does not. So students in rows can see the choices ofthe students in front ofthem as well as to the side. The same is true with thumbs. 

3. 
You can change your answer with a thumb or a voting card much faster than having to rub out and scrawl down a different answer on a mini-whiteboard. 


What often plays out is something like this... 
Jacob thinks the answer is C, but he is not 100% sure. Out of the corner ofhis eye, Jacob notices Josie has gone for B. Josie is always right. So, just before he reveals his answer, in an act of close-up magic that David Blaine would be proud of, Jacob's C quickly becomes a B. 
The obvious issue with this is we do not get a chance to see what Jacob -or any other student participating in the tactical delay-ac¡tually thinks. We end up with the very biased sample of responses we are attempting to avoid. 
Here are two solutions to the tactical delay I have seen work well: 
1. Use cards, but disguise the back 
The students at St Alban's school in the image above have the ABCD cards stuck to the front of their books (along with Tom Bennett's behaviour expectations in the middle). The orientation of their book signals their vote. Crucially, you cannot see how your classmates in front ofyou have voted, so the number of potential influencers falls to the same level as you would get with mini-whiteboards. 
When I first heard about this, my initial impression was that it would be difficult for the teacher to distinguish between the choices. But, as you can hopefully see in the picture, it is pretty easy -here a clear consensus around answer A has formed. And you have the extra advantage that you don't need to hand out or collect a set ofABCD cards each lesson, or risk students' own loose sets getting lost. 
You can achieve the same effect by making the backs ofyour ABCD cards plain (instead of printing them double-sided), or using tech¡nology such as Flickers where it is not possible to tell what another student has voted. 
2. Heads Down, Votes Up 
Students are told to think about their answer, then lower their heads so they can't see anyone else, and then when the teacher says, they reveal their answers by holding up their cards or thumbs. 
This technique works just as well with mini-whiteboards as it does with ABCD cards or thumbs. Once the teacher has taken in all the information -making a brief note on a scrap of paper the names of students who have gone for each answer -the students are told to put their answers down and lift their heads back up. 
This may all sound a bit daft and unnecessary, but I have found it really does increase the number of students willing to participate openly and honestly when responding to a question. Any peer pres¡
sure or compulsion to conform is removed. Moreover, it makes the 
subsequent discussion much more fruitful. 
Look at this image again, and think about the girl on the back row, Jenny. 
Jenny thinks the answer to a question is A. Without Heads Down, Votes Up, when Jenny votes she will see everyone else thinks the answer is C. Assuming Jenny hasn't managed to quickly hide her choice in the face ofoverwhelming evidence, when the teacher then asks her to share her reason, Jenny may well clam up and refuse to respond, assuming she is wrong. If Jenny is wrong, I want to hear her explanation so I can help her. And ifJenny is right, I want to hear her explanation so I can use it to support other students. 
But if I use the technique of Heads Down, Votes Up, then Jenny will never know she is in the minority, and her explanation is more likely to be forthcoming. 
3. Have ABCD cards in student planners Handing out voting cards can be a bit of a faff. If you cannot stick them to the front of students' books, then perhaps you can organise to have the cards printed in students' planners. That way they al¡ways have the cards with them (or at least, they should always have them with them), and less time is wasted handing them out or col¡
lecting them in. You can also get mini-whiteboards into planners so your students are fully kitted out. 
4. Use voting systems alongside mini-whiteboards 
An ideal combination of tools to use for answering a multiple¡choice question is a mini-whiteboard and a voting system. As we discussed above, something like ABCD cards have the significant advantage that even a bespectacled teacher like myself can quickly discern whether there is a consensus of opinion or a divergence when faced with a sea of cards held aloft, whereas a poorly scrawled 'B' in tiny font on a mini-whiteboard is much more difficult to read. But mini-whiteboards allow students to do working out on them. So now, when I ask a multiple-choice question, I encourage my stu¡dents to do their working out on their mini-whiteboards but show me their answers using the ABCD cards. I can then say something like: 'Sarah, I see you have gone for A. Please can you show us your mini-whiteboard so we can see your working.' 
5. Consider not using technology to collect students' responses Let's end with a controversial one. Now, I am a massive fan of technology. I regularly use Desmos to support my students' under¡standing of graphing and GeoGebra for all things geometrical. But I never, ever use technology to collect in students' votes, despite lots ofincredible technologies like Flickers out there. There are three reasons for this: 
1. 
Technology issues. Unfortunately, technology can let us down. Hardware, software or Wi-Fi issues have no doubt plagued us all as teachers. 

2. 
Time. Even when technology is working well, it still takes more time to collect students' responses using technology than using an offline means. With Flickers you are only relying on one piece of technology -the teacher's smartphone or tablet. But even then you need to slowly scan the room, go back and scan any cards not picked up by the camera, ask Tom to hold his card up a bit higher, wait a few seconds for the data to process, and then read and interpret the results. Compare that to saying 'three, two, one, show me your cards ... thank you ... right, Anna, why did you pick A?' 

3. 
Distorted message. This is the big one. The message I say to my stu¡dents is, 'This is a very low-stakes assessment. I am asking this question so I can find out what you know and what you don't know, purely so I can help you. So I want you to be open and honest with your responses.' I think that message gets distorted if students then see me collecting the data. Why is he recording our answers? What is he going to do with them? Will they affect our grade? Will it go on our reports? As soon as students sense the stakes rising, the incentive to opt out for fear ofbeing wrong or find a way to check their answer against someone else's be¡fore revealing starts to increase. As a result, the validity and ef¡fectiveness of our check for understanding falls. 


Voting systems challenge

What's the problem? 
As I have argued over the last two tips, using tools of mass partic¡ipation has many advantages over one-to-one questioning tech¡niques, such as Cold Call. However, one issue is that it takes more time to elicit mass participation via mini-whiteboards and voting systems than it does to fire questions at individual students. 
How can we get the benefits of speed and mass participation? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
How regularly do you ask students to respond verbally and at once to aquestion? 

ò
What are some ofthe challenges you face? 


What's the tip? 
Step forward, Call and Response. 
I discussed Call and Response with Charlie Burkitt as it is a key technique used at Michaela Community School across all subjects. It is the means of mass participation that felt the most unusual for me in my current practice. But as I often see Call and Response in operation when I visit high-performing schools across the country, I have been happy to experiment with it over the last few months, and I am now a huge fan. 
The idea is that the teacher says the start of a statement, for exam¡ple, 'The interior angles of a triangle ... ' and then the class responds in unison with ' ... add up to 180 degrees.' 
Of course, you cannot tell for definite if everyone replies -a proficient lip syncer or a strategic hand over the mouth are ways students could opt out-but a dissenting voice or two are easier to hear. And while Call and Response is not as versatile as the other means of mass participation we have looked at, it is quick, fun, and different. It can generate a real sense of cohesion and belonging in a class. And as advocates of cognitive science will appreciate, if students can automate the facts most suited to Call and Response, then they can draw upon them to solve complex problems without taking any priceless attention away. 
Here are nine ideas to make Call and Response as effective as possible. 
1. Make wait time explicit and non-verbal 
Allowing adequate time between asking the question and asking for the answer is important for all forms of questioning, but it is partic¡ularly important for Call and Response. 
If you say, 'a nine-sided shape is called a ... ', you can guarantee a student will shout out too soon and blow both the check for under¡standing and the opportunity for the rest of the class to retrieve the answer. 
Instead, try: 'Okay, we are going to do Call and Response for this next question, so think first in silence and wait for my countdown... a nine-sided shape is called a ... three, two, one... ' 
Doing the countdown in silence by holding up your fingers is also useful, so as to not disrupt the flow between the two parts of the statement. 
2. Use Call and Response for short statements 
Call and Response works best for short statements. These are often the kinds of things that are suited to knowledge organisers. You don't want to use Call and Response when the response is too long ('The way we solve simultaneous equations is ... '), or when there is more than one possible correct answer ('To solve an equation we...') 
In maths, statements such as the following work well: 
ò 
Prime numbers... have exactly two factors. 

ò 
Perimeter is ... the distance around a 2D shape. 

ò 
Median is ... the middle number of an ordered list. 


3. Use Call and Response for strategies 
Call and Response is also well suited to remembering certain strate¡gies. Things like: 
ò 
Right-angled triangle ... Pythagoras or trig. 

ò 
Quadratic equations... set equal to zero. 

ò 
Drawing cumulative frequency ... plot the upper bound. 


4. Use Call and Response for rules and expectations 
Call and Response can also be used to help students remember ¡and to reinforce -classroom rules and expectations. Things like: 
ò 
When someone else is talking ... look, listen, think. 

ò 
Handing in homework... open at the correct page. 

ò 
Hardest job in the classroom ... helping someone else. 


These can be used at the start of a lesson, at the end of a lesson, as a reminder when things are not going well, or -as we discussed in Tip 7 -as an acknowledgement that things are going well. 
5. Add Call and Response statements to knowledge organisers Seeing as Call and Response statements are best suited to knowl¡edge organisers, it makes sense to add them to knowledge organis¡ers. Having two columns, with the first part of the statement on the left and the second part of the statement on the right, works well. In addition to self-quizzing, students can also be encouraged to quiz each other, with one student saying the first part of the statement, and the second finishing it off. This also works well in reverse, with a student saying the answer and the other student having to remember what the statement is. Quizzing in this way can also be 
used by keen parents who want to help their students with their learning. 
6. Use Call and Response for a relevant prior knowledge check 
Call and Response can be used at any stage of the learning episode, but I have found it to be particularly effective to recall relevant prior knowledge. So, ifyou are teaching a unit ofwork on circle theorems, and you realise there are going to be lots of triangles knocking around, you might start the unit ofwork by saying, 'The interior an¡gles of a triangle ... ' 
7. Use Call and Response during the We Do phase Another place Call and Response works well is during the middle phase of a worked example commonly referred to as We Do. At this stage, students have hopefully picked up some key knowledge from 
the teacher modelling phase of the worked example. Hence, you can often fire off a question and call upon students to respond in 
.
unison. 
In this instance, it does not need to be a statement. It could be a question with either a finite number of answers, or one that re¡quires a short reply. 
For example, after a worked example on expanding double brackets, you may want a Call and Response for: 
ò 
This first thing we are going to do is multiply this x by... x 

ò 
x multiplied by x gives us... x squared 

ò 
Is our expression fully simplified? ... No. 


In Tip 66 I have scripted an example We Do exchange so you can see how Call and Response works alongside other means ofparticipa¡tion such as Cold Call, Partner Talk and mini-whiteboards. 
8. Plan three Call and Response statements for each topic 
One maths department I worked with chose to look at an upcoming Year 7 topic and use departmental meeting time to plan three state¡ments that all teachers could use Call and Response for. This was the pilot. It went well, so it became something colleagues planned for each topic, including the three statements in the scheme of work. Before you know it, Call and Response has become a norm that the students and teachers buy into and look forward to using. 
9. Use Call and Response outside oflessons 
This one sounds a bit cringey, but it builds on the culture men¡tioned above. I have visited a few schools where a teacher will pass a student in the corridor and say, 'The digits of numbers in the 3¡times table ... ' and without breaking stride, the student has replied ' ... add up to a multiple of 3.' 
The most extreme example ofthis I saw was when a teacher walked onto the yard at lunch, right into the middle of a game of football, and shouted, 'If you do it to one side of an equation... ' and in uni¡son, the players shouted back ' ... you do it to the other side' and then resumed their game. 
The first time you try this, it could well sink like a lead balloon. But the more you stick at it, and the more unashamedly positive you are, the more contagious it becomes. 
Call and Response challenge

3. 
Explanation/worked example

Practice

Plenary/Exit Ticket.

Consider using Call and Response to assess understanding ofshortfacts in an engaging way.

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip14

OVER TO YOU 
1. 
How does this tip fit into your current practice? Ido exactly that Ido something like that Ido nothing like that 

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3.

What's the problem? 
'Okay, discuss the answer with your partner.' 
It seems so simple, right? What could possibly go wrong? 
Well, you only need to listen in to som e of those conversations, or consider the responses when you ask a pair a follow-up question, to know that there is room for improvement. 
Having a positive, productive discussion about a concept that students may not be secure with and may have differing views on is a complex skill. So, just as we are likely to support our students as they develop their proficiency with the content we teach, it makes sense to do the same with the discussions students have with their partners. 
I will refer to those paired discussions as Partner Talk. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How regularly do you use paired discussions in yourteaching? 

ò 
What aresome of the challenges you face? 


What's the tip? 
Partner Talk differs from the three other tools of mass participation we have looked at so far in this chapter because you are unlikely to have the opportunity to see or hear everyone's response. So instead, the focus shifts to ensuring that everyone participates and that par¡ticipation is as effective as possible. 
Here are 15 ideas to do just that: 
1. Give students enough time to think individually first 
Two things I often observe with paired discussions: one partner dominating or the first few seconds wasted with lots of urns and ahs as students start their thinking. Both issues can be addressed by ensuring students have time to think independently, in silence, before the paired discussion. This allows students to benefit from the act ofretrieval, and gives both parties the best chance to arrive at the conversation with something useful to contribute. 
Of course, how long this thinking time needs to be is a key question. In Tip 22 we dive deep into the concept of Wait Time, but -spoiler alert -research suggests that teachers do not give enough of it. Finding a way for students to indicate they are ready is key here. If students are answering on mini-whiteboards, they could hover their boards to indicate they are ready, or raise their thumb if using another form of participation. Then, when you can see enough stu¡dents are ready, instigate Partner Talk. 
2. Ask students to write down their answer or key points 
We know working memory capacity is limited. If students are chal¡lenged to listen to their partner while simultaneously trying to hold on to their answer and explanation, something is going to give, and it is usually the listening that suffers. What results is a rather mean¡ingless exchange, where one student speaks while the other tries to block out that noise so they can remember what they wanted to say. 
An easy way to avoid this is to give students the opportunity to write down their answer and explanation before Partner Talk be¡
gins. Mini-whiteboards work well for this. With their ideas written down, working memory is unburdened, so they can dedicate more attention to listening to their partner. 
3. Use mini-whiteboards to support Partner Talk As well as being a tool of mass participation in their own right (Tip 12), and a great place for students to record their own thoughts prior to Partner Talk, mini-whiteboards can also help support the paired discussion that follows. Students can write nice and big, place both boards between them during their discussions, annotate and edit each other's work, and there aren't the constraints ofneatness, ownership or permanence that are associated with writing in exer¡
cise books. A mini-whiteboard is a great tool for collaboration; an exercise book is not. 
4. State who talks first in advance 
This sounds a bit daft, but precious seconds can be lost as students either begin talking over each other, or fall silent expecting the other to speak first. Announcing in advance that the person on the left goes first is a good one, or the person closest to the door (door¡side partner), or the person with the name first in the alphabet (assuming figuring this out is not more cognitively demanding than answering the question itself). 
5. Give a conversation prompt Knowing how to kick-start Partner Talk can be challenging, espe¡cially if this is new to students. A simple suggestion such as, 'The person on the left starts by saying: I think the answer is _ be¡
cause... ' can help add an important structure to the conversation. We discuss other verbal scaffolds in Tip 27. 
6. Announce when it is time to switch speaker 
'Okay, now it is time for the person on the right to speak and the person on the left to listen.' 
This ensures all students get a chance to share their thoughts as well as listen to their partner's. 
7. Ensure students have something to discuss Once both students have shared their answers and explanations, 
what do they do next? Again, a structure can be helpful. 'Okay, now it is time to discuss: 
ò 
If your answers are different, can you understand why, and can you come to an agreement on which is correct? 

ò 
If your answers are the same, are your explanations also the same, and what is the clearest way to explain this to someone who doesn't understand it yet?' 


Anticipating different scenarios ensures no pair can sit there in si¡lence as they have nothing to talk about. 
8. Reinforce the importance oflistening 
Having removed some of the burden on working memory by asking students to write down what they want to say, next we have to ensure that they are actively listening to their partner and not just waiting for a gap in the conversation. The easiest way to achieve this is, when questioning some of the pairs after Partner Talk, ask them not for their answer and explanation, but for the answer and explanation oftheir partner's. If this is done regularly, students learn pretty quickly that listening is just as important as talking. 
9. Reinforce the idea ofrehearsal 
Partner Talk is often referred to as the pair component ofThink¡Pair-Share. I prefer the phrase Think-Rehearse-Share. The conversa¡tions students have with their partner are always a rehearsal for something else. 
The discussions may be a bridge between thinking of an answer yourself and sharing it with the rest of the class. Having had an opportunity to test-drive their answer and explanation in front of a pair of friendly ears may cause students to refine their words based on the feedback of their partner. This puts them in a much stronger position to share their answer and explanation with the rest of the class if called upon. Or these discussions may be a rehearsal before students are asked to work independently on a task, so it is their chance to test out their understanding and seek to plug any gaps. 
Encourage students to think of Partner Talk as an opportunity to rehearse so they make the most of it. 
10. Use Partner Talk following a check for understanding 
Imagine you have asked a question and elicited mass participation from your students, perhaps on mini-whiteboards or ABCD cards. You can see that around half ofthe class is correct. Instead of diving into a whole-class discussion or teacher explanation, this is an ideal time for a bit ofPartner Talk. 
We can use the same prompts as before: 
'Right, I can see we have a split in opinion around the room. 
Exciting! I want the person closest to the door to share their 
answer and explanation, and then listen to their partner's. 
Ifyour answers are different, can you understand why, and 
can you come to an agreement on which is correct? If your 
answers are the same, are your explanations also the same, 
and what is the clearest way to explain this to someone 
who doesn't understand it yet?' 
You can then ask students to show their answers again, changing them ifthey want. The chances are the proportion of correct an¡swers will increase significantly. In these cases, Cold Call a student who changed their answer and ask them to explain to the class why they did so. If there is still a significant split of opinions, you can step in and explain the correct answer. 
11. Better too short than too long 
Try to end paired discussions at the point when the bubbliness, en¡thusiasm and engagement feel at their peak, as opposed to waiting until things have started to quieten down and students' attention has drifted somewhere else. With Partner Talk being a rehearsal for something, this means students can take that energy and enthusi¡asm into the next phase of the lesson, whether it is a willingness to contribute to the whole-class discussion, or eagerness to crack on with their independent work. Doug Lemov uses the analogy of end¡ing the discussion on the crest of a wave. 
12. Be careful not to get in the way 
Have you ever wandered over to a pair of students locked in intense discussion, only to find that as soon as they sense you nearby the conversation dries up? Or the conversation loses its flow and be¡comes a stilted performance? 
We want to hear what our students are discussing, so we can check understanding, support, or share their thoughts with the rest of the class. But we don't want to get in the way. There is no easy solution to this, but it is worth being aware of. Things tend to improve the more students realise that you listening to their conversation is the norm. You can make this explicit by telling them in advance that you will be circulating and ensuring they continue their discussions when you are near. There may also be positions in your classroom where you can stand and still hear, but your presence is not quite so intrusive. 
13. Combine Partner Talk with Cold Call 
The beauty of Partner Talk is that it can be combined with Cold Call. So, after students have had their discussion, the teacher can say, 'Tom, what do you think the answer is?' And ifTom responds with the classic 'I don't know', then you can say, 'Well, what did you discuss with Michelle?' Partner Talk ensures all students have a chance to participate and increases the efficiency and effectiveness of subsequent questioning. 
14. Ask questions to find the best paired discussions to share 
Instead of Cold Calling students following Partner Talk, you can be more strategic in your hunt for good discussions to share with the class. I find the following three questions useful to ask: 
ò 
Put your hand up ifyou disagree with the answer ofyour partner. 

ò 
Put your hand up ifyou changed your mind during your discus¡


.
s1on. 
ò Put your hand up ifyour partner said something you found interesting. 
And start the discussion from there. 
15. Consider using the 4-2 approach for longer tasks 
If my students are working on a longer task (anything six minutes or over), then I like to use what I call the 4-2 approach. This involves four minutes of silent, independent work in books, on worksheets, or on mini-whiteboards, followed by two minutes of paired discus¡sion. I find four minutes is sufficient time to make some progress so students have something to talk about, without being so long that students get frustrated if stuck. And two minutes is long enough to have a useful discussion, but short enough to keep that discussion focused. And then you can repeat the cycle as many times as you need. During this, I will walk around listening and supporting (but not getting in the way), and then call upon some pairs to share the fruits of their discussions. 
Partner Talk challenge

Tip 16 -Six ideas to improve group work 

Tip 12  Tip 13  Tip 14
