Tip 15 Pa rtner Talk  Tip 16 Groupwork  Tip 17 The holy trinity  

What's the problem? 
No point beating around the bush -for the last 10 years, I have pretty much avoided all group work in my lessons. I love silent, in¡dependent work. I am a huge fan of Partner Talk. But as soon as the number ofparticipants grows beyond two, I am out. 
Why? 
Well, firstly it is my own bias. I am not a fan ofworking in groups myself. This is perhaps best encapsulated by the following pie chart about what we learn from group work. 

The information How to work withother people How to do entire projects on my own How much I hate people 
But my view on group work is also born out of my experiences of trying it in the classroom. Sure, I played the game when Ofsted visited, and all of a sudden my nice rows of tables were shunted together into groups of four. But I could never get it right. Groups would invariably consist of students either messing about, free-rid¡ing, or dominating. The result was always that one or two children 
had done the majority of the work, and the other members did not have a clue what was going on. 
So, for many years, independent work and paired work were more than enough for me, thank you very much. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How regularly do you use group workin your teaching? 

ò 
What are some of the challenges you face? 


What's the tip? 
Over the last few years I've been lucky to see enough skilled teach¡ers use group work to think I am missing out. Here are six ideas for making group work as effective as possible. 
1. Have a group size ofthree 
A group size of four was always my go-to ... for no discernible rea¡son. But in Building Thinking Classrooms in Mathematics, Peter Liljedahl cites research to suggest that three is the optimal group size. This gives the right amount of diversity to allow for creativity and the gener¡ation of ideas, without being so large that subgroups start to form and student voices get lost. 
2. Make the groups random and change them each lesson 
In terms of group make-up, I have tried two things. I have let stu¡dents choose their own groups, which can be problematic in terms of students choosing friends versus who they work with best, or less confident students feeling left out. Or I have chosen the groups myselfin an attempt to create homogeneous or diverse groupings based on my perception of student ability. Homogenous groups led to issues with differentiation -I was constantly having to provide extreme challenge or support for different groups, or set alternate tasks. Diverse groups meant students adopted clear roles -Miran is smart, so she will do all the work, whereas Tom isn't that great at maths so he will have nothing useful to say. 
Peter Liljedahl suggests that randomising the groups frequently¡once an hour, so once every lesson for me -is the key. Randomi¡sation removes all social pressure, and frequent changing of group members reduces the chances of group roles forming. It also pro¡motes a culture in the classroom where students can -and are expected to -work with anyone, and provides students with a wider range ofperspectives and approaches. 
3. Make the randomisation explicit 
I find this result from Peter Liljedahl's research particularly in¡teresting. Even when teachers genuinely randomised the groups, students didn't believe it. This led to students questioning why they were in certain groups, and falling back into the roles that randomi¡sation had been implemented to avoid. Instead, the randomisation needs to be made explicit. 
There are lots of randomisers available online, but I find students are inherently suspicious ofthese, believing the teacher has set things up. Instead, a pack of playing cards can be effective. Set the deck so there are three of each card (three aces, three 7s, etc.) and the same number of cards as children in the class. Then ask stu¡dents to choose a card from the deck, which will determine their group -all the aces work together, all the 7s work together, and so 
on. 
Of course, this will take time. But, if we follow the advice from Chapter 2, students will get quicker at doing it. We can also help by clearly labelling each desk with the group it corresponds to, so all the 7s know immediately where they need to go. 
4. Use whiteboards 
Group collaboration is difficult if students are working in books. There are issues of ownership, neatness and permanence. All of these can be addressed by equipping groups with whiteboards to write on. As we discussed in Tip 12, I am a fan ofthe larger A3 mini¡whiteboards for all kinds ofwhiteboard work, but they really come into their own during group work. Students can have their own boards, but they can also have access to a master board, where the group combines their ideas. 
5. Use vertical, non-permanent surfaces In Building Thinking Classrooms in Mathematics, Peter Liljedahl explains that 'in the 15 years I have been engaged in the thinking classroom re¡search, nothing we have tried has had such a positive and profound effect on student thinking as having them work in random groups 
at vertical whiteboards. Students were thinking longer, discussing more mathematics and persisting when the tasks were hard.' 
Liljedahl argues that the act of standing improves visibility, energy, encourages collaboration, and makes the students feel less anony¡mous. This leads to less non-thinking behaviour, such as slacking, stalling and faking. 
Now, the ideal here is that there are large whiteboards placed all around the classroom walls that groups can stand in front of and collaborate. Often this is not feasible, but there are some cheaper alternatives. Many classrooms have windows, and these can be written on with special pens. Alternatively, for a fraction of the price of a permanent whiteboard you can buy magic whiteboards ¡large, reusable sheets that stick to any surface -and stick these on classroom walls. I'll put links to these on the resources page. 
As well as aiding group collaboration, working in this way also helps the teacher with their in-class formative assessment as stu¡dents' work is much more visible than when writing at their desks. To make this practice even more effective, Liljedahl suggests that the teacher uses a different coloured pen to their students, so their annotations, suggestions and questions stand out. This helps the students as they work on tasks, but also aids the teacher, as when they return to the group, they can immediately see the support or challenge they have already given. 
6. Make the group accountable 
Even ifwe get the size, randomisation and classroom geography of our groups optimal, there is one key factor remaining in deter¡mining the effectiveness of our group work: group dynamics. If it is possible for one student to do all the work and the rest of the group to have a sit-off, then we are in trouble. 
Maths teacher Sammy Kempner loves doing group work. But to ensure his students do not work as ineffectively as my students, Sammy has one very important principle: the group is responsible. 
The theory 
The importance of responsibility is the exact finding from psycholo¡gist Robert Slavin's research into group work: 'Cooperative learning is most consistently effective when groups are recognised or re¡warded based on the individual learning of their members.' 
The practice 
But what does this mean in practice? 
Brace yourself for this one... 
Let's imagine students have been set a task where they are permit¡ted to work as a group. At the end of the task, Sammy chooses a student in the group to answer a question. Often, he will choose the student whom he thinks is least likely to know the answer (this is another of Sammy's strategies that we will discuss in Tip 30). 
If the student gets the question wrong, it's the group that gets blamed. Sammy does not mention the individual. In fact, he even goes so far as to physically tum away from them to face the rest of the group. And then the interrogation begins: 'Why doesn't he know the answer? He's in your group. This is your responsibility. What exactly have you guys been doing?' 
Sometimes the student in question will attempt to intervene with 'oh sorry sir, I meant to say ... ' but Sammy cuts them off: 'No, no, no, don't worry, this is not about you, you've done your best. These guys just haven't helped you properly.' 
Sammy will regularly impose sanctions in such a situation. The group might be asked to stay behind at the end of the lesson until the person in question can explain the answer properly (and answer 
a related question just as well to demonstrate that they have not just memorised the answer). 
The other scenario is just as important. If the student quizzed gets the question right, then it is the group that is praised. The individ¡ual does not get mentioned. Again, turning to face the rest of the group, Sammy says something like, 'Well, you guys have clearly done an amazing job working together and supporting each other, because that was a brilliant explanation.' 
What?? 
Now your reaction to reading this might be similar to mine when I first heard it: shock, followed by a hundred reasons why this would not work in your classroom -the students would complain, the par¡ents would complain, it's not fair, and so on. 
And indeed, the first time you did this, it would probably be a disaster. But what about the fifth time you did it? What about the tenth? Then students know exactly what the rules are. They know that it is not enough for them to know the answer. They know they cannot just crack on with their work independently while the rest of the group has a rest. Equally, they know they cannot just free-ride and let someone else pick up the slack. The group lives or dies with the success or failure of the individual, so everyone's incentives are aligned. 
How does this work in mixed-attainment classrooms? 
Sammy explained that this approach is particularly effective with mixed-attainment classes, or with groups with some high-achievers in them. They are the types of students who may naturally work on their own. Now they are being asked to work with and probably support students who may not have grasped things as quickly as they have. Now, initially, that might be frustrating. But ifthis is presented as a challenge to overcome and a skill worth developing (pragmatically, being able to explain, communicate and reason above simply being able to do is likely to get them lots of marks on exams), then they get on board. Indeed, Sammy has a mantra to support this that I think is so important for students to be aware of: helping others is the hardest job in the classroom. It's great that you know how to do this yourself, but now the real challenge begins... do you know it well enough to help someone else? 
Group work challenge

When doing group work, have visibly random groups and create the conditions so that the group is responsible -and held accountable -for the success of its members

What's the problem? 
Matt wants his students to solve 13 -2x =x + 4. 
He doesn't just want the answer; he wants full working out and to check students' understanding of each step. He also wants as many students as possible to participate. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Ifyou were Matt, which means of participationwould you use? 

ò 
How do you decide which means of participation to use forthe different questions you ask? 


What's the tip? 
If the question is a relatively straightforward one -factual recall or a short procedure, for example -then relying on a single means of participation discussed in this chapter is usually the most appro¡priate. But for questions with more challenge, especially when you want students to explain their answers, combining a few of the means of participation works really well. 
My favourite combo is what I call the holy trinity: mini-whiteboards . Partner Talk . Cold Call. 
Let's see how the holy trinity could play out for Matt's question. 
'Okay, so silent thinking for this next question. I want you to write your working out and any notes on the back of your mini-whiteboards, and your final answer nice and big on the front ... (wait) ... Solve 13 -2x =x + 4 (Matt writes the question on the board) showing all your working out.' 
*** 
'Hover your mini-whiteboards ifyou have finished so I can see how much extra time we need... Good, okay, so back row, show me your mini-whiteboards in three, two, one... middle row in three, two, one... and finally front row in three, two, one... 
'Okay, in a moment I am going to ask you to talk to your partner. I want the person nearest the door to put their mini-whiteboard in the middle of the desk and explain each step of their working out. The other person should listen really hard. Then, when ready, swap roles. I will give you one minute for this, and I will remind you after 30 sec¡onds to swap ifyou haven't already done so.' 
*** 
'Becky, I saw a really interesting answer on your mini¡
whiteboard. Please can you explain it to us?' 
'Ifat, what was your partner's answer and reason?' 
'Hands-up if anyone has changed their answer following 
their paired discussion?' 
An alternative to this is to swap the paired discussion for group work, ensuring that the group is responsible (Tip 16). 
Whatever you choose, I find the holy trinity (mini-whiteboards . 
Partner Talk . Cold Call) to be a powerful combination in terms 
of mass participation, engagement and generating data I can work 
with. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider combining several means of participation to maximise participation, information and depth of thought.

What's the problem? 
The strength of the tools of mass of participation in this chapter is that they provide information that we can then respond to: 
ò 
When faced with a sea of mini-whiteboards, we notice an inter¡esting response we want to discuss with the class. 

ò 
Following a vote, we notice some students have gone for A, oth¡ers have gone for B, and some have gone for D. We want to hear from students in each of those groups. 

ò 
As we circulate the room during Partner Talk or group work, we overhear some brilliant discussions that we want to share. 


What did I use to do in each case? Make a mental note. A mental note of the students I wished to call upon or the points I wished to discuss. And what invariably happened when I went to retrieve my mental mote at the required moment? It had disappeared. 
Already in this book, we have discussed how student attention is limited. But so is ours. And in a lesson, there are plenty of things that can happen in the time between noticing the thing we want to share and actually sharing it. A student asks us a question, we need to tell someone to talk a little quieter, we notice something else, our mind wanders. All of these things compete for our finite attention. 
And before we know it, we have forgotten the very thing we wanted to remember. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Do you take mental notes? 

ò
Do you sometimes forget them? 


What's the tip? 
Mental notes are rubbish. Instead, take actual notes. 
When faced with a sea of mini-whiteboards, ABCD cards or thumbs, have a piece of paper (or mini-whiteboard) with you that you use to write down the names of students or interesting answers. When you circulate the room, take a piece of paper with you and jot down all the interesting things you see and hear that you want to discuss. 
Then it doesn't matter if your attention gets taken somewhere else. You have off-loaded what you wanted to remember onto that piece of paper, and it will still be there, waiting patiently for you, when you need it. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Instead of relying on remembering examples to discuss and students to call upon, write them down on a piece of paper.

2.

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
Here are four things that sound like really good ideas: 
1. 
Replacing hands-up with Cold Call for all questioning. 

2. 
If a student gets a question wrong or says they don't know, come back to them again to see if they now understand. 

3. 
Use mass participation strategies so you can see all students' responses to a question. 


4. Provide lots of opportunities for students to struggle. But each can go very wrong. 
1. 
Cold Call puts students on the spot, and for many that is a terri¡fying experience. 

2. 
Getting a question wrong in front ofyour teacher and your peers can feel like the worst thing in the world for some students and cause them to not want to contribute at all in the future, or even opt out of learning altogether. 

3. 
It is fantastic when everyone contributes an answer to a ques¡tion. But what about the child who is the only one to get some¡thing wrong? 

4. 
Learning happens when students think hard. But if that struggle is too much for students, and they have no prior experience of struggle leading to success, then they may well down tools, give up, and hence learn nothing at all. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to any ofthese issues? 

ò
What are the solutions? 


What's the tip? 
There are times when what the theory says goes against what we know is best for our students in the moment. 
Primary teacher Chris Such articulated this really well in his fifth tip on my podcast -Depressurise learning. We want to hear from all our students, we know we shouldn't let students off the hook if they get a question wrong, we know it is best to see as many responses as possible, and we know thinking hard is good for learning. But we need to consider the potential long-term implications ofthese peda¡gogical decisions. 
Chris makes the point that embarrassment (or -its more extreme form -humiliation) is one of the most toxic and damaging emotions a student can feel. And yet, despite our best efforts as teachers, it is so easy for our students to feel it. Chris argued that we should take steps to ensure that students don't feel embarrassed, even if this means making suboptimal pedagogical decisions in the short term. 
So, for our four scenarios above we might: 
1. 
Continue using hands-up until mistakes become normalised. This is a broad generalisation, but the more confident students who volunteer answers during hands-up are also likely to be the best at dealing with mistakes, especially if framed in a positive way. After a few lessons of using hands-up, and showing that mistakes are a normal part of learning and nothing to be feared, you may then be in a better position to switch to Cold Call and involve some ofyour quieter students. 

2. 
If asking a question using Cold Call, we could ask five students what their answers are and their reasons, and then explain the correct answer and reason ourselves. By the time we offer our explanation, students are likely to have forgotten who in the class said what, so no one is embarrassed. 

3. 
If we cannot make Heads Down, Votes Up (Tip 13) work, we might choose to avoid using voting systems or mini-whiteboards for now, replacing them with Partner Talk or a series of mul¡tiple Cold Calls as described above, until students' confidence 


.
increases. 
4. We may purposefully choose a less demanding task or activity to enable our students to feel successful. Then, with that founda¡
tion of success driving their resilience, we can start to introduce struggle. 
These decisions may be suboptimal in the short term but should be seen as an investment that will pay offin the long term. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Considerwhere your students are in their learning and confidence with atopic before choosing which tool to use.

CHAPTER4 
CHECKING FOR UNDERSTANDING 
Teaching without checking for understanding is like painting with our eyes closed. In this chapter, we look at why we need to check for understanding, and the importance of two different types of wait times in our questioning. Then we discuss different types of questions we could ask our students to check their understanding, including a deep dive into learner-generated examples and diagnos¡tic questions. We also consider when to check for understanding (including Exit Tickets), and how to create a culture in our class¡rooms where students are not afraid to be wrong.

Tip 20 -Think of questions as checks for misunderstanding 

What's the problem? 
One of the (many) mistakes I made early in my career was to as¡sume that if a student got a question correct in the moment, then they understood whatever concept the question was testing. An¡other thing to tick off the list in my bid to help my lucky students conquer the whole of mathematics. 
The problem is, of course, that it is one thing knowing the answer to a question at the moment when that concept is fresh in your mind and you are surrounded by lots of helpful cues, but it is a whole other ball game answering a related question at a point in the future when it is not so fresh and there are not quite so many cues around to help out. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Have you experienced students forgetting something they once knew? 

ò
What is the reason they do this? 


What's the tip? 
This is the difference between performance and learning, as discussed at length by the researchers Robert and Elizabeth Bjork, and others. 
When our students get a question correct, especially when that question relates to a concept that is fresh in their minds (a sequence ofproblems following a worked example, perhaps, or an Exit Ticket at the end of a lesson), we are observing performance, not learning. The only way to know if students have really understood something is if they can perform consistently over time. This is why a carefully considered retrieval schedule is so important-something we will discuss in Tip 81. 
I think a good way to remind ourselves ofthis is to undertake a rebranding exercise. When we ask students a question based on something they have just been taught, we are not checking for un¡derstanding, we are checking for misunderstanding. Because, while a correct answer to a question on a concept fresh in students' minds cannot tell you that they have truly understood the concept, an incorrect answer is a strong signal that they have not, and dictates that the teacher needs to respond in some way. 
So, as we venture deep into the tips, techniques and tricks involved in checking for understanding throughout this chapter, it is worth bearing in mind two things: 
1. 
A correct answer at the moment is a signal that you can move on, but that idea needs revisiting several times in the future to ensure we distinguish between performance and learning. 

2. 
An incorrect answer is a signal we need to respond. 


ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Think of your checks for understanding as checks for misunderstanding, so you don't mistake perfor¡mance for learning. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip20

What's the problem? 
Me:  (Following another of my award-winning explanations) 'Okay, so does that make sense?'  
Students respond with a nod.  
Me:  'Brilliant, okay so let's crack on with this task that builds on this idea.'  
10 minutes later...  
Me:  'Okay, so it turns out that my explanation didn't make any sense. Let's start again.'  

Can you relate to this? 
The disconnect between what students think they understand and what they actually do understand has long fascinated me. My Di¡agnostic Questions co-founder Simon and I studied this. We added a measure of confidence below each question on our Diagnostic Questions app:1

NEXT 
Students answer a question by choosing A, B, C or D, offer their explanation, and then decide how confident they feel in their an¡swer by selecting an emoji-crucially, they do this before they know ifthat answer is correct or not. We mapped these emojis to a linear scale from Oto 100. 
So, we now have a measure of understanding (whether a student gets the question correct or not) and a measure of their perception of understanding (their confidence rating). 
How do the two compare? 
The following graph shows the results of 18,000 students, aged 6 to 16, who have answered and rated their confidence on at least five questions. Each dot represents a student, and the larger the dot, the more questions answered. On the x-axis, we have accuracy, which is the proportion of questions each student got correct. On the y-axis we have each student's confidence rating averaged over the questions they answered. 
Student confidence versus accuracy (p=0.52) 

0 20 40 60 80 100 
Accuracy(%) 
It is worth taking a moment to study the graph and pick out any¡thing that interests you. 
The first thing to say is that comparing the two measures in this way is problematic as they do not use the same scale. 
Second, we end up with a positive correlation -it seems the more confident a student is, the more likely they are to get a question correct. 
But just look at the spread. 
There is a huge spread of accuracy for different confidence values. Crucially, given a particular confidence value, we cannot be sure how well a student understands the topic. Someone with an average confidence rating close to 100% may know it all, or they may know very little. I am sure we can all think of students we have taught who would live in the top-left ofthe graph ('Relax, sir, I know what I am doing'), and those in the bottom-right ('I just don't get ANY of it'). 
So why is there such a disconnect between students' perception of their understanding and their actual understanding? There are three reasons: 
1. Dunning-Kruger effect 
Dylan Wiliam offered our first clue -the Dunning-Kruger effect. This is a cognitive bias where people with limited knowledge in a given domain greatly overestimate their own knowledge relative to objective criteria. So, when I ask my students if this makes sense, they probably think 'yeah, that example makes total sense'. The problem is, it is the only example they have seen. Perhaps it is a poorly chosen one as discussed in Tip 58, or perhaps I have pro¡vided so many cues, hints and scaffolds that they have needed to do little thinking of their own. But the more experience they gain in the concept when they are given a task to work on without my sup¡port, the more they realise their understanding was not where they thought it was. This can be difficult to avoid when asking questions such as 'does this make sense?' As Dylan pointed out, the first rule of the Dunning-Kruger Club is that you don't know you are in the Dunning-Kruger Club. 
2. Emotional resonance 
Clare Sealy offers a second explanation for the disconnect that is particularly important for relevant prior knowledge. Let's imagine we are about to teach our students probability tree diagrams, and we have identified adding fractions as relevant prior knowledge. When we say to students 'do you understand how to add fractions?' students may reply 'yes', not because they remember how to add fractions, but because they remember the feeling ofunderstanding. When we are successful at something, our bodies release endorphins. When students knew how to add fractions last year, it felt great, and that feeling sticks. We remember the positive emotion associ¡ated with knowing something at the time, more than we may re¡member the content itself. 
3. Curse ofknowledge Added to the mix is that as teachers we are likely to suffer from the 'curse ofknowledge'. We know a lot about our subject, and that can make it very difficult to think about what it is like to not know things about our subject. This can reduce the quality of our expla¡
nations and make us fail to see how someone could possibly not understand. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you noticed the disconnect between students' perception of their understanding and their actual understanding? 

ò 
How haveyou tried to addressthis?


I I 
What's the tip? 
Due to the curse of knowledge, we cannot rely on the fact that our explanations have made sense. Due to the Dunning-Kruger effect and the power of emotional resonance, we cannot rely on our stu¡dents telling us they understand something. 
So, in short, we need to check for understanding. 
The rest of this chapter will be devoted to exactly how we might do this. But I want to begin here with a really powerful tip from Doug Lemov. 
If you are anything like me, you will find it incredibly difficult to eliminate all the 'does this make sense?' and 'do you understand?' from your teaching vocabulary. So turn this into a positive. 
First, the fact that you want to ask a question shows you realise there is a need to check for understanding to overcome our curse of knowledge. And then, every time you feel yourself about to ask one ofthose questions, use it as a cue to ask a better question -one that actually checks students' understanding and not their perception of their understanding. 
You can take this one stage further and use a tip from Chapter 2 where we looked at habits. Tell your students to watch out for any time you say something like 'does that make sense?' or 'does every¡one understand?' Write these on the board somewhere so students can refer to them. Then, any time students spot an infringement (and you know they will), you have to do something like putting ú1 in a charity jar. This should help you control your habit, and may also cause your students to pay more attention to what you are 
.
saying. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Anytimeyou feel like asking something like 'does that make sense?', use that as a cue to ask a better question.

Tip 22 -Lengthen wait times after asking a question

What's the problem? 
Before we consider better questions to ask our students to check their understanding, we need to address a big issue. 
How long do you think you wait, on average, between asking a question and calling upon a student to answer that question? Ten seconds? Five seconds? 

This will obviously depend on the nature of the question and the means of participation, but I am looking for an average. Of course, the best way to know this figure for real is for someone to sit in your lessons, record every gap between asking a question and students being called upon to answer, and calculate the mean. 
Researcher Mary Budd Rowe did exactly that. In her paper, 'Wait Time: Slowing Down May Be a Way of Speeding Up!', Budd Rowe draws upon 20 years of research into the gap between question and answer (what she calls 'wait time'). She reaches the astonishing (and rather worrying) conclusion that... wait for it... teachers typically wait between 0.7 seconds and 1.5 seconds before speaking after they have asked a question, and often this gap is too short to be reliably mea¡sured on a stopwatch! 
Having reflected, I think there are three reasons why my wait times are so short: 
1. 
The desire to keep up the pace and keep my students engaged. This is undoubtedly due to my long-held, hard-to-budge fear of silence in lessons. If no one is talking, then surely no one is learning. 

2. 
The dreaded curse ofknowledge -'I mean, come on, how long do you really need to work out 10% of 45?' 

3. 
Trying to satisfy the 'quicker' students. I think this is a real problem for me. I will ask a question, and even if I have banned hands-up, I can tell when one or two students are ready-they have that pleading look in their eyes, desperate to be called upon. And bizarrely, the fear ofkeeping those few students waiting any longer seems to override the need to give the other students in the class, who are larger in number, adequate think¡ing time. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How much of this can you relate to? 

ò 
What is the solution? 


What's the tip? 
Fortunately, there is a relatively easy fix to this problem: lengthen the time between asking a question and calling upon students to answer. 
But how long? Budd Rowe suggests that a wait time ofjust three sec¡onds is enough to bring about the following benefits: 
1. 
The accuracy ofstudent responses increases -in the language of psychol¡ogist Daniel Kahneman, increased wait times give students an opportunity to overrule the instant, reflex response oftheir System 1, and instead allow their slower, more considered System 2 to step in. This in turn increases the reliability of our checks for understanding. 

2. 
The length and complexity ofstudent responses increases -the extra time allows students to give more comprehensive answers, which again allows teachers to get a better sense of the depth of their knowledge. 

3. 
There is an increase in responses by students who don't typically respond -that extra thinking time is good for all students, but it is your quieter, less confident students who will benefit most, having more time to consider their answers and thus be more willing to share. English teacher Jamie Thom suggested allowing students an opportunity to jot down any thoughts on their mini-whiteboards during this wait time as this can reduce the anxiety that stems from trying to remember what they have to say. 

4. 
Students retrieve more, and hence learn more -without sufficient wait time, some students will resort to guessing, while others may not even have the opportunity to process the question. In both cases, a retrieval opportunity has been wasted. 


What about for more complex questions? 
Budd Rowe finds that many of the above benefits plateau with wait times above three seconds. But that does not mean we cannot use our own professional judgement to give students longer wait times depending on the nature ofthe question, our knowledge of our students, and so on. Three seconds should be seen as the absolute 
. . 
m1n1mum. 
But, ifwe are giving our students less than one second of thinking time when we should be giving three, it is a fair assumption that we might be underestimating the wait time needed for questions that we already give longer wait times to. So, it might be good practice to increase all our wait times by a couple of seconds to be on the safe side. 
Two ideas to increase our wait times 
So, how can we increase our wait times? The obvious answer is we just wait longer between question and answer. But if average teacher wait times are as short as Budd Rowe finds, that suggests that we need some concrete strategies at our disposal to avoid slip¡ping back into our old habits. However much time you think you give, you probably give a lot less. 
Here are two ideas that have worked for me. 
1. Tap out an extra three seconds 
Whenever I ask a question and I am about to call upon the students to answer, I tap out an extra three seconds with my fingers, either on my leg or the palm ofmy other hand. I find the physicality of this act reminds me to do it. 
2. Use mass participation strategies 
Increasing the proportion of the questions we ask that elicit mass participation (Chapter 3) can help increase wait times as they slow things down. But we need to be careful. 
Mini-whiteboards naturally slow things down as students have to write down their answers. But if students are given, say, five sec¡onds to think of their answer and write it down, how much of that time is spent thinking versus scrambling to write down the answer in time? Here a simple tweak helps: ask a question, but count to three before asking students to write down the answer. 
Similarly, Partner Talk slows things down as students both explain and listen to the answer and reason oftheir partner. But launching straight into Partner Talk following a question can have the same problems as immediately Cold Calling a student. Students need to be given adequate time, in silence, to process their own thoughts before beginning their discussion with their partner. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
However longyou normally wait betweenasking a questionand getting a response, addthree seconds to 
it.

What's the problem? 
In Tip 22 we learned that teachers typically give students less than a second to think about a question before calling upon them to re¡spond. We further learned that extending this wait time to a mere three seconds can have significant benefits on participation, learn¡ing and our check for understanding. 
But there is a second wait time we need to consider: the time be¡tween hearing the response of our students and our reaction to that response -in other words, the time after an answer is given. 
Please think about a question you asked your students in the most recent lesson you taught. Once you had heard a response, what did you do? Did you: 
ò 
React straight away by saying something like 'not quite', 'bril¡liant' or 'interesting'? 

ò 
Repeat or reframe the student's answer? 

ò 
Immediately go to another student to hear what they thought about that answer? 


Crucially, did you give your students as much time to process the answer as you gave them to process the question? 
Mary Budd Rowe calls this period wait time 2. 

Wait time 1 

Wait time 2 

If wait time 1 is letting the question hang in the air, wait time 2 is letting the answer hang. 
Perhaps unsurprisingly given what we saw in Tip 22, teachers tend to undervalue the importance ofwait time 2. On average teachers allow less than one second for their students to process an answer be¡fore reacting to it in some way. 
Interestingly, Budd Rowe explains: 'Based on research it is clear that wait time 2 is more important than wait time 1 in many of its effects.' And this is an issue, because if a teacher decides to focus their effort on extending their wait time, the chances are they mean the time between asking a question and calling upon the response -in other words, wait time 1. Wait time 2 is often overlooked in the eagerness to react to the given answer or move on. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
How longdo you allocate forthissecond wait time? 

ò
How could you makethis an impactfulpart ofyour questioning strategy? 


What's the tip? 
Budd Rowe suggests that if teachers can allow a minimum ofthree sec¡onds for wait time 2, the following things are likely to happen: 
1. 
Students have an opportunity to self-explain -students can compare the answer they have just heard with their own answer, attempting to reconcile any differences. 

2. 
Behaviour and attention improves -when students realise that answers are respected and considered, they pay more attention to them. 

3. 
Student-to-student exchanges increase -with more time to reflect on an answer, students have more to discuss with each other. 

4. 
Teachers use student responses better -if teachers can let an answer hang instead ofbouncing to the next student, they have more time to consider the answer and how best to use it. 


Three ideas to increase wait time 2 
Here are three ideas to help us improve this second wait time: 
1. Increase wait time 2 by tapping out an extra three seconds 
Same as the last tip. When I hear a response to a question, I tap out an extra three seconds with my fingers before reacting. During this time my students are silent. I want to give them the opportunity to process the answer on their own. 
I try to do this for every question I ask, whatever the means of participation. It may be clear that increasing wait time 2 would be useful for responses to complex questions, especially when you plan to collect a selection of responses from students for further ex¡ploration. There I might leave around eight seconds of silence after each response (the five I would usually leave, plus a bonus three). But I would do the same for a quick-fire response, such as when John tells me that the internal angles in a triangle add up to 180 degrees. Here I will leave three seconds of silence (the zero I would usually leave, plus a bonus three), confirm John is correct, and then move on. 
Increasing wait time 2 feels far less natural than wait time 1¡students might look at you in the way they would look at a video stream that is buffering, wondering when you are going to start up again. But the more you do it, and the more you prompt self¡explanation (see point three below), the more natural it becomes for everyone. 
2. Avoid giveaway signals 
An adequate wait time 2 provides the potential for the four benefits described above to happen. But this could all be blown by a signal from us. 
Imagine a student gives an answer, and we immediately respond with something like 'brilliant!', 'close', or 'no'. In each case, we eval¡uate the response. Or we may have a non-verbal tell, like looking up to the sky, sighing, or turning away whenever an answer is wrong¡we all have them, and students pick up on them. 
If we evaluate a student's answer, it doesn't matter ifwe subse¡quently allow time for students to think about the answer; we have reduced that time's potency. Students who realise they are correct have little incentive to keep paying attention as they have done their thinking, students who find out that they are wrong may feel demotivated and stop thinking, and paired discussions suffer be¡cause being able to discuss if an answer is right or wrong and why is likely to be more fruitful than discussing just why an answer is right or wrong. 
To avoid such giveaway signals, I find a simple 'thank you' to ac¡knowledge the student's response is enough, followed by silence for however long you have allocated for wait time 2. The key is to react exactly the same way whether the answer is correct or incorrect. 
3. Prompt self-explanation 
Unfortunately, an adequate wait time without any giveaway signals is still not enough to guarantee the valuable self-explanation behav¡iour that we want our students to exhibit. Psychologist Alexander Renkl finds that the majority of students do not self-explain spon¡taneously. If we want students to make the most of wait time 2 we may need to provide some structured support, at least initially. 
A simple prompt could be: 
'Thank you, Matthew. Now time for silent thinking. Do you 
all agree with Matthew? If you do, is your reasoning the 
same? If not, how would you explain your reasoning?' 
As long as you do the same for both wrong and right answers, you won't give the game away, and hence provide every opportunity for students to think and learn. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
However long you normally wait between hearing the response to aquestion and acting upon that response, add three seconds to it to support students to make the most ofthat extra time.

Tip 29 
Exit Tickets 
What's the problem? 
So far in this chapter we have established the need to check for understanding and allow adequate wait time after a question and an answer. The next thing to consider is what the questions we ask our students should look like. 
There are two things to consider here. 
1. Participation ratio 
We met this concept back in Tip 11. How many of our students are participating in class through discussion, writing and answering questions? Obviously, we want a high participation ratio, but we also need to consider how we are going to collect the responses from our students to ensure our check for understanding is reliable. So, what are our means of participation? 
2. Think ratio 
This is another concept from Doug Lemov. Here the question is 'how hard are our students thinking?' In the past my checks for understanding have been rather shallow, centred around the recall of facts and the reproduction of procedures. So, while I was eliciting responses from lots of students, I was not really checking the depth oftheir understanding. How do our checks for understanding reflect this concern? 
I have a lot to say on this matter, but before I dive in, I want you to see where you are at. Think of a topic you have taught recently. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What types of questions did you ask your students to check their understanding? Write themdown on a piece of paper. 

ò 
How did you ask your students to respond to each of those questions? 


What's the tip? 
Below are 10 different classes of questions I use for checking under¡standing. The first eight are from Pointon and Sangwin's question taxonomy: 
1. Factual recall  
2. Carry out a routine calculation or algorithm  
3. Classify somemathematical object  
4. Interpret situation or answer  
5. Proof, show, justify-(general arg ument)  
6. Extend aconcept  
7. Construct example/instance  
8. Criticise a fallacy  

When analysing over 400 pieces of coursework and examination questions in computer algebra, Pointon and Sangwin found that 60% of all questions inspected belonged to class 2, while only 3% of questions belonged to classes 6 to 8. They concluded that the vast majority of questions asked may be successfully completed by fol¡lowing routine procedures, without the use of higher skills. The true depth ofunderstanding is rarely assessed. 
I find these categories of questions really useful when thinking about the variety and depth ofmy checks for understanding. 
I'm going to show you an example of each type using questions shared by maths teacher Dan Lewis on the topic of the highest common factor. 
1. Factual recall 
Example: The common factors oftwo integers are 1, 2, 3, 6 and 12. What is the highest common factor? 
This is about as basic a check for understanding as you can get. But it is an important one. Do students know what they are actually trying to work out? Have they correctly interpreted the definition, explanation or sequence of examples and non-examples you have shown them? Factual recall questions can be answered using Call and Response or mini-whiteboards, or can be put into a multiple¡choice format and answered with voting systems. 
2. Carry out a routine calculation or algorithm 
Example: Find the highest common factor of 18 and 30. 
This is the most common question type I use to check for under¡standing in maths. Can students successfully perform the method I have taught them? We start this process in the We Do of a worked example, continue it in the Your Turn, and it culminates in the flu¡ency practice that follows. 
Mini-whiteboards are my go-to here. If working out is required, I will ask students to do that on the back of their mini-whiteboards (or in their books if the whiteboards only have one writable side) and then write their final answer nice and big on the other side. I can then easily see the spread of answers when students hold their boards up, and then I can delve deeper into a student's thinking by asking to see their working out. 
Multiple-choice diagnostic questions also work well. A wrong answer can reveal the specific nature of a student's misunderstand¡ing. But if there are several steps to the procedure, then a series of diagnostic questions is needed, or you can go for an open-response format on mini-whiteboards. 
3. Classify some mathematical object 
Example: I have an odd integer and an even integer. Which ofthe following could be common factors: 1, 2, 3, 4? 
Now we get to the question types that I did not ask enough ofwhen checking for understanding. Being able to classify based on a con¡cept is really important. This avoids the idea of requiring technical definitions and instead replaces it with something far more useful ¡the challenge to exemplify. Knowing what a concept is and what it is not is a true test of understanding, far more so than applying an algorithmic procedure without thought. 
The answers to such questions could be displayed on mini-white¡boards, but it is the conversations that underpin them that are potentially so powerful. So I use the holy trinity (Tip 17). I give stu¡dents time to write down their working and any notes on one side of the board, and then their final answer on the other side. I ask to see these so I get a sense ofwhere the class is at. Students then go into paired discussions to compare their answers and reasons, and I then Warm Call based on what I saw on the mini-whiteboards. 
4. Interpret a situation or answer 
Example: Ifthe highest common factor oftwo numbers is 6, are both integers even, both odd, or one odd and one even? 
This will be the last time I say 'here is a question type I did not ask enough of when checking for understanding', because you can just copy and paste that for the remaining four categories. The idea of giving the answer and asking what you can infer about the question is a powerful one. The holy trinity again works well for this. 
5. Proof, show, justify 
Example: Show, with some examples to help, that the highest common factor oftwo consecutive integers is always 1. 
Proof does not need to be in the formal, algebraic sense here. But questions like these encourage students to try examples in a log¡ical, systematic way as they construct their arguments. For many students, this can be the first time they realise something very special about mathematics -ifwe are careful about our choices of examples, we can make arguments that we know will apply to any possible number, even though we haven't tested it. Such questions are challenging. 
Here I like to adopt the 4-2 approach that we discussed in Tip 15. As a reminder, that involves four minutes ofindependent work, in books or on mini-whiteboards, followed by two minutes ofpaired discussion. I will walk around listening and supporting (but not get¡ting in the way), and then call upon some pairs to share the fruits of their discussions. 
6. Extend a concept 
Example: Find the highest common factor of 12, 18 and 20. 
Here we have an opportunity to push the concept a little further. That may mean introducing more challenge (as in the example above), or it could mean weaving in different areas of mathematics -algebraic expressions, for example. Depending on the question, you might be able to assess this in the same way as the routine al¡gorithm question. 
7. Construct an example/instance 
Example: Find a pair ofintegers with a highest common factor of8. 
Now we have hit the jackpot. I am so obsessed with asking students to construct their own examples based on certain conditions, and so convinced it is one of the best checks for understanding a teacher can undertake, I am going to dedicate a whole tip to it next (Tip 25). This is perfectly suited to the holy trinity. 
8. Criticise a fallacy 
Example: Ben says he has found two different odd integers with a highest common factor that is even. Explain why he is wrong. 
Identifying and explaining mistakes is so important. Once again, we move beyond a test ofwhether students can carry out the algo¡rithmic routine that an overreliance on type 2 question types can bring, and instead study someone else's attempt to carry out that algorithmic routine. You could even go one further and tag on the end,'... and explain how you would help Ben understand'. The 4-2 approach followed by Cold Call would work well here. 
9. Ask 'what do you understand?' 
Example: What do you understand about the highest common factor? 
In Tip 21 we addressed the common tendency for teachers to ask, 'Do you understand?' and the issues with that question. Tom Sher¡rington has a really practical solution. Instead of asking 'do you un¡derstand?', ask 'what do you understand?' Students could answer on mini-whiteboards and discuss with their partner before you call upon individual students to share their thoughts. 
10. Write the hardest question you know how to answer 
Example: What is the hardest question you know how to answer on highest common factors? 
Building on Tom Sherrington's idea, we can delve a bit deeper into what our students know by challenging them to write the hardest question they feel confident about answering correctly on a given topic. Asking for the hardest question -as opposed to just a question -pinpoints the boundary of a student's knowledge. If we ask them to write the written solution to their question separate from the question itself-on either side of a mini-whiteboard, for example ¡we can then ask students to swap questions with their partner, try to answer the question, and the subsequent paired discussion can focus on checking and explaining. This makes a great plenary or homework activity. 
Reflection 
Please go back to the list of questions your wrote at the start of this tip. 
ò 
Label each ofthem with one of the 10 categories above. 

ò 
Are there any question types that you do not use regularly that you think are important? 

ò 
If so, write a question for each question type for a topic you are teaching soon. 


My students can't answer these!! 
This is a response I received from a head of department following a workshop on checking for understanding. He was concerned that many ofthe 10 types ofquestions discussed above were too difficult for his students. We then sat down and looked at his scheme of work and supporting resources and found that students were rarely asked to do anything more than develop fluency in following a set procedure. So he was right that they would struggle. 
If students cannot answer questions of, say, type 4 onwards, then we need to look at the explanations, representations and activities we are providing. The questions in the check for understanding are not the issue. 
Monosemic and polysemic questions 
One final practical thing worth bearing in mind when considering how you will check for understanding is the nature of the answer you are looking for. Here a distinction shared by science teacher, Adam Boxer, on my Mr Barton Maths podcast is useful. 
Some questions have only one correct answer, such as 'solve the equation Sx -4 = 16'. These are monosemic questions. When checking for un¡derstanding using a monosemic question, we can simply say, 'If your answer is this, you are right. If it is anything different, you are wrong.' 
But as soon as we start asking students to generate their own exam¡ples, justify an assertion or criticise a fallacy, the number of correct possible answers increases. Here we have polysemic questions. Even simple numeric or algebraic questions may be polysemic as the an¡swer could be written in a number of equivalent forms. 
When checking for understanding using polysemic questions, we need to be more careful. If we simply take one of our students' answers and say it is correct, we risk other students in the class who have equally correct, but different, answers thinking they are wrong. 
Adam suggests we decide how to deal with polysemic questions based on their purpose. So, if a question is prerequisite knowledge for the idea we are teaching, we will want to spend more time on it. Here we may take a student's response, then ask if there are any different responses (if using mini-whiteboards we may have seen different responses ourselves), and evaluate each one. If the ques¡tion is not prerequisite, we may instead choose to offer up some other correct answers ourselves and tell the students that if they have put something different that they suspect might also be cor¡rect, to put a star next to it and ask later in the lesson when we are walking around the classroom. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Considervarying thetypes of questions you ask students when checking their understanding to assess the depth of that understanding.

What's the problem? 
For the vast majority of my teaching career, my focus has been on improving the quality of the examples and questions I give my students. When I first started out, my examples and questions were woeful because they were the last thing I thought about in my plan¡ning process -most of my time and attention was spent cutting, sticking, laminating and animating. 
In recent years, my reading of research and conversations with peo¡ple far more experienced and knowledgeable than me has caused me to think much harder about the examples and questions I use with my students. So, I now borrow from research into interleaving when designing sets ofproblems that look similar on the surface but have different deep frameworks (Tip 71), and try to use key prin¡ciples from variation theory when designing sequences of questions for what I call Intelligent Practice (Tip 72). 
But notice both of these rely on me as a teacher to come up with examples and questions. And that makes perfect sense, as I can use my knowledge and experience to design examples and questions that progress in difficulty, avoid erroneous generalisations, confront misconceptions, or are most like the exam questions students will encounter, and so on. 
However, this means my students' experiences are confined to what I present to them. And that is very unlikely to be complete or opti¡mal, both for me as their teacher as I attempt to paint an accurate picture of their understanding, and for my students as learners as they strive to demonstrate what they can do. So, how can I support my students in generating their own examples and questions in a way that is structured, challenging, and engaging? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Do you challenge studentsto come up with their own examples? 

ò
Ifso, how do you structure this? 


What's the tip? 
It turns out the answer had been sitting on my bookshelf for over a decade. 
Thinkers -a 2004 ATM publication by Chris Bills, Liz Bills, Anne Watson and John Mason -contains a set ofprompts (or what I will refer to as 'frameworks') that challenge students to construct some mathematical objects which exemplify a concept or technique. Each of the frameworks is designed to draw out students' natural power to generalise. 
Here I will share my take on two of the frameworks from the book, and a third framework that I have adapted from an idea Michael Pershan shared with me. It will work best ifyou play along at home, so grab yourself a pen and paper. This time the topic is ... fractions! 
Framework 1: Give an example of... 
Please turn your paper so it is landscape and divide it into four roughly equal quadrants . 
... 
ò In the top-left box, please write a fraction that is equivalent to two-thirds. Write it nice and big. 
Have you got one? 
ò 
Okay, then in the top-right box, please write down another fraction that is equiva¡lent to two-thirds. 

ò 
We are on a roll now, so in the bottom-left box, please write another fraction that is equal to two-thirds, but make it as interesting an example as you can, whatever you take interesting to mean in this context. 

ò 
Finally, in the bottom-right box, please write a fraction that is not equivalent to two-thirds, but which someone might think is equivalent to two-thirds. 


In the classroom, my students respond on mini-whiteboards in¡stead of paper, setting their boards out as described above. I pause after each challenge to give my students sufficient time to think and write. My students work silently and independently, keeping their eyes on their own boards. 
Here is an overview ofhow this activity progresses:

After sufficient thinking time (I can get a better sense of this by circulating the room) I ask students to hover their mini-whiteboards face down to show me they are ready. They then show me their boards on a count of 'three, two, one... ' starting with the back row. For more on effective mini-whiteboard use, see Tip 12. 
A student's mini-whiteboard might look like this: 
20 h 
4¡
30 



2X 3X 

... 
Seeing all responses allows me to get a sense of my students' un¡derstanding, as well as to ensure all students are participating. I pay particular attention to the responses of my weaker students, look¡ing first at their top two examples, and then at their third and fourth examples. I make a physical note of any examples I wish to discuss with the class later -interesting correct or incorrect answers. 
Partner check 
Next, I ask my students to swap mini-whiteboards with each other and check each other's work. They do so by ticking any examples they agree with and putting a question mark next to any they don't. Students thus have an opportunity to practise checking for equiva¡lent fractions as well as generating their own, with the added twist that they could well be presented with challenging examples to check for their partner's third and fourth ones. I say to my students, 'If you disagree with any particular answer, have a conversation about it with your partner, and if you still disagree, let me know.' This always produces interesting examples that we can discuss as a class in the next stage of the process. 
Whole-class discussion 
Following partner check, we discuss any examples that students disagreed on, together with any that I picked out when students showed their boards or interesting examples I have prepared in advance. I record each of these examples on my whiteboard so they are visible. For each example, I ask students to think on their own if it is correct, vote with thumbs up or down, then discuss with their partner, and then I can Cold Call -the means ofparticipation holy trinity strikes again (Tip 17). 
With a selection of interesting examples and non-examples on the board, the whole-class discussion might be an opportunity to gener¡alise: 'How can we quickly spot if a fraction is equal to two-thirds? How can we quickly spot if it is not?' 
Boundary examples The power of the Give an example of framework for learner-generated examples is how it draws out boundary examples -examples, in this case, that are right on the cusp ofwhat it means to be an equivalent fraction. Asking for an interesting example challenges students to push a concept to its limits. Asking for an example someone might think is correct but is not -in other words, an interesting non-ex¡ample -tests whether students can step over the boundary of the concept, as well as challenging them to appreciate where others might struggle. Sure, I could come up with a selection ofboundary examples myself for us to discuss, but -big claim alert! -I know of no better way to check my students' understanding ofa concept than to set them up to think ofthese boundary examples themselves. Some examples will inevitably lie on the wrong side of the boundary, but these are the very non-examples we need in order to fully understand a concept. 
Incidentally, I used to ask students to think of a hard example for their third one, but this led to students simply making the numbers bigger (2 trillion over 3 trillion was a classic). Asking for interesting ex¡amples is better for generating boundary examples. 
Other examples Other examples of Give an example ofthat I have used in maths lessons include: 
ò 
Give an example of a triangle with an area of 12cm2. 

ò 
Give an example of a linear equation with a solution of 4. 

ò 
Give an example offive numbers with a mean of 8. 

ò 
Give an example of an expression that differentiates. 

ò 
Give an example of a pair of corresponding angles. 

ò 
Give an example of an enlargement of a rectangle with a length of 8cm and a width of 5cm. 

ò 
Give an example of a quadratic equation with a repeated solution. 


Can you think of a Give an example ofthat you could use for an upcom¡ing topic? 
Framework 2: Additional constraints 
Additional constraints is certainly more challenging as a teacher to write, but it can lead to some superb thinking and discussion among students. 
The best way to explain how it works is with an example. Let's stick with fractions. This time we will turn our piece of paper so it is in portrait orientation, and divide it up into five roughly equal rows. 

Here we go: 
ò In the top row, please write a pair offractions that add together to give 1. 
Have you got a pair? Okay, here is an overview of how this activity progresses: 
Whole-class
See all 
Partner
Constraint 
discussion 
responses 
check 
(if needed) 

See all students' responses 
With the first constraint announced (the two fractions must add to 1), I give students thinking and writing time, and then ask them to hover their mini-whiteboards face down to let me know they are ready. I then ask students to show me their mini-whiteboards. I do this at each stage ofAdditional constraints, whereas Iwait until students have all three examples when using Give an example of The reason for the difference is that Additional constraints generates a sequence of ex¡amples, and an unchecked error early on messes up everything that follows. 
As before, I make a physical note of any misconceptions and inter¡esting responses. 
Partner check 
I then prompt students to swap boards with their partners so they can check each other's example, marking it with a tick or a question mark. Once again, I tell my students that if there is a disagreement that they cannot resolve between them, let me know and we can discuss it as a class. As we get further through the sequence, the partner check is also an opportunity for students to discuss how they constructed their examples. 
Whole-class discussion 
At any stage, I'm free to instigate a whole-class discussion. I will do this if I've spotted any interesting answers or if a pair has a dis¡agreement from their discussion that needs resolving. If, however, all seems in order, I will skip this step and move on to the next constraint. 
Additional constraint 
Then we loop back to the beginning as we add in an additional constraint. 
ò In your second row, I want a pair offractions that add to one, and the two frac¡tions must have different denominators. 
Can you think of a pair of fractions that work? 
Some students may have gotten lucky and find their first example also matches this additional constraint. In that instance I ask them to check that is definitely the case, and if so, can they come up with another example that also matches the constraint? 
What follows is another round of showing me their mini-white¡boards, then partner check, and then whole-class discussion if needed. 
At this stage, a student's mini-whiteboard might look something like this: 
3 I
+
4-4¡
h I
+
8 4¡
This continues as we work through the complete sequence of con¡straints, each building on the previous. So, all together we have: 
Think of a pair of fractions that: 
ò 
add to 1 

ò 
... and have different denominators 

ò 
... and the same numerator 

ò 
... and one fraction is improper 


ò ... and the numerator is 13. Can you think of an example for each? Here are a few ideas for helping the Additional constraints framework 
run as smoothly as possible. 
Utilise variation theory 
I often encourage students to look closely at their first example and see if they can modify it in any way to match the second constraint. 
This is another example ofhow I like to use a key idea from variation theory-that meaning is found as things change amidst a background of sameness. This offers support to students who are struggling as they have a starting point, but also can provide chal¡lenge to other students as they seek to explain the relationship be¡tween the adjacent examples. 
Speed demons 
I use the hover your board technique as described in Tip 12, so I can easily tell which students have their examples ready to show me. If I have one or two speed demons, hovering their boards like mad men ready to show me their response, I will challenge them to think ofthe most interesting additional example they can that also matches the constraints. 
Ifstudents get stuck 
As constraints are added and the difficulty ramps up, students will get stuck. Circulating the room as students are working helps you pick up on this. In these cases, I stop the class, ask if anyone thinks they have an example that works, and we check it together. I then ask that student to describe their strategy for coming up with the example. I can then ask the rest ofthe class to either use that strat¡egy to come up with their own example, or -if they already have an example -see if they can come up with the most interesting answer they can think of. 
Allow collaboration 
Another option as the challenge increases and students start to get stuck, is to allow collaboration. I like to ensure my students have had silent thinking time themselves, and then allow them to work with their partner to see if they can find an example that works be¡tween them. 
Extra challenge 
A nice challenge to ask at the end ofthe sequence is if students can think oftheir own additional constraint to tag onto the end that still allows another example to be generated. Leaving the final con¡straint ('and the numerator is 13') would allow for this in the above sequence. 
Strategy and generalisation 
As we progress through the constraints, I'll use the whole-class discussion to ask students how they constructed their examples. Not only does this offer a great insight into their understanding, but it can also put us on the path to finding more examples that work as we move to generalisation. 
Other examples Other examples ofAdditional constraints that I have used in maths lessons include: 
Think of a set of six numbers that: 
ò 
have a mean of 5 

ò 
... and a mode of 2 

ò 
... and a median of 4 

ò 
... and a range of10 


ò ... and two of the values are non-integers. Write down a sequence of numbers that is: 
ò 
linear 

ò 
... and is descending 

ò 
... and contains at least one non-integer term 

ò 
... and has a 3rd term of 15 

ò 
... and has an 8th term of 13. 


Can you think of a sequence of additional constraints for an upcom¡ing topic? 
Framework 3: What if? When Michael Pershan came on the podcast, he inspired an addi¡tional framework for student-generated examples called What if? 
Here is how it could play out for another fractions example. This time you will need your piece ofpaper split into two columns: 
ò 
On the left-hand side, write down a fraction ofan amount problem in the form 

t ofc, where a is an even number and the answer is an integer. Also write down the answer. 

ò 
As an example, I am going to go for ~ of30, which gives me an answer of24. 

ò 
You cannot use any of the same numbers I have used. 


I write my example and answer on the left side of my board so students can see. I will continue to write my examples and answers throughout the sequence that follows. 
Students then write their example and answer in the left-hand col¡umn oftheir mini-whiteboards, hover, show me on cue, then check each other's work. 
I add the additional instruction that if a student has used the same numerator, denominator or amount as their partner, then the student closest to the classroom door must change their example, and their partner must help them come up with a new one. This is really important to ensure each pair has two different examples to com¡pare during the sequence that follows, and that both examples are different to mine. 
And then I introduce the first What if? 
ò 
What ifyou double b? 

ò 
So, looking at my example, I would now have fo of30. 

ò 
We write the new example in the right-hand column. 


My board looks like this:

4¡
of 30
5

Notice, I have written the question in the right-hand column, but not the answer.

After announcing each What if?, I encourage students to take a moment to predict what they think might happen for their example and write this prediction on their mini-whiteboard. 
ò 
Will the answer increase, decrease or stay the same? 

ò 
Can they predict an actual value? 


Students then work out the answer on their own, writing it below their new example in the right-hand column. I then prompt them to pause and reflect: 
ò 
Did the answer match their prediction? 

ò 
How would they explain what is going on? 


This opportunity for self-explanation is key. Modelling how I as a teacher make predictions and reflect, and sharing examples from students that I see on mini-whiteboards around the room, is impor¡tant to promote good practice. 
See all responses 
Then I ask students to hover their boards and show me when called upon so I can make a note of any interesting responses.

Partner check 
Next I ask students to discuss their answer with their partner. Here I tell them to do three things: 
1.

Whole-class discussion 
I then Cold Call a few pairs to see what relationship they uncovered. With a number of different examples to work with, including my own, we can try to explain what is going on. 
The next What if? We continue in the same way for each new What if? Students write each new example in the right-hand column, and then rub that out when we have finished discussing it. Their first example always remains visible in the left column as that is always the example we compare against. I model this each time with my own example. 
Here is the full sequence of What ifs: 
ò 
What ifyou double b? 

ò

ò

ò

ò

ò

ò

What do you predict will happen for each of these changes? 
The link to Intelligent Practice 
This structure is closely related to my use ofvariation during Intelli¡gent Practice (Tip 72). Indeed, you could prompt students to use the same Reflect, Expect, Check, Explain format for each modification: 
ò 
Reflect -what has changed? -the denominator has doubled. 

ò 
Expect -what do you think will happen to your answer? -it will halve. 

ò 
Check -work out the actual answer and compare to your predic¡tion -it matches! 

ò 
Explain -can you explain the relationship between answers, first on your own and then with your partner? -we are now dividing by a number that is twice as big, so our answer will be halfthe 


.
size. 
But the power of the What if? structure comes from the fact that stu¡dents are all working on different examples and yet noticing the same rela¡tionships. Often you hear exclamations such as, 'Wow, that happened with mine too!' during the paired discussion. This allows us to move towards explanation and generalisation when we pick a few of these examples to share as part of the whole-class discussion. 
Other examples Two other examples of What if? that I have used are: Start with five numbers, all different, that have an integer mean. What if. .. 
ò 
You double all the numbers? 

ò 
You subtract 1 from each number? 

ò 
You add 10 to one of the numbers? 

ò 
You include an extra number equal to the mean ofyour 5 numbers? 

ò 
You copy your 5 numbers and work out the mean of the set of 10? 

ò 
You double your 5 numbers and work out the mean of the set of 10? 


Start with the first five terms of an ascending linear sequence, and work out the nth term rule. 
What if. .. 
ò 
You subtract 1 from each term? 

ò 
You double each term? 

ò 
You start with the same number but go up by 1 more than before? 

ò 
You change the signs of the terms of the sequence? 


ò You reverse the sequence? Can you think of a sequence of What ifs for an upcoming topic? 
The holy trinity strikes again 
In Tip 17 we discussed the holy trinity ofparticipation: mini-white¡boards . Partner Talk . Cold Call. We can see a version of it here in each of the three frameworks: 
ò 
First students have an opportunity to think independently, and I can see all their responses -a check for both effort and understanding. 

ò 
Then they are challenged to check each other's work, discuss strategies and resolve disagreements. 

ò 
Finally, I can call upon individual students to share their thoughts on interesting answers, and add clarity when needed. 


Let me end with a few questions I regularly get asked when dis¡cussing these three frameworks. 
Do I need mini-whiteboards to use these frameworks? I tell you what, if a mini-whiteboard manufacturer fancies sponsor¡ing this book, just let me know, because it seems to be all we ever talk about. In theory, you could use books or pieces of paper, but mini-whiteboards are really useful for all three of these frameworks for learner-generated examples, for the following two reasons. 
1. Mini-whiteboards make checking for understanding much easier 
ò 
I can more easily see student responses as I circulate the room. 

ò 
I can use 'hover your board' to see when my students are ready far easier than if students are working in books and I say, 'Put your pens down.' 

ò 
At any stage, I can ask my students to show me their mini¡whiteboards so I can get a snapshot ofwhole-class understand¡ing. This also allows me to make a physical note of any miscon¡ceptions or interesting examples that we can discuss as a class. 

ò 
I can grab a board and stick it under the visualiser or hold it up so everyone can see. 


2. Mini-whiteboards make peer assessment and discussion much easier 
Partner Talk is a key element of all three frameworks, and such discussion is made so much easier with mini-whiteboards. Students can swap boards much easier than they can swap books, and mis¡takes and messy annotations can be rubbed away. 
When should I use these frameworks? I use these three frameworks in three key phases of a learning episode: 
1. 
As a relevant prior knowledge check. These three frameworks allow us to revisit concepts students have met in the past in a more interest¡ing and challenging way. 

2. 
As a supplement (or alternative) to fluency practice. Students gain valuable practice both in the generation of their own examples and in the checking of their partners'. The opportunity to generalise and describe their strategy also takes this above and beyond the type of fluency practice I would usually give my students. 

3. 
As an end-of-lesson check for understanding. Students may have been working on a concept all lesson, gaining valuable fluency prac¡tice. But what do they really know? The depth of challenge elicited by these three frameworks, together with their inherent mass participation, makes them an ideal end-of-lesson activity. 


How long should these frameworks take? 
Great question, terrible answer -it depends. If students' knowledge is not secure and you need to do lots of discussion, questioning and modelling, then they may take up to 30 minutes. If students' knowl¡edge seems secure, then you might spend five minutes on a frame¡work. However, in this case, you may take the opportunity to probe students' understanding further, focusing on interesting examples and non-examples, student strategies, or explaining relationships. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider using learner-generated examples to check understanding and promote positive peer discussion.
