What's the problem? 
Multiple-choice questions get a bad press. Students can just guess the answer. You can't assess reasoning. The exam isn't multiple choice, so why use them in lessons? 
I love a specific type of multiple-choice question -a diagnostic question -and I am looking forward to telling you why, and exactly how I use them to check my students' understanding. But first, I want to know where you are at. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What role do diagnostic questions play in your teaching? 

ò 
What is your process for asking them and collecting in the information? 


What's the tip? 
You will be sick of hearing about diagnostic questions by the time you finish this book. As we will see in this tip, they are a key tool in my checking for understanding strategy, but later we will also learn how they can aid responsive teaching (Tip 37 and Tip 38), allow us to assess prerequisite knowledge (Tip 51) and even improve home¡work (Tip 91). 
The key to a good diagnostic question is that you should learn something from each incorrect answer. So, it is not just a case that Lucas does not understand this because he got the question wrong. Lucas' choice ofwrong answer reveals the specific nature ofhis mis¡understanding. That is valuable information as it helps us decide the most appropriate support to give -something we can plan in advance. 
When I built diagnosticquestions.com, I decided to only use questions with four choices of answers -one correct and three incorrect. But diagnostic questions come in many forms. Some have more than one right answer, others may have no right answer. 
Different types ofdiagnostic question 
A misconception I often hear is that diagnostic questions are only useful for assessing factual recall or simple procedures. While they can certainly assess both of those, they can also be used to assess different things. Below are some examples, and ifyou want an extra challenge, see ifyou can figure out why a student might choose each of the wrong answers. 
1. Factual recall 
' ' ' ' ' ' ' ' 

' ' 
po 
Which of the following is true? 

p + q = 90░ p + q = 100░ p + q = 180░ p + q = 360░ This question assesses whether students can remember what adja¡cent angles on a straight line sum to. Or we can ask a more challenging version: 
' ' ' ' 
' ' ' ' ' 
._.__ no 
48░ ' 
' ' 
[not to scale] 
What is the size of angle n? 

132░ 142░ 102░ 312░ 
Here students must recall the relationship, identify the relevant an¡gles and carry out the calculation. 
2. Procedures 
Sometimes it makes sense to assess a procedure with a single diag¡nostic question: 
What is the mean of the following numbers? 2,2,3,5,8 

20 4 3 2 Whereas on other occasions, we may choose to isolate a single step in the procedure: Tom and Katie are arguing about a first step to solve this equation: 
m -I = 20 
4 
Tom says you can write: ; = 21 Katie says you can write: m -l = 80 Who is correct? 
Only Only Both Tom Neither is Tom Katie and Katie correct 
I will often break a multistep procedure down into a series of diag¡nostic questions to help me identify exactly where in the process students are struggling, and why. 
3. Concepts 
This is the type of diagnostic question that tends to be overlooked, especially in maths. Here are two examples: 
Tom and Katie are arguing about factors and multiples. Tom says two numbers might not have a highest common factor. Katie says two numbers might not have a lowest common multiple. Who is correct? 
Only Only Both Tom Neither is Tom Katie and Katie correct 
Tom and Katie are discussing the conjecture about two numbers, P and Q: 

Tom says a counter-example is P = 1, Q = 2 Katie says a counter-example is P = 0, Q = 2 Who do you agree with? 

Only Only Both Tom Neither is Tom Katie and Katie correct 
Hopefully, you can imagine the rich discussions that could follow. 
Once we have chosen our diagnostic question, it is time to use it in the classroom. Here are three ways to do that. 
1. Challenge students to get a diagnostic question correct 
I work hard to establish a routine for answering diagnostic ques¡tions in my classroom -they are a high-value activity structure (Tip 6) as I use them in most lessons. In Tip 3, we discussed how a teacher, Anna, might introduce diagnostic questions to her stu¡dents, emphasising the importance of: 
ò 
Lowering the content demands initially 

ò 
Breaking the routine down 

ò 
Modelling each step 

ò 
Practising each step 

ò 
Writing the steps of the routine down 

ò 
Justifying the steps of the routine 

ò 
Checking for understanding 

ò 
Being aware of the Valley of Latent Potential 

ò 
Not letting standards slip 

ò 
Providing opportunities to retrieve the routine and the justifica¡tions. 


I begin with a cue: 'Okay, time for a diagnostic question.' This sig¡nals to the students that they need to be quiet and look at the board. 
I then reveal the question: 
What is 10 more than 43? 




44 53 430 52 
I give my students adequate thinking time in silence. In Tip 22 we discussed the importance of the wait time between question and answer, and how as teachers we are likely to underestimate how long we give for it. Obviously, the time allocated will change de¡pending on the class, the nature of the question and the difficulty of the concept, but as a general rule I allocate: 
ò 
Between five and 20 seconds to think about the answer -stu¡dents are allowed to do working out during this time, and 

ò 
15 seconds to rehearse their reason for their choice silently. 


So, about 30 seconds wait time per question. 
The silent rehearsal part is crucial. Not only does it allow students to practise their reasoning, but it also saves so much time when we collect responses later. I support students in two ways: 
1. 
I offer a reminder about halfway into this period of silent think¡ing to rehearse their answers. 

2. 
I suggest they make notes about their reasoning on mini-white¡boards or in their books. 


Then it is time for students to share their answers. We have discussed the means of participation in Chapter 3. For diagnostic questions, I like my students to answer with ABCD cards that are visually distinct to allow me to quickly process their choices, and I encourage them to write working out and reasoning on mini¡whiteboards. 
Very quickly I will have a sense of understanding in the room. What I see in front of me will determine what I do next. That is where re¡sponsive teaching kicks in, and is the subject of the next chapter. 
2. Challenge students to explain the wrong answers to a diagnostic question An advantage of a good diagnostic question over an open-response question is that there are some carefully chosen wrong answers visible. Once you have established the correct answer, you can ask 
your students to explain why someone might choose each of the wrong answers. 
Try it yourself for the 'What is 10 more than 43?' question above. Why might a student think the answer was 44,430 or 52? 
Students could think about this independently first, writing thoughts down on mini-whiteboards, then engage in a paired dis¡cussion to compare their reasoning, and then you can call on some pairs to share their thinking. 
This assesses students' deeper understanding of a concept. It also allows them to practise their verbal and written communication, which will stand them in good stead for those 'discuss, explain, de¡scribe' exam questions that students often struggle with. 
3. Challenge students to make the wrong answers correct With the correct answer established, can students change the 
original question as little as possible to make each ofthe wrong answers correct? As little as possible is the key here. 
This ensures you are assessing students' understanding of the con¡cept at the heart of the question, and not allowing their attention to wander into different realms of maths. Using the question above as an example, there are lots of questions for which 44 is the correct answer, but I am hoping students will think of '1 more than 43' or '10 more than 34'. 
Can you do the same for the remaining wrong answers on the ques¡tion above? 
When to use diagnostic questions to check for understanding 
There are three points in a lesson where I will typically use a diag¡nostic question to check for understanding: 
1. 
To assess relevant prior knowledge (Tip 51). 

2. 
As a hinge point in a lesson to determine whether we can go on to the next idea, or we need to back track (Tip 28). 

3. 
At the end of a lesson as an Exit Ticket (Tip 29). 


Diagnostic questions are quick to ask, quick to gather data, and -as we shall see in Chapter 5 -can lead to some fascinating discussions between students. 
But they will not be my only ways of checking for understanding. In Tip 24 we saw lots of different types of questions we can ask stu¡dents to assess the depth of their understanding. Some of these are suited to diagnostic questions, others will not be. Likewise, while an often-missed advantage ofdiagnostic questions is that in answering them students have to effectively rule out tempting wrong answers that increase their challenge, I also want to see what students know without such cues around. So, in a typical lesson, my checks for understanding will consist of a mixture of diagnostic questions with ABCD cards and open-response questions with mini-whiteboards. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider using diagnostic questions as part of your checking-for-understanding toolkit.

Tip 26 
Diagnostic questions 
What's the problem? 
When we ask our students to respond to a question verbally, for example following a Cold Call or after Partner Talk, we want them to be able to show us what they can do. The problem is that often the 
verbal responses we get from students are rather short and shallow. 'Five', 'triangle', '3x + 2'. 
It then takes some prompting and support to draw out the depth of understanding that the students possess but are unable or unwill¡ing to articulate. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do your studentsstrugglewith verbal responses? 

ò 
What do you do to support them? 


What's the tip? 
Tom Sherrington suggests we have some stock phrases, relevant to our subject, that we ask students to use when they are giving verbal answers. This can help keep students at the depth ofthe answer we are hoping for. 
Here are six examples. In each case, the teacher can write the stock phrase on the board so students can refer to it, model excellence where needed, and then encourage students to respond in this way: 
1. I think the answer is _,because 
'I think the answer is 5 because you need to do the division before the subtraction.' 
This is about as simple a stock phrase as you can get, but one that is really well suited to maths. It moves students' thinking away from assuming that knowing the answer is sufficient, towards challeng¡ing them to justify and communicate their thinking. 
2. I think the answer is . This means 
'I think the mean is 25cm. This means the plants in group A are taller on average than the plants in group B.' 
Another phrase well suited to maths. This helps students focus on the meaning ofthe result they have worked out. If students dedicate a lot of attention to the process of arriving at the answer, they may forget to put that answer into context. This prompt helps with this. 
3. First_, then_ 
'First, you find the size of angle A, and then you use this answer to work out angle B.' 
This one works really well ifyou want a student to describe these¡quence of steps to solve a multistep problem. 
4. First _, second_, finally_ 
'First, you give the direction of rotation, second, you give the angle of rotation, and finally, you give the centre of rotation.' 
If you want students to give three reasons, or three examples, this simple verbal structure ensures they have them to hand. 
5. An advantage is _.A disadvantage is _ 
'An advantage ofthe mean is it includes all the data points. A disad¡vantage of the mean is that it can be distorted by an outlier.' 
This prompt is ideal for longer-form responses in other subjects, but still can be useful for discussing the more subjective areas of maths, such as considering the merits of measures, procedures or approaches. 
6. Both~ however~ whereas_ 
'Both questions have an addition and a multiplication; however, in the first example, you do the addition first, whereas in the second example, you do the addition second.' 
This one is great to encourage students to respond to the classic question 'what is the same and what is different about these two questions/examples?' I often use it in the 'reflect' part of Intelligent Practice (Tip 72). 
What is particularly powerful about these six examples is they are the kind of structured answer you would want in a written response, and yet we don't normally speak like that in normal con¡versation. Therefore, it is easy not to practise them. But by asking students to respond verbally in this way, we provide an explicit rehearsal for writing, and therefore give students a better chance of being more successful at those dastardly -and poorly answered ¡exam questions that ask students to 'explain, describe, show' etc. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
To improve students' depth of thinking, and to better prepare them for written responses, provide struc¡tures for their verbal responses.

What's the problem? 
So, instead of relying on a sea ofnods or a chorus ofyesses, we have established that we need to check for understanding. And we have considered some different types of questions that we can use to check our students' understanding. 
The next question to consider is when do we check for understand¡ing? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
When do you check for understanding across asequence of lessons? 

ò 
What purpose does each check for understanding serve? 


What's the tip? 
Here are six key times I will check for understanding over the course of a lesson or a sequence of lessons, along with some thoughts on the types of questions from Tip 24 that are most suitable. 
1. Before a new idea 
This is the relevant prior knowledge check that we will discuss in Chapter 7. Unless we know whether the foundations are secure, any new idea we try to build on top risks crumbling to the ground. I like to use multiple-choice diagnostic questions for the prior knowledge check for three reasons: 
ò 
They provide a nice variety from how my students will partici¡pate in the Do Now (Tip 88) and the rest of the lesson. 

ò 
They are quick to ask and collect responses to. 

ò 
Wrong answers reveal the specific nature of any misunderstand¡


.
1ngs. 
However you choose to check for understanding during the prior knowledge check, we have a duty to respond. If the knowledge is not secure, we need to pause and support students. We will look at ideas for doing this next chapter. 
2. After an explanation 
Of all the lessons I am lucky enough to watch, it is in the explana¡tion or modelling phase where the check for understanding tends to be weakest. There seems to be an assumption among teachers -and believe me, I have held this -that a really clear explanation, supplemented by a well-chosen example, is sufficient to ensure understanding. As we have no doubt all seen when students subse¡quently tell us they are stuck, they don't know what to do, or start doing something completely different to what we have taught them, it is clear that good explanations and examples are necessary but not sufficient. 
We need to check for understanding after an explanation or a model. Many teachers do this during the explanation, regularly stopping to ask 'what is the value to this?' or 'why did I do this?' I don't. I like my explanations to flow in the way I have planned them out. I can avoid having to ask these questions because I have al¡ready assessed my students' relevant prior knowledge. 
But I certainly do need to check for understanding after my explana¡tion. There are two ways I do this: 
1. 
We Do. This is the bridge between a worked example and stu¡dents' first attempt at independent practice. As we discuss in Tip 66, I like to vary the means of participation during the We Do. 

2. 
Your Turn. This is a single question on the same skill and of the same difficulty as the worked example. I typically ask students to answer the Your Turn on mini-whiteboards, so I am able to 


get a sense of the whole class's understanding. We discuss the 
Your Turn in Tip 67. 
Again, if the check for understanding reveals a problem, I need to respond. 
3. After some instructions This check for understanding is often overlooked as we focus our at¡tention on the content of our lessons and not the structure of tasks. But I am sure we have all experienced instances where students 
have told us they don't know what they are supposed to be doing, or have started doing something completely different. 
Before explaining what I want students to do next, a simple thing to do is to say, 'Okay, I am about to explain to you what I want you to do next. After I have explained the task, I am going to ask some¡one here to tell me what I want you all to do. So, you had better be listening.' And then Cold Call a student or two to ensure they understand. 
If the instructions are more complicated, it is a good idea to give students an opportunity to explain the instructions to their partner and listen back to their partner's explanation. This gives students an opportunity to verbalise their understanding and get feedback before Cold Calling. 
4. After a task 
If the task is a relatively long one, students may be at several differ¡ent points. As you walk around the room observing with a purpose (Tip 7 4), you will have a sense ofwhere understanding is at. But it is always worth asking a quick question to check students have un¡derstood the idea that the task is centred around. 
This is your classic hinge point. Often it occurs around halfway through a lesson. It provides you with evidence in the moment as to whether you should press on with the next planned part of the les¡son, or revisit a core idea. 
Hinge points may be a diagnostic question with ABCD cards, an open-response question on mini-whiteboards, or a probing question that students consider on their own, discuss with their partner and then you Cold Call. 
5. End ofa lesson The end of a lesson is a great time for a final check for understand¡
ing. You have taught students something, they have practised it, and now you want to see what they have understood. 
A mistake I have made in the past is making the last question I ask students in a lesson the trickiest -some kind of challenge or exten¡sion question. The problem with this is many students may get the question wrong and therefore leave the lesson feeling like a failure. Also, I do not get a sense ofwhether students have understood the key components of a lesson. 
Instead, Daisy Christodoulou suggests ending the lesson with a question everyone should get right. This could form part of an Exit Ticket-something we will discuss in Tip 29. 
6. End ofa topic 
The end of a topic is another great time for a check for understand¡ing. Often these are end-of-topic assessments, done on paper in test conditions, with various stakes attached to them. Two quick things to say here. First, such assessments are a great opportunity for re¡trieval, so don't be afraid to include some content from before this topic unit. Secondly, ensure students have an opportunity to method select -in other words, as well as testing whether students can use a particular technique to solve a problem, ensure you are also testing whether they can choose what technique they need to use. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider all the different times we need to check for understanding, and what the most appropriate form of that check might be.

What's the problem? 
One of the most common times to check for understanding is at the end of the lesson. And I have done some shockers. 
When I started my career in the early 2000s, the classic way to end a lesson was to ask students to indicate, via the medium of the thumb, whether they understood the lesson. A sea ofthumbs-up would inevitably appear, and I would drive home safe in the knowl¡edge that I had nailed yet another lesson. We know from Tip 21 that relying on self-report is a bad idea. 
Things improved a tiny amount in the gimmick-ridden late 2000s and early 2010s when it was trendy to spend the last few minutes asking students to summarise what they had learned in the lesson in a text and then in a tweet, complete with emojis and hashtags. 
Both approaches weirdly seem to ignore the most obvious way to check students' understanding, and that is to ask them a question. Hence the Exit Ticket-so named as it can be filled out by the stu¡dent and handed to the teacher on the way out of the lesson. 
But like all good ideas, the Exit Ticket is also easy to mess up, and before you know it you have something nearly as ineffective as the approaches it is designed to replace. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Do you use Exit Tickets? 

ò
Ifso, what do they look like? Ifnot, why not? 


What's the tip? 
Here are a few ideas to make Exit Tickets as effective as possible. 
1. Ensure the Exit Ticket is quick to ask 
This is not a Low-Stakes Quiz; this is a quick check for understand¡ing. In maths, it should be two or three questions at most, and should take students no longer than five minutes to answer. 
2. Ensure students answer the Exit Ticket independently Exit Tickets are designed to give a reliable measure of the class's understanding. If students are allowed to collaborate, the data is compromised. We have the illusion ofcollective wisdom, whereby a pair ofstudents get the question correct, but we have no way ofknowing ifthey would have been able to do so independently. Explain to 
students that this is a low-stakes check for understanding, so you know who needs what help so you can support them accordingly. 
3. Ensure the Exit Ticket delivers useful data on that lesson Exit Tickets need to tell you something useful about your students' understanding, and tell you it quickly. So question choice is really important. I avoid questions that weave together several skills. If students struggle with those sorts of questions, I have to play de¡
tective to try to figure out why, and this takes time. Instead, I pick questions so they are each focused on one particular idea. 
4. Think about the format ofthe questions 
Varying the format of the questions on an Exit Ticket is a good idea to get a sense of students' depth of understanding. But we also need to remember the requirement for them to be quick to mark. 
Thinking back to the different checks for understanding we looked at in Tip 24, I structure my Exit Ticket as follows: 
ò Question 1: Factual recall using a multiple-choice diagnostic question. 
ò Question 2: Carry out a routine procedure using an open-response question. 
ò Question 3: Construct an example using the Give an example of format described in Tip 25. 
Here is one of my Exit Tickets. 
Exit Ticket Name ........................... . 
1. 
Expand: 
3(p + 1) 

3p + 3 3p + 1 p+3 3p + 4 
2. Multiply out 2z(z -7). 
3. Write 3 examples of single bracket questions that expand to give 12x -18, and make your third example as interesting as possible. 
Standardising the format of the Exit Ticket in this way serves two purposes. First, I get used to it, so I get quicker at flicking through the responses and identifying insights. Second, my students get used to the format, so get quicker at responding and also can dedi¡cate more attention to the content as the structure is familiar. Once again we have the power ofroutines we discussed in Chapter 2. 
5. Be strategic when analysing the Exit Ticket Teachers are busy. If Exit Tickets become burdensome, then teach¡
ers stop doing them. It should take no more than five minutes to flick through a class set of 30 Exit Tickets. 
Looking at my Exit Ticket above, I start my flicking by looking at each response to the multiple-choice question -this is super quick ifthe students have circled the answer clearly. If the majority of stu¡dents have got this question wrong, I stop as I now have my starting point for the next lesson. 
If all is well, next I look at the open-response question, which takes a little longer to check. Again, this is where I stop if my students are struggling. 
If most of my students are getting the questions correct up to this point, I turn my attention to the learner-generated examples ques¡tion. Here I am more selective as analysing these answers takes more time. I choose to look at two of my lower-achieving students, two students in the middle, and two of my higher-achieving stu¡dents to get a sense of the understanding in the class. 
I don't advise marking them (although I know some teachers do), and I certainly do not add written feedback. Instead, note down problem questions, common responses, and anything else that you can feedback to the class next lesson. 
6. Ask a question everyone should get right 
Daisy Christodoulou suggests that each lesson should end with a question everyone should get right. This could be the first question on your Exit Ticket. If everyone gets this question correct, then stu¡dents should get a boost in confidence. If they don't, then you have useful data that your lesson did not go to plan, and you can respond next lesson. 
7. Ensure the Exit Ticket feeds into the next lesson This is really important. Once you have analysed the responses from the Exit Ticket, plan how you will respond to them. The best 
time to do this is at the start of the next lesson. Here are a few options: 
ò 
You may decide you need to go over the concept in more detail, using a different explanation and series of examples. 

ò 
You could put up a fully worked solution to a troublesome question from the Exit Ticket, and then challenge students to complete a similar question. 

ò 
You could put up a selection of answers -one right and the rest wrong-and challenge students to identify the correct answer and explain the mistakes made in the rest. 

ò 
If you have a learner-generated example, you could display a selection of examples -again, some correct and some wrong ¡and challenge students to mark them. 


Not only is this responding to your students' needs, but if students see you are acting on what they do in lessons, then they start taking the Exit Ticket more seriously. 
8. Consider using a diagnostic question projected on the board 
Biased opinion alert, as I absolutely love a diagnostic question, but they do make a superb Exit Ticket. As discussed above, they could be printed out for students to answer alongside two other ques¡tions. But they can also be used with no printing required at all. 
Simply project a diagnostic question on the board in the last five minutes ofthe lesson and ask your students to think and vote using the flow described in Tip 26. 
What is the size of angle b? 


67░ 113░ 123░ Not enough informatio11 
Based on your students' responses, you now have two options: 
1. 
If the majority is correct, you can use the remaining minutes to dig deeper, challenging students to think about why someone might have chosen each of the wrong answers and how we could help them, or to change the question as little as possible to make each of the wrong answers right. 

2. 
If the majority is not correct, you can spend the remaining min¡utes explaining and offering up another example, armed with the knowledge of the specific nature ofyour students' misunder¡standings based on their choice of answer. 


Both of these mean you can respond in the moment, offering sup¡port and challenge without the usual time delay that accompanies other forms of Exit Tickets. And there is no printing required! 
We will discuss the process ofresponding to diagnostic questions in more depth in the next chapter. 
9. Don't be afraid to ditch the Exit Ticket 
This might be the most important tip of all. Our teaching should be dictated by the needs of our students and not the confines of the bell. We may plan a beautiful Exit Ticket before the lesson, but during the lesson our students struggle unexpectedly. So we slow things down, and offer more explanation and more practice. As a result, they don't make as much progress as we had planned, and so our Exit Ticket is no longer suitable. That is no problem at all. As we have seen in Tip 28, the end ofthe lesson is just one place where we might check our students' understanding. If needed, we can skip the Exit Ticket, and pick up the next lesson exactly where this one left off. A shoe-horned, inappropriate check for understanding does no one any good. 
Alternatively, we may be able to think of a more appropriate ques¡tion to ask our students based on what has happened in the lesson, and ask students to respond either on mini-whiteboards or by copy¡ing their answer on a blank piece of paper. 
10. Consider including a retrieval question on the Exit Ticket 
Back in Tip 20 we discussed the distinction between learning and performance. The correct answer to an Exit Ticket immediately following a lesson on that subject matter is a test of misunderstanding -we are observing performance. For a more reliable test of under¡standing, we need to see what students have retained in the future. 
As we will see in Chapters 10 and 11, we can provide retrieval opportunities via Do Nows, Low-Stakes Quizzes and mixed-topic homework. But ifExit Tickets are working for you and your stu¡dents, you could consider having the first two questions on an Exit Ticket related to today's lesson, and the third question related to content covered in the past. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Ifyou choose to do an Exit Ticket to check your students' understanding, ensure that Exit Ticket is as effective as possible.

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3. 
When are you going to try this idea for the first time? Please give the exact date and time. 

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
Right, this following story does not make me look great, but here we go. 
During my training year, I was observed by someone from the uni¡versity. In the same room was my mentor from my current school placement. During the observation, I asked a particularly challeng¡ing question. I could see from the looks on most of the kids' faces that they did not have a clue what I was going on about. So my eyes drifted off in the direction of Ben. Ben was smart. Ben was always right. So, I asked Ben the question and, low-and-behold, Ben got the question correct. And so I carried on with the lesson. 
The bloke from the university left suitably impressed. And then -and this is the worst bit -my mentor congratulated me on my strategic selection of Ben to answer that question. He knew as well as I did that, had I picked most other students in the class, their answer would have been wrong and I would have been compelled to respond. But I picked Ben, and Ben got it right, so that wasn't an 
.
issue. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Canyou relate to any ofthis? 

ò
What is the solution? 


What's the tip? 
One of the key reasons to use tools of mass participation like mini¡whiteboards or ABCD cards, is you cannot get away with doing what I did. In the face of overwhelming evidence of a lack of understand¡ing, you have to face facts and respond. This is what I meant in Tip 11 when I said mass participation keeps your teaching honest. So, if you can get the responses of all your students, then do so. 
But if a tool of mass participation is not available -maybe we don't have access to mini-whiteboards or the question is not suitable to be answered in that way because a lengthy explanation is needed ¡we need another strategy up our sleeve. Cold Call is good, but ifwe are going to sample one or two students and we want this to act as a good gauge for the understanding ofthe rest ofthe class, then per¡haps we need to be more strategic with which students we ask. 
Maths teacher Sammy Kempner's first tip was to pick the student least likely to know -so essentially do the exact opposite ofwhat I did in my observation lesson. 
These might not be your lowest attaining students. They may be students who tend not to be listening or those who may have missed some earlier lessons, or who may have struggled with this particular idea in the past. The key is that if someone was going to get this particular question wrong, it would be them. 
If that student gets the question correct, then there is a good chance -not a guarantee -that the rest of the class will also understand. This is related to Adam Boxer's idea of looking at the answers of your lowest attaining students first when using mini-whiteboards, and once again fits into the category ofkeeping our teaching honest. 
Sammy outlined four ways in which this might play out in the classroom. 
1. Cold Call 
Pick your least-likely students to answer a question. Their response acts as a gauge to assess the class's understanding. Sammy went further and suggested you start by asking for the explanation of one ofyour least-likely students and then jump to another ofyour least¡likely students, and then to another, sometimes in mid-explanation ('Okay Josh, can you finish off what Flo was saying?'). This checks that students are listening to each other, and also gives them a plat¡form upon which to build. 
2. Paired or group work 
You've told this class you're going to test them after the task by giving them a similar question, and you're going to pick someone to explain. You pick the student least likely to know in that group to act as a gauge ofwhether or not the pair or group has done a good job. You don't do what I have done in the past and say, 'Emma's group, explain the answer', because then the group self-selects the spokesman and you get an unreliable check for understanding. 
3. Written work When you're circulating and you've asked students to write some¡
thing down, you go and target the student least likely to be doing the right thing. 
4. Instructions 
You give really simple instructions and you want to just make sure that, even though you said it very clearly and you said it very slowly, they've understood the instructions. So you pick the student that's least likely to have listened or understood. 
Now, of course, we need to be careful here. One of Chris Such's tips was to depressurise learning, and if we are constantly putting the same few students on the spot, our lessons will not be a joyous experi¡ence for them. 
I think there are two ways we can overcome this while still adhering to the principle of asking the student least likely to know. 
1. Share the responsibility 
As we have already seen in Tip 16, Sammy Kempner does a lot of group work in his lessons, and if somebody in the group does not understand something, then it is the group's responsibility to make sure they do. So, when doing our checks for understanding, we can ensure that we always provide students with the opportunity to think for themselves, and then share their thoughts with their partner or group. Then when we ask the student least likely to know, firstly, they will be in a stronger position to understand, and secondly, if they don't understand, then the responsibility is shared between the other student(s) in that pair or group. 
2. Create a culture oferror 
This is the big one. If we have a classroom where being wrong is seen as a normal part of the learning process, then students will feel much less pressure to be right all the time and be more willing to admit when they are struggling. In Tip 32 we will look at several ideas to create such a culture in our classroom. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
To get a gauge of our class's understanding, and to keep our teaching honest, pick a student least likely to know.

Tip 29 
Exit Tickets 
What's the problem? 
Imagine you have set your students some work. It may have been a Low-Stakes Quiz, homework, an exercise to work through -any¡thing that your students do not yet know the answers to. When 
your students have finished, you reveal the answers, either by read¡ing them out, projecting them up on the board, or going through the working yourself. You then want to get a sense ofhow the class did -which are the questions they struggled on and, just as important, why did they struggle? 
My go-to strategy has always been to say, 'Is anybody still stuck on anything?' The problem with this is I was often met with a wall of silence. There is a real danger that we take such silence as a signal of understanding, when in fact it could mask a significant number of students who have difficulties but are reluctant to say so in front of their peers. 
I need a better check for understanding. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to any ofthis? 

ò
What is the solution? 


What's the tip? 
Fortunately, Tom Sherrington has a solution. He suggests the teacher says, 'Put your hand up ifyou got 8 out of 10' (or the equiva¡lent proportion if the quiz is out of a different number). 
Let's imagine that Miran puts her hand up. The teacher can then say, 'Oh wow, that's a great score, Miran. Which two questions did you get wrong?' 
Miran is feeling pretty good. She has received praise. Eight out of10 is a good score, and she has only made two mistakes. As such, she is likely to be willing to share the nature of her struggles. 
From here, the teacher is free to ask things like 'did anyone else struggle with question three like Miran?' And now as the rest of the students realise they are not alone, they are more likely to raise their hand, thus giving the teacher a clear signal whether this is a whole-class issue that needs dealing with now, or whether it can be dealt with on an individual or small-group basis later on. 
The teacher can then find another student who scored 8 out of 10 and repeat the same process. 
After this, a question such as, 'Is anybody still stuck on anything?' is likely to elicit a more reliable response as the floodgates have now been opened, and the discussion of mistakes has been normalised. 
Eight out of 10 seems to be the sweet spot. Students who have scored 10 out of 10 have no mistakes to discuss, whereas students with lower scores like 5 out of 10 may feel reluctant to share as they are embarrassed about the number of mistakes they have made. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
To dig deeper into students' understanding, ask who got 8out of 10 and build from there.

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
Have you ever taught a student who did not like being wrong? Of course you have! How about a student who not only didn't like being wrong, but took steps to actively hide the fact any time they were wrong? I would imagine you have seen that as well. 
Teaching is hard enough without students hiding mistakes from us. Such actions reduce the effectiveness of our checks for under¡standing, cause us to overestimate student understanding, and thus make decisions about what to do next that might not be appropriate for the needs of our students. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you experienced students who are reluctant to share their errors and misunderstandings? 

ò 
What do you do to support them? 


What's the tip? 
We can make it harder for students to hide their mistakes from us by using tools of mass participation such as mini-whiteboards and ABCD cards. But students will always find ways around this, either copying off their mate, switching answers, or opting out of the process. No matter how good our tool of mass participation, it can¡not get to the root cause of the problem -students' embarrassment at being wrong. As primary teacher Chris Such described in his tip -Depressurise learning -embarrassment is one of the most debilitating 
feelings a student can have, and we should take all steps to remove that from our classrooms. 
So, we need a more sustainable solution, and that is where the notion of a culture oferror comes into play. Students in classrooms that have a culture of error established are not afraid to make mis¡takes as they see making mistakes as a natural part of the learning process. They are also not afraid to admit to their mistakes because they realise they won't be judged or embarrassed, either by their teacher or their peers, and admitting mistakes and misunderstand¡ings is the first and necessary step to resolving them. In a culture of error, there is no embarrassment. 
So, how do we create such a culture of error? Here are 10 ideas. 
1. Avoid describing questions as easy, simple or routine 
We have already discussed this in Tip 8, but it warrants another mention. If a question is described as 'easy', there is an expectation that students should get it right. If they don't, they feel embar¡rassed, and seek to hide their error or misunderstanding. 
2. Use tools ofmass participation 
One of the problems with allowing students to volunteer to answer questions by raising their hands is that most students are only likely to volunteer when they are sure their answer is correct. This skews the proportion of answers heard in the classroom that are right, which means being wrong and learning from the experience is not the norm. Using tools of mass participation can help here. When students respond using mini-whiteboards (Tip 12) or via voting systems (Tip 13), we can focus on and celebrate interesting wrong answers, and thus help build a culture where being wrong is a perfectly normal and important step on the path to understand¡
.
1ng. 
3. Use the word 'might' 
When asking questions that require students to explain something, inserting the word 'might' can make a load of difference. 
ò 
'Why would someone think the answer is 5?' seems like there is a single right answer, and thus may feel like a test. 

ò 
'Why might someone think the answer is 5?' feels like an invita¡tion to share an opinion with less of a fear ofbeing wrong. 


4. Ifa student gets a question wrong, say you used to do that 
Another one from Chris Such here. It is all too easy to feel embar¡rassed ifyou sense you are the only person in the room to get a question wrong (even ifyou are not). Chris suggests we say to a student something like: 'Do you know what, I remember when I first learned this, and I used to think like you. So don't worry about it, it is only natural. Let me show you something that helped me... ' 
5. Ifa student laughs at another students' misunderstanding, don't stand for it 
We can say that errors are a natural part of the learning process and are welcome in this class, but all it takes is for one student to make a mistake and another student to laugh, smirk, or nudge their mate and everything falls apart. Do not stand for it. Sanction that child, and make sure everyone knows why. And do the same time and time again. 
6. Make mistakes yourself 
This is a really powerful one. Occasionally make a mistake yourself. This can serve two purposes. The first is it makes it clear that any¡one can make mistakes, even the teacher. Second, we can use this as a check for understanding-are students paying attention? Do they understand the concept enough to recognise a mistake? 
Of course, we should not overdo the mistake making. A teacher making mistakes in every example does not exactly instil confi¡dence. But a few timely mistakes, especially early on when estab¡lishing the classroom culture, go a long way. 
7. Use materials that normalise errors One of the (many!) reasons I like using diagnostic questions is that each question has three plausible wrong answers built into them. And, as we will see in Chapter 5, I make a point of discussing these wrong answers -'why might a student make them?' and 'how could we help someone who thinks this better understand?' This makes mistakes, and the discussion of them, a regular part of our class¡room routine, so that when students make a mistake it is no big deal. Far from it, in fact, because students learn that I am grateful to 
learn about their mistakes so we can discuss and analyse them as a class and all be better off. 
There are other materials we can use to normalise errors. In Tip 24 we looked at the 'criticise a fallacy' question type that can be used as a check for understanding. And in Tip 86 I will describe how I get my students to do corrections by creating review cards, which in¡volve students describing their mistakes. 
8. Have a pool ofphrases that help frame mistakes in a positive and productive way 
Such phrases might include: 
ò 
'I made a mistake here, but now I can see ... ' 

ò 
'I didn't read the question carefully enough, next time I need to 

ò 
'This mistake has helped me realise ... ' 

ò 
'I got that wrong, can we talk about why?' 


Maths teacher Craig Gouk suggests having such phrases visible around the room so students can use them when describing their difficulties. 
9. Don't record everything 
The vast majority of our checks for understanding should be low¡stakes. As soon as we start recording student responses in our mark books, the stakes go right up, alongside the anxiety, pressure, and the tendency for students to opt out or cover up mistakes. I never record data from diagnostic questions used in the classroom, and in Tip 87 I will explain how I never record data from Low-Stakes Quizzes. The lower the stakes, the more willing students are to make mistakes. And anyway, what are you really going to do with all that recorded data? 
10. Build a foundation ofsuccess 
The research into motivation, resilience and growth mindset is complex. But one thing that regularly comes out of such studies is the importance of success. Students who have experienced past success are more motivated, more resilient, and are likely to interpret any struggle as a blip (or even a necessary stop-off) on their journey to understanding. Students who have experienced past success do not mind admitting mistakes as that state of mis¡understanding is perceived as temporary. Students without that foundation of success are more likely to perceive their error as yet another sign that they are stupid and hence take steps to avoid the embarrassment of admitting that. 
Of course, making students feel and be successful is the whole point of teaching, and is no mean feat. But it is worth trying to instil a feeling of success -being careful with our choice of questions and activities -before we attempt to build a culture of error. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Take steps to create aculture of error in your classroom to improve your checks for understanding.

Tip 33 -Three ideas to encourage students to ask questions 

What's the problem? 
Have you ever had a quiet class? After each Thursday afternoon with a certain Year 10 class, I would have killed for a quiet class. But the problem when students don't talk a lot is it can make checking for understanding much more difficult. Sure, with a little pushing and prodding, students may answer our questions, but they rarely ask a question of their own. And no matter how good our check for understanding, without a willingness to ask questions, we run the risk of missing a flaw, limitation or ambiguity in our explanation, or failing to uncover an insight from a student that we could all benefit from. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Have you taught some quiet classes? 

ò
How do you encourage quiet classes to ask questions? 


What's the tip? 
Our discussion about reducing embarrassment in Tip 8 is important here. With a culture of error, students are more likely to ask ques¡tions as they will feel confident that their questions will not be derided. 
But there are some more specific steps we can take to encourage our students to ask questions. 
1. Don't say 'does anybody have any questions?: say 'ask me a question' 
The first is a request that is easy to opt out of; the second is a command that has to be followed. It is a subtle difference, but an important one. 
I have found that asking for these questions to be written down (again, mini-whiteboards work best ifyou want to use the questions straight away) as opposed to Cold Calling students to share them verbally increases honesty and the participation ratio. 
A step up from this is to ask each student for not one, but two ques¡tions. This means any obvious question that everyone is thinking of can be dealt with, and then we are left with a wider variety of care¡fully considered second questions that can be really insightful. 
2. Use Post-it notes 
This is from English teacher, Mark Roberts. He describes how after an explanation or a task he asks each student to write a question on a Post-it note and then come and stick it on the board at the front. Mark is then able to go through these questions and address any misunderstandings or insights. Again, the fact that the question does not need to be verbalised is important here, as well as the rela¡tive anonymity of a Post-it note on a wall versus a question spoken or written on a mini-whiteboard. 
3. Use Partner Talk 
We have already considered how I see discussions with a partner as a rehearsal in Tip 15. Here they could be a rehearsal for sharing a question with a wider audience. Students may have thought of a question, but then appreciate running it by a single pair of ears and having the opportunity to clarify and modify it, before being called upon to share that question with the whole class. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Take steps to encourage studentsto ask questions to improve your checks for understanding.

CHAPTERS 
RESPONSIVE TEACHING 
Checking for understanding is one half of a strategy -the other is responding to those checks. In this chapter, we look at how to respond to different responses from our students, including the classic 'I don't know'. We then turn our attention to how to deal with the common scenario where some students in our class do understand and others don't. Finally, I suggest a way students can record stimulating classroom discussions above and beyond simply copying down a procedure.

What's the problem? 
One ofmy favourite conversations on the Tips for Teachers podcast was with maths teacher, Sammy Kempner. We have already discussed Sammy's approach to group work in Tip 16, and how he asks the student least likely to know in Tip 30. Here I want to look at how Sammy deals with the answers given by individual students. 
Sammy described two ways he tricks his students to test if they really understand something. First, he makes deliberate mistakes while going through the solution to a problem. He lets the mistake hang there, and if his students do not immediately pick up on it, then Sammy knows they have either not been concentrating or not been listening. Many teachers do something similar. 
But it is the second tip that really caught my attention because it is a lovely inversion of something I have been doing for years. 
Picture the scene... 
I have asked Tommy to talk us through how to solve a quadratic equation I have put on the board (lucky boy). The first line of his explanation is correct, so I say nothing. So is his second line. But he makes a classic mistake on his third line (a minus sign has gone missing). What do I usually do? Say something like, 'Tommy, are you sure about that?' 
And because I have not commented up to this point -and because I only ever comment in this way when I have spotted a mistake ¡Tommy knows he has made a mistake, and so starts thinking about where the mistake is. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you done something like this before? 

ò 
How could we improveupon it? 


What's the tip? 
All of this is fine. But how about this as an alternative. 
The first line ofTommy's explanation is correct, and I stay quiet. Tommy's second line is also correct ... but now I say, 'Oh okay, so that is your answer? Hmmm... Tommy, are you sure about that?' 
And now Tommy has to really think. He thought he was correct, but now I am questioning him. Is he right? He looks again at his work¡ing out. It seems okay. Crucially, is he confident enough to say, 'Yes, sir, I am sure'? 
And what ifI throw this out to the rest ofthe class: 'I am not so sure about this one, guys. On your mini-whiteboards, write a big tick if you agree with Tommy's answer, or write a different line ofwork¡ing out ifyou disagree ... Okay, show me your boards in three, two, one... ' 
And now I have two things: a measure ofwhole-class understand¡ing and a culture where everyone needs to listen hard to everyone else. 
Here are some other scenarios where this approach could be used: 
ò 
'Is that the final answer, or is there something else we need to do?' 

ò 
'Is that answer actually feasible or sensible?' 

ò 
'Have you actually read what the question is asking you to do?' 

ò 
'Are you sure they are the correct units?' 

ò 
'Have you made a classic mistake here?' 


In the past I asked each of these questions exclusively when a student has got something wrong. But by asking them sometimes when students are right and sometimes when they are wrong, we reduce the giveaway signal (or our 'tell') and prompt our students to be prepared to back up any answer with sound reasoning. 
This is all part of a classroom culture that normalises error and pro¡motes good, hard thinking at all times. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Considertricking yourstudents by questioning them whether answers are correct or incorrect.

What's the problem?  

Have you ever done anything like this: 
Tip 37 
-
Some students don't understand 
-
Tip 38

-
don't understand 
Molly nods unconvincingly. 
Or how about this: 
Teacher: How do we simplify ...Dan?
~i 
Dan: You cancel 7 from the top and bottom. 
Teacher: Yes, you divide the numerator and denominator by the com¡mon factor of 7. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to these interactions? 

ò 
Is there anything wrongwith them? 


What's the tip? 
Both of these are examples of what teacher educator Doug Lemov calls rounding up. 
Rounding up involves a teacher responding to a partially or nearly correct answer by affirming it, and in so doing, adding critical detail (perhaps the most insightful or challenging detail) to make the an¡swer fully correct. 
This may sound harmless enough, but there are a number of issues with rounding up: 
1. 
The student thinks they have fully understood. Affirming an answer is a signal to a student that their understanding is where it needs to be, so thinking can stop. 

2. 
Standards in the class fall. 

3. 
The norm in the class becomes 'the teacher does the hard work'. 


So, what do we do instead? Saying 'nope, you are wrong' is likely to dampen student motivation and confidence, and a 'not quite' fol¡lowed by asking someone else fails to give the student a chance to build on what they clearly do know. 
Michael Pershan's approach is to ensure you always end an interac¡tion with the student saying something smart. 
Let's look again at those two interactions and see ifwe can improve things. 
Exchange I 
Teacher: What is a good first step when trying to solve 6x + 5 = 29 ... Molly? 
Molly:  You move the add 5 to the other side, so it becomes a take¡ 
away 5.  
Teacher:  Let's improve this answer together. The add 5 does not move,  
but it does seem to disappear from the left-hand side of the  
equation. What happens to it?  
Molly:  I'm not sure.  
Teacher:  How did you know that taking away 5 would be involved?  
Because that is correct.  
Molly:  Because taking away 5 is the opposite of adding 5?  
Teacher:  Yes. Can you remember the proper words we use for taking  
away and opposite?  
Molly:  Subtracting and inverse?  
Teacher:  Love it. So, what do we do to make the add 5 look like it  
disappears?  
Molly:  Subtract 5?  
Teacher:  Yep, and ifwe do something to one side of the equation...  
Molly:  We do the same to the other side.  
Teacher:  Exactly. Right, Molly, let's put this all together. What is a good  
first step when trying to solve 6x + 5 = 29?  
Molly:  Subtract 5 from both sides of the equation.  
Teacher:  Because ...  
Molly:  Because subtracting 5 is the opp... I mean the inverse of  
adding 5.  
Teacher:  Superstar.  
Exchange 2  
Teacher:  14How do we simplify ... Dan? 21  
Dan:  You cancel 7 from the top and bottom.  
Teacher:  We need to be more precise with our language, Dan. What is  
a better word than cancel?  
Dan:  Divide?  
Teacher:  Yes, and why did you pick 7?  
Dan:  Because it is a factor of 14 and 21.  
Teacher:  You are on a roll now. And the proper name for the top and  
bottom of a fraction?  
Dan:  Numerator and denominator.  

Teacher: Okay, so let's put it all together. How do we simplify ~i ? 
Dan:  We divide the numerator and denominator by 7, because 7 is  
a factor of 14 and 21.  
Teacher:  Loving your work.  

These interactions clearly take more time than rounding up, but they promote understanding, keep standards high, and establish the norm that students do the hard work. And by ending the inter¡action with a correct answer, we ensure motivation and confidence remain high. 

Avoiding rounding up 
Rounding up is really hard to avoid. Here are a few common defi¡ciencies in students' answers that teachers often round-up, so you can be on the lookout. How many ofthese can you recognise in your own teaching? 
ò 
Informal language. The student uses non-technical language. The teacher supplies the correct language. 

ò 
Problematic method. The student answers a question correctly, but uses a method that is problematic (not mathematically correct, does not scale, etc.). The teacher repackages the student's an¡swer with the correct method inserted. 

ò 
Unclear answers. The student answers a question correctly, but their explanation is long and unclear. The teacher interprets their response and relates it back using clear, concise language. 

ò 
Half-finished answers. The student only gives the first part of an an¡swer, so the teacher finishes it off. 

ò 
Different question. The teacher asks one question, but the student answers a slightly different one, so the teacher answers the orig¡inal one. 


ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Avoid the temptation to round-up students' answers.

Tip 36 -Seven ideas if a student says 'I don't know'

'

Tip 38 
-

Students stil I
-
-
discussion 
don't understand 
... 
What's the problem? 
Have you ever asked a student a question and they have responded with 'I don't know'? 
Of course you have! 
Tip 34 looked at how we might respond to a student who gives the correct answer, Tip 35 is about responding to a student who gives a partially correct answer, but now we need to turn our attention to what happens if a student gives no answer. 
What do you do in that situation? 
The problem with 'I don't know' is that it tells us nothing. 
ò 
Does the student literally not know anything about this? 

ò 
Or are they nervous about being wrong? 

ò 
Or can they just not be bothered? 


We could just accept the 'I don't know' and move on to another student. This is certainly the easiest and quickest thing to do. But there are three problems with this: 
1. 
We communicate that a lack ofeffort is okay. Once students learn that they can opt out, many will. Saying 'I don't know' is far more appealing than thinking hard, especially when the result of that hard thinking may be that you get the question wrong. 

2. 
We communicate that not understanding is okay. One of the most impor¡tant facets of our job is to help students understand our subject. If 'I don't know' is the end of the conversation, then we are not doing our jobs properly. 

3. 
We communicate that we don't care. This is the big one. A teacher who sticks with a student after several instances of 'I don't know', shrugs, glares and even a final 'leave me alone', is certainly an¡noying, and is certainly a pain in the you-know-what, but is also one that cares. And students see that and appreciate it, even if they give every single impression that they don't. 


But dealing with an 'I don't know' is easier said than done. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you respond when astudent says 'I don't know'? 

ò 
What challenges do you face? 


What's the tip? 
Let's imagine you have asked a student what is ~ of 30, and they 
have responded with 'I don't know'. Here are a few ideas that you could try. 
1. Ask 'what was the question I just asked?' This is a good starting point. If the student cannot tell you (as many will not be able to), then you cannot be sure 'I don't know' is due to a lack of understanding. Remind them (as sternly as is appropriate) 
of the importance oflistening, get another student to repeat the question, and return to the first student. 
2. Ask 'what do you know?' 
Once we have established the student has heard the question, we can proceed to probe their understanding: 
'So, you don't know ¥of 30, but what do you know? 
Nothing? Really, I don't think that is true. How about ~ of 
::> 
30? No? Okay, how about ¢of 30? Yes, now we are getting 
somewhere. So, what did you do to get ¢of 30? ... ' 
And so on. Establishing what a student does know about the idea you are asking about allows you to build accordingly, and gives the student that important sense of success. 
3. Use statements, not questions 
This one is from Dylan Wiliam, and I have never considered it be¡fore. The idea is that when responding to a question you can either be right or wrong, whereas responding to a statement is just like taking part in an everyday conversation. 
Using our ~ of 30, it may play out like this: 
::> 
3
Teacher: Tom, 5 of 30... (wait) ... I think we can find one-fifth ... 
(Let the silence linger.) 
Tom: Yes, by dividing by 5 ... 
On the page it may look weird. Indeed, in the classroom it may feel weird. But it is surprising how readily humans jump to fill in those awkward silences, and once the lid has been lifted and students are speaking, answers that you can work with are forthcoming. 
4. Go around the class . student chooses best answer and why 
This involves moving away temporarily from the student who said they didn't know, and asking a few other students what they think the answer is and why. You then return to that student and ask them to choose their favourite response from what they have heard and justify that choice. If the student lacks confidence, this gives the student some support to work with, and may help them be more willing to contribute. If the student is just being lazy, then this makes it clear that you can't get off the hook that easily. 
5. Secure the right answer . repeat . stretch This is one of my favourite responses. Here is how it may play out: 
Teacher: Okay, Tom. I am going to ask Olivia to tell us the answer, but you need to keep listening because I will be back... Olivia, 
what is ~ of 30? 
Olivia: 18. 
Teacher: Why? And a full sentence please. 
Olivia: ~ of 30 is 18 because to find ! of 30 you divide 30 by 5 to 
give you 6, and to find ; you multiply your ! , 6, by 3 to 
give us 18. 
(wait) ... Good. Okay, Tom, how do you work out ~ of 30 ¡
Teacher: answer in a full sentence please. 
You do 30 divided by 5 to give you f and then multiply that
Tom: answer by 3, which gives you 18. 
Teacher: And how would that approach change to find ; of 30? 
Instead of multiplying the f by 3, you would multiply it byTom: 4. So, you would get 24. 
Teacher: And if instead we wanted to work out l. of 20? 
4 
You do 20 divided by 4 to give you ! and then multiply that
Tom: answer by 3, which gives you 15. 
Teacher: Tom, you have nailed it. 
A few important things have happened here: 
ò 
Tom has realised that saying 'I don't know' is not the end of the conversation. 

ò 
Indeed, he has realised that it is actually more work than think¡ing about the answer to the original question. 

ò 
And he has left the exchange feeling successful. 


6. Partner Talk 
In Tip 15 I suggested framing Partner Talk as a rehearsal. Here it is a rehearsal for sharing an answer with the teacher in front ofthe class. 
We could either pre-empt 'I don't know' by allowing Partner Talk following a question (allowing for independent thinking time first). 
Or we could use Partner Talk in response to an 'I don't know' ¡'Okay, everyone, please discuss what you think the answer is and why with your partner.' 
When you then ask the question, you might still be met with an 'I don't know', but the beauty ofthis is you can then ask, 'Well, what did you discuss with your partner?' and get the conversation flow¡ing that way. 
7. Use a multiple-choice diagnostic question With a multiple-choice question, students are selecting between a 
finite number of answers. If they do not know what the correct an¡swer is, are there any answers they know cannot be correct? 
3 
-of30 = 
5 




6 50 12 18 
The student may be able to rule out Bas it is greater than 30. Or A because it is less than one-half. This process of ruling out may start to build confidence and also get the dialogue off the ground. 
This works best ifyou use the multiple-choice question initially. But ifyou plan this out in advance, you might be able to make this work with open-response questions by asking questions like: 
ò 
'Will the answer be greater or less than 30, and why?' 

ò 
'How about greater or less than 15?' 


The thing each of these ideas have in common is they are more likely to get students responding with something other than 'I don't know'. It is unlikely to be the correct answer initially, but it is a plat¡form that you can build from.

What's the problem? 
The focus of the tips in this chapter so far has been on dealing with responses from one or two students -tricking them to test their understanding, never rounding up, and dealing with the dreaded 'I don't know'. 
But what happens ifyou collect responses using a tool of mass par¡ticipation -mini-whiteboards or a voting system, for example? 
If the responses suggest that the vast majority know the correct answer, then you might check one or two students' reasoning and then move on. Likewise, the response is relatively straightforward if the vast majority ofyour students do not know the correct answer -you need to intervene, probably with an explanation and a follow¡up question. 
But what if the responses suggest that about half ofyour students know the correct answer and half don't? To either carry on or stop the whole class will not be appropriate for one of these groups. To let one group carry on with the next part of the lesson often leads to bedlam, with different students at different points, and it is near impossible to pull the lesson all back together. And the last thing you want to be is a glorified leaflet-hander-outer, dishing out ex¡tension tasks left, right and centre (which you have zero time to go through) while also trying to help those who are struggling with the initial question. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What do you do in this situation? 

ò 
What challenges do you fa ce? 


What's the tip? 
Here I am going to propose one approach to responsive teaching. I find it works most of the time. But for those times when it doesn't, I have a follow-up approach that we will look at in the next tip. #cliffhanger 
I designed the following approach for use with multiple-choice di¡agnostic questions. It works best if students have to make a choice between a finite number of options -ABCD, true/false, always¡sometimes-never. However, as I will discuss at the end, it can also work with open-response questions. 
Okay, so imagine you have asked your students to answer the following diagnostic question -maybe as part of a relevant prior knowledge check (Tip 51), or a hinge point in the lesson (Tip 28), or as an Exit Ticket at the end of the lesson (Tip 29): 
Which ofthe following means the same as 
ab2 

axbxb axbx2 axbxaxb axaxb 
Here is a decision tree I have designed that illustrates what I do based on my students' responses: 
( Ask, quesuonl 
i 
See all students' J

80%+ success? 
Quick check of

yours!
