Take a moment to have a good look at the decision tree: 
ò 
What makes sense? 

ò 
What doesn't? 

ò 
What questions do you have? 


The first two steps assess student understanding -the rest of the steps are responsive teaching. We discussed the first two steps in Tip 26, but as a quick recap: 
ò 
First, I project the question on the board. 

ò 
My students consider the answer and the reason for it, following the routine I have established (Tip 3). 

ò 
I allow adequate thinking time (Tip 22). 

ò 
I then ask my students to respond all at the same time, usually with ABCD cards, occasionally with fingers, but my favourite is 


when students do working-out on mini-whiteboards and show 
their choice with ABCD cards (Tip 13). 
ò And then the fun begins ... 
80%+ success?... Yes! 
If 80% or more of my students have got the answer correct, I follow the path on the left-hand side. In a class of 30, 80% means that I am okay with up to six students getting the question wrong. Six feels like the maximum number of students that I can realistically make time to speak to individually in the lesson to double-check they now understand the concept in the question. The chances of these stu¡dents subsequently understanding without my individual support are increased because following the vote: 
1. 
I call upon one of the students who has the correct answer and ask them to explain their reasoning. 

2. 
I pause for a few seconds to allow students time to reflect upon this answer (Tip 23). 

3. 
I confirm this is the correct answer. 

4. 
If needed, I offer my own explanation, filling in any gaps and adding any necessary clarity. 

5. 
I ask a student who got the question wrong to repeat the expla¡nation back to me. 

6. 
And then we move on. 


Quick and snappy... once the routine has been established and re¡hearsed, of course. Three extra points about this: 
1. No opt-out 
Step 5 is an important one. I want to create a culture where it is okay to not know something in the moment, but then the onus is on the student to do all they can with the support that follows to see if they can understand it. Learning is effortful. Switching off is not an option. And once students learn that I will be back to check, their attention and efforts are focused on the explanations given by other students and myself. We discussed some additional strategies for dealing with a response of 'I don't know' in Tip 36. 
2. Relevant prior knowledge 
The process illustrated by the decision tree is why I use diagnostic questions to assess relevant prior knowledge before I teach any new idea. In the past I have made assumptions -'Of course my students will know this, they are in Year 10!' But if that assumption proves to be false, then we will be in trouble as I try to build a new idea upon shaky foundations. Diagnostic questions are quick to ask, quick to collect responses from all students, and quick to respond to. If students' responses suggest the knowledge is secure, I can move on without using up too much lesson time. Iftheir responses reveal that understanding is not secure, then it is going to take longer, but it is a good job I have picked up on this now before this gap in knowledge gets tangled up in the weeds of the new idea. So, if in doubt about a piece of relevant prior knowledge, I will always ask a question about it. We discuss how to list, prioritise, assess and re¡spond to relevant prior knowledge in Chapter 7. 
3. Extra challenge 
If student responses are 90%+ correct and I have a little spare time, then I may set one of two additional challenges. They both start with the same prompt: 
Fantastic, so we can see A is the correct answer. Now, on 
your mini-whiteboards, I want you to ... 
ò 
Option 1: write down why you think a student may think B is correct. 

ò 
Option 2: change the question as little as possible so B is the correct answer. 


After giving them sufficient thinking time, I ask students to hold their mini-whiteboards up so I can see their answers, then swap answers with their partners so they can check each other's work, and then we can discuss one or two interesting responses as a class. 
I don't set one ofthese challenges for every question, as they take time. Crucially, if I am assessing relevant prior knowledge and I have a few diagnostic questions to ask, then I don't know if a later question is going to cause my students problems and hence take up more time. But if we have got through the relevant prior knowledge assessment quickly, then setting one of these challenges for the final question can spark some deeper thinking in students. 
80%+ success? ... No 
But what if fewer than 80% of students have the correct answer? In this instance, my tool of mass participation has identified an issue, and it is my job as a teacher to respond to that issue. I have two favourite strategies for this, both ofwhich rely on me withholding the correct answer for the time being. If students are told what the correct answer is prematurely, those that are wrong may lose con¡fidence and be reluctant to participate, and those who are correct may think their work here is done. Withholding the correct answer keeps the thinking and discussion going. 
Both options rely on students having mentally rehearsed their explanation for their choice of answer when initially thinking about the diagnostic question -a key part of the routine I have established (Tip 3). This means they are in a position to share their reasoning with their partner or the class immediately, which helps the pace, energy and efficiency ofboth of the following options. 
Option I: Partner Talk 
We discussed Partner Talk in Tip 15 as one of our tools of mass participation. Partner Talk works really well at this stage ofthe diag¡nostic questions flow. 
I say to my students: 
Okay, so we have some disagreement! We love disagree¡ment! Everybody remain silent for the next set of instruc¡tions. In a moment -not yet! -I will ask you to turn to your partner and say, 'I think the correct answer is ... because ... ' Then listen hard to what they have to say. The person on the left goes first. Then: 
ò 
If you don't agree on the answer, can you convince your partner you are right? 

ò 
If you do agree, are your reasons the same? And what is the best explanation you can come up with between you to help someone who is struggling? 


The first few times I do this, I will have these instructions visible for students to refer to. I will also Cold Call students to check: 'Ellie, who speaks first? Michael, what do you do ifyour answers are the same?' But the more you do this, the more automated the routine becomes, and the less teacher input needed. 
Option 2: Choose students to explain their choice Having observed the spread of answers, I make a physical note of the names of students who have gone for each answer -just one name for each. I find that quickly writing names down versus trying to hold the information in my head means I can free up attention to listen to their subsequent explanations. 
I then say, 'Molly, I see you have gone for answer A. Please can you tell us why.' 
A few points about this: 
ò 
I always start with A, then move to B, then C, then D. This avoids giving away what the right answer is. 

ò 
I allow time for students to process each explanation. 

ò 
I do not evaluate each response; I simply thank the student for sharing. 

ò 
If no one has chosen a particular answer, I will leave it out of the discussion. 

ò 
To reiterate, if students have already mentally rehearsed their explanation after they have decided what the correct answer is (as the routine asks them to do) this part of the process is super speedy. If not, then they start trying to explain on the spot, and you end up with a lot of 'umm', and 'well', and 'sort of', and time ticks away. 

ò 
My role is to listen, add clarification where needed, and ensure the other students are respectful and listening. A quick Cold Call along the lines of 'Zara, how did Molly explain her answer just then?' keeps them on their toes. 


Which option to choose? 
Partner Talk tends to work better when there are two very popular answers (one right, one wrong), whereas choosing students to share their explanations works well if students' responses are spread across three or four ofthe responses. But these are not strict rules, and it is perhaps just as important to ensure students are exposed to both strategies across a year. 
Wait time 
In Tip 23 we discussed the importance of providing students time to reflect upon answers before moving on. This only needs to be a few seconds of silence, but it allows students to process what they have heard from their partner or from their peers in the class, and con¡sider how it fits in with what they think. 
Revote 
Both options are then followed by a revote. Students can stick with their original answer, or change based on the new information they have heard. As well as observing whether we have now hit 80% cor¡rect, I am also making a physical note of any switchers -students who initially got the question wrong, but have now switched their an¡swer to the correct one. 
Just like with the first round ofvoting, the result ofthe revote will determine my next response. 
Cold Call some switchers 
If the success rate is now 80%+, I Cold Call some of the switchers. These are the critical students at this stage. These are students who previously did not understand, and I need to ascertain ifthey now do. Their vote alone is not enough. Sometimes when quizzed why they switched, students will say things like, 'Well, Josie thinks the answer is B, and Josie is always correct.' Not quite the reasoning I was looking for. I want evidence that they understand their mistake and why the answer they have now chosen is right. I usually say something like, 'Matthew, originally you thought the correct answer was B, but now you have switched to A. Please could you tell us why B is wrong, and why A is correct?' 
I usually do this with two or three students, choosing those who have switched from different answers where possible. I may have identified these students during the revote, or I can simply ask stu¡dents: 'Please put your hand up ifyou have changed your choice of answer for the revote.' 
You could adopt Sammy Kempner's approach-'when doing group work, make clear the group is responsible' (Tip 16) -and hold the switcher's partner to account (assuming they voted for the correct answer initially) if they cannot correctly explain why they have switched. 
Explain answer 
This part of the process is super important and also really easy to overlook. By this stage ofthe process, the correct answer will almost certainly be given by at least one of the students called upon. But students will also have heard lots ofwrong answers, either from their partner or from the students called upon to explain their rea¡sons. We need to remember that the reason we have travelled down this part of the decision tree is that fewer than 80% of our class knew the correct answer. So, it is crucial at this stage that we make it crystal clear what the correct answer is. 
I like to physically cross out the wrong answers on the board, adding narration that builds on the explanations shared: 'We know it cannot be Bbecause... ' 
I leave the correct answer as the only one left so students have a visual ofwhat the correct answer is. And then I offer my own expla¡nation as to why it is correct, being as clear and concise as I can (it helps if I have written this down as part ofmy lesson preparation), trying to use the best features of the student explanations shared: 'As Cal said, this must be the right answer because... ' 
Follow-up question 
We could leave it at that and hope our explanation has done the job. But I prefer to have that little bit extra evidence, especially ifthis question is being used to assess relevant prior knowledge. So, I like to ask a follow-up question on the same area. 
This does not need to be another diagnostic question. It could be an open-response question and students jot the answer down on their mini-whiteboards. It could be more than one question if I sense stu¡dents need extra practice. 
The key is I want some evidence that students' understanding is heading in the right direction. I need to remember that a few min¡utes ago a significant number of students got the initial question wrong. Students telling me they now get it is far less reliable than them showing me they do. 
If I can prepare these follow-up questions in advance when plan¡ning the lesson sequence, then the question quality is likely to be higher, and I can free up attention in the lesson to support my students. I write each follow-up question on the slide immediately after the diagnostic question. And if I don't need to use these ques¡tions in that lesson, there will be plenty of opportunity to use them again, so it will not be a wasted effort. 
<80% 
But what if fewer than 80% of students get the revote correct? Well, then we have another problem on our hands because we now have some students who have understood this from the start, and others who are totally confused. Time for another strategy ... but one which will have to wait until the next tip. 
How does this approach work with other types ofquestions? 
As I mentioned at the start, this approach is particularly suited to multiple-choice diagnostic questions. The alternate answers are baked into the question itself, and so are available for students to discuss. 
However, let's imagine we ask an open-response question (so, one without a list of options for answers), such as 'write out ab2 in full' or 'what is the formula for the area of a triangle?' 
We could record a selection of answers on the board that we have seen when using a tool of mass participation (e.g. mini-whiteboards or Cold Call following Partner Talk). Now we have our correct an¡swer (hopefully!), together with a series ofplausible wrong answers. We can then ask students to vote on each answer (one at a time with a show of hands), and hence join the flow diagram at the second stage where we now see whether we have an 80%+ success rate. 
With open-response questions we can capture a sample of student responses and essentially create our own, bespoke diagnostic question.

THE TIP IN ASENTENCE

What's the problem? 
In Tip 37, I outlined an approach to dealing with a situation where some of the class know the correct answer to a problem and some of the class do not. I find this works a lot of the time. 
But what about when it doesn't? What about when you have a group of students who are still confused, and another group who are ready to move on? If the question you are asking is designed to assess relevant prior knowledge, then it is clear that the students who are still struggling need your support. 
But what on earth are you going to do with the rest ofyour class? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What do you do in this situation? 

ò
What challenges do you face? 


What's the tip? 
Here are two strategies that I have found useful to provide a chal¡lenge to those students who understand, but in a way that allows me to focus my attention on those students who are struggling. 
Like Tip 37, both strategies work particularly well when using a multiple-choice diagnostic question as a tool to check for under¡standing. But, as I will show, both strategies can also be used with open-response questions. 
Built into every diagnostic question are incorrect answers -but not just any old incorrect answers. These incorrect answers have been chosen (assuming the question is a good one) to reveal the specific misunderstandings of the students selecting them. This is, of course, super helpful for the teacher as it enables them to diag¡nose the error and thus tailor their intervention accordingly. But the incorrect answers can also be used with students who have got the question correct. 
Strategy 1: Explain the incorrect answers 
Imagine you have asked your students to answer the following di¡agnostic question: 
Which ofthe following means the same as 
axbxb axbx2 axbxaxb axaxb 
Ruby has shown she knows the correct answer is A. While I support the rest of her classmates who are struggling, I set Ruby the chal¡lenge of explaining each of the wrong answers. Specifically, I say to Ruby (and anyone else who voted A and is happy they understand why): 
ò 
Choose one of the incorrect answers and write a sentence to ex¡plain why someone might think it is correct. 

ò 
Then explain with words, a diagram, whatever you like, how you would convince them that it cannot be the right answer. 

ò 
If there's time, do the same for the other two wrong answers. 


It is one thing to know what the correct answer is. It is a whole new level of understanding ifyou can identify and then communicate a specific reason why someone might not get the correct answer. And an even deeper level of understanding ifyou can think of a way to help them understand the error of their ways. 
Strategy 2: Make the incorrect answers correct 
Here is a nice alternative we could set Ruby. Can she change as little as possible about the question to make one ofthe wrong answers right? The addi¡tional constraint of changing as little as possible not only increases the challenge, but it also compels the student to focus on the critical features of the question -in this case, what an expression would look like ifyou needed to add the numbers, raise a number to the power of another, and so on. This allows Ruby to be creative and as¡sesses specific skills not covered by the original question. 
Who assesses this work? 
This is the big problem with setting extension work -someone has to let the students know iftheir answers are correct or not. Otherwise, there could be a whole heap of new issues that you are blissfully unaware of. But as your attention is (rightly) focused on the students who need extra support with the initial question, real¡istically you don't have time to check their work and offer feedback. 
Fortunately, help is at hand in the form of the other students for whom you have set the same task. I say to these students, 'Once you have finished, find someone else close by who has also finished, and check their work.' 
For Strategy 1, students can compare explanations. For Strategy 2, students can answer each other's questions to see if they lead to the three answers. If there are any issues, students can let me know, but I find they can sort out most disagreements between themselves. 
Better than this, I can also use their responses. The explanations from Strategy 1 can be used to support students who are struggling -students may have found an explanation, model or analogy that really hits home. The questions from Strategy 2 can be used as an extra check for understanding to see if my intervention with those students has been successful. 
How do these approaches work with other types ofquestions? 
Just like in Tip 37, neither 'explain the incorrect answers' nor 'make the incorrect answers correct' need to be confined to use with diagnostic questions. A teacher could ask any open-response question, record a selection of answers on the board that they have seen when using a tool of mass participation, establish which is the correct one, and then set students the challenge ofeither explaining an incorrect answer or thinking of questions to make each of the in¡correct answers correct.

Develop a strategy to respond if some of your students still do not understand following your first intervention.

What's the problem? 
Some of my favourite teaching moments are when students are locked in a deep discussion. Some students think the answer is this, another group think it is that, and a maverick at the back thinks it is something completely different. Each group makes their case, and eventually we tease out the correct answer, addressing each of the other answers in a thoughtful, respectful way. 
At the end of such discussions, I am always left with the same thought: 'I wish my students had some way of recording that.' 
Sure, the most important thing is that the discussion took place and students were paying attention. I have been teaching long enough to remember the dark days when every conversation you had with a student needed to be 'evidenced', usually by some pointless but pretty 'verbal feedback given' stamp. No, what I am talking about here is recording the richness ofthe debate, in particular recording the thinking and reasoning that went into deciding not only why the right answer was right, but why the wrong answers were wrong. I want students to be able to refer back to that, especially if their orig¡inal thinking was incorrect. 
In the past I have experimented by staying to students, 'Okay, now summarise this discussion in your books.' But that rarely works. The lack of structure and the vagueness of the instructions are barriers. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Would you liketo be able to record these discussions? 

ò 
Have you tried any techniques for doing this? 


What's the tip? 
Recently, I have been experimenting with a different approach. To start with, we need to choose a multiple-choice diagnostic question. As I will explain at the end, this technique also works with open¡response question types, but having a finite set of answers visible makes standardising the format ofwhere students capture the thinking a lot easier. 
We also need a certain type of diagnostic question. As we will discuss in Chapter 7, I often use diagnostic questions to assess rel¡evant prior knowledge before we encounter a new idea. But here I am going to use a diagnostic question as a hinge point. In other words, I am going to use the diagnostic question to assess students' under¡standing of the new idea up to this point, so I can decide whether we need to spend more time on it, or whether we can move on to the next idea. 
We need to make sure the question we choose (or write) is a good one. In particular, we need a strong set of distractors (the posh word for the wrong answers). Our discussion is going to be based on thinking about why the right answer is right and why the wrong an¡swers are wrong. We don't want any incorrect answers that are easy to dismiss, or that do not have a plausible reason for why a student may select them. 
Here is the question I have chosen: 
What is the size of angle k? 


40░ 70░ 110░ Not enough infor1nation 
Take a moment to work out the right answer, and consider why someone might choose each of the wrong answers. 
This question will act as a hinge point during a sequence of lessons on angle relationships. I am using the question to determine whether we should move on to angles in a polygon, or do some more work on angles in a triangle. But instead ofjust having an¡other example in students' books together with the correct proce¡dure, I want evidence of some deeper thinking. 
When I want to record a discussion, I tweak the flow we discussed in Tip 37: 
ò 
At the appropriate point in the lesson, I project the question onto the board. 

ò 
After adequate thinking time, I call upon each student to vote si¡multaneously to indicate what they think the correct answer is. 

ò 
I choose a student who has answered A and ask them to explain their reason. Then I ask a student who has chosen Band ask them to explain their reason. Then C, then D. 

ò 
I ask students to revote, and I make a note on a piece of paper of anyone I notice who has switched answers, or I can simply ask at the next stage, 'Please put your hand up ifyou changed your answer in the revote.' 

ò 
I ask a selection of these switchers why they have changed ('Seb, you originally thought the correct answer was D, but now you have changed to B. Please tell us why you think B is correct and Dis not.') 

ò 
I state what the correct answer is -in this case, B. 

ò 
I ask students to think independently: 


. 
What is the best explanation ofwhy B is correct? 

. 
Why might someone choose answers A, C and D? 


ò 
In pairs, I ask students to compare their answers to these two questions and come up with the best version ofboth between them. 

ò 
Writing on mini-whiteboards to support independent thought and paired discussion works well as students feel a bit freer to express themselves, and we can use the boards for the next part of the process. 


Now it is time for the whole-class discussion and to fill out the Recording our Thinking sheet. 
Here is a blank template: 
The correctansweris ___ 
An incorrectanswer is ___ My bestexplanation is: 
Someone mightchoose thisbecause... 
Anincorrect answer is ___ 
An incorrectanswer is ___ Someone might choose this because... 
Someone mightchoosethisbecause... 
You can ask students to copy the diagnostic question into the space in the middle, but where possible I like to have this printed. This saves time, keeps things neater, and avoids the risk of students making errors in the copying: 
The correctansweris __ My bestexplanation is: An incorrectanswer is __ Someone might choose thisbecause... 
What is the size of angle k? 

e e G e 
40░ 70░ 110ò Not enough information 
An incorrect answer is __ Someone might choose this because... 
An incorrectanswer is __ Someone mightchoosethis because... 
There is a downloadable copy of this on the resources page at the end of the tip. 
I then turn the class's attention to the correct answer, B. I Cold Call a few students to get their best explanation for why Bis correct, en¡couraging them to hold up their mini-whiteboards to illustrate their thinking. I then offer my own explanation, utilising what I have just heard. 
I then give students time to write up the explanation that makes the most sense to them -which might not be the one I gave -in the appropriate box on the Recording our Thinking sheet. When they have finished, I ask them to swap explanations with their partner and discuss any key differences. The fact that students know someone else will be seeing their work provides an extra incentive for clarity, and also helps them learn from each other. 
Next, we look at the first incorrect answer. Again, I Cold Call a few students, and then offer my take as to why a student might think this, making use ofmy students' insights. I then challenge students to explain why in their own words on the Recording our Thinking sheet, before again swapping with their partner. 
This continues for answers C and D until their sheets look some¡thing like this: 
The correctansweris __ My bestexplanation is: An incorrectanswer is __ Someone might choose thisbecause... 
What is the size of angle k? 

e e G e

I then give students time to write up the explanation that makes the most sense to them -which might not be the one I gave -in the appropriate box on the Recording our Thinking sheet. When they have finished, I ask them to swap explanations with their partner and discuss any key differences. The fact that students know someone else will be seeing their work provides an extra incentive for clarity, and also helps them learn from each other. 
Next, we look at the first incorrect answer. Again, I Cold Call a few students, and then offer my take as to why a student might think this, making use ofmy students' insights. I then challenge students to explain why in their own words on the Recording our Thinking sheet, before again swapping with their partner.

The sheets can then be stuck in students' books or stored in their folders. 
Why might this be a useful thing to do? 
Here are a few reasons: 
1. 
Students get to practise written communication. Getting students to write coherent sentences explaining their thinking in maths is tough. And they always come a cropper when asked to do this in an exam. So, any additional practice can only be a good thing. 

2. 
Discussion ofwrong answers is a step on the road to mastery. Knowing some¡thing is fine, being able to explain it is better, and being able to explain why others might not know it is better still. 

3. 
Students can refer back to reasons and not just answers. Looking back at correct answers is useful, but not as useful as a commentary on why an answer is correct, and why other answers are not. This could prove really useful if a student has forgotten key aspects of this particular idea when revising. 

4. 
Variety. Students may become tired of answering questions in books or on mini-whiteboards. This is something different. 

5. 
Improves our check for understanding. Reading students' explanations provides a fantastic insight into their understanding of a con¡cept, above and beyond simply answering a question. 

6. 
Promotes positive student discussion. Students can work together in a positive, structured way that they seem to enjoy. 

7. 
It looks good in books. This is at the bottom of the list for a reason, but I know how students' books look is important in some schools where book scrutinies can lead to serious consequences for teachers. The Recording our Thinking sheet looks good, looks 


different, and can placate the powers that be so you can actually crack on with doing your job. Indeed, I once had a member of SLT (an English teacher) proudly proclaim that this looks almost as good as what they do in English. I'll say no more. 
A few tips to make this process run more smoothly 
Like many things, the first time you try this idea it is likely to be a bit of a car crash. Here are a few suggestions that I have found useful: 
1. 
Explain why you are doing it. Emphasising the importance ofwritten communication and the power of studying mistakes may help get the student buy-in you need. 

2. 
Encourage personalisation. The first time you do this, students are likely to simply copy the explanation you have given. Encourage them to make sure their explanation makes sense to them. Re¡mind them that the Recording our Thinking sheet is for their forgetful future selves, and no one else. The most important thing is that they have an explanation that they understand. 

3. 
Encourage annotation. Likewise, annotations around the question to add meaning are to be encouraged -I have purposefully left gaps on either side of the question for that purpose. 

4. 
Encourage students to tell the story ofthe question. This is related to personalisation. We know humans are hard-wired to find stories engaging and memorable. Encouraging students to write things like 'first I thought it was C, but then I realised ... ' or 'Emma ex¡plained why the answer was B like this... ' may help this knowl¡edge stick. 

5. 
Showcase excellence. When you see examples ofthe above on a student's work, ensure all students get to see it, either by hold¡ing it up, sticking it under the visualiser, or using the technique we discuss in Tip 40 to get an image of a student's work on the screen. 

6. 
Keep at it. The more times you do it, the smoother it will go. 


Do I have to use multiple-choice questions? As I explained above, the finite number of answers is a real benefit when standardising the format of the Recording our Thinking sheet and keeping the discussions focused. But ifyou want to use an open¡response question, you could get students to respond on mini¡whiteboards, collect all the different answers on the board, and then select four answers (ensuring one is correct!) to base the exercise around. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider developing a format that allowsyourstudents to capture worthwhilediscussions in writing.

What's the problem? 
Much of this chapter has been focused on responding to verbal answers from our students. Now let's think about responding to written responses. 
We may see these as we circulate the room, written on mini-white¡boards, in books, or on worksheets. We may see them as students hold aloft their answers to a longer-form question. We may spot common wrong answers we want to discuss explicitly, interesting answers we want to delve into, or examples of excellence that we want to showcase to set a standard. 
In each of these cases, we need to get students' work in front of the class. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
How do you share students'work with the rest ofthe class? 

ò
What challenges do you face? 


What's the tip? 
There are two aspects to consider -the technical side and the peda¡gogical side. 
Actually showcasing students' work 
Here are five ways to do this. 
1. Hold up the students' work 
This works fine ifyou have a relatively small class and the student has written nice and big on their mini-whiteboards. As soon as you find yourself in a bigger room, with students writing in books, or solving multistep problems on their boards, you need another strategy. 
2. Use a visualiser 
Visualisers allow you to pop a book, worksheet or mini-whiteboard underneath them and have the image appear immediately on the board for all to see. You are also free to annotate and highlight the work with your pen. It is also worth taking a still image of the stu¡dent's work using the camera function on your visualiser so you have a record of interesting work that you can use in the future in the ways we will discuss below. 
3. Use cloud storage 
For many years, this was my go-to. Take a picture of a child's work on my phone, upload it to a cloud service (e.g. Google Drive or Drop¡box), ensure my desktop computer was also logged into the same cloud service, wait for the file to sync, and then copy and paste the image into whatever presentation software I was using. It works fine, but it is a bit of a faff. 
4. Use MathsUniverse.com 
If you have not tried the whiteboard function on mathsuniverse.com, make sure you are surrounded by cushions and other soft furnish¡ings because it is likely to knock you right off your feet. The creator, Jake Gordon, kindly recorded a tutorial for me when he discussed sharing students' work on the Tips for Teachers podcast (link in the resources). It is ridiculously easy. You type the same room code into your phone and desktop, take a photo on your phone, and it literally appears straight away on your computer's screen. No log-in, no reg¡istration, and no cost. Amazing. 
5. Have a guided gallery walk 
If students have been working in groups on vertical, non-permanent surfaces (Tip 16), then you can take the class on a strategic tour around the room, stopping to discuss interesting work produced by groups. This process is made easier ifyou draw a box around inter¡esting work you wish to discuss and preserve on the board as you circulate the room during group work, and number these boxes in the order you wish to discuss them. 
The pedagogy behind showcasing students' work 
Once we have got to grips with the technical side, we need to think about how we are going to make the process of sharing a student's work as smooth and as positive as possible. Obviously, this will dif¡fer from class to class, student to student, and situation to situation. All of these are helped by using the steps discussed in Tip 32 to cre¡ate a culture in your classroom where mistakes are not hidden but shared as the key learning opportunity that they are. 
In case it is helpful, here are a few examples ofwhat I will say to students in different situations. Please adapt accordingly. 
Josh has made a mistake and lacks confidence in maths: 
'Josh, I'd love to borrow your work for a minute. You have made a mistake, but I tell you what, you will not be the only one. And if I can use your work to highlight this mistake, you would be doing me and the rest of the class a massive favour because we could get to the bottom of it, and then there will be less chance any of us will make the mistake in the future.' 
Charlotte has got a question correct, but does not like the attention: 
'Charlotte, wow, that is great. Now, I know you probably won't like this, but you will be doing me a huge favour if I can just borrow your work to show to the rest of the class. You see, I want everyone to do work to your standard, and one of the best ways to make that happen is if I can show everyone what good work looks like. I will make sure ev¡eryone knows how lucky they are to have work of this stan¡dard support them.' 
Miran has got most of a question correct, but has made one mistake: 
'Miran, some lovely stuffhere. You have made one mistake. But do you know what, it is a really interesting mistake that I want everyone to learn from. I would love to be able to show this to the rest ofthe class, and see ifanyone can spot what the mistake is, and then we can all discuss it. I think this will be the most valuable part of this lesson.' 
The key is to make the experience as positive as possible for the student, but not to patronise or be dishonest. I am careful not to say I am glad a student made a mistake, because I really don't want my students getting things wrong. But once that mistake has been made, I genuinely am glad if I can share it with the rest of the class. 
If circumstances dictate that sharing a particular student's work is not a good idea, then you can always anonymise it, either by borrowing it and continuing to walk around the classroom before showcasing it, ensuring you don't mention any names, or quickly copying down the mistake yourself onto your mini-whiteboard and sharing that version. You know what will work best for your students. 
Ask other students to discuss the work 
The logical thing to do is to ask the student whose work it is to discuss how they arrived at the solution. But any time I've done this, engagement in the class seems to drop. Far more effective is to showcase the work, and say something like: 
ò 
'Okay, here is Josh's work. There is a mistake. Silent thinking: try to find the mistake, and when you have it, write it on your mini¡whiteboard.' 

ò 
'Here is Charlotte's work. There are three features I think are excellent. Silent thinking: what are they? Write each one down on your mini-whiteboard.' 

ò 
'Here is Jenny's work. Jenny has used a really interesting method. Silent thinking: see ifyou can understand Jenny's method, and ifyou can, then use Jenny's method to solve this problem on your mini-whiteboards ... ' 


Each of these can be followed by a paired discussion, Cold Call, and any additional clarification from the teacher. 
Keep a copy ofthe work you show 
It is worth saving the images of students' work that you take during the sharing process as they might come in useful. Here are a few suggestions of what you might do with the images in the future: 
ò 
Use with this class in future lessons or homework 

ò 
Use with other classes as an identify the mistake activity 

ò 
Use to aid future planning of this topic 

ò 
Share with colleagues with whom you share this class 

ò 
Share with colleagues in departmental meetings 

ò 
Use in CPD sessions 

ò 
Use as evidence ofwork for performance management.

THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider how you will share students' work to respond to their written solutions.

CHAPTER6 
PLANNING 
For many years my lesson planning was poor. I would start by trying to find a nice, shiny bright activity (ideally involving cutting and sticking) and go from there. In this chapter, I describe what I do now. It starts with a discussion ofwhat makes a good scheme ofwork. Then we turn our attention to the planning process, think¡ing about sequences oflessons, considering what our students are thinking about, the importance ofwriting out student responses, and how to anticipate the mistakes our students may make. Finally, we take a look at how we can create an environment conducive to Deep Work.

What's the problem? 
This first set of ideas is for anyone with responsibility for creating or adapting schemes of work within a department, or anyone who might be responsible for a scheme ofwork in the future. 
When I am lucky enough to work with a maths department, I am often shown their schemes ofwork. For those not familiar with this term, the scheme ofwork is a document that -at its most basic -tells all teachers in that department what they need to teach to each of their classes, and in what order they need to teach it. Many schemes ofwork aim to do much more than this, with lesson-by¡lesson breakdowns, including suggested or prescribed examples, explanations and activities. 
Schemes ofwork come in many forms. They may be homemade ¡produced by the head of department, the departmental leadership team, or a collaboration between the whole department -or they may be provided by external parties (such as academy chains, awarding bodies, publishers or resource creators), used off the shelf, or adapted to match the needs of the school. 
Hours and hours go into trying to create the perfect scheme ofwork. Indeed, most schemes ofwork I see are described as a 'work in progress', forever being tinkered with or completely rewritten at the end of the year ready for a relaunch in September. 
But here's the thing. Having seen more schemes ofwork in the last two years than I have in the previous 15, and crucially having had the opportunity to watch how those schemes play out in the class¡rooms of teachers of different levels of experience and style, I have concluded that very few provide the level of support, consistency or high expectations across a department that the author intended. I will often ask a head of department why one of their colleagues is using a particular resource, or pitching the lesson at a certain level, and be met with the response of 'I don't know, it's not what the scheme ofwork says'. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Ifyou are responsible for ascheme of work in your school, how confident are you that it is enacted in the way you intend, and how do you know? 

òAsa 
teacher who uses ascheme of work, how easy is it to know exactly what you need to be teaching? 


What's the tip? 
Here are seven ideas I have picked up from studying schemes of work that are consistently enacted as intended in the classroom by colleagues of all experience levels. They are ideas that are not tied to any particular pedagogy or level of prescription. 
1. Watch your least experienced members ofstaff use your scheme work 
If you want to get a sense ofhow well your current scheme ofwork functions as a resource to support the planning process, there is no better way than to watch some of your least experienced colleagues use it. 
Invariably the scheme ofwork has been created, chosen or adapted by the more experienced members of a department. We often talk about the curse ofknowledge as a plight suffered by teachers at the ex¡pense oftheir students. But the curse ofknowledge is equally preva¡lent between teachers. The way an experienced teacher has written a scheme of work with an intended use is rarely how it is actually used by their less experienced colleagues. 
Watching a less experienced member of staff use the scheme of work as their starting point to plan an upcoming topic is a fascinat¡ing exercise, and one I recommend you try. This is not the same as asking them how they use the scheme, as they will tell you what they think you want to hear. Actually sit down and watch them use it, and listen to them describe what they are doing and why. Using a screen recording tool like Loom is a good way to do this, so your presence doesn't affect things and you can study the footage and commentary later. 
This exercise might reveal a few quick fixes to improve your scheme. Perhaps it is not clear that colleagues need to look at a certain tab or reference a certain document. Maybe it makes more sense to have one column to the left of another column. Or -as is often the case -it might reveal that colleagues are not using the scheme ofwork as intended at all, and more substantial changes are needed. The ideas below might help you make those changes. 
2. Don't have lesson-by-lesson breakdowns 
Many schemes ofwork have lesson-by-lesson breakdowns: 'lesson one, cover this, lesson two, cover this'. Or they may have a Power¡Point containing all the resources for lesson one, and then another for lesson two. 
The problem here is that we are assuming it is possible to accu¡rately predict how quickly students will grasp an idea. It is not. Schemes ofwork provided by external bodies are particularly prob¡lematic here, presenting content that will somehow fit into a single lesson, no matter if it is 45, 50 or 60 minutes long. 
Often students need longer than a lesson for a given idea. If the teacher is religiously following the scheme -and why wouldn't they? -this can lead to explanations cut short, practice curtailed, and tasks abandoned halfway through so the teacher can squeeze in a 10-second breathless plenary and feel content that they have covered all the lesson objectives. 
Or the flip side: sometimes understanding is stronger than pre¡dicted and hence students get through the prepared material for that lesson quicker. In those instances, the final 10 minutes of the lesson are often given over to dead time, with little learning taking place as the teacher has not prepared anything else. 
It is our students who should dictate the pace we go at, not the bell. And schemes of work should reflect this by avoiding lesson-by-les¡son breakdowns. 
This may be obvious to you, but it needs to be made explicit to your colleagues. Many times I have asked a teacher why they have not spent more time on an activity when it was clear that students needed more practice. Every single time the response is the same: 'I only have one lesson to teach this.' 
An interesting version of this is schemes ofwork that have support¡ing resources per idea, objective or small step. This is fine, in theory, as long as your colleagues do not assume that each idea, objective or small step is to be covered in a single lesson. Again, this may be obvious to you, but I have seen enough lessons recently to conclude it is not obvious to all. 
3. Make relevant prior knowledge explicit Unless we have a good sense of what our students need to know ¡and what they actually do know -before we teach a new idea, we 
risk everything crumbling down around us as we try to build on shaky foundations. 
Good schemes ofwork make relevant prior knowledge explicit for each of the objectives. The best schemes ofwork provide questions to assess that prior knowledge. Without this, we are left hoping that colleagues will have the knowledge and experience to be able to identify exactly what prior knowledge is relevant and how to assess it. Why take that risk? 
Finally, ifyou are listing this prior knowledge in a column on an Excel spreadsheet or a section of a Word document, don't call it 'prior knowledge', call it something like 'prior knowledge to assess' or 'check for understanding'. This helps reinforce the message that this isn't just information for teachers to glance over as their eyes make their way across to the objectives column. No, this is some¡thing they need to act on -to assess and respond to in the lesson. We will discuss prior knowledge in Chapter 7. 
4. Have example questions alongside objectives 
Here is an excerpt from an actual maths scheme ofwork. Brace yourself... Year 8 -Topic 2: Circles 
Time: 5 lessons 
Objectives: 
ò 
Investigate pi. 

ò 
Calculate the circumference of a circle. 

ò 
Calculate the area of a circle. 

ò 
Given the circumference or area, calculate the radius or diameter. 


ò Work with arcs and sectors. That's it. A few questions you might have: 
ò 
How are we to investigate pi? 

ò 
How much depth do we go into when calculating the circumference? 

ò 
Do students need to derive the formulae or just know them? 

ò 
Do they need to leave answers in terms of pi and/or use calcula¡tors? 

ò 
And how exactly are we to work with arcs and sectors? Treat them as colleagues and offer them a cup of tea? 


The person who wrote the objectives may know what they mean. More experienced members of staffmay be able to guess. But every¡one else? 
Just as I am not convinced most lesson objectives are useful for students because they are too subjective and technical to infer meaning from, I don't think they are that helpful in schemes of work. As with anything we want someone else to understand, exam¡ples are needed. 
The best schemes ofwork I see have two types of example ques¡tions per objective. 
The first is what I call Check-Point questions. These are questions that assess the core knowledge of an idea. They are questions the teacher hopes every single child in a class will be able to answer after explanation and practice. 
In Tip 24 we met Pointon and Sangwin's taxonomy for categorising questions as part of the chapter on checking for understanding. As a reminder, their eight categories are: 
1. 
Factual recall 

2. 
Carry out a routine calculation or algorithm 

3. 
Classify some mathematical object 

4. 
Interpret a situation or answer 

5. 
Proof, show, justify 

6. 
Extend a concept 

7. 
Construct an example/instance 

8. 
Criticise a fallacy. 


Of these categories, Check-Point questions tend to fit into the first two categories. Using 'calculate the area of a circle' as an example objective, we might have: 
ò 
Factual recall. What is the formula for the area of a circle? 

ò 
Carry out a routine calculation or algorithm. Calculate the area of a circle with a diameter of 9cm. Give your answer to 1 decimal place. 


Notice how in that second question we communicate to colleagues that students need to know: 
ò 
How to change from diameter to radius 

ò 
How to deal with non-integer radii 

ò 
How to enter the calculation into a calculator 

ò 
How to convert the answer to a decimal 

ò 
How to round the answer. 


You don't want too many Check-Point questions -one or two per objective is enough. Check-Point questions may be used as hinge points in a lesson (Tip 28), providing teachers with evidence of stu¡dents' understanding in the moment as to whether they can move on to greater challenge, or they need to back track. Or they may be used as part of Exit Tickets (Tip 29) at the end of a lesson, giving teachers a sense of where students are at, allowing them an oppor¡tunity to respond accordingly the next lesson. 
In addition to Check-Point questions, the best schemes ofwork I see have Aspirational questions. These test a deeper level of understand¡
.
1ng. 
Aspirational questions tend to fit into the latter categories of Pointon and Sangwin's taxonomy. For example, for our 'calculate the area of a circle' objective, we may have: 
ò Interpret a situation or answer. Sketch a shape for which this could be the calculation to find the area: 16n -9n cm2 
ò Proof show, justify. Show that the shaded areas of these two shapes are the same: 
ò Extend a concept. Tom, Katie and Jack are cutting shapes out of card. Each sheet of card is 12cm by 10cm. Tom cuts out the largest circle he possibly can. Katie cuts out the largest semicircle she possibly can. Who has the greatest amount of card left over? 
Aspirational questions keep our colleagues' expectations for their students high. In maths, it is all too easy to stick to teaching proce¡dures. Once students have calculated the area of a few circles, they are probably ready for something else. But what? Aspirational ques¡tions provide a loftier goal to aim towards, and compel colleagues to plan more carefully how they are going to support their students in getting there. 
By the way, even if you follow a textbook or have bought into a comprehensive curriculum package, I still think highlighting Check¡Point and Aspirational questions for each objective is a sensible idea. Textbooks and curriculum packages tend to come with lots of questions. Having carefully selected questions you want every single child to answer correctly provides a key check-point to see where students are at, and well-chosen challenging questions pro¡vide a target to aim towards. Without these, lessons can turn into a progression through question after question with no accountability or overall purpose in mind. 
5. Have a careful curation ofhigh-quality tasks Have you got a shared departmental folder on your school's net¡work? Is it beautifully organised and curated? Or is it a dumping ground for any old PowerPoint, Word document and PDF, with files 
called things like 'JP8a -Copy -Copy(2)' and -my personal favourite -the incredibly helpful 'Worksheet1'? 
There may well be some quality stuff in there, but there is a cost in terms of the time it takes colleagues to find it. Also, a certain level of expertise is required to sift through, find the gold, and ignore the rest. The same with multiple links in a 'resources' column on a spreadsheet. 
The best schemes ofwork I see have one (or at most two) really high-quality tasks per learning objective that you suggest (or re¡quire?) every colleague to use. These have been chosen and road¡tested by the most experienced members of the team, and ideally are the sort of tasks that can be adapted for use with students of different achievement levels. This allows for joint planning and re¡flection as a department. 
For example, for calculating the area of a circle, I love this from the late, great Don Steward: 
Find asingle ring with the same area 

Source: Steward (2017) 
Treat yourself to having a go at this for a few minutes. How would you adapt the task to make it more accessible? How would you adapt it to make it more challenging? 
Having this single task tied to that objective and spending time thinking how we can adapt it to provide more support and chal¡lenge as needed is likely to be far more impactful on teaching and learning than having a folder of tasks of varying quality, and little support, that colleagues have to wade through. 
6. Appreciate that less really is more with schemes ofwork 
Perhaps the most important lesson I have learned from studying schemes ofwork over the last few years is that less is definitely more. This is clear when I see two weeks on a Year 7 scheme of work to cover fractions, decimals and percentages, and I know the students will not have an opportunity to do anything beyond following a series of algorithms, because there simply is no time. 
Spending more time on topics is the only way to ensure students understand them. 
But it is also true when I see a 32-slide PowerPoint for a lesson. Often the first 30 slides are rammed full of worked examples and fluency practice. The intentions are good -the author wants to en¡sure colleagues have the right amount of support and challenge for their classes, and the assumption is that colleagues will pick and choose appropriately. But my time watching teachers suggests that less experienced colleagues are likely to start at slide one and work their way through everything until the bell goes. And hence, even ifthere are some beautiful activities and problems at the end of the PowerPoint, the students never get to experience them. 
The best schemes ofwork I have seen strip things right down. For each objective they have: 
1. 
Questions to assess relevant prior knowledge 

2. 
A Check-Point question 

3. 
A high-quality task 

4. 
An Aspirational question. 


And that is it. This gives colleagues enough freedom to pitch the lesson to meet the needs of their students. But it also provides enough direction to ensure the journey is coherent, the expectations are high, and the students' experience is not a total lottery whereby the winners are the ones with the strongest teachers. 
7. Support colleagues with the enactment ofthe scheme ofwork Let's imagine you have made all the changes described above. Your scheme ofwork is looking amazing. But an amazing scheme of work on paper is meaningless if it is not enacted as intended in the classroom. As I described above, many heads of department are 
shocked when we drop into lessons together and they see how their vision is actually being translated in the classroom. 
By far the most successful way I have seen colleagues supported with the enactment of a scheme of work is to spend departmental meeting time actively engaging with subject content. 
When you are lucky enough to have all your department together, it is an absolute waste to spend that time dealing with admin tasks. Stick those in an email. Instead, dedicate time to one of the upcom¡ing high-quality tasks from your scheme ofwork, such as the Don Steward circle problem shared above. 
First, let colleagues engage with the mathematics. Let them try the problem on their own, and then work through it with a partner. If subject knowledge and confidence is an issue, be strategic with your pairings so colleagues can support each other as they engage with the content. 
Then ask them to stop and put their teacher hats on. Now they are familiar with the task, there is one key question to consider: how can we ensure all students gets something valuable out of the task? 
To help get to the answer, I find it useful to ask each teacher to think about running the task with one of their classes and consider the following four questions: 
1. 
How will they ensure the task is accessible to all students? Will they need to adapt the task? How will they introduce it? How will they scaffold their support? How will they facilitate students supporting each other? 

2. 
How will their students participate? Will they work individually, in pairs, or in a group? Will they collaborate from the start, or after a period of silent individual work? 

3. 
How will they check students' understanding? How will they do this during the task? How will they do it at the end of the task to get a sense of what students have learned and discover interest¡ing insights to share with the rest of the class? 

4. 
How will they challenge students further? If a student finishes the task early, what will they ask the student to do next? 


Collect together ideas on the board, and before you know it, col¡leagues are feeling both confident and enthused to try the task out with their classes. 
Working through tasks regularly in this way adds an energy and focus to departmental meetings, saves planning time, allows col¡leagues to benefit from each other's experience and ideas, and of¡fers support for colleagues whose subject knowledge may not be as strong. It really is a game changer. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Find out ifcolleagues are using your scheme of work in the way you intend, and if not, consider making improvements in key areas.

What's the problem? 
Ifyou have read Tip 41, you might be thinking to yourself, 'Yeah, that sounds great, but my scheme of work does not look like that, and I have no influence over it.' 
Fear not! We can use some ofthe key principles for good schemes of work to aid our own planning, no matter what scheme of work we may be following. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
When planning the first lesson on atopic, what is the first thing you do? 

ò 
How does what happened in that lesson feed into the planning of your second lesson? 


What's the tip? 
Here are six ideas you might like to build into your own planning. 
1. Plan sequences before lessons 
Planning individual lessons without first considering the full se¡quence of lessons within a topic is like going for a walk with your head down, and only looking up at the end of the journey to find out you are not where you wanted to be. Instead, consider starting your planning by mapping out the sequence of lessons. This does not need to be in any great detail. But knowing where a sequence is going is likely to lead to a more coherent learning journey for your students than taking things one lesson at a time. 
Maths teacherJemma Sherwood uses a PowerPoint file, copying and pasting ideas for explanations, examples, questions and tasks for a whole sequence oflessons onto blank slides, and then looks at them using the Slide Sorter view so she can see the bigger picture. 
2. Don't be afraid to tell your own story 
The order I used to teach would be the exact order the objectives were presented in the scheme ofwork. So, if adding and subtracting fractions came before multiplying fractions, then adding and sub¡tracting fractions was what I was going to teach first. 
As soon as we start thinking in terms of sequences oflessons, then each sequence becomes a story to be told. And the order in which we teach the different ideas within the sequence determines the narrative. 
Let's look again at the order in which the objectives are presented for the circles unit in our scheme ofwork: 
ò 
Investigate pi 

ò 
Calculate the circumference of a circle 

ò 
Calculate the area of a circle 

ò 
Given the circumference or area, calculate the radius or diameter 

ò 
Work with arcs and sectors. 


What story does this order tell? Something like: 'Here is this won¡derful, magical number called pi. Here is a circle. Pi lets us calculate two key features of that circle -the circumference and the area. We can also do those calculations backwards. Now, what about ifwe chop that circle up a bit? ... ' 
This is okay as a narrative ... but it is not the only one. 
Here is another: 'Here is this wonderful, magical number called pi. Here is a circle. Pi lets us calculate the circumference of that circle. Once we have the circumference of a full circle, we can also calcu¡late the circumferences of semi-circles, quarter-circles, and sectors of circles. We can even work backwards. Now, what else might we want to calculate about our circle? Yes, the area ... ' 
I like the second narrative better. The first feels disjointed. It's ordered roughly in terms of the challenge of these concepts in isola¡tion. But jumping back and forth from one idea to the other does not help students in their initial exposure to a concept. The second feels more coherent. We focus on one concept, go deep on it, and then move to another. 
But that is my personal preference. The key point is that there are lots of different ways to get to our destination, and before we dive 
into our planning it is worth thinking about the story we want to tell our students to help them get there. 
Inspired by Jemma Sherwood, I map out a sequence oflessons using PowerPoint and then use Slide Sorter to shape my story. I have seen colleagues go old-school and do the same using objec¡tives on pieces of paper. 
3. Don't be beholden to the bell With our sequence oflessons mapped out, we can plan our first lesson. The rest of this book will be dedicated to thinking about the 
explanations, practice and tasks that make up our lessons. But the key takeaway here is that any lesson is unlikely to go to plan. 
Some things take longer than we anticipate, and some things take less time. If we need to slow down, backtrack, reteach and continue the next lesson where we finished this lesson without our beauti¡fully planned plenary, then that is what we must do. Likewise, ifour students are ready for the next idea, let's give it to them now. 
We need to ensure we are dictated to by the needs of our students and not the bell. Having a sequence of lessons mapped out in ad¡vance will help with this. 
4. Reflect after each lesson 
Sequences need to be as responsive as lessons. If we plough through them slide after slide, example after example, task after task, the journey may be coherent, but it is also rigid. 
So, at the end of each lesson, I pause and see where I am at. If I used an Exit Ticket (Tip 29), what did it tell me? If not, what did my checks for understanding throughout the lesson tell me (Tip 28)? Do students need to practise the last idea more? Would they benefit from a greater challenge? Or are they ready for the next idea? I can then adapt my sequence of lessons accordingly, finding more ma¡terial if needed, or skipping over planned content that is no longer optimal. 
5. Plan the start ofthe next lesson based on the previous lesson 
Many schools dictate that lessons should start in a certain way, often called the Do Now. This is a task designed to settle and focus students. We will discuss the Do Now in Tip 88, but here I want to make the point that I don't plan the Do Now in advance when mapping out a sequence oflessons. I want it to be as relevant to the needs of my students as possible. 
If my checks for understanding revealed that my students' under¡standing is not secure, then the next lesson may start with a fully worked solution on the board and a related problem for students to try. If I feel students need a bit more practice of the last lesson's core idea, then my Do Now may consist offour questions based on that content. If the evidence suggests all went well last lesson, then the Do Now may consist of retrieval questions based on ideas students encountered in the past, or questions designed to prime or assess relevant prior knowledge for the upcoming lesson. 
I try to make each stage of my teaching responsive to the needs of my students, and the start of a lesson is no different. 
6. Plan questions before activities 
My planning used to be very activity-driven. Often I would see a nice activity someone had shared and think 'I'm using that tomor¡row', regardless ofwhat I was supposed to be teaching. The problem with planning activities first is you have no criteria for which activ¡ity to use. It may look lovely, but does it do what you need it to do? If you don't know what you need an activity to do, that is an impos¡sible question to answer. 
I had things the wrong way round. I think a more sensible way to order our thinking when planning is to consider: 
1. 
What new idea do my students need to know? (Objectives from the scheme ofwork.) 

2. 
What does an understanding of this idea look like? (Questions students answer.) 

3. 
How will I help my students develop that understanding? (Expla¡nations and activities.) 


When planning a lesson I begin by writing or sourcing: 
ò 
A question that I hope everyone will be able to answer correctly by the end of the lesson. This is the Check-Point question de¡scribed in Tip 41. This question could be used as a hinge point in the lesson or an Exit Ticket at the end of the lesson. The Check¡Point question gives us a check for understanding, and also helps focus the planning of our explanations and activities. 

ò 
Some more challenging questions. These are the Aspirational questions we discussed in Tip 41. They move beyond simple procedure, either extending a procedure or testing a deeper un¡derstanding of the concept. The Aspirational questions keep our expectations for our students high. 


Once you know where your students need to get to, you are in a better place to plan the explanations, examples and tasks to help them get there. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Even ifyour scheme of work does not support your planning in the way you would like, there are still things you can do to improve the planning process.

What's the problem? 
Here is an unfortunate irony: some of the worst lessons I have taught have been the ones that I have planned the most for. Ver¡bose explanations, a procession of worked examples, check after check after check for understanding. And activities? Oh yes, plenty of those. Silent work, paired tasks, group tasks, quizzes, card sorts. You name it, it was in the plan. 
And needless to say, the lesson was a car crash. Specifically, the problem was twofold: 
1. 
I never managed to fit everything in, meaning there was some really good stuff towards the end of my plan that my students never got to experience. 

2. 
Of the stuff I did manage to fit in, it was rushed as I had one eye on my watch. This meant my students never got as much out ofwhat I planned as I had hoped because we had no time for depth. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you experienced overplanning? 

ò 
Have you taught lessons that sound like mine? 


What's the tip? 
Teacher educator Harry Fletcher-Wood's advice is that most lesson plans would be improved by removing half of the content from them, and spending more planning time and more lesson time on what is left. This advice applies across most phases of a lesson. 
Explanations 
Instead of a lengthy explanation, be really concise and clear. Use fewer words, and make sure those words are easy for your students to understand. Support your words with a visual and give students time to process it. We discuss explanations in Chapter 8. 
Worked examples 
Instead of several worked examples that you have to whizz through, choose one or two that you have thought carefully about. Spend time going through them. Consider modelling them in silence first, and then using carefully considered self-explanation prompts to give students a better opportunity of understanding the process. Follow this up with a We Do and a Your Turn to check students are on the path to learning. We also discuss modelling in Chapter 8. 
Tasks 
This was the biggest problem for me. Instead of planning lots of different tasks, choose one or two high-quality ones that help stu¡dents practise exactly what you want them to practise. Spend your time planning questions and explanations that support or challenge students through these tasks so everyone can work on the same thing. This avoids the need for costly switching, where a new task has to be handed out, explained and assessed. 
This doesn't just apply to single lessons either. Try to build up a bank of task structures that you can use across different topics. That way both you and your students can spend less time and at¡tention thinking about the structure of the task, and more thinking about the content. We discussed such high-value activity structures in Tip 6. 
Checks for understanding 
Instead of lots of checks for understanding throughout the lesson, choose one or two critical points, and think carefully about what questions to ask and how you will assess students' answers. Just as important, plan how you will respond if that check reveals that understanding is not where you hoped it would be. We discussed checking for understanding and responding to that check in Chap¡ters 4 and 5. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
When planning alesson, it is definitely acase of quality over quantity-see ifyou can halve the content and make better use of what is left.

OVER TO YOU

2.

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
In my first book, How I Wish I'd Taught Maths, I told the Swiss roll story. I described how I had tried to teach a key concept about fractions via the medium of the confectionery item known as the Swiss roll. Years later, when I talked about the lesson to one of my students, she vividly remembered the Swiss rolls, but not a lot else. 
For this book I want to share two stories -one where the issue may be clear, the second where it is more subtle. 
The orange 
When Dani Quinn, former head ofmaths at Michaela Community School, appeared on my Mr Barton Maths podcast, I asked her to share her favourite failure. Dani chose a lesson involving oranges. 
The surface area of a sphere is 4nr2. The area of a circle is nr2. This means the surface area of one sphere will exactly cover the area of four circles with the same radius. Great news, thought Dani. That means ifyou take a real-life example of a sphere (an orange) and peel it, the peel should exactly cover the blank templates of four circles with the same radius. Real life maths and an engaging demonstration. And so lots ofpeeling, juice-squirting and fun en¡sued. What could be wrong with that? 
Dani described two problems. 
First, the demonstration does not quite work. The peel sort ofcovers the four circles, but not quite. Oranges are not perfect spheres, it is hard to measure their radius, bits ofpeel go missing, overlaps happen, and so on. So students end up thinking that the formula is more of an approximation than an exact mathematical relationship. You see something similar when introducing pi with string. 
But it is the second problem I want to focus on here. What will the students remember from that lesson? The formula for the surface area of a sphere? Or all the peeling and squirting? 
Laws of indices 
Recently I watched a Year 10 lesson on laws of indices. Here are the questions the students were working through as part of their inde¡pendent practice: 
Simplify these expressions writing the answers in index form 
1.(a)23 x22 
2. 
(a) a3 x a2 (c) a x aÀ3 (d) a5 X a12 

3. 
(a) 23 7 22 (c) 7-3 7 7 


4. (a) a3 7 a2 

ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you had similar experiences to Dani with her oranges? 

ò
Can you see what the issue is with the indices practice? 


What's the tip? 
In a 2018 interview, author and professor of psychology Daniel Will¡ingham explained: 
'Everybody appreciates that without attention there is no learning. And if children aren't paying attention then they're not going to learn anything from the lesson. 
'The idea that "memory really depends on what you think about" is so obvious once it's articulated, but it's something that a lot of people hadn't thought about, and I think it is as important as the idea that "without attention there is no learning".... Whatever children are thinking about is essentially what they're paying attention to and that's what they're going to remember.' 
In his fantastic book, Why Don't Students Like School?, Willingham builds on this theme: 
'Review each lesson plan in terms ofwhat the student is 
likely to think about. This sentence may represent the most 
general and useful idea that cognitive psychology can offer 
teachers.' 
The reason students remembered Swiss rolls instead of fractions in my lesson, and oranges instead of the surface area of a sphere in Dani's, is because that was exactly what they were thinking about. The experience was memorable, but the intended takeaway was not. 
But what about the indices lesson? 
Here we don't have an obvious distracting surface feature. But let's think about what the teacher intends the students to be thinking about-the laws of indices. However, notice how once you realise that when powers are being multiplied you add the indices, you are good to go for all parts of questions 1 and 2. Likewise, when you re¡alise you need to subtract when dividing powers, that is questions 3 and 4 sorted. And the same for multiplying with question 5. Hence, students only had to think about what index law is required three times. The majority of their attention was directed towards carrying out the number operations, especially those with negative numbers. That's what students were struggling with and thinking hard about. The impact of this was made obvious in the next lesson in the Do 
2
Now retrieval starter when students were presented with m6 + m in isolation, and they had no idea what they had to do with the numbers. 
A better version of this worksheet would have had simpler numbers to begin with, and a lot more switching between operations. Per¡haps starting like this and then progressing to algebraic bases: 
5
6 53
1. X 
7
6 73 
,2. X 
3. 
ò
76 ò 73 
76 
4. 
73 
5. (76)3 6. 7 X 73 
Then students' attention would have been on what operation was needed and why. 
You also see this issue when prior knowledge is woven into a new concept, and either the prior knowledge is not secure, or the diffi¡culty of the prior knowledge is pitched too high. In these cases, stu¡dents end up paying more attention to the prior knowledge than the new concept, and hence they don't remember as much of the new concept as we would hope. In Tip 57 I describe a lesson I saw on the area of a trapezium that had this very problem. 
When we choose activities and tasks for our students to do, a key question we should ask ourselves is 'what are my students likely to be thinking about when doing this task?' And if it is not what we want them to be thinking about, then we should reconsider the task. 
Where is the fun?? 
When I speak to teachers about this, particularly with reference to Swiss rolls and oranges, many are concerned that this means lessons cannot be fun -that we have to pick boring activities with¡out any colour or flavour. I am not sure that is true. I want my stu¡dents to enjoy my lessons. I think the best way to achieve this is to make them feel successful and knowledgeable, but I also want them to enjoy the activities I choose for them. 
I think there are activities that are both fun and worthwhile learning experiences. The key is to consider how much of students' attention is on the structure of the activity, and how much is on the content of the activity. Students have limited attention. When cutting up Swiss rolls or peeling oranges, that is pretty much all students think about due to the novelty factor, and so it is all they remember. But you may be able to think of activities where more of our students' attention is shifted towards the relevant content. The high-value ac¡tivity structures we discussed in Tip 6 are an example of this. Once the structure of an activity becomes familiar, more attention can be dedicated to the content. 
Of course, there is an argument for having fun in lessons for fun's sake. Students will remember and cherish those moments. The mistake I have made is trying to tie those moments into key learn¡ing intentions, and that is when we can get into trouble. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
When planning activities and explanations in your sequence of lessons, always ask yourself, 'What are my students likely to be thinking about?' And if it is not about the content, then reconsider the task.
