What's the problem? 
In my early days, I was a cocky teacher. I was a decent mathemati¡cian at school, and relished the challenge ofbeing stuck on a tricky maths problem. 
So, if someone had suggested to me that I should write out the responses I would hope my students would give to every single question I planned to ask in each lesson, I would have politely declined. You see, I didn't need to. I could evaluate a student's re¡sponse to a question in the moment because I could quickly work out the answer myself. As if I needed to bother going through all the questions in advance. Come on, live a little! 
This cavalier approach went wrong for me in three ways. 
First, it cost a lot of precious lesson time. A student would give an answer, and I would have to figure out ifit was right. Or a student would show me their work so far, and ask me if they were on the right track. Or a student would get stuck, and I would need to try to figure out the best way to help. Each of these interactions may only take a few seconds, but those seconds add up. 
Second, just as important as taking time, these interactions took up my attention. If I am having to think hard about what the correct answer is, either working it out on the spot or trying to remember what it was when I looked at it the previous night, a significant part of me is not present in the lesson at that moment. I cannot respond to the needs of my students effectively as my mind is elsewhere. 
Finally, on several occasions, I was caught out. I had given my students a question to answer that was either far too difficult, introduced something they had never been taught, or was math¡ematically impossible. This knocked their confidence as they as¡sumed that their inability to work out the answer was down to their inability with mathematics. I rarely feel worse in lessons than when that happens. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you plan out answers in advance? 

ò
Can you relate to any of the problems I encountered? 


What's the tip? 
I think writing down -yes, physically writing down -the responses you hope to get for every question you ask students in lessons (what I will call 'ideal student responses') is one of the most impor¡tant parts of the planning process. It allows you to better determine ifthe material you have planned is appropriate for your students, saves you time, and frees up your attention to be more present and effective in the lesson. 
Here are six ideas to make this process of writing down ideal re¡sponses even more effective. 
1. Write the working out, not just the answer 
How often do maths teachers say to students 'show your working, not just the answer'? We need to take that advice ourselves when it comes to writing down ideal student responses. Having the answers to hand is great for checking students' final answers, but having the working out to hand means we can better help students who are partway through a problem, and better identify the specific place they may have gone wrong. So, show your working out! 
2. Carry the responses around with you in the lesson 
Carrying the piece of paper you have written the ideal student re¡sponses on means you always have them with you. So, if a student is stuck, or wants to check their work, you can do so immediately. And ifyou are observing their practice with purpose (Tip 74), you can pick up on any issues straight away as you know what you are looking for. 
3. Use the same format as the students The previous idea can be enhanced ifyou write your ideal answers in the same format as your students. So, ifyour students are an¡swering on a worksheet, then you write your answers on the same worksheet instead of a random piece of paper. If they are answering on a mini-whiteboard split into quadrants, then write your answers on a mini-whiteboard split into quadrants (or at least a piece of 
paper laid out the same way). This makes it much quicker to com¡pare your answers to your students and respond accordingly. 
4. Write down ideal responses for oral as well as written responses 
Of course, we do not just give students questions to respond to in books, or on worksheets or mini-whiteboards. We also ask ques¡tions for students to respond to orally. We need to plan ideal re¡sponses to these types of questions as well. This is especially true if we ask a question to assess knowledge of a complex procedure, or a probing question to assess conceptual understanding. 
For example, in maths, I may ask my students to explain whether a square is a type oftrapezium and to justify their answer. 
One student I call upon starts discussing the two pairs ofparallel sides. Another student wants to talk about the side lengths. A third student mentions the angles. We have had a great discussion, but now I want to add some clarity. The problem is, I can't remember the exact answer I had thought about last night. My attention has been drained from listening, moderating and responding to my stu¡dents' insights. What should have been a moment of concise clarity becomes a muddled mess as I try to pull everything together. 
If instead I have thought about the question before the lesson and written the ideal answer down, then a simple glance at my notes will bring last night's clarity of thought into the madness of today's lesson. 
5. Use written ideal responses to stretch students' answers 
ò 
I ask my students, 'What is a quadratic equation?' 

ò 
I call upon one student who says, 'An equation with an x¡squared in it.' 

ò 
I glance down at the ideal response I have written in front of me. 'Does it need to be x?' 

ò 
The student refines their answer. 'An equation where the vari¡able is a square.' 

ò 
I glance again at my piece of paper. 'Better, but there is some¡thing missing.' 

ò 
I ask all the class to think on their own about what is missing, and then discuss it with their partner. 

ò 
I then come back to the same student who says, 'A quadratic equation is where the highest exponent of the variable is a square.' 


Having the ideal response in front of me makes it so much easier to stretch my students. My attention can be directed away from trying to remember the ideal response myself, and towards my students and their answers. 
6. Plan ideal responses with a colleague 
This is useful when trying to solve a particularly tricky written prob¡lem, such as an exam question. It always helps to have your answer confirmed, but also to see if there are better or alternate methods to approach the problem. 
But planning responses with a colleague really comes into its own when thinking about ideal responses to the more probing questions we plan to ask our students. Think about the example above: 'Is a square a type of trapezium?' If you write down your ideal response to that question and ask a colleague to do the same, you can guar¡antee that those two answers will not be the same. This allows you to have a positive, productive discussion about the critical features of an ideal response that leaves you both much better prepared to ask and respond to that question. 
Surely I don't have to do this for every question?? 
Okay, you can probably get away with the quick-fire, one-word responses. But for anything remotely complex that you want your students to write down or respond to orally, consider planning it in writing beforehand and bringing into the lesson. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Always write out written responses to the questions you plan to ask your students to free up your time and attention in lessons.

What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
I spent most of the first three years of my teaching career in a permanent state of shock. Not just at the workload, the school din¡ners, and the horrors ofbreak duty by the bike sheds, but at the re¡sponses of my students. 
I would give award-winning explanations to convey key mathe¡matical concepts, and then lo and behold, several of my students would proceed to mess up. All of a sudden you could add fractions by adding the numerators and the denominators, negative signs were appearing and disappearing at random, certain events had probabilities greater than 1, angles in a pentagon were adding up to 500 degrees, and every trigonometry question seemingly required the sin ratio. 
Each lesson I had to fight the urge to scream, 'Are you trying to wind me up?!?' 
Out of all the things a novice teacher is likely to struggle with, anticipating where students are likely to go wrong is one ofthe most common. And it is not just novice teachers. To this day I am regu¡larly surprised by the responses of my students. 
This surprise is problematic. Because in the midst of a lesson you need to identify the error, diagnose the error, and respond to the error. And you need to do this while Amber is saying 'sir, sir, sir', Tom has just poked Calum, and for some unknown reason, Josh is out ofhis seat, walking towards you with a suspicious look on his face. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you been surprised by astudent's answer and not responded optimally? 

ò 
What steps do you take to try to prevent this from happening? 


What's the tip? 
Being able to anticipate where your students may go wrong, devise a strategy to identify those errors, and then plan how you are going to respond to them before the lesson is a game-changer. 
Here are four ideas to make that work. 
1. Anticipate student errors bywriting out ideal student responses 
Writing down ideal student responses (Tip 45) allows us to better predict where our students might go wrong. If we just glance at a question, or even work out the answer in our heads, we are less likely to spot potential errors due to the curse ofknowledge -what seems straightforward to us will probably not be for our students. But physically writing down full responses slows us down and helps us pick up where students might go wrong. 
I like to annotate my ideal answers with these potential errors. Here are three ways: 
1. 
Put the solution at the centre of a page and have the errors spring off to the side like a spider diagram. 

2. 
Divide your page into two columns. Write out the working out and the answer on the left, and make notes about potential er¡rors on the right (see image below). 

3. 
Write ideal solutions on alternate lines and then try to fill in the blank lines with potential errors students might make at that stage of the solution. 


4-2
+
5 3 12 10
--+
15 15 
22
-
-
15 7
-
-
I 15 
J"ust add numerators
ò 
and denominators 

ò 
Don't c;icf_just numerc;itors 

ò 
Add denominators 



(t) 

Doing any of these exercises with a colleague will also be helpful. It is important you both list the errors independently and then com¡pare your lists. 
2. Anticipate student errors by planning diagnostic questions 
This is my favourite way to anticipate errors. It works best if you team up with a colleague, preferably one who is teaching the same topic as you. 
1. 
Take one piece ofblank paper for yourself and give another blank piece of paper to your colleague. 

2. 
Independently, you each write a question you plan to ask your students on one side of your respective piece of paper. The questions should be different, and should be suited to assessing via multiple choice -so if it involves a multistep process, ask a question focused on one step in the process. Using a Check-Point question you created when planning your lesson (Tip 42) works well for this. 

3. 
On the other side ofyour piece ofpaper, write the correct answer to your question and then what you consider to be the three most plausible wrong answers a student might give. Three is a good number to pick. It forces you to think beyond any obvious errors, but also means you have to prioritise. Your colleague does the same for their question on the back of their piece of paper. 

4. 
Turn your pieces ofpaper back over to the question side and swap with each other. 

5. 
Without turning the piece ofpaper over, you write the answer to your colleague's question on the question-side, and what you consider to be the three most plausible wrong answers to their question. Your colleague does the same for your question. 

6. 
When you have both finished, it is time for the big reveal. .. turn over your pieces of paper and compare your choices of answers to theirs for both questions. 

7. 
Can you see why they chose their wrong answers? If not, ask them. Can they see why you chose yours? Justify your choices. 

8. 
Finally, work together to agree on the most plausible three wrong answers for each question. 


I find this a really useful exercise. It is pretty quick to do and the structure tends to provoke positive, productive discussions between colleagues. It works particularly well if one teacher in the pair is a novice teacher and the other is more experienced. The novice teacher benefits by considering errors that their lack of experience would have hidden from them, and the more experienced teacher benefits from seeing a different perspective. 
And to top it all off, the pair now has two diagnostic questions that they can use to check the understanding oftheir students in lessons (Tip 26). Annotate a copy of the question with the reasons students may choose each of the wrong answers based on your discussion, along with how you are going to explain the correct answer. 
3. Identify student errors by using your written notes 
Just like with the ideal solutions, there is no point in trying to remember these potential errors. Bring them into lessons. Whether you have potential errors annotated on your ideal answers or they are in the form of the wrong answers to a diagnostic question, you want them with you. 
Carry them around the classroom while students work on the prob¡lems, or have them in front ofyou while you ask questions. This will free up your attention to be more present in the lesson and re¡sponsive to your students. 
An idea I really like is counting up the number of times you see an error. During independent work, you could do this by making tally marks on your annotated solutions as you walk around the class, or quickly totting up the frequency of a particular response if students answer using mini-whiteboards or ABCD cards. This allows you to get a sense ofwhether this is a whole-class issue that warrants a whole-class intervention (stopping the class, explaining, giving fur¡ther practice, etc.), or ifit is isolated to one or two students and thus can be fixed with a couple of conversations. This is a key compo¡nent of observing with a purpose -something we will discuss more in Tip 74. 
4. Respond to student errors 
It is all well and good to anticipate and identify errors, but what should we do about them? Primary teacher Emma Turner describes how she builds extra slides into her PowerPoint presentation to be used if she identifies the errors she has predicted. These slides might include: 
ò 
Links to some prior learning that is not secure 

ò 
Different representations 

ò 
Additional worked examples 

ò 
Extra practice 

ò 
Simplified practice. 


And then she clicks through what she does not need if her students seem to understand. 
There is no right or wrong way to respond to student errors. But here is the most important thing: you can plan your response in advance. 'So, if I see this particular error, and lots of students have done it, I will do this... ' 
The more thinking you can do outside ofthe classroom, the more prepared, present and responsive you are likely to be inside the classroom. 

ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Anticipate errors and plan how you are going to identify and respond to them as part of your lesson planning process.

Tip 47 -Two ideas to help teachers engage in Deep Work 

Tip43  Tip44

What's the problem? 
Back in my pre-children days (the Golden Age as I like to refer to it out oftheir earshot), I used to love getting into work around 7am. I would head straight to the maths office, say hello to my fellow early bird Erica, sit down at a computer, and prepare to enjoy a solid hour of unbroken, focused lesson planning. I would have mulled over a few ideas for examples, explanations and tasks during the car jour¡ney to work, and now my fingers were poised at the keyboard ready to create what I hoped would be a worthwhile and enjoyable learn¡ing experience for my students. 
But just before I started, I would have a quick check of my emails. 
And that was that. For the next 20 minutes, I would find myself responding to various requests for data, meetings, cover work, photocopying, attendance at staff football, etc. Meanwhile, both the time and Oust as important) the attention I had set aside for lesson planning were dripping away. 
'Just reply to the emails later,' I hear you say. 
I tried that. Even if I tried to focus on the task in hand, my attention would regularly drift back to those unanswered emails. 
My lesson planning inevitably suffered, either taking longer than I intended (and hence eating into another part of my day) or not being ofthe quality I had hoped because I could not dedicate my full attention to it. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do disruptions have anegative impact on your lesson planning? 

ò 
What steps do you take to avoid them? 


What's the tip? 
If this has been a problem for you too, here are two ideas to consider. 
1. Schedule in time for Deep Work 
In one of my favourite books that are not-about-education-but-are¡super-relevant-to-education, Cal Newport extols the virtues of what he calls Deep Work -professional activities performed in a state of distraction-free concentration that push your cognitive capabilities to their limit. These efforts create new value, improve your skill, and are hard to replicate. It is the state of mind in which we do our very best work. 
Newport explains that the cognitive state conducive to Deep Work can take up to 20 minutes to enter (and almost always longer than five minutes), and most people can sustain such a state for a maxi¡mum of 90 minutes before needing a break. 
Planning lessons is a good example of a task well suited to Deep Work. It involves: 
ò 
Mapping out a sequence of lessons, considering where you want your students to get to and the best narrative to help them get there. 

ò 
Deciding what students need to know to best understand a new idea, and how you are going to assess this. 

ò 
Deciding how to introduce, explain and model a new idea. 

ò 
Choosing the examples you will use, followed by the practice questions and the rich activities to help develop your students' understanding. 

ò 
Considering how you will check their understanding and re¡spond in the most effective way. 


All of this is complex, and when we give planning the attention it deserves, we do great work. 
There are other activities teachers undertake that are also suited to a state of Deep Work. Writing reports, marking assignments, plan¡ning meetings, deciding how to best support a colleague, writing a departmental policy, planning CPD -all of these require and benefit from our sustained attention. 
The key point here is that time needs to be scheduled in order to allow teachers to enter the state conducive to Deep Work. Much of a teacher's life is dictated by the bell. The Deep Work described above is certainly not possible during lesson time. Likewise, it is not pos¡sible in the two minutes we might have free between classes or the five minutes we grab at break. These longer sessions -20 minutes minimum, ideally longer given the time it takes to enter into such a state -need to be planned for and built into our schedule. 
It might be before school or staying behind after school. It might be at home in the evenings or weekends. It might be in a free period. It might be over a long lunch break. But if we want to engage in the type of cognitive work that produces the best results, we need to plan for when that is going to happen. 
2. Create the conditions to allow for Deep Work 
Blocking out time for Deep Work is not enough. Indeed, notice that is exactly what I had done in my example above. I had come into work early to engage in some lovely, focused planning. But then I switched tasks. And switching tasks is a Deep Work killer. 
When we switch from task A to task B, our attention does not im¡mediately follow-a residue of our attention remains thinking about the original task. And that is now attention we cannot dedicate to the more valuable task. 
Let's look at three things prevalent in schools that regularly force teachers to switch tasks, and consider how we might deal with them. 
1. Emails 
Emails are rife in schools, pinging through from morning until night. The thing about emails is that they are items on someone else's to-do list. One colleague may need data on a specific pupil and so sends you an email. Another colleague wonders ifyou can swap rooms next week, so sends you an email. And so on. In re¡sponding to each of these, you find yourself ticking tasks off other people's to-do lists at the expense ofyour own. 
That quick check of emails introduces a new target for our atten¡tion. Even if we do not reply in the moment, we'll be forced to turn back to the primary task with a secondary task left unfinished. The attention residue left by such unresolved switches dampens our performance. 
But replying to emails is so tempting, because replying to emails feels like you are getting something done. You see a visual indica¡tion ofyour progress and efforts as the number of unread items ticks down pleasingly towards zero. The same cannot be said of staring at a blank PowerPoint slide or piece of paper trying to think of the perfect example or explanation to use with your students to convey the complex new idea you want them to understand. After 10 minutes you may still have a blank page or a load of crossings out. We are hard-wired to want to see the immediate fruits of our labour, so checking and replying to emails can be addictive in a way that Deep Work is not. 
The solution is simple ... don't check emails regularly. Instead, schedule in set times to check them. The end of the day works well for me. It is when I am winding down cognitively, and replying to (or deleting) that final email and reaching the heralded 'inbox zero' is a positive way to end the day. It is a key component of my shut-down routine -another idea that Cal Newport talks about as a way to mark a clear end to the workday. But when attempting Deep Work, ensure your email platform is closed. 
The usual push-back-and I said this myself-is that you need to check emails regularly in case you miss something important. And indeed, missing something is a risk. But how many of the emails you receive are of such importance that replying later in the day would make a difference? How many of those emails actually require a reply at all? A colleague of mine, Simon, had a policy of never checking his emails -'If it is that important, someone will come and find me.' Simon was one of the happiest teachers I ever worked with. 
2. Other people 
Other people are a pain. Has a colleague or a student ever said, 'This will only take a few seconds, I promise'? It is not the time any 
of these acts take -firing off a quick reply or showing a colleague where the spare glue sticks are hidden may indeed only take a few seconds -it is the fact that they enforce a break in concentration, and switching back to the meaningful task is costly in terms of time and cognitive effort. 
We need to find somewhere quiet where we will not be disturbed. The staffroom or departmental office might not be the best place. An unused classroom is ideal. I used to find the canteen the period before lunch was good as it was open but there were no kids (and I could get my lunch early). Failing that, a decent pair of noise-can¡celling headphones block out sounds and provide a pretty clear sig¡nal to leave you alone. 
3. Mobile phones 
Mobile phones may be the worst offender of all when it comes to entering a state of sustained focus. Not only can we use them to access every potential distraction imaginable, but they also have two unfortunate characteristics when it comes to engaging in Deep Work. 
The first is that every single app on our phones has been designed with three goals in mind: to get you to open the app, to get you to keep it open, and to get you to open it again. Hence, when trying to avoid checking Twitter or playing a game, or trying to close an app down after a quick look, you are competing against the finest minds in Silicon Valley plus billions of dollars of research. 
The second issue with mobile phones is that even their mere pres¡ence might be enough to prevent you from entering a state of Deep Work. When I talked to psychologist Bradley Busch he described studies where merely having your mobile phone in sight, or even knowing that it is accessible in the same room, is enough to divert your attention away from the important task you are working on and towards that little rectangular prism of temptation. 
The solution here is to either turn offyour mobile, turn off notifica¡tions, or hide it in a trusted colleague's desk any time you want to embark on some Deep Work. Don't rely on yourself to fight the urge! 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Schedule in timefor Deep Work, and then create the conditions to allow yourselfto flourish.

What's the problem? 
Surely not another tip about email! 
I am afraid so. But, as I discussed in Tip 47, email plays such a key role in the day-to-day life of a teacher, I think it is worth spending one more tip thinking about how we can make them as effective as possible. 
Does any of this sound familiar to you: Email 1-Mr Lynch: 'Can we have a catch-up aboutJake Evans in your Year 8 class?' 
Email 2 -Mr Markland: 'Sure, when works for you?' 
Email 3 -Mr Lynch: 'I am free tomorrow, period 4?' 
Email 4-Mr Markland: 'I am teaching all day.' 
Email 5 -Mr Lynch: 'Okay, Friday period 3?' 
Email 6 -Mr Markland: 'I have a meeting. I can do period 4?' 
Email 7 -Mr Lynch: 'I am seeing a parent then. Period 5?' 
Email 8 -Mr Markland: 'I am free then but I have got to fit in a mentor meeting. How long do you need?' 
Email 9 -Mr Lynch: 'I reckon about 20 minutes.' 
Email 10 -Mr Markland: 'Okay, should work ifwe do the first half. Where do you want to meet?' 
Email 11-Mr Lynch: 'Can we meet in the maths office?' 
Email 12 -Mr Markland: 'There's intervention going on. Staffroom?' 
Email 13 -Mr Lynch: 'Fine. See you then.' 
Thirteen emails that may unfold over the course of a school day, so every time you log onto your computer there is another enthralling update. Or-and I think this is worse -this exchange constantly pings back and forth during your free period when you had in¡tended to get something meaningful done. As we discussed in Tip 47, this constant switching between tasks, no matter how quick it is to fire off the next reply, has a cognitive cost in terms of the atten¡tion residue it leaves behind. 
Oh, and here is another thing. What is the meeting about? Despite 13 emails, it still remains a mystery. When the meeting actually goes ahead, the chances are Mr Lynch may need to bring something along (Jake's book or recent assessment) or do some follow-up work that he could have started in advance. Without this, the meeting is unlikely to achieve all it needs to do, and thus may require another meeting! 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you experienced email chains like this? 

ò 
Are there any steps you take to reduce their length? 


What's the tip? 
In short, we need to improve our email efficiency by aiming to close 
the loop. Once again, this is an idea I got from Cal Newport's Deep Work. 
If Mr Lynch had read Deep Work, his initial email might have gone something like this: 
Hi Nick, I'd like us to meet for 20 minutes to discuss Jake Evans' progress in maths. 
I can do the following times: 
ò 
Wednesday, period 4 

ò 
Wednesday, period 5 

ò 
Friday, period 3 

ò 
Friday, period 5. 


If one of these times is suitable for you, please reply letting me know which one. If not, please suggest three other times that work for you across the next three days. 
I propose we meet in the maths office. Ifthat is not suit¡able, let me know and we can meet in the staffroom. 
Specifically, I am concerned Jake is not performing as well as he was earlier in the year, and I want to see ifwe can fig¡ure out the cause of the problem and what we can do about it. In advance of the meeting, please could you think of any factors that may have caused this drop-off, and any practi¡cal solutions we could employ? 
Please could you bring along Jake's maths book and his recent end-of-term test so we can see if there are any clues in there? 
Thanks so much. 
Notice the email contains a few key features: 
ò 
The purpose is clearly specified. 

ò 
The duration is clearly specified. 

ò 
A selection of times and dates are offered, and with a definite call to action if they are not suitable. 

ò 
The same goes for the location. 

ò 
Any preparation required in advance is specified, along with anything the recipient should bring along. 


The email above should require a single reply, and help the meeting be as efficient and effective as possible. 
And if Mr Lynch has not read Deep Work, then Mr Markland's reply to the initial 'Can we have a catch-up aboutJake Evans in your Year 8 class?' could go like this: 
Hi Colm, 
I am not sure how long you need. 
I can free up 30 minutes at the following times: 
ò Thursday, period 3 
ò Friday, period 5. I can free up 60 minutes at the following times: 
ò 
Thursday, period 4 

ò 
After school Thursday. 


If one of these times is suitable for you, please reply letting 
me know which one. If not, please suggest three other 
times that work for you across the next three days, along 
with how long you need. 
The maths office is usually busy, so I propose we meet in 
the staffroom. If that is not suitable, please suggest another 
venue. 
In advance of the meeting, please can you let me know: 
ò 
A list of the key things you want to discuss 

ò 
Anything I need to prepare in advance 

ò 
Anything you need me to bring to the meeting. 


Thanks. 
Sure, these initial emails or initial responses take longer to compose than firing off a quick answer or a quick question. But they un¡doubtedly save time in the long run. And remember, it is not just time they save. By limiting the number of email back-and-forths, we are also limiting the number of times our attention needs to switch away from a cognitively demanding task, thus decreasing the atten¡tion residue and increasing our ability to succeed in that task. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
When sending or replying to an email, aim to close the loop in as few exchanges as possible.

CHAPTER 7 
PRIOR KNOWLEDGE 
As Dylan Wiliam says, we need to start from where our students are at, not where we want them to be. In this chapter, I describe my process for identifying, prioritising and assessing relevant prior knowledge for any new idea I am teaching, and finally how to re¡spond to that assessment. There is an opportunity for you to play along, planning out prior knowledge for an upcoming lesson. 
Chapter overview

Tip 49 -Plan relevant prior knowledge 
Tip 53
Tip49 
Tip 50 
Tip 51 
Tip 52 
. .
~ ~ 
Per idea notPlan 
Prioritise 
Assess

What's the problem? 
Let's kick things off with yet another example from my Teaching Hall of Shame. 
I was teaching my Year 9 students how to calculate the area of a circle. In the previous lesson, we had learned all about the wonders ofpi (yes, string was involved) and how it could be used to calculate the circumference, and now it was time to see what else that mag¡ical number could do. We looked at a fancy animation ofwhere the formula for the area of a circle comes from, and then dived straight into the worked example. 
It was then that the problems occurred. Some students had clearly forgotten the difference between diameter and radius, even though we did it yesterday. Others could not substitute numbers into the formula. To square a number, all of a sudden you could just multi¡ply it by two. There was a widespread issue with entering pi into the calculator. Half ofthem had seemingly lost the ability to round. And don't get me started on how many of my students either ignored the units at the end of their answer or wrote down centimetres (for an area 
.
question..11). 
I could not believe what was happening. How could my students not know this? Take squaring, for example. Students have been squar¡ing numbers since Year 5. Heck, I even taught them squaring myself in Year 7. I just about managed to hold off screaming, 'How can you not know this?? You are in Year 9!!' 
But as Dylan Wiliam points out, how does a student qualify to be in Year 9? They are in Year 8 and have a birthday. And as we will see when we come to discuss memory in Chapter 10, students forget things really, really quickly. 
What ensued was nothing short of a disaster. Every time I solved one issue, another one would emerge. And all the while my stu¡dents' attention was being diverted away from the new idea I wanted to teach them -how to calculate the area of a circle. 
The mistake I had made, ofcourse, was that I had not dealt with the relevant prior knowledge required to have the best chance of under¡standing this new idea beforehand. Had I done so, these problems would still have become apparent, but I would have been able to tackle them in isolation from the new idea. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to this? 

ò 
How do you deal with relevant prior knowledge? 


What's the tip? 
Nothing students learn is completely new-it all builds on or combines ideas they have met before. But our students' attention is limited. So, when explaining a new idea, I want as much ofmy students' attention as possible to be focused on how knowledge that is familiar to them fits to¡gether in this novel way. Ifinstead, as was the case described above, their attention also needs to be diverted to thinking hard about that sup¡posedly familiar knowledge (parts of a circle, substitution, squaring, 
calculator use, rounding, etc.), then the chance of them successfully processing this new idea is significantly diminished. 
So, how do we stop this from happening? In this chapter, I will describe my process oflisting, prioritising, assessing and respond¡ing to this relevant prior knowledge. I suggest playing along with a lesson you are teaching so you can better see which aspects ofthis approach you could use to enhance your planning. 
1. Choose a question you want everyone to answer by the end oftoday's lesson 
This shouldn't be an extension or challenge question, but the kind of question you would hope everyone in the class would be able to answer by the end of the lesson. This is the Check-Point question described in Tip 41. 
For the area of a circle lesson, my Check-Point question is: 
'Calculate the area of a circle with a diameter of 9cm. Give 
your answer to 1 decimal place.' 
2. Write a list ofrelevant prior knowledge 
This stage is easier said than done, especially for less experienced teachers. We all suffer from the dreaded curse of knowledge. Therefore, what is obvious to us may not be obvious to our students (surely you know what the diameter is??). But the Check-Point question can help. 
Answer the Check-Point question in full, on paper. While answering it, ask yourself, 'What things have my students encountered before that will help them answer this question?' 
The same three techniques we used in Tip 46 can help here: 
1. 
Put the solution at the centre of a page and have the relevant prior knowledge spring off to the side like a spider diagram. 

2. 
Divide your page into two columns. Write out the working out and the answer on the left, and make notes about relevant prior knowledge on the right. 

3. 
Write the full solution on alternate lines and then try to fill in the blank lines with relevant prior knowledge needed at that stage of the solution. 


Once you have your list of relevant prior knowledge, challenge a colleague to come up with their own list for the same Check¡Point question. You can then compare lists and adapt yours where needed. This is a really useful exercise to do in a departmental meeting for an upcoming topic. 
My list of relevant prior knowledge for the 'area of a circle' lesson might look something like this: 
ò 
Identify parts of a circle 

ò 
Change from diameter to radius 

ò 
Square (and square root) a number mentally 

ò 
Use the pi button on a calculator 

ò 
Use the power and root buttons on a calculator 

ò 
Substitute into formula 

ò 
Convert to a decimal on a calculator 

ò 
Round to decimal places. 


Take some time to create a list of relevant prior knowledge for an upcoming topic. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Choose aquestion you want every student to be able to answer by the end of your lesson, and use that to determine relevant prior knowledge.

How does this tip fit into your current practice? Ido exactly that I do something like that Ido nothing like that 

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3.

Tip 50 -Prioritise relevant prior knowledge 
Tip 53

What's the problem? 
So, we have our list of relevant prior knowledge. What next? 
In an ideal world, we would assess all relevant prior knowledge -it is, by definition, useful to understand the concept we are teaching. But we each operate under time constraints, so we need a system. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Ifyou do assess relevant prior knowledge, how do you determine which relevant prior knowledge to assess? 

ò 
And in what order do you assess it? 


What's the tip? 
Prioritise, don't assume 
When faced with a long list of relevant prior knowledge, the temp¡tation is to say 'they can already do this, they can already do that' and so on, and only assess the knowledge you suspect they are not secure on. It is especially tempting to make such assumptions about things students did last lesson (and so surely must remember it) or concepts that you know they met at a very young age (and so surely most know it). 
But that is a big risk. If our assumptions turn out to be wrong, it will take a lot of time to untangle the mess further down the line, and our students' confidence may suffer. 
So, ifyou have determined something to be relevant prior knowl¡edge for an upcoming idea, then you need to assess students' understanding ofit. Because -and this is the key point-if that understanding is secure, then the process is quick: ask the ques¡tion, collect the responses, and move on. This also builds students' confidence as they feel successful. But if that understanding is not secure, then console yourself with the fact that it is a good job you discovered that now. 
So, prioritise relevant prior knowledge, and do not make assump¡tions. 
How to prioritise relevant prior knowledge 
I really like the approach of @MBDscience1 who explains: 
'Prior knowledge falls somewhere on a spectrum. Some 
of this prior knowledge is definitely prerequisite -if they 
don't know this, they will really struggle to make any sort 
of meaning from what you're teaching them. Ifthey don't 
know this stuff, you need to stop and you need to teach 
it to them before you continue. Some of it is probably not 
prerequisite (but is still useful stuff to know). This stuffyou 
don't need to teach them before you start.' 
She suggests using a diagram such as this: 
She suggests using a diagram such as this: 
Probably not Definitely prerequisite prerequisite 
And then deciding where each item of relevant prior knowledge from your list would fit. 
For example, for my 'area of a circle' list: 
ò 
'Identify parts of a circle' and 'change from diameter to radius' have to be assessed. If students do not know those, we might as well all pack up and go home now. We did it last lesson, but a lot can happen in 24 hours. They go on the right of the scale. 

ò 
But if students cannot 'convert to a decimal on a calculator' or 'round to decimal places', while this is by no means ideal, we can survive without it this lesson. We can adapt our Check¡Point question by leaving our answers in exact form or copying out the first five decimal places. They go to the left of the scale. 


To make the prioritising run as smoothly as possible, I like to assign numbers or letters to each of the items in my relevant prior knowl¡edge list: 
1. 
Identify parts of a circle 

2. 
Change from diameter to radius 

3. 
Square (and square root) a number mentally 

4. 
Use the pi button on a calculator 

5. 
Use the power and root buttons on a calculator 

6. 
Substitute into formula 

7. 
Convert to a decimal on a calculator 


8. Round to decimal places. I then position the letters/numbers on the line: 
Probably not Definitely prerequisite prerequisite 
Finally, just like with the listing part ofthe process, comparing your order of priorities with a colleague working from a similar list is a fascinating activity and can lead to a positive, productive discussion. 
Take some time now to do this for your list of relevant prior knowledge. 
Help!! 
Having had the pleasure to do this exercise with hundreds of teach¡ers, I can assure you that this process is tough. The majority ofwhat you have on your list may well be considered prerequisite. 
It is worth thinking about whether you can adapt the requirements ofyour Check-Point question if needed in the lesson, so students don't need to use all the prior knowledge you have listed. In my circles example above, I placed the relevant prior knowledge related to calculator use at the left of the scale, because I can still teach the core concept of the area of a circle without this. Thinking this way might indicate that some prior knowledge can be placed to the left ofyour scale. 
Finally, even ifyou decide all the prior knowledge you have listed is prerequisite, a priority order is still important so we can focus on the 'most prerequisite' at the point ofthe lesson where we have the most time, and our students have the most attention. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Devise asystem to prioritise the items on your list of relevant prior knowledge.

Tip 51-Assess relevant prior knowledge 

What's the problem? 
We now have a prioritised list of relevant prior knowledge for the lesson we are about to teach. Nice. 
But it is one thing having the list. Now we have to do something with it. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What types of questions do you use to assess relevant prior knowledge? 

ò
What means of participation do you use to enable students to respond? 


What's the tip? 
Once we have our list and our priorities, it is time to use it. 
Types of questions to assess relevant prior knowledge 
Due to time constraints, a single question on each item of relevant prior knowledge will have to do. These questions should test under¡standing of the prior knowledge up to the level students need. We are not aiming to stretch students' understanding ofprior knowl¡edge at this point-just check it is at a level sufficient to build a new idea upon. The questions also need to be easy to get data back from, especially ifwe are assessing relevant prior knowledge in class (as opposed to for homework). For these reasons I use monosemic questions, and they tend to be factual recall or procedural. For more on different question types to use when checking for understand¡ing, see Tip 24. 
Assess the most prerequisite first 
The key is to begin by assessing the item of relevant prior knowl¡edge you determined was the furthest to the right on the diagram in Tip 50 -in other words, the most prerequisite. If prerequisite knowledge is lacking, then we need to respond to it and this could take time. So, we need to start with the thing that is most important at the point in the lesson when we have the most time to deal with it, and our students have the largest reserves of attention left. If this means we do not get the chance to assess some of the relevant knowledge to the left, this is not ideal, but if anything is getting missed, it should definitely be this. 
Possible means ofparticipation during the relevant prior knowledge assessment 
Here are some means ofparticipation I often see for assessing rele¡vant prior knowledge: 
1. 
Students answer in books. 

2. 
The teacher calls for volunteers (hands-up). 

3. 
The teacher chooses students to answer questions verbally (Cold Call). 

4. 
Students respond on mini-whiteboards. 

5. 
Students respond using voting systems with diagnostic ques¡tions. 

6. 
Use a prior knowledge quiz at the end of the previous lesson. 

7. 
Use a prior knowledge homework before the lesson. 


Before I share my thoughts, please can you take a moment to consider the medium via which you usually assess relevant prior knowledge. 
Okay, so when we are assessing relevant prior knowledge, we need two things. 
1. Information quickly so we can respond in the moment 
We cannot ask a question and then have to wait for a prolonged period of lesson time to find out students' answers. We need to get cracking. For this reason, I don't do my prior knowledge check in books. 
2. Mass participation 
Mass participation is always important, but it is especially needed with prior knowledge. If we make dodgy assumptions based on the responses ofone or two students, and it turns out that for many students in the class prior knowledge is not secure, then we are building on shaky foundations and trouble lies ahead. For that rea¡son, I don't use hands-up or Cold Call for checking prior knowledge. 
Some options for the assessment ofrelevant prior knowledge 
My in-class prior knowledge checks either consist of open-response questions that I project up on the board one at a time and students respond on mini-whiteboards, or I use diagnostic multiple-choice questions with ABCD cards. I get information quickly, everyone responds, and we can deal with each question in turn. Using diag¡nostic questions has the advantage that I can identify the specific nature of student misconceptions, and it is a change in format from books or mini-whiteboard work. We discussed using multiple¡choice diagnostic questions as a way to check for understanding in Tip 26. 
I see a lot of teachers assessing relevant prior knowledge as part of the start of the lesson (the Do Now), with a series of questions on the board for students to complete at their own pace. I don't because I want the prior knowledge check to be dynamic -I ask a question, all students answer, and I respond based on their an¡swers. That flow is compromised if students have worked indepen¡dently on a series of questions, and then I try to turn their attention back to question 1. So, my prior knowledge check comes after my Do Now. We discuss Do Nows in depth in Tip 88. 
I like the idea of a prior knowledge quiz at the end of the previous lesson, as the start of the next lesson can then be directed to sup¡porting students with specific areas they are struggling with. But I rarely find I have the time to do that. 
Likewise, setting a prior knowledge homework before the lesson can work really well. Ideally, this will be marked before the lesson (automatic marking or something like Carousel Learning is great for this), or students could quickly mark it at the start of the lesson, thus saving valuable lesson time. But this requires a culture where all students complete these pre-quizzes and homework, or you are back to guessing. We will discuss ways to help students take home¡work more seriously and thus boost the participation ratio in Tip 90. 
Circumstances and constraints will affect what is possible with the prior knowledge check, but hopefully this has given you some ideas to reflect upon. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
When you have your list of relevant prior knowledge, decide how you will assess students' understanding starting with the knowledge you deem to be most important.

Tip 52 -Respond to prior knowledge assessment

What's the problem? 
Let's imagine you have assessed some of the relevant prior knowl¡edge on your list and you find out that the majority ofyour students are not secure on it. What do you do? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you respond ifprior knowledge is not secure? 

ò 
What do you do with those students who do seem to understand? 


What's the tip? 
If our tools of mass participation reveal a lack ofknowledge or misunderstanding, we need to deal with it. We cannot build upon shaky foundations. 
Responding could involve: 
ò 
A brief explanation followed by another question 

ò 
A paired discussion about what the correct answer is, followed by another question 

ò 
Reteaching, followed by several further examples. 


My choice will be determined by the scale of the misunderstanding and the importance ofthe concept for the new idea. 
Notice that each of these options ends with a second check for un¡derstanding. We need reliable evidence from our students. 
What about the students who know it? 
Teachers are often concerned about how to deal with students who get the prior knowledge check question correct. Do they need more challenge? Should I set them another task? I don't think so. This is all part of the obsession with the need to differentiate that has gripped the teaching profession over the last decade. I say two things to students who get the prior knowledge check question correct: 
1. 
'Great, you got that question correct, but now can you under¡stand my explanation and get my follow-up question correct as well?' 

2. 
'The hardest job in this classroom is to help someone else. It is brilliant that you understand, but now the real challenge begins: can you explain it to someone else in a way that helps them understand?'

Tip 53 -Assess relevant prior knowledge for each idea, not for the whole sequence  
Tip 49 Plan  ~ ..  Tip SO Prioritise  .  Tip 51 Assess  ~ ~  Tip 52 Respond  .  Tip 53 Per idea not sequence  

What's the problem? 
If a scheme ofwork contains a list ofrelevant prior knowledge, it is usually for the sequence oflessons as a whole, as opposed to knowledge relevant to each key idea within the sequence. Thus, the logical thing is to assess relevant prior knowledge for the whole sequence oflessons at the start ofthat first lesson, and then go from there. 
While this seems more efficient, it is problematic. Take my 'area of a circle' example from the start of this chapter. We had done pi and circumference the lesson before. And yet still students were strug¡gling to distinguish between the radius and diameter and to enter pi on their calculators. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you assess prior knowledge for asequence of lessons? 

ò 
Do you face any challenges with this? 


What's the tip? 
Having mapped out my sequence oflessons (Tip 42), I list and prioritise relevant prior knowledge for each key idea within the se¡quence using the approach described in the chapter. 
If the second idea in a sequence contains prior knowledge that was also relevant to the first big idea, I still assess it again. Students for¡get things quickly, and if that knowledge is secure then the assess¡ment is quick and students get a sense of success. 
Similarly, if something is not relevant prior knowledge for the current idea but is for a question I plan to ask several lessons in the future, and I assess it now, two things could happen: 
1. 
We get bogged down dealing with any gaps in that prior knowl¡edge even though it is not relevant to that lesson. 

2. 
Even ifwe do manage to sort out the gap in the moment, stu¡dents may well have forgotten it again by the time the lesson comes around. 


One extra bonus ofthis approach to relevant prior knowledge 
Dealing with relevant prior knowledge in the way described in this chapter has one extra advantage -it allows our explanations and modelling of new ideas to flow. Whether we are telling a story, using an analogy, drawing on multiple representations, or using Silent Teacher (all things we will discuss next chapter), we can proceed without constantly stopping and saying, 'David, what does this mean?' or 'Bilal, can you remember how to do this?' We have 
already asked these questions, got the responses, and dealt with them accordingly. We can now focus our attention on explaining the new idea in a clear and inspiring way, and our students can focus their attention on following it. You may still choose to ask questions to keep students engaged and active in the process, but you are not compelled to ask those questions. Dealing with rele¡vant prior knowledge prevents explanations from becoming unduly fragmented. 
Do you really think I have time to do that?? The push-back I often hear from teachers when I talk about listing, prioritising, assessing and responding to relevant prior knowledge is that they simply do not have time -neither time to plan, nor time in lessons. Let's look at each. 
Time to plan 
We can save a lot of time by sharing the job of listing, prioritising and finding good assessment questions among members of our department. Maybe start with a pilot -do it for one unit with one year group. Split the team up into pairs, with each pair working on the prior knowledge for a single sequence of lessons. And then reflect on what works well and what needs to change. There is also rarely a need to write the assessment questions yourself-banks of these are available, whether it be on diagnosticquestions.com, other sites, or packages you subscribe to. Finally, keep in mind that once you have done this once for a sequence oflessons, it shouldn't need more than a quick refinement in subsequent years. 
Time in lessons 
Teachers tell me they have one lesson allocated on their scheme ofwork to teach the new idea, and if they spend 15 minutes going over stuff students should already know, then they will never have a chance to teach the new content. Of course, the wider issue concerns jam-packed schemes ofwork that leave no room for con¡solidation and depth. But if changing that is out ofyour hands, then console yourself with the fact that if relevant prior knowledge is not present, you have little option but to pause and intervene. Plough¡ing on regardless will only cost more time in the future. 
I like Clare Sealy's leaky pipe analogy (paraphrased from our conver¡sation): 
Imagine you want to build a water pipe from Point A to Point B. As you build the pipe, you notice some leaks ap¡pearing. Option 1 is you plough on regardless as you have to get to Point Bin the allocated time. The issue is, when you get there your pipe can hardly carry any water because it is so full of holes. Option 2 is you pause and mend each hole as you discover it. Sure, you may not get to Point B, but you are a lot further along the way than when you 
started, and you can carry a lot more water than in the first 
. 
scenano. 
Option 1 is the race through the scheme of work that teachers often feel bound to. It simply doesn't work. Sure, you may have covered all the content, but students are likely to understand and retain lit¡tle. And here is the interesting thing: when it comes to end-of-unit tests, these students often perform worse than students who have not covered all the content, but understand and remember the parts ofit they have covered. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Assess the relevant prior knowledge for each idea in your sequence, not for the sequence as awhole.

CHAPTERS EXPLANATIONS, MODELLING AND WORKED EXAMPLES 
This chapter is all about how we introduce, explain and model new ideas to our students. We discuss how to show students that a new idea matters, and how to deal with technical language and key deci¡sions. We talk about the importance and practicalities of live mod¡elling, before taking a deep dive into my worked-example process, including Example-Problem Pairs, Silent Teacher and making use of self-explanation prompts. We also discuss what happens after that first worked example in terms of the We Do and Your Turn, and exactly what our students should be copying down. It's a big one, so 
make sure you are sitting comfortably.

approach 



What's the problem? 
I don't know how true this is in other subjects, but often students perceive maths as a series of disconnected topics. So, last week it was sequences, today we are learning the area of a trapezium, and in a couple ofweeks we will be plotting some straight line graphs. Great. Our students can be forgiven for wondering what on earth the point is. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Is this an issue you have faced? 

ò 
What steps have you taken to address it? 


What's the tip? 
Before diving into a new idea, it is important to show students why this new idea matters. Here are five ideas to help do that. 
1. Connect prior, present and future knowledge 
Nothing we teach our students is truly new. Everything builds on something they have encountered in the past, and everything is rel¡evant to something they will encounter in the future. Following our treatment of relevant prior knowledge (Chapter 7), we can say: 
'Why did I ask you a load of random questions about sub¡
stitution, squaring and rounding? Well, let me show you ... ' 
Parts of a circle -¡
-
me of a Volume of
J. Volu I
Substitution 
L sp here _J Lrevolution 
Square& 
Volume of a 
Area of a circle . 
-
square root 
cylinder 
Use a ,.. 
calculator 

Round to decimal places -¡
'These ideas will help you with what we are learning today 
-calculating the area of a circle. And once we nail this 
topic, it will set us up for meeting cylinders in Year 9, cones 
and spheres in Year 10, and even the beauty of the volume 
of revolution in Year 13.' 
I built this diagram in PowerPoint. Relevant prior knowledge on the left, new idea in the middle, and what that new idea leads to on the right. In the 30 or so seconds that this diagram is visible, it shows the purpose ofthe prior knowledge check and gives students a glimpse ofwhat is to come in the future. Above all, it shows stu¡dents that maths is not a series of random, disconnected topics. 
Once you've built this for one concept, you can edit it really quickly for another concept. And ifyou are a scheme ofwork creator, this could be a great addition. 
2. Connect the Big Ideas 
When I interviewed curriculum expert Mary Myatt she discussed the importance of Big Ideas. Big Ideas are the key principles that un¡derpin our subject. Students encounter these Big Ideas at various stages of their school life, and they help students appreciate the connected nature of their subject. 
Every subject has them. History has the idea of democracy, science has the idea of evolution. In maths, we have the list proposed by Randall Charles in the Journal ofMathematics Education Leadership, linked to in the resources at the end of the tip. 
An example of a Big Idea in mathematics that relates to the area of a circle is estimation -what happens if we replace the value of pi with 3, or 3.14? Students will have encountered estimation before, when learning to round, multiplying decimals, or deciding whether the answer to a calculation is likely to be greater or less than a given amount. 
The key is making the Big Ideas explicit. So, after we have discussed estimating in the context of the new idea of circles, we can pause, write 'Estimating' on the board, and challenge students to write down three topics they have studied in the past that used the Big Idea of estimation. 
The holy trinity (Tip 17) ofmini-whiteboards, paired discussion and Cold Call works well here, supplemented by examples you have prepared. 
This only needs to take a couple of minutes, but is another way to help students see the connections that exist in our subject. 
3. Share hinterland knowledge All subjects have core knowledge -the critical facts, methods and tech¡niques that students must understand and retain if they are to be successful with our subject. But to reduce teaching to focusing only 
on the core knowledge would be to give our students a shallow and rather dull experience of our subject. 
And so we have Christine Counsell's idea of hinterland knowledge. This is the rich array of content, stories and examples that give meaning to the core. Hinterland knowledge provides the intrigue and sig¡nificance that provides a foundation for all that follows. Hinterland knowledge makes core knowledge matter. 
Maths teachers have a rich history to draw upon. Every technique our students need to learn was born from a need in a time gone by, and that cast of characters involved in their creation are worth sharing. Daniel Willingham describes stories as being psychologically privileged as humans have evolved to enjoy and retain the narrative structure. 
Let's imagine I am preparing to teach students about the mean. Having assessed relevant prior knowledge involving number opera¡tions and calculator use, and shown where averages fit into the big picture of mathematics, I begin:1 
'You may have heard of someone called Pythagoras. Now, 
in Year 9 we will learn that Pythagoras and his friends got 
more than a little obsessed with right-angled triangles. But 
they also did a lot of other things, one ofwhich was invent¡
ing what we will be looking at today -the mean. 
'But here is the twist. What Pythagoras called the mean, is 
not what we called the mean. 
'For Pythagoras, the mean was the middle number of a three-term series, but only when that middle term was in some 'equal relation' to the other terms. That equal rela¡tion might be that it was equidistant -like 4 in 2, 4, 6 -but it also might mean they were halfway between each other in another type of sequence -like 10 is the middle of 1, 10, 
100. All Pythagoras was bothered about was what number was in the middle. A bit weird, hey? 
'Fast-forward to the 11th century, and we meet the Iranian mathematician, Al-Biruni. He wanted a way to get a sense of a list of numbers so he could compare it to another list of numbers. So, he invented the midrange: the number in the exact middle of the two most extreme results. So, if you had 1, 3, 4, and 19, the midrange would be... 10. Notice how the midrange takes no account at all of the numbers in between. 1, 17, 18 and 19 would also have a midrange of 10. Can you think of another set of numbers that would have a midrange of 10? ... 
'By the 1500s, there was a need to take account of all the numbers, first because of magnets, and then because of ships. 
'Magnets first. So, it turns out that there is a difference between the angle of magnetic north and the angle of true north. This difference is called magnetic declination (impress your mates with that one). And to work out the magnetic declination, scientists would take, say, eight mea¡surements, and then come up with a single value represen¡tative of those eight. This value is the mean. 
'And ships? Well, if a ship was damaged and the crew had to throw some goods overboard to save weight, the voy¡age's investors would take a financial loss equal to their investment. Again, they needed a way ofworking out what everyone would have to pay, and so we have the concept of an average, later defined as the mean. 
'These days, the mean is everywhere. Sports, finance, world health, schools, you name it, it is there. Can you think of anywhere else that uses the mean? ... 
'And today we are going to learn how to calculate it and use it... ' 
Sharing hinterland knowledge is brief. A few minutes at most. It could be accompanied by images and videos, or told with no other stimulus at all. The stories could come from history -Google and Wikipedia are a teacher's friends here -or they could be personal anecdotes. The best forms of hinterland knowledge show meaning in the wider world, the development of ideas over time, and the connection between other ideas. 
One of the most fun parts of the hinterland is that its delivery does not need to be as formally presented as other things we are likely to do over the course of a lesson. It doesn't matter if we walk around the room. There is no requirement for a formal check for understanding or to whip out a mini-whiteboard. The precision of language or even conciseness does not matter as much. Things like tension, emotion and humour matter more. But-and it is a big BUT -just because we are not doing any ofthose things does not mean that sharing hinterland knowledge is not important. Hinterland is all about the story and the effect it has on sparking our students' in¡trigue for all that follows, and that is very important indeed. 
4. Introduce a headache 
The exploration of hinterland knowledge may have uncovered why a particular idea was needed in the world -what problem did it solve? For some ideas, we can make this need more explicit by hav¡ing our students experience a headache. 
The headache-aspirin approach was pioneered by maths educator, Dan Meyer. The idea is that we try to show our students that, without this new idea, their life at this very moment would be a bit painful. In other words, before we provide the aspirin (the new idea), we need students to experience a headache. 
Here are a few examples of headaches I have tried to create in maths: 
ò 
Asking students to calculate 693 + 693 + 693 + 693 + 693 + 693 + 693 + 693 before teaching long multiplication. 

ò 
Asking students to work out 23651 + 23648 on their calculators before teaching laws of indices. 

ò 
Asking students to copy down the mass ofthe Earth as 5970000000000000000000000 kilograms before teaching stan¡dard form. 

ò 
Asking students to calculate x 35 x lL x 1. before teaching


42
55 6 14 7 
prime factorisation. 
ò Asking students to find two values ofx that make the expression x2 + Jx -28 equal to zero before teaching factorising quadratic 
.
expressions. 
ò 
Asking students to fill in the gap in 65,8_30,527 to make a multi¡ple of 3 before teaching divisibility laws. 

ò 
Winning at 'rigged' games before teaching probability. 

ò 
Showing students multistep contextual questions with numbers before teaching the no-numbers approach (Tip 73). 


In each case it is the struggle that provides the purpose for the new idea we are about to teach. 
The headache-aspirin approach might not be appropriate for every topic, but if in your exploration of hinterland knowledge you un¡cover why an idea was invented and popularised, it is worth consid¡ering whether you can create a situation where students experience that need themselves. 
5. Show why the idea matters to you It doesn't matter how well we link together previous topics and Big Ideas, how thorough our planning ofhinterland knowledge is, and how well we engineer a headache; if our hearts are not in it, our 
students will sense it, and it is likely to have a detrimental effect on how they perceive the upcoming new idea. 
We have to be positive and enthusiastic about all aspects of our subject. We may be the only role model our students have in our subject. As a maths teacher, I have lost count ofhow many parents have told me at parents' evenings -in front of their child -'I always found maths boring' or 'I always preferred English'. If our actions reinforce this view, then it will become self-fulfilling. But we have an opportunity to be a lone voice in the child's life that injects en¡thusiasm into the learning of our subject. 
For many topics, that is easy. I absolutely love a quadratic equation and a fraction. I could happily teach them all day long with a big smile on my face. But 3D shapes and density? No thank you. For those areas of our subject that don't enthuse us, here are some ideas to help: 
1. 
Talk to a colleague who likes teaching it. What excites them about it? How do they convey that excitement to their stu¡dents? 

2. 
Talk about what learning this idea will lead to -'Today we are doing more practice of fractions so we can get so good at them, it will unlock a whole world of other interesting maths.' 

3. 
Tell a story of success about how you either conquered the sub¡ject yourself as a child or how you taught it to students in the past and initially they didn't like it, but in the end, they grew to love it. 

4. 
Find a hook. This could be an image, video, newspaper article, story-anything that brings what you are about to teach to life. 

5. 
Plan an introduction. Think carefully about what you are going to say to your students, and either script the introduction or write down a set ofbullet points that you keep with you in the lesson to make sure you don't forget. 


And if this is not possible, then we need to ensure our actions do not have a negative impact on our students' view ofthe topic. When I interviewed primary English specialist Chris Such, he made this point clearly: 
'I'm not saying we are dancing around the room and saying this is incredible. It is, through your body language, not doing the opposite. Not giving the impression that you don't like this stuff or it is boring. Because I have seen that happen in classes where there are students who genuinely do love this stuff. I remember I was a teaching assistant in a lesson about sedimentary, metamorphic and igneous rock and the distinctions between them, and the teacher was like, "Uh, it's rocks, isn't it?", and there were at least a couple ofkids in the room who were like, "Yeah, IT'S ROCKS!! I LOVE THIS STUFF." At the very least we should be careful not to curb our students' enthusiasm.' 
Do you really think I have time to do that?? Of course, you don't need to include each of these five ideas every time you teach a new idea. But taking the time to think what the best way is to show students that this new idea matters should help get the enthusiasm, engagement and attention needed to help stu¡dents understand the content. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider how best to show students that the new idea you are about to teach them matters.

Tip 55 -Use related examples and non¡examples to explain technical language
