What's the problem? 
Imagine someone came up to you on the street and said, 'What is a triangle?' 
Apart from crossing to the other side of the road in a hurry, how would you answer? 
Now, imagine you had to teach your students what a triangle is. How would you go about it? 
A sensible place to start is to use the definition of triangle, which is 'a plane figure with three straight sides and three angles.' 
The problem with this definition -and I believe this is true of many of the definitions we may be tempted to give our students -is that it requires a fair bit ofknowledge to understand. What is a plane figure? What is straight? What is a side? When we find ourselves defining terms in a definition, we are in trouble. 
Okay, option two is we show our students an example of a triangle. Now, ifyou ask most people -adults or children -to draw an exam¡ple of a triangle, they produce something like this: 

This is a prototypical example. It has a horizontal 'base' and is isosceles or equilateral in nature. The problem with single exam¡ples, especially if they are prototypical, is that students latch on to irrelevant features and assume they are necessary. So, students ex¡periencing this example of a triangle may assume that all triangles have horizontal bases or equal sides. We will see a similar issue with the example of the trapezium in Tip 57. 
Better is to give them a wider selection of examples: Our students' understanding of a concept has now been expanded. They realise that triangles do not need a horizontal base or to be isosceles. But this is still not sufficient, because to truly understand what something is, we must also understand what it is not. 

So, a selection of examples and non-examples are likely to be useful: 

This is a triangle; this is not a triangle. Non-examples are crucial for understanding. They help students edge closer to the boundary of what is and what is not a triangle. 
But I think we can go one better than this. And not just for triangles, but for any technical language we want our students to understand. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you explain technical language to your students? 

ò 
What has been your experience using examples and non-examples? 


What's the tip? 
Maths -like most subjects -is full of technical language. If students do not understand that technical language, then they are going to struggle to understand any new idea that is built around that language. Can you really understand how to solve area problems involving triangles ifyou don't fully understand what a triangle is? Can you really understand how to solve an equation ifyou don't fully understand what an equation is? 
Hence, we need to deal with that language before we dive into any explanations or modelling. 
Take a look at this selection of examples: 
1 5. 
2. 6. 
3. 7. 
4 8. 
There are three principles at work here: 
1. 
Sequence a mixture ofexamples and non-examples. Once again, to truly understand what something is, we must also understand what it is not. 

2. 
Include boundary examples. Boundary examples are examples right on the edge of-in this case -what it means to be a triangle or not. Example 3 has straight sides, but the fact it is not a closed shape tips it over the edge. Example 8 looks weird, but it is still a triangle. 

3. 
Make consecutive examples related so students can attend to the critical feature. This is the big one. Making the examples related takes a key principle from variation theory. As Marton and Pang explain, 'We make novel meanings our own through the experience of differences against a background of sameness.' 


By only changing one thing from one example to the next, students' attention is drawn to that change and the impact it has upon the answer. 
ò 
Example 1 is a triangle. In Example 2 the sides and angles of the triangle stay the same but the orientation changes. That change is not enough to stop the shape from being a triangle, so that change must not be significant. 

ò 
Example 5 is a triangle. In Example 6, the orientation stays the same, the angles stay the same, but the sides become curved. That change is enough to tip the example from being a triangle to not being a triangle, and so that change must be significant. 


If several features change between examples, the critical features are not as easy to identify. 
Notice there is a reset halfway through the sequence. Examples 4 and 5 are not directly related to each other. I will often do this when I move from the bottom of one column to the top of another. It is more challenging for students to spot the connection when they have to shift their attention to a different part of the board, and a reset is often nice because it allows us to pursue a different related sequence. If you want all your examples to be related consecutively, then a snake-like arrangement weaving from top-left, to top-right, then down one, then left one, etc. reduces the visual jump. 
How does this work in the classroom? 
This is my process: 
1. 
I reveal each example one at a time -this ensures students are focused on the single example I want them focused on, and not wandering down the slide to think about the later examples. 

2. 
I give my students time to think independently and silently. 

3. 
I then ask my students to vote individually using thumb-up/ thumb-down -this ensures mass participation and gives me critical information as to my students' current levels of under¡standing. 

4. 
Following each vote, if there is a split of answers, or we have gone from an example to a non-example, or there is a partic¡ularly interesting boundary example, I ask students to discuss with their partner -more mass participation, and an opportu¡nity for them to rehearse and refine their thinking. If the an¡swer seems straightforward and I can see that all my students have answered it correctly, I will skip this step. 

5. 
I then reveal the answer, and give students sufficient silent thinking time to process the answer -students now have new information that may match what they thought or go against it. 

6. 
I then explain the answer clearly, ensuring I refer to previous examples: 

a. 
Example 5 is a triangle -it has three straight sides that form a closed shape, just like examples 1 and 2. 

b. 
Example 6 is not a triangle -unlike example 5, it does not have three straight sides. 





This is a pretty speedy process. Example, vote, discuss, answer. Perhaps five minutes for the sequence above. Students tend to be engaged as they try to decide whether the latest adaptation is enough to change things, and enjoy reasoning as to why. 
Checks for understanding 
At the end ofthis process, I need to check my students' understand¡ing, and I need mass participation. Here are two ways of doing this. 
1. Give an example 
The framework for learner-generated examples discussed in Tip 25 works really well here. 
Non-
Partner 
Whole-classAnother 
Interesting 
See all 
.
Example i.¡
i.¡
i.¡
example . example . 
example 
responses <heck discussion 
Students split their mini-whiteboards into quadrants and are chal¡lenged to sketch: 
1. 
An example of a triangle 

2. 
Another example of a triangle 

3. 
An interesting example of a triangle 

4. 
Something someone might think is a triangle, but is actually not. 


Try this out yourself on a bit ofpaper. 
As we discussed in Tip 25, asking students to generate their own examples provides an excellent window into their thinking. And by encouraging them to think about boundary examples we can test the very limits of this understanding. 
Students then show me their boards, so I can ensure everyone is participating and make a note of any interesting answers. They then swap mini-whiteboards with their partners. Students check whether they agree with their partner's examples, and if there is a dispute, they raise their hands. This is a sure sign that they have uncovered an interesting example or non-example, and so we dis¡cuss it as a class. 
2. Frayer diagram 
An alternative to my follow-up challenge is to ask students to com¡plete a Frayer diagram like this: 
Definition Characteristics 
Examples 
Non-examples 

This has the added advantage that you can give students the formal definition if appropriate, and students can make their own notes in the characteristics section. 
If using the Frayer diagram, I would still suggest asking students for interesting examples and non-examples (ones that other people might put in the wrong categories). I find this provides a reliable window into their understanding of a concept and can lead to some engag¡ing, productive discussions. 
Etymology 
Once students have got to grips with a piece of technical language via a sequence of related examples and non-examples, I also like to take a very quick look-no more than two minutes -into the ety¡mology of the key terminology. There are two reasons for this: 
1. 
There is often a memorable story attached to the origin ofthe word. 

2. 
We can often break up the word into component parts that stu¡dents have seen in other words. This helps forge connections between different areas ofour subject. 


My two favourite sources for etymologies are: 
ò 
Google 'etymology __', filling in the blank with the key term 

ò 
etymonline.com. 


I like to search for the etymology live in the classroom in front of the students, just to add a bit of drama to the proceedings. 
So, for 'triangle' we find out that it originates from the Latin trian¡gulum meaning 'three-cornered, having three angles'. We can break the word down into tri meaning three and angulus meaning corner or angle. 
Just like with definitions, etymology alone will never be enough to help students understand. Notice also that delving into the et¡ymology following the sequence of examples and non-examples (as opposed to starting with it) means that students are both more interested in the concept and in a better place to understand the significance ofthe roots of the term. 
The formal definition? 
Understanding the true meaning of a piece of technical language via related examples and non-examples is far more important than knowing the formal definition. But students may need to remember a formal definition (for use in an exam, for example). The key is this definition needs to come after students have got to grips with a concept -only then does the definition have any chance of making sense. If I use the Frayer diagram, there is space in one of the quad¡rants to write the formal definition. 
Another example: adjacent angles on a straight line 
To demonstrate the breadth of this approach, let me show you another example. This time, let's look at some technical language that has the unfortunate property that its meaning in the maths classroom is subtly different to its meaning in the rest ofthe world ¡
adjacent angles. 
Outside of the maths classroom, 'adjacent' simply means 'next to'. But when learning the relationship between adjacent angles on a straight line, adjacent means something very specific that is hard to communicate in words. 
I begin by telling my students the relationship: 'Adjacent angles on a straight line sum to 180 degrees.' 
And then I show them the following sequence of examples, one at a time:

Notice that in Example 1, the angles are adjacent, but not in the mathematical sense. In Example 4, the angles are adjacent, but not all on the same straight line. In Example 6, we have angles on a straight line that are not adjacent. All of this is far easier to com¡municate via related examples and non-examples than through a waffly definition. 
As before, this sequence would be followed by a check for under¡standing-either a Give an example offramework or a Frayer diagram, teasing out interesting examples and non-examples of adjacent an¡gles on a straight line. 
Students are then in a strong position to work out the size of miss¡ing angles when given numbers, as their attention is not split be¡tween thinking about the concept and carrying out the calculations.

Here are some other examples of mathematical technical language that I have explained using this approach:

ò 
And possibly my greatest ever challenge ... attempting to teach a then 2-year-old Isaac what is three and what is not three. Hats off to Early Years teachers. 


A good exercise at this point is to either try to map out a series of related examples and non-examples for a topic you are teaching soon, or try to do so for one of the topics on my list. I promise it is more fun than it sounds. 
As I stated in the introduction, I am deliberately avoiding examples from other subjects in this book as I do not have the expertise or ex¡perience to know how they work. But I couldn't resist including this from Daisy Christodoulou. She described how a similar approach could be used in English to better understand the concept of a verb. Can you identify the verb in each ofthese sentences: 
ò 
I slowly run to the park. 

ò 
I run to the park. 

ò 
I went to the park for a run. 

ò 
I didn't go to the park for a run. 


ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Use related sequences orexamples and non-examples instead of definitions to draw students' attention to the critical featuresofa technicallanguage to enablethem to understand it better.

Tip 56 -Fourteen ideas to improve the explanation of a concept

details 
What's the problem? 
~ 
I 
I 
Tip63 
Tip 64 
Tip 62 
Silent 
Self-
EPP 
explain
Teacher 
We have assessed our students' prior knowledge (Tip 51), shown them why this idea matters (Tip 54), and ensured they understand any relevant technical language (Tip 55). Things are going well. 
Now it is time to provide an explanation of the concept at the heart of the lesson. Without this explanation, we end up diving into a worked example cold, and all the work we've done to build up rele¡vance and meaning could be undone. 
But what should that explanation look like? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you plan your explanations out in advance? 

ò 
What, for you, are the features of agood explanation? 


What's the tip? 
1. Plan two explanations 
I now plan not one, but two explanations. The first comes before the worked example, and is designed to explain the concept to students before a worked example that uses that concept. The second expla¡nation -which we will discuss in Tip 68 -comes after the worked example and provides an opportunity to reflect on the worked exam¡ple students have just experienced. 
This dual approach to explanations is inspired by the Wood-Trees¡Wood approach that Adam Boxer described on my Mr Barton Maths podcast. As the saying goes, people often cannot see the wood for the trees, thus they fail to appreciate the big picture as they are too bogged down in the details. This is the same when students get bogged down in the nitty-gritty of a worked example. They pay so much attention to the individual steps, that there is a danger they don't have an opportunity to attend to the process as a whole. As a result, their knowledge becomes fragmented and their learning lacks purpose or meaning. 
So the first explanation looks at the concept from a higher level (the wood), the worked example then zooms in closer (the trees), before we finally zoom back out again to reflect on what we have done (back to the wood). 
Here are some ideas to improve this first explanation. 
2. Understand what the explanation ofthe concept is, and what it is not 
The explanation of the concept is not the sharing of hinterland knowledge, nor is it the worked example. 
Let me show you this distinction by thinking about the mean. 
ò 
Hinterland knowledge would be the story of Pythagoras, Al¡Biruni, magnets and ships that we discussed in Tip 54. The purpose is to introduce the concept, and show students why it matters. 

ò 
The purpose of the worked example is to show students how to implement the concept. This often involves a formal procedure. So, in the case of the mean, this is where we demonstrate how to calculate the mean of a list of numbers. 

ò 
To ease the jump from the hinterland to worked example, we use an explanation of the concept. This takes a higher-level look at the concept students have just met and sets them up to learn about its implementation. 


When explaining the mean, I will draw three towers: one two blocks tall, one three blocks tall, and the other seven blocks tall. 
B 
I will explain how we can share out these blocks so all the towers are of the same height, to associate the idea of the mean with equi¡table distribution. I will rub a block out from the tallest tower and draw it on top of one of the shorter towers so students have a con¡crete representation of the process that is happening. Alternatives would be to use actual blocks or to animate. 
Notice this provides a visual, higher-level explanation of the mean, which will support students in understanding the more abstract procedure in the worked example that follows. 
3. Tell students that this is something they need to remember Once you understand the purpose of this explanation, it is impor¡tant that your students do too. During the sharing of hinterland knowledge, I would expect students to be paying as much attention as they might when listening to someone read them a story or when watching a movie -they are attending to the overall narrative arc, but the specific details are not essential (it is not crucial students remember the exact date that averages were used with cargo ships, for example). But the explanation is different. Here we certainly want students to pay attention to the details of what we are saying. 
So it is worth beginning our explanation with something like 'okay, now I need you to really concentrate for this bit... '. 
4. Say less 
When sharing hinterland knowledge, you may well have waxed lyrical to convey the emotion that the story required, which is great. But during the explanation, we need to be careful about the words we choose to use. It is definitely a case of quality over quantity. The best explanations are clear and concise, with gestures and accom¡panying models doing a lot of the explaining for us. 
5. Plan the explanation in advance The best way to deliver a clear and concise explanation is to plan it in advance -something I never used to do. What followed were years of waffly, overly complicated, muddled, overly technical ex¡
planations that knocked students' confidence and took up precious lesson time. 
As a minimum, we need to think through what we are going to say and what we are going to show. Maths teacher Jemma Sherwood suggests taking this further and rehearsing your explanations, ei¡ther in front of the mirror or with a colleague or a loved one. Some people like to script their explanation in advance; for others, bullet points is enough. Whatever you decide, the key is to arrive at the lesson knowing what you want to say and show to your students. 
6. Connect explanations to things students know 
The best explanations move from the familiar to the unfamiliar. It is an opportunity to build a new idea on the foundations of some¡thing students have already encountered and have knowledge of. If you are following the approach in this book, you will have as¡sessed relevant prior knowledge and explained technical language before this stage of the process, so will be confident that students are where they need to be. This means you can refer to concepts and ideas connected to that prior knowledge as you construct your explanation. In the case of the mean, I can confidently talk about summation and division, as I will have assessed students' under¡standing prior to the explanation. 
7. Go from concrete to abstract Related to the point above, explanations that move from concrete to abstract tend to be better understood by students. Concrete ex¡amples are more easily relatable, allowing a firmer grounding in the reality of the new idea. Then you can move to the more abstract 
principle. Presenting the mean as an equitable distribution is my way of doing this. 
8. Use models 
Models really help provide the concrete structure to hang this initial explanation on. Luckily, maths is full of them. Bar models for ratio, balancing scales for equations, number lines for arithmetic, double¡sided counters, algebra tiles, money, Cuisenaire rods, and so on. All of these provide a physical structure to help students get to grips with the idea before its formal treatment in a worked example. That is why I use the towers ofblocks when introducing the mean. 
9. Use demonstrations instead ofproof 
One of the longest-running debates on my Mr Barton Maths podcast over the years has been whether it is okay to show students how to do something without them first knowing why it works. My view is that if the why is more difficult to understand than the how, then you risk losing students as you try to explain a complex idea that they have no buy-in to. Instead, I prefer to show students how to do something, and then return to the why at the point when they are more knowledgeable and feel more successful and curious about the idea. 
But this doesn't mean that I dive into worked examples cold. Indeed, the whole aim of this explanation is to lay the foundations necessary to best understand the new procedure that is coming. So in this part ofthe process, I prefer demonstrations over proof. 
With the mean, my explanation involving towers is a demonstra¡tion of the concept using a specific example; it is not a proof ofwhy the procedure for calculating the mean works. 
When teaching Pythagoras, my explanation will involve a demon¡stration that the relationship holds true in a concrete scenario. I will start with a 3-4-5 triangle, perhaps using pebbles as Pythago¡ras himself is purported to have used, to ensure students have something literal to focus on. I will then use GeoGebra to vary the side lengths and show the relationship remains invariant. This is enough for now. The proof can come later, either in the reflection (Tip 68) or later on in the learning episode. 
10. Start from a blank canvas 
This is from science teacher, Adam Boxer. The control of informa¡tion is really important when constructing an initial explanation. If possible, start with a blank page and slowly build up your model, step by step, directing students' attention as you go, matching up your verbal explanation with the visual you are constructing. That helps students focus on what is relevant and reduces the risk of overwhelming them. If the model you wish to use is complex and not suited to being drawn from scratch, then I would suggest putting it on the screen and giving students sufficient time to take it in as a whole, before drawing their attention to the first element you wish to explain. 
When explaining the mean, I start with a blank page and sketch my towers ofblocks, one by one. 
11. Consider providing a cue during the explanation 
A tip from history teacher, Kate Jones. The encoding specificity principle (there's one to impress your mates) suggests that providing a cue when first exposed to a new idea improves the chances of that knowledge being successfully retrieved when the cue is used in the future, versus when no cues are present. 
The cue could be lots of things. It could be the finished model that you have constructed as part ofyour explanation -so, when I re¡visit the idea of the mean in the future, I could put an image of the towers up on the board to help students better remember the ex¡planation. Or the cue could be verbal -perhaps one ofthe Call and Response chants we looked at back in Tip 14 or a key phrase you keep repeating-'the mean is all about sharing', for example. 
In the long term, you don't want students to be dependent on the cue, but in the early stages of getting to grips with an idea, these cues can be really helpful, and the explanation stage is a great place to introduce them. 
12. Question, don't tell 
Maths teachers Sammy Kempner and Michael Pershan both suggest questioning throughout the explanation, using a series of quick¡fire Cold Calls, Call and Response, or answers on mini-whiteboards, all ofwhich are described in Chapter 3. This serves four useful purposes: 
1. 
It connects the new idea to something familiar. 

2. 
It is an opportunity to revisit relevant prior knowledge. 

3. 
It allows students to experience success early with the new idea. 

4. 
It keeps students engaged and actively involved in the explana¡tion process. 


With my explanation of the mean, I could ask, 'How many blocks in total?' and 'Which operation would allow me to share the blocks be¡tween the three towers so they all have equal amounts?' 
My only caveat would be to ask questions you are confident students will get right. The explanation process should build confi¡dence, connecting the new idea to what students already know. A wrong answer and contagious confusion at this stage risks derailing everything. Of course, the chances of this happening are greatly reduced ifyou have undergone a thorough assessment ofrelevant prior knowledge as described in Tip 51, but even then, there will be occasions where I will say to students, 'Okay, be quiet now, you need to listen carefully to this bit.' 
13. Say it again? 
This is a controversial one. A student may say that they don't understand your explanation, and the obvious reaction is to try to say it again in a different way. But remember, this is the first time students have encountered this idea. Perhaps they don't need an¡other explanation, they just need to hear that same explanation again. So, ifyou have put the time into planning and practising your explanation, try repeating it and you might find that the student now understands. 
14. Have students stand up during the explanation 
Let's end with a weird one -for me, anyway. The default in my classroom is that my students are always sitting, especially when I am giving explanations or instructions. Of course they are! I have never considered they would do anything else. If pushed for a ra¡tionale, I would say that it gives my students the best chance to attend to what I am saying. In Building Thinking Classrooms in Mathematics, Peter Liljedahl suggests I am wrong. Instead, Liljedahl argues we should ask our students to stand and cluster together around us when we give explanations. With our students standing close by we give these explanations verbally, ask questions, and add key points, models and solutions on the board as we go. The rationale is that students are more engaged and alert when standing, and evidence is cited that when they return to their desks, they are quicker to en¡gage in thinking behaviour. 
Most classrooms lack a large enough space at the front to house all the students, so Liljedahl suggests students cluster around a small 
space by the whiteboard, with some students standing behind two or three desks. 
This is firmly outside of my comfort zone, but my initial explo¡rations have been promising. The act ofstanding seems to jolt some students to life, it seems to be much harder to be passive when standing, and that extra alertness does seem to follow through into the worked example or task that follows the explanation. 
If students standing during explanations works for you, I see no reason why you could not have your students standing while you do a worked example, give instructions for a task, or go through the solution to a tricky problem. As ever, I will leave you to experiment as to what works best for you and your students. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Explain the concept you are about to model in aclear, concise, relatable way.

What's the problem? 
Imagine I want to teach my class how to rearrange formulae. My Check-Point question (Tip 41) is: 
2
Rearrange c = a2 + b2 to make a the subject. 
Following Chapter 7, I begin by listing and prioritising the relevant prior knowledge my students need. Things like: 
ò 
Interpret algebraic notation 

ò 
Know the order of operations 

ò 
Know how to balance an equation 

ò 
Know the inverse operations for the four operations 

ò 
Know the inverse operations for squares and roots. 


I have assessed all this and am happy that knowledge is secure. I have taught my students what a formula is using related examples and non-examples (Tip 55). I have shared hinterland knowledge about some cracking formulae used throughout history, and I have explained that we are going to use our algebra skills to manipu¡late some formulae. I am on a roll, and so I dive into the worked example: 

But there is a problem. My students are struggling to follow. I have failed to account for a certain type of knowledge -a decision. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you seewhat that decision is? 

ò 
How would you deal with it? 


What's the tip? 
Teachers often fail to notice decisions for the very reason that ex¡perts make decisions without even realising they are making them. Decisions are the epitome of the curse ofknowledge. 
In the example above, students may have been wondering how you decide if something is the subject of a formula or not. More specifically: 
ò 
How did he know he has not finished on Line 2? 

ò 
How did he know he has finished on Line 3? 


To me this is obvious. To my students, it is not. And as their atten¡tion turns to these questions, they have less to attend to how every¡thing else fits together. 
Being aware of these decisions is clearly important. Ifwe know them, we can draw students' attention to them during our explana¡tion and modelling. But I think we can go one better. 
I think we should teach the decisions that underlie a new idea or process separately and explicitly. That is the best way to help our students treat those decisions in the exact same way as we do: make them automatically and unconsciously. This frees up valuable attention that can be dedicated to noticing how relevant prior knowledge and decisions fit together in the novel, complex process our students are learning. 
Identify the decisions 
Identifying these invisible decisions is not easy. I find the write a line, leave a line technique works well. So write out the full solution leav¡ing every other line blank. Then go back over the solution and ask yourself, 'What decision have I made that helped me know what to write on this next line?' As ever, doing this exercise with a colleague is helpful. 
Teach the decisions 
We could try to explain these decisions to students, but as we saw with definitions of technical language, this can be challenging. To see what I mean, try to put into a sentence how you decide if some¡thing is the subject of a formula or not. 
Got one? Hold on to it for one minute. 
Okay, instead of an explanation, I think a carefully varied sequence of examples and non-examples is the best way to teach decisions. Here is a sequence of examples I use to teach students how to cor¡rectly make the decision concerning whether a letter is the subject of a formula or not: 


1
2. b+3=a 12. 
-=b+3 
a

a=bc-6 
a= j b;3

9. 
a=bc-a

a 

01
10. a=bc-
Look back at your explanation. Does it cover examples 10, 11, 12 and 13? 
Notice there is no requirement at all to rearrange any of the for¡mulae. This is a sequence designed to isolate a key decision in the complex process of rearranging formulae so students can achieve fluency in it. So when students come to learn the procedure of rear¡ranging from start to finish, it is one less thing they need to worry about. Notice also the use ofrelated examples and non-examples, with just one thing changing from example to example, so students' attention is focused on one critical feature at a time and the impact it has. 
In class, we use the same approach as we did for explaining techni¡cal language in Tip 55.

The teacher explanation of the decision at each stage is key, ensur¡ing you make reference to other examples: 
ò

ò 
Q9 -a is not the subject because it appears on both sides of the equation, unlike in question 8. 


Check for understanding 
As with technical language, I can then check my students' under¡standing by using the Give an example of framework (Tip 25) or by asking them to complete a Frayer diagram. Both approaches make use of the power ofboundary examples, challenging students to think of interesting examples of formulae with a as the subject, and examples of formulae where someone might think a is the subject, but in fact it is not. As before, students can check each other's work and we can discuss any contentious examples as a class. 
An example with more possible answers 
In the formula example, the choice was either 'yes' or 'no'. But a similar approach can be used when there are more than two possi¡ble choices for our answers. 
Recently I was watching a Year 8 lesson about calculating the area of a trapezium. 
The worked example the teacher had gone through was this: 

7cm

Can you predict what happened next? Students breezed through question 1. Maths is easy! Life is great! Then came question 2. Students had three issues: 
1. 
Halving odd integers. In question 2, they are required to work out 13 7 2. 

2. 
Multiplication involving decimals. In question 2, they are re¡quired to work out 6.5 x 5. This was a huge issue and was the focus of the majority of their cries for help. 

3. 
Deciding which sides were the parallel sides and which were the height. All of a sudden, there are two 'heights': the 10cm and the 9cm. Some students chose the 10cm, others tried to work out the mean of these two sides. 


In all three cases, notice how the single, prototypical worked exam¡ple did not help. 
Sorting these issues out took a long time. Many students were confused and demotivated, especially after the elation of cracking question 1, and the lesson was derailed. 
The first two issues concern relevant prior knowledge that the teacher could have assessed before explaining how to calculate the area of a trapezium (Chapter 7). Using the prioritisation exer¡cise in Tip 50, operations with decimals would be considered low priority as students can understand the core concept of the area of the trapezium without knowing this. Indeed, as operations with decimals were proving so challenging for students, a better version of the worksheet would have involved more examples with integer calculations early on. This is another example ofwhy we need to ask ourselves what our students are likely to be thinking about when planning our lessons (Tip 44). In this instance, students' attention was drawn towards carrying out those calculations and away from the purpose of those calculations. 
The third issue is an example of a decision that can be taught ex¡plicitly and separately from the more complex process of calculating the area of a trapezium. 
A sequence of examples like this might be useful: 

This time students will need to respond using mini-whiteboards as they need to write down letters and not just vote 'yes' or 'no', but otherwise, it proceeds in the same way as with the 'subject of a for¡mula' sequence above, including the final check for understanding involving student-generated boundary examples. 
Notice there are no numbers to worry about. So all students' atten¡tion is on the structure of the trapezium and what they can deter¡mine about the sides. 
Following this sequence, students would be much more confident and fluent in labelling sides of the trapezium, and so could divert more attention to the entire process of calculating the area of a trapezium -labelling sides, substituting into a formula, carrying out the calculation, including the units, etc. 
Four common types ofdecisions in maths 
Here are four common types of decisions to be on the lookout for that could be addressed in this way, along with three examples of each: 
1. What method do I need? 
ò 
Is this trigonometry or Pythagoras? 

ò 
Do I need to factorise to isolate the variable? 

ò 
Do two minuses equal a plus here? 


2. Is this answer in the correct form? 
ò 
Is this the subject of the formula? 

ò 
What units do I need? 

ò 
How many digits should be in my final answer after round¡ing? 


3. Is the answer feasible? 
ò 
Could this be an answer to a probability question? 

ò 
Should the answer be positive or negative? 

ò 
Should the answer increase or decrease? 


4. What are the relevant numbers I need? 
ò 
What are the parallel sides and the perpendicular height? 

ò 
What is the final amount and what is the original amount? 

ò 
What goes on the x-axis and what goes on the y-axis? 


A useful exercise is to think of a decision for an upcoming topic you are teaching and try to plan a sequence of related examples and non-examples to teach that decision, or try that exercise with a de¡cision from my list above.

Identify key decisions that need to be made when getting to grips with anew idea, and teach these explicitly and separately from the new idea itself.

What's the problem? 
Okay, so we have dealt with relevant prior knowledge, technical language and decisions. We have shown that what we are about to teach has a purpose and explained the concept at a higher level. Our students are in a great position. Now it is time to think about a worked example to convey the new idea we want our students to understand. 
When planning lessons for the first 12 years ofmy career, my top priority was always focused on the task I wanted my students to do. It had to be bright and shiny, and ifit could involve cutting and sticking, even better. Worryingly lacking from my planning process was any consideration of the examples I was going to use. I figured I would make these up on the spot or just use the ones that came with the PowerPoint I had downloaded off the internet. 
I now realise the importance of the worked examples we share with our students -not just how we present them, but the examples we choose. They are often students' first encounter with an idea. They shape what is to come. They determine whether we are building upon solid or shaky foundations. 
Here is a challenge for you. Think of a topic you are teaching soon for which you will use a worked example. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Which example will you use? 

ò 
How did you choose it? 


What's the tip? 
Here are five things I try to do or avoid when choosing the worked examples to share with my students. 
1. Get the pitch right 
Even when you have done all the preparation described in the tips so far, it is impossible to predict how a group of 30 students will respond to a new idea. But in general, I try to make my first exam¡ple straightforward as opposed to challenging. I want my students' initial experience of this new idea to be a positive one. I want them to taste success in the hope it will motivate them to keep putting in the effort. Challenging examples can come later when my students are more confident and knowledgeable. 
But -and it is another big but -straightforward does not mean prototypical. As we saw in the trapezium example in Tip 57, that can lead to erroneous generalisations, something we will now dis¡
cuss more... 
2. Try to avoid erroneous generalisations 
We obviously want the examples we share with our students to show the correct way to do something. But -in maths anyway ¡it is all too easy to use examples that may lead students to think something is true that isn't. Here are several examples I have unfor¡tunately used in my own teaching. See ifyou can spot the problems before reading on: 

ò 
Using a 6 by 3 rectangle when teaching area -students often muddle up the related concepts of area and perimeter. With a 6 by 3 rectangle, you get the same answer ifyou use the wrong method. 

ò 
Using 10% lots when teaching percentages of amounts -stu¡dents may think you divide by the percentage to find it, so di¡vide by 5 to find 5%. 

ò 
Using 2 squared when teaching students about powers -a key misunderstanding students have is they multiply, so 8 cubed becomes 24. 2 squared gives the correct answer no matter which way you do it. 

ò 
Using !! as an early example of fraction simplification -just cancel the 6s and it works. 

ò 
Using 5, 6, 8, 9, 12 when teaching the mean -the answer is 8, which is also the median. 

ò 
x works out to be 132░ -students may think this relationship always holds true, or mistake it for vertically opposite angles being equal. 

ò 
Using (0, 0) as the first centre of enlargement that students' experience -they may notice that you can simply multiply the coordinates ofthe object by the scale factor to get the coordi¡nates of the image. Better to start with a centre such as (2, 1) and then use the origin later as a special case. 


Here is a brilliant one that @ericblock shared on Twitter: 

Can you spot the issue here? Work out the answer before reading ahead... 
Students often mistakenly calculate the mean from a frequency table by adding up the data column and dividing by the number of rows. In this example, that erroneous method works! 
Of course, these examples all make lovely investigations as each of them begs the questions, 'Are there other examples that work like that?' and 'Can we generalise?' But in terms of examples to help build students' early knowledge of a concept on, they are problem¡atic. 
Maths teachers, can you think of any others? And non-maths teach¡ers, are there examples of these in your subject? 
Experience is one way to avoid these. But better still, ensuring you plan for error (Tip 46) and work through any examples you are giv¡ing your students (Tip 45) will help. 
3. Avoid repeating elements within a worked example 
When students are new to an idea, we want to give them all the support we can to help them focus on the critical aspect at the ap¡propriate time. This is made more challenging when we repeat ele¡ments within an example. 
Take this equation: 
X 
--2 = 10 
2 
As we solve it, we may say things like 'a good first step is to add 2 to both sides of the equation'. 
But which 2? Sure, we can gesture towards it, but what about the student not paying attention? Or what about later on when students look back at their notes? By ensuring elements within an example are unique we give our students the best chance of focusing on ex¡actly what we are talking about. 
4. Have a single intended object oflearning per worked example 
There is a danger we try to do too much in a single example. If we are teaching students to expand a single bracket, we could have a non-linear term outside the bracket, a negative term inside the bracket, and we could use m instead of the standard x. Ifwe are teaching rotations, the centre of rotation could be in the second quadrant, it could be inside the shape, and we could rotate by 270 degrees. 
Here I like the idea of an intended object oflearning, which I got from Anne Watson. What is the one thing you want your students to learn from this example? What do you want them to focus their attention on? What is the one thing you want them to notice? Choose an example that focuses on that intended object alone. And then in subsequent examples, move on to other intended objects of learning. 
One intended object oflearning is plenty. We are building founda¡tions. The more carefully we tread in these initial stages, the more confident our students feel, and the more time we will save later on. 
5. Use a pair ofrelated worked examples We have already seen in Tip 55 and Tip 57 that holding most fea¡tures of an example constant and only changing one critical feature can help draw students' attention to that critical feature and the 
impact it has on the solution. This is a key component ofvariation theory. 
Let's return to my favourite quote from Marton and Pang: 'We make novel meanings our own through the experience of differences against a background of sameness.' 
Several studies suggest a careful sequencing is particularly impor¡tant to do in the modelling phase: 'Choosing multiple examples carefully to allow learners to distinguish between essential as opposed to non-essential aspects may enhance learners' perception of deep struc¡tures.' {Sun, 2011) 
'The questions for teachers and designers are: 
1. 
what juxtaposition of items, sequential presentation, or use of materials or layouts can make the underlying relationships discoverable? 

2. 
how can I make it likely that all students appreciate the under¡lying structures intended in the design process?' (Watson et al., 2016) 


We can tap into this power by presenting examples in pairs. Fur¡thermore, we can design the pairs to help our students focus on the intended object oflearning as described in point 4. 
Here is an example. When teaching my students to solve linear equations, one thing I want them to think hard about (the intended object of learning) is what operation they do first when solving. More specifically, how does the structure of the equation impact the order of the inverse operations we need to perform on each side of the equation in order to isolate the variable? 
I can draw their attention to that with this pair of examples: 
f. 
-I -10
2 Y.-1 
10
2 
Only one thing changes, so any change in the procedure can be at¡tributed to that change. 
Students can be supported in their thinking about this by the use of self-explanation prompts that we will discuss in Tip 64. 
One more pair of related examples, this time when factoring qua¡dratic expressions: 
x2 + 7x + 12 
x2 +Bx+ 12 
Here I want to draw students' attention to how they select the cor¡rect pair of numbers from all the ones they have listed that multiply to give 12. The coefficient ofx is the only thing that changes, so this critical feature informs students' choice of the final answer. 
In subsequent examples, I can vary the constant term or the sign. And this returns us to point 4 above -trying not to do too much in one pair of examples. 
A good test ofwhether you have written a good pair of related ex¡amples is to show them to a colleague and ask, 'What do you think the intended object oflearning is here?' 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Think carefully about the examples you use with your students as they provide the foundation of under¡standing upon which we build.

What's the problem? 
I don't know if this is true in other subjects, but in maths, there are loads of preplanned PowerPoint lessons available. They may have come from a colleague in your school, a resources site, or part of a textbook or curriculum package you have purchased. 
These PowerPoints often come with fully worked solutions to examples, with each line ofworking beautifully set out, appearing one after the other with a simple click of the mouse. There may also be lovely digital versions ofmaths equipment, such as angle mea¡surers and protractors, that appear in the correct place to solve the problem. 
What could be better than that for the time-poor teacher, especially one with handwriting as bad as mine? 
I think there are five issues to consider. 
1. Quality control 
In Tip 58 we discussed five things teachers might consider when choosing their worked examples. It is always worth checking if the choices of examples used in these resources are good ones. 
2. It is harder to get the pacing right 
I go far too fast. It is all too easy to click merrily through a Power¡Point ifyou don't have to do the thinking or writing yourself. 
3. It is harder to focus students' attention If complete lines ofworking out appear at the click of a button, it is 
much more difficult to direct students' attention to something im¡portant at the start of the line ofworking out. 
4. There is less flexibility 
If a student suggests an answer that is perfectly correct but is written in a form different to that the PowerPoint uses, I find myself having to say things like, 'Yes, you are right, but I am going to write it like this.' 
5. The disconnect This is the big one. As students watch lines ofworking magically appearing in front of their eyes, they may well be wondering: where on earth did that come from? The connection between student and teacher is so important in the learning process, and it risks being 
disrupted if some invisible third party appears to be providing all the answers. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you use preprepared resources when modelling? 

ò 
Have you experienced any of these issues? 


What's the tip? 
Model techniques live. For maths teachers, this can perhaps be summed up best by the mantra 'do the maths, don't reveal the maths'. 
Students need to witness the teacher building up a solution from scratch. This wipes out any mystique, giving the students the feel¡ing that they can -or will be able to -do this themselves. Starting with a blank canvas also improves your pacing, allows you to better focus students' attention on important parts of the solution at the right moment, and gives you greater flexibility to incorporate stu¡dents' contributions. 
What does this mean practically? Well, with preprepared Pow¡erPoints, this means deleting all the animated working out, and just leaving the question itselfwhich you can model live using the whiteboard pen. Many teachers have switched to OneNote for mod¡elling as they find it offers greater flexibility than PowerPoint. Or, as we will see in Tip 60, it may mean abandoning the PowerPoint and whiteboard altogether in favour ofwriting in an exercise book under the visualiser. 
But what about technology? 
I am a huge fan ofusing technology in the classroom. Maths teachers are blessed with websites such as Desmos and GeoGebra, which can demonstrate algebraic and geometric concepts instantly and dynamically, along with a whole host of gifs, animations and applets. But when it comes to examples, we need to be careful. I remember for years I had a really fancy PowerPoint that showed students how to rotate an object. There was digital tracing paper with just the right level of transparency, a lovely clip-art pencil, and 
then -the piece de resistance -I had designed the animation so the trac¡ing paper rotated about the pencil point. It took me flipping ages. 
The problem was I then expected my students to do the same thing... but with actual tracing paper and pencils. There was a dis¡connect between what I was doing and what I expected my students to do. 
Now I demonstrate anything my students need to do in exactly the way they need to do it. This often means stepping away from the whiteboard, and then under the visualiser I will rotate objects, con¡struct shapes, measure angles and tap into my calculator. I need to ensure I show the students exactly what to do with the same tools I expect them to use. 
I can still make use of technology to delve deeper into concepts, test hypotheses, generalise or quickly illustrate answers to practice questions. But when modelling, it's back to basics. 
Answers to practice questions 
It is worth pointing out that I have no issue with projecting neat solutions to practice questions for students to self-mark. This can save loads of time, and allows students to focus on the exact question they need to think harder about instead ofwaiting for the teacher to go through questions they are fine with one at a time. But in the modelling phase, I think it is really important that we do as much live as possible. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Always model techniques live to your students in the exact way they will need to apply the techniques themselves. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip59 
ª 
OVER TO YOU 
1. 
How does this tip fit into your current practice? Ido exactly that Ido something like that Ido nothing like that 

2. 
What will you need to change to make thistip work for you? 

3. 
When are you going to try this idea forthe firsttime? Please give the exact date and time. 

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
Some students are notoriously bad at setting out their work. Despite telling them time and again to underline the title, line up their equals signs, draw clear diagrams, or use specific symbols, they never do it. And if we value a certain way of setting out work -par¡
ticularly during the modelling phase of a learning episode ¡ this can  
be an issue.  
In the past, I have done all my classwork on a whiteboard ¡ both  

during the modelling phase, or when going through how to do a particularly troublesome practice question. The problem is that the whiteboard screen looks very different to my students' exercise books. One is clear, the other contains squares. One is landscape, the other is portrait. I have digital ink, they have a boring old pen. So, despite my best efforts to model exactly how students' work should look, the disconnect between what students saw me doing and what they had in front of them seemed to be a barrier. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to this? 

ò
What is the solution? 


What's the tip? 
Use a teacher worked-examples book! 
I first saw this in action during a visit to Ellesmere Park High School, and I immediately loved it. The teacher had an exercise book exactly like his students, and he used it to do all the live modelling by plac¡ing his book under the visualiser: 

And when a calculator was called for, instead of using a fancy emulator like I would have done, instead he used the exact physical model his students all had so he could perform the exact action he wanted them to do: 

This is such a simple idea, but a potentially powerful one for the following reasons. 
1. A teacher worked-examples book makes it clear how the stu¡dents need to set their work out. 
If I put something in the margin, students can also put it in the mar¡gin. If I leave a blank line before the next line ofworking, students can do that too. Our finished products should look identical, with no need for any interpretation by my students. 
2. A teacher worked-examples book helps convey a message: what I am doing at my desk, you are doing at your desk, so all of us are doing this together. 
Even if the teacher is modelling techniques live (Tip 59), it can still seem like students are watching something magical unfold at the front of the class. The teacher is stood up, using digital ink on a giant vertical surface, but when students turn to the blank page in their books the world looks completely different. Sitting 
down, using a book exactly like our students, can help reduce this disconnect. 
3. If you use a teacher worked-examples book every lesson, you end up with a comprehensive record of examples. 
This could be used for: 
ò 
Photocopying and giving to students if they missed the lesson 

ò 
Showing to a teacher who you share the class with or who will be teaching the same topic to another class 

ò 
Sharing in departmental meetings to stimulate a discussion on methods and setting work out 

ò 
Aiding future planning by having a valuable resource to look back on. A PowerPoint may have the example you used, but a teacher worked-examples book also has the method you used to answer it. 


Make use ofthe adjacent page 
A nice addition to the teacher worked-examples book is to do each example on a double-page spread of the book. Use one page for the live modelling under the visualiser, and use the adjacent page (out of sight of the visualiser) for notes. These notes could be key ques¡tions you want to ask, tricky lines of working you want to ensure you have correct, good explanations you want to share, or potential mistakes you want to point out. Basically, the adjacent page is the place to record the things you have planned in advance and want to ensure you remember. It helps free up your working memory, so you can pay full attention to your live modelling. You can also use it after the example to make a note of anything important that happened -like a student making an unexpected mistake or asking a question you hadn't considered -so you can remember this in the future. 
Not one, but two 
I have since experimented with using two teacher worked-examples books -a planning book and a live book. I use the planning book when I plan out my examples. I answer the example fully, which gives me a chance to rehearse the maths, but also think about how I want it set out, and see if there are any potential twists or turns I need to be aware of. I also jot down any key questions I want to ask my students, or any other notes that might be relevant. I can then transfer these notes to the adjacent page of the live book as de¡scribed above. 
But I don't have a visualiser :-( My two favourite classroom tools are a set of mini-whiteboards and a visualiser. I would take those ahead of a class set ofiPads any day. You can get a decent visualiser for around ú40. But ifyou don't have one, then using a background to your presentation that is as close to your students' books as possible will allow you to get most of the benefits described above. 
OneNote works far better than PowerPoint for this. Combined with a graphics tablet, it offers a smooth writing experience, allowing you to sit at your desk -or a student's desk -and construct examples from a blank canvas. OneNote has the advantage that you have a permanent digital copy ofyour examples that can be used for future planning, as well as easily shared with students and colleagues. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Model techniques live by using an example book that is exactly the same as your students' books.

What's the problem? 
Here are a few questions for you to consider: 
1. 
How neat are your students' worked examples? 

2. 
How easy is it for your students to use these worked examples when they are practising in lessons? 

3. 
How easy is it for your students to use these worked examples when they are revising? 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What are your answers to these questions? 

ò
Ifany ofyour answers flagged an issue, what is the solution? 


What's the tip? 
My answer to each of these -in the majority of cases -is a resound¡ing 'not very'. So, what is the solution? You guessed it... a student worked-examples book! 
I have visited several schools where students have their own dedicated worked-examples book that is separate from their practice book. Any time students copy down an example it goes in the for¡mer, and any time they work through problems it goes in the latter. 
Having watched many students work this way, I think there are three reasons why student worked-examples books are worth considering. 
1. Students' work is a lot neater in a worked-examples book 
Students seem to take more care in their notes ifthey go in a special book. If all their work goes in the same book, and they glance back to the previous page and see some scruffy work they produced when ploughing through practice questions, it can then be tricky to switch back into neat mode. But with a book dedicated to neat, clear examples -especially when those examples are carefully modelled by the teacher -the chances of students producing the same stan¡dard increase. 
2. A worked-examples book helps students when practising in a lesson 
With a dedicated examples book, there is no more flicking back¡wards and forwards when students are practising. They can have the example open in front of them to refer to as they work through the practice questions. This feels particularly important in the early stages of students' getting to grips with the idea, allowing them to dedicate as much attention to thinking hard about the idea as possible. 
3. A worked-examples book helps students when revising Ask students if they use their exercise books for revising and most will say no. Those that do will find it a challenge. How do you find the topic you need help on? And how many pages of scruffy work do you need to sift through to find the example you are looking for? But with a book filled exclusively with examples, this becomes much easier. I have even seen some students add page numbers and a 
contents page to help them find exactly what they need in as little time as possible.

Consider giving your students adedicated book for their worked examples.

What's the problem? 
So far in this chapter we have discussed the pre-modelling work, choice of examples and the practicalities of modelling. Now I want to turn our attention to the pedagogy of that modelling -what in maths is often referred to as the 'worked example'. 
In my early days as a teacher, my lessons would consist of a block of several worked examples followed by extended student prac¡tice. The problem was that my students always struggled with the practice. 
This is no surprise. Many studies (e.g. Atkinson et al., 2000, and more recently, Alreshidi, 2021) have found that a single worked example followed by practice related to that worked example, and repeat, is more effective in terms of students' performance than a series of worked examples followed by extended practice. This more productive approach is known as an Example-Problem Pair. 
Experience taught me that Example-Problem Pairs were more effective than my old way before the research did. But then I made another mistake. I would model a worked example on my board, and then on the next PowerPoint slide, I would give my students some practice questions. Again, they struggled. They could not re¡late the worked example to the practice as well as I had hoped. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What does your board or book set-up look like when delivering worked examples? 

ò 
Has this changed over the years? 


What's the tip? 
A simple change to my board-or book ifusing the teacher worked¡examples book described in Tip 60 -made a lot of difference. In¡stead of doing a worked example and then wiping all trace ofit by moving on to the next slide, I started setting my board out like this: 
Worked Example 
Your Turn 
This simple change to my Example-Problem Pairs means that when I have finished the worked example and my students attempt the related question (the Your Turn), they can compare the two questions and the steps required to arrive at the answer. This kind of scaffolding is exactly what students need at this early stage of trying to get to grips with an idea. Then, upon the successful com¡pletion of the Your Turn question, students can attempt related practice without the side-by-side set-up as their fluency and profi¡ciency develops. In Tip 96, we will discuss how I still use this board set-up when responding to troublesome questions in homework and tests as part of whole-class feedback. 
But in recent years, I have changed my board set-up for worked ex¡amples to the following: 
Worked Example 
Thinking 
Your Turn 

As we will see in Tip 64, the middle column is where I write the self¡explanation prompts that support my students in paying attention to the critical aspects of the worked example to further develop their understanding at this early stage.

Consider how your board or book set-up can facilitate understanding of your worked examples.

What's the problem? 
Okay, so we have done the background work, chosen our examples, and set up our board. What does our modelling ofthe worked exam¡ple look like? 
I am obsessed with the worked-example phase of a learning episode. When I am lucky enough to watch a lesson, I always pay close attention when a teacher is modelling -both to what the teacher is doing and what the students are doing. 
Broadly speaking, I see two different ways of modelling worked examples. 
Discussion-heavy 
Here the teacher involves their students lots in the modelling process. They may ask questions like, 'Okay, how should we start?' or 'Who thinks they know what to do next?' There are two issues with this approach: 
1. 
It can take ages -I have seen a teacher spend 20 minutes trying to tease out how to add two fractions with the same denomina¡tor. 

2. 
It can be really confusing-students speculate about things they don't know lots about, and thus lots of wrong ideas circulate around the room. 


Teacher-led 
Here the teacher takes the lead, explaining as clearly and concisely as possible how to solve a particular problem. This issue here is that it can be a passive experience for many students. Some may choose to deliberately opt out of the learning process, keeping their eyes on the teacher but switching their minds off. Others may try to follow what is going on, but with no opportunity to pause, think and re¡flect, they can end up getting little out of it. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What does your worked example process looklike? 

ò 
What challenges do you fa ce? 


What's the tip? 
One of the most popular ideas from How I Wish I'd Taught Maths was Silent Teacher. 
Silent Teacher is exactly what you would imagine it to be. The teacher models a worked example in silence. 
The reason I do this is that I am trying to avoid the split-attention effect. When we ask students to watch what we are doing on the board and listen at the same time -either to our explanation or the responses of their fellow students -we are in danger of overwhelm¡ing the channel of their working memories that processes audio: the phonological loop. When students read text (and this extends to numbers and mathematical symbols), they first process the text as an image, but then essentially read it in their heads as an internal monologue ('factorise x squared plus three multiplied by minus ten'). The phonological loop deals with this internal monologue. But ifwe also add more audio on top ofthat -explanations or student responses -then this also needs to be processed by the phonologi¡cal loop. It is difficult to process two audio streams at once. This is why most people find reading and listening to conversations simul¡taneously very difficult. 
The solution I propose is to split the watching and listening part of the modelling phase into two separate activities, the first of which I call Silent Teacher. 
Now, just before we dive in, I want to address something dear to my heart. The way I explain concepts to my students -ofwhich Silent Teacher is a key part -definitely fits underneath the umbrella of explicit instruction. I am the expert in the room, doing my best to help my students understand via explanation, modelling and ques¡tioning. But it is all too easy to dismiss any approach to teacher-led instruction as lecturing, broadcasting, or simply promoting mimicry in students. As I hope you will see as I describe my approach, beginning with Silent Teacher and progressing through the self¡explanation (Tip 64), We Do (Tip 66) and Your Turn (Tip 67) phases, my students are actively involved in knowledge construction. Both sides ofthe education debate are prone to lazy generalisations. For me, dismissing explicit instruction in this way is the same as sug¡gesting an inquiry approach leaves students fumbling alone help¡lessly in the dark. 
Now I have got that off my chest, here are 14 ideas to help make Silent Teacher effective. 
1. Tell your students what you are doing and why 
This is always a good idea when trying out a new technique, but is particularly important when doing something as weird as suddenly not talking during an example. Explain the challenges of listening and reading simultaneously. 
2. Don't start until you have silence 
It is much easier to start Silent Teacher from a platform of silence, than to hope students slip into a state of silence when you start modelling. Front-loading the means of participation is important here: 'Okay, we are about to do Silent Teacher, so no talking, just watch and think.' 
3. Model by hand If you, like me, are not a neat writer, it is all too tempting to pre¡animate a PowerPoint with the steps of a solution, and then click through in silence. But, as we discussed in Tip 59, starting from a blank canvas and writing out the steps of the solution by hand is key. It helps control the flow of information, and supports you in 
directing students' attention where you need it to be at each stage of the solution. 
4. Gesture 
When we don't have our voice to communicate, we need to use something else. Silent Teacher can be enhanced by carefully planned gestures. This may mean pointing to the part of the so¡lution that you want students to think about at this stage, mov¡ing your hands from side to side to indicate that something has changed, or adjusting your facial expression to indicate confusion and then relief. 
5. Teach students how to behave when doing Silent Teacher This is the big one. For Silent Teacher to be effective, the extra atten¡tion saved from not having to listen or write needs to be redirected to thinking hard. If students take Silent Teacher as an opportunity to have a daydream, then they will learn nothing. So, as well as 
being silent, I teach my students a certain behaviour that allows them to be active participants in the process. 
I tell my students, 'Every time I pause during Silent Teacher and step away from the board (or move my hand away from the visu¡aliser), I want you to ask yourself "what has he just done, and what do I think he will do next?'" 
As we will see in Tip 64, this prompt is designed to enable students to engage in both types of productive self-explanation behaviour: 
ò 
Principled -tries to explain what is going on. 

ò 
Predictive -tries to explain what is going to happen next. 


6. Use Reflect, Expect, Check, Explain 
Telling your students to consider what the teacher has just done and what they think the teacher will do next may be enough to bring about this productive self-explaining behaviour. But, ifyou use my framework of Reflect, Expect, Check, Explain for Intelligent Prac¡tice (Tip 72), you may also wish to apply it to Silent Teacher: 
ò Reflect -what has he just done? 
ò 
Expect -what do I think he will do next? 

ò 
Check -what did he actually do next? 

ò 
Explain -do I understand why? 


7. Provide a cue for self-explaining Whichever framework we use, we can support students to engage in this productive self-explaining behaviour by providing a cue when it is time to think in this way. The most obvious cue is to pause. But we can make it even more explicit by tapping our pen on the crucial part of the solution, and then stepping away from the board, or lifting our hand from the visualiser. Not only does this 
provide a cue to think, but it also gets us out of the way so students can focus their attention on the steps of the solution. 
8. Allow adequate wait time before and after a step 
Our old friend wait time is back again. As we saw in Tip 22 and Tip 23 there are two types of wait time we need to be aware of-wait time between question and answer, and wait time between answer and the evaluation ofthat answer. We need to provide sufficient time for both of these during Silent Teacher. 
Tapping the pen and stepping away from the board provides wait time 1 -what is going to happen next? But then when we write the next line ofthe solution, it is important to pause briefly again so students can reflect on what happened and see if it matches their prediction -what did happen? As ever, it is this second wait time that is usually cut short, so don't be afraid to tap out three seconds after writing the next line before you make your next move. 
9. Ask students to stand during Silent Teacher 
In Tip 56, we discussed how Peter Liljedahl, the author of Building Thinking Classrooms in Mathematics, extols the virtues of having students stand during explanations. Asking students to stand and cluster around the front of the classroom while they watch the silent worked example gets them closer to the action, may break any lethargy from sitting, help them focus more, and make passive day¡dreaming more difficult. If you have a class where behaviour is not an issue, it is certainly worth experimenting with. 
10. Allow students to make notes after Silent Teacher 
I don't want my students writing anything down during Silent Teacher. I want their full attention on me. But after Silent Teacher they are likely to have some questions and observations. It is a good idea to give them a minute to reflect on the example as a whole, and jot these down. Mini-whiteboards work well here. This allows stu¡dents to free up attention for the next stage of the worked example process and gives them a checklist of questions they can tick off as their understanding grows. 
11. Allow paired discussion after Silent Teacher 
Obviously, there is no talking during Silent Teacher. But you may choose to allow students to discuss what they have noticed and what questions they have after Silent Teacher. I would advise al¡lowing them time to write down their observations and questions independently first, then compare notes and see if they can answer each other's questions. Also, I do not take any questions myself at this stage because I know what is coming next and I want to keep the pace and focus high, but I know many teachers who do. 
12. Break up Silent Teacher 
For some short procedures -adding two fractions, expanding a pair ofbrackets -it is fine to do the process from start to finish in silence. But for some more complex procedures -solving simultaneous equations, tricky formulae to rearrange -you may choose to break it up a bit. So, do a line or two in silence, and then ask students questions about what you have done. We will discuss what makes an effective question to ask in Tip 64. 
13. Don't feel you have to do Silent Teacher for everything 
I find Silent Teacher really powerful, but I won't do it for everything. I tend to use it for the first example of a new idea, but for subse¡quent examples, I may choose to switch between short bursts of si¡lence and then questions, or studying completed worked examples. Likewise, I have taught several classes that -for many different rea¡sons -just didn't take to it. Find out what works best for you. 
14. Be ready for some funny looks 
Even ifyou have prepared your students, even ifyou have prepared yourself, Silent Teacher feels really weird the first few times you do it. So be ready.
