However we choose to model our worked example, our students may still miss critical features. After all, at this stage of a learning episode, concepts are new, so knowing what to attend to and think about is tough. Any extra help we can give them to support their understanding will be welcome. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What sorts of questions do you ask your studentsduring or after modelling? 

ò
What do you know about self-explanation prompts? 


What's the tip? 
Step forward self-explanation prompts. 
A substantial body of research suggests that a certain way of think¡ing -self-explaining -helps support students' understanding and sub¡sequent application of complex ideas. 
Self-explanation: the theory 
I hope you will forgive the extra theory in this tip, but I feel it's necessary because self-explanation is becoming yet another lethal mutation, and I want to make clear exactly what the research says (or, at least, my interpretation of it). 
What does it mean to self-explain? 
Chi (2000) describes self-explaining as 'a knowledge-building activ¡ity that is generated by and directed to oneself'. It is distinct from explaining to your partner or to the teacher. 
Salle (2020) expands on this: 'Self-explaining means explaining something to oneselfby generating information not provided in the given material and by creating inferences to organise given or new information in order to make sense of the material.' 
What happens ifstudents self-explain? 
Plenty of good stuff. Berthold and Renkl (2009) found that self¡explanation can deepen students' understanding of the concepts they are trying to learn. Richey and Nokes-Malach (2015) found that self-explaining can draw attention to the errors students make while solving the problem so that they can correct them. And ifthat wasn't enough, Lombrozo (2006) finds that one of the main benefits of self-explanation is that it can increase students' ability to trans¡fer the knowledge and skills learned with one concept to different situations that call for the same knowledge. 
To summarise, the two key benefits of self-explaining are: 
1. 
It helps students better understand the current problem. 

2. 
It makes it more likely that students will be able to transfer what they learn to solve related problems. 


Do students self-explain naturally? 
Bad news, I am afraid. Renkl (1997) explains that 'the majority of learners do not spontaneously engage in successful self-explana¡tion strategies'. 
Do some students self-explain naturally? 
Yes, and I bet you can guess who. Chi et al. (1989} explain that 'high¡ability students tend to spontaneously self-explain more often than low-ability students'. 
Correlation or causation? 
Is self-explaining just a characteristic of having more background knowledge? If so, then we probably cannot teach it to our students. 
Good news! Nathan et al. (1994) explain that 'since induced self¡explanation enhances performance, it is likely that the self-expla¡nation process somehow mediates the learning of good problem solvers, rather than simply being a side-effect of good problem¡solving'. 
Furthermore, 'since students can be taught to apply these methods, learning deficits in many domains may be addressed by a very ac¡cessible instructional intervention'. 
Self-explanation prompts 
So, how do we teach our students to self-explain? This is where self¡explanation prompts come into play -in other words, nudges to help our students engage in this productive way of reflecting and thinking. 
On Ollie Lovell's outstanding ERRR podcast, psychologist and researcher Alexander Renkl described two types of self-explaining behaviour: 
1. 
Principled -tries to explain what is going on 

2. 
Predictive -tries to explain what is going to happen next. 


In this tip we are going to focus on principled self-explanation prompts -in other words, following an action we have taken during an example, can students explain why we have done it? We have already seen the other type -predictive self-explanation prompts ¡when we looked at using Silent Teacher when modelling in Tip 63. 
But not all self-explanation prompts are created equally. The key, Renkl explains, is to connect the case (the specific example) to the general principle. That way students can transfer what they notice to other problems instead of the insight being anchored to the spe¡cific example they are studying. 
Let me show you what I mean. 
Imagine this is my example: 
X
--I= 10 
2 +I ( ) +I X
-11 
2 )(2 ( ) )(2 
X = 22 
A principled self-explanation prompt could be, 'Why did we add 1 to both sides of the equation?' 
That certainly focuses students' attention on that critical first step. But it is specific to this example. What happens if the next example students study requires them to add 5 to both sides ofthe equation? Or subtract? Or square root? 
A better self-explanation prompt is, 'How do we know which opera¡tion to do first?' 
Students' attention is still focused on the same place, but now their thinking is more general, and hence more likely to transfer successfully. 
A good question to ask yourself when writing a self-explanation prompt is, 'If the numbers completely changed, would the prompt still work?' 
More examples 
Here are some other principled self-explanation prompts I have used in my maths lessons: 
ò 
Mean from a list of numbers -how do we know what to divide by? 

ò 
Adding fractions -how do we know which denominators to use? 

ò 
Area -how do we know which units to use? 

ò 
Probability -what value must our answer be less than and why? 

ò 
Factorising expressions -how do we know if our answer is in the correct form? 


Notice how each of these is not tied to a specific example. 
Are there any downsides to self-explanation prompts? 
Yes, and it is quite an interesting one. Rittle-Johnson et al. (2017) point out that while self-explanation prompts focus attention on particular aspects of the to-be-learned material, the prompts draw students' attention away from other information. If that informa¡tion is pertinent to the understanding of the concept, students may miss it. They conclude 'self-explanation activities must be designed to carefully balance attention to all of the important content'. 
So, we always need to ask ourselves, 'What is the intended object oflearning?' In other words, what do we want our students to be focusing on at this specific stage in the example? And we must de¡sign our self-explanation prompt accordingly. 
Tips for writing good principled self-explanation prompts 
Here are a few guidelines that have helped me: 
1. 
Decide what the intended object oflearning at this stage of the example is. 

2. 
Always try to connect the specific case to the general. 

3. 
Three or four prompts per example (or pair ofrelated examples ¡Tip 58) is a good amount. 

4. 
Good generic prompts to include are: 

. 
Is this example in the correct form? 

. 
What is the same and what is different about this example? 




Self-explanation prompts: in practice 
As we discussed in Tip 62, I set my board or teacher book up so there are three columns. The self-explanation prompts go in the 
middle: 
Worked Example 
Write 24 and 72 as a product of their prime factors 
24 
6 / ~ 4
I\ I\ 
0 0 0 0 
24= 2 X 2X 2x 3 
72 
9 / ~ 8
I\ I\ 
0 0 0
I\ 4 
0 0 
72 = 2x2x 2x3x3 
Three key things here: 
Thinking 
Your Turn 
Why are the numbers not in the correct form? 
What pairs of numbers can we choose for the first row? 
Which types of numbers do we circle? 
What order do we write our answer in? 
What is the relationship between the two answers? 
1. 
I verbalise each prompt and write it down so the prompt is not transient -my writing is terrible, so typing the prompts out in advance and animating works for me if I am doing the example at the board. 

2. 
I line each prompt up with the relevant line ofworking out ¡this helps students better understand the worked example, but also aids transfer when students attempt the related Your Turn problem. 

3. 
I give students time to think about their answers in silence be¡fore proceeding. 


When to ask self-explanation prompts 
Here are the two times I use self-explanation prompts: 
1. 
All the way. I go through the worked example from start to finish using Silent Teacher (Tip 63). After giving my students time to reflect upon the example as a whole, I begin to ask them the self-explanation prompts one at a time. 

2. 
Break it up. I do one line of a worked example in silence, pause, and then ask students the relevant self-explanation prompt. I will break it up for more complex procedures, and with classes where periods of sustained silent focus have not been embed¡ded yet. 


Collecting student responses to the self-explanation prompts 
The usual means of participation from Chapter 3 are available: 
ò 
Cold Call 

ò 
Mini-whiteboards 

ò 
Write in exercise books 

ò 
Paired discussion 

ò 
Or any combination of the above. 


One option I often use is to give students a chance to think in silence, and then provide the explanation myself without eliciting an answer from them first. This may sound crazy, but it is quicker, makes use of the fact that self-explaining is an internal process, and avoids the potential for confusion to spread. This approach delays the check for understanding until the subsequent Your Turn ques¡tion, and I know for many teachers the idea of asking a question and not eliciting a response is a step too far. 
Explaining the answer 
Whenever and however we choose to ask a self-explanation prompt, it is important that we end the process with a clear, concise answer, and then pause to allow students to reflect on it. Self-ex¡plaining only works if students have something correct to compare against, and then time to make that comparison. As described in Tip 45, teacher explanations are likely to be improved ifyou have written your responses to the self-explanation prompts before the lesson, and rehearsed those responses. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Use goodself-explanation prompts to help your students think more deeply about an example.

What's the problem? 
Students copying down a worked example is an integral part of most maths lessons. But it is rarely effective. Here are five issues I often see. 
1. Asking students to copy a worked example while you model and explain means they are less likely to understand 
Right, let's deal with the big one at the outset: it is definitely a bad idea to ask students to watch, listen and copy simultaneously. 
It is so obvious, right? And yet, that is exactly how my worked examples would play out for the first 12 years of my career. As I was modelling at the front, I would be expecting my students to be watching me, thinking hard about my questions and copying down what was on the board. The result would be a sea of heads down at the very point I wanted them watching, or students reading off the board at the point I wanted them listening. 
Whether you label this as the split-attention effect or just common sense, this is clearly wrong. Was it any wonder my students strug¡gled when it came to practising? 
2. Copying worked examples can take ages 
Let's say, on average, a particular example takes 90 seconds to copy down -this is often the case when there is a diagram involved or lots of explanatory notes. Not too bad, you may think. But those 90 seconds add up, and that time has an opportunity cost -it is time that could be spent discussing, self-explaining, or practising. In other words, doing things far more conducive to learning. 
And of course, not all students will take 90 seconds to copy down the worked example. Some students will finish before others and be sat there with nothing to do. Some students will take an age, and potentially not finish the copying in the time permitted by the teacher. I have spent much time in lessons staring pleadingly at a child to go a bit quicker writing that sentence or finishing that dia¡gram so we can crack on. In the end, I give up and say something like, 'Okay, Jake, you can copy the rest down later' -as if that time ever comes. 
The time a student takes to copy down may not be due to laziness, meticulousness or messing around, but because of special needs. Indeed, the advice given by many learning support departments in schools is that copying down should be avoided where possible. 
3. Students often make mistakes or lack accuracy when copying worked examples 
It is very easy to make a mistake when copying from the board, particularly if a student is rushing to get everything down in time. There is also the issue of accuracy, especially where diagrams are concerned. I remember watching a group ofYear 8s trying to copy down an example about pie charts. Those that tried to draw it ac¡curately with a compass took ages and didn't finish in time. Those that went freehand ended up with a scruffy assortment of egg shapes with segments of different sizes. Mistakes and inaccuracies could clearly cause problems if students refer back to these exam¡ples for support in the future. 
4. Worked examples often don't make much sense when students look back at them 
Worked examples in maths rarely progress top to bottom, left to right. Think about filling in a two-way table -sometimes we will need to start by working out a number in the bottom-right-hand corner. Or doing short division -we progress left to right, but also from bottom to top as we fill in remainders and digits of the solu¡tion. Students may follow this fine in the moment as the teacher demonstrates, but how about when they look back at this same static worked example in the future? Can they really remember the order of the steps they used to solve it? Where on earth did that 5 come from? Indeed, if students copy the solution after the teacher has demonstrated, they may well copy the solution in a different order than it was presented, thus reinforcing this confusion. 
5. The myth ofcopying down This is the big one that had me fooled for years, and was the reason I insisted my students copied down all worked examples and notes 
in full, no matter how time-consuming: I believed students learned from copying down. 
They don't. Nothing magical happens when students copy down worked examples and notes. 
Having notes is not the same as having knowledge. 
A good illustration of the myth of copying down is those students who consistently produce beautiful notes all year -both in class and when revising-and then mysteriously bomb out in their exams. Copying down does not equate to understanding. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
When do your students copydown worked examples? 

ò 
Do youagree with any of the concerns raisedabove? 


What's the tip? 
Here are six ideas to improve the copying down phase of an example. 
1. Whatever you ask students to write, ask them to do it after you have finished modelling 
In Tip 63 and Tip 64 we discussed how I now split up the modelling and questioning phases of my worked examples. So, it is no sur¡prise that I also do the same with the copying phase. I ask my stu¡dents to copy down the worked example after Silent Teacher and the self-explanation prompts, or if I am breaking up Silent Teacher and self-explaining, I will ask them to copy down each line of working after we have considered it. In either case, this copying is done in silence after students have had an opportunity to think about what they are writing down. 
2. Print out some ofthe worked example 
If it is a fairly quick example, then there is no need to do this. But if the example involves a diagram or lots of text to copy out, then printing that part of the example might make sense. The key is to consider how long it will take your students who write more slowly to copy down diagrams or text. If it is longer than it will take for you to hand out part of the example and your students to stick it in their books, then it is worth considering. 
Let me dig into diagrams a little deeper. If an example requires stu¡dents to sketch a diagram, then they should sketch it because this is a key skill that needs developing. Likewise, if an example requires students to construct a diagram accurately (with a compass, pro¡tractor, etc.), again they should do so. I'm talking about examples where a diagram is provided that students use to solve the problem. If they would be given the diagram in an exam question, then there is no point in them copying it out in lessons. 
3. Ask students to answer the self-explanation prompts It is no good having a correct example -no matter how neat-if stu¡dents do not understand what it means. Hence, during the copying 
phase, I try to create the opportunity for students to personalise the example so it makes sense to them. 
I've found the easiest way to do this is to ask students to write their answers to the self-explanation prompts (Tip 64) around the exam¡ple as annotations, or below the worked example as notes. Students will already have had a chance to think about these answers during the discussion, so the challenge here is to put their answers in their own words so the example makes more sense to them, both now and when they revisit the answer in the future. Further discussing these answers with their partner at this stage is useful. 
4. Ask students to write notes for their forgetful future self 
I love the phrase 'forgetful future self'. It comes from Peter Liljedahl in Building Thinking Classrooms in Mathematics. The idea is we challenge students to write useful notes that their future self, trying to recall this topic at some point in the future, would understand. This might involve answering the self-explanation prompts -but only the ones they think they will forget. Or it might mean numbering the steps of a solution, or writing down a phrase the teacher or another stu¡dent says that they find useful. Again, these notes could be made as annotations around or below a worked example. This approach is all about giving students autonomy and ownership over their notes. They will likely find it difficult to know what to write at first, but paired discussions and showcasing of excellence can help. 
5. Ask students to look away and write 

Instead of asking students to copy a worked example or make notes straight away, we can first challenge them to reproduce the solution steps from memory. We can hide these steps on the board or simply ask students to look down at their books and see what they can remember. This forces students to be more actively involved in the process, and anything they forget could be the focus of their notes. 
6. Don't bother with any notes at all! 
Let's end with a controversial idea. Quite a few of the highest-per¡forming schools I visit do not bother asking students to copy worked examples or make notes. They use the time saved to provide more opportunities for students to practise the ideas themselves. 
These schools also tend not to bother about how neat students' books are or worry about evidencing classwork, replacing much work in books with work on mini-whiteboards. In these cases, the worked example can be left on the board for reference while stu¡dents are practising. 
While this may seem ridiculous, it's worth asking yourself why copying down examples and doing work in exercise books is impor¡tant. If the answer is so that students have a resource to use when they revise in the future, then are you sure your students actually do that? 
And two more questions while we are at it. If tomorrow you sud¡denly stopped asking students to copy down worked examples, would they suffer? And what would you do with the extra lesson time? 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Make the copying down part ofan example separate from following the example, and take steps to make it as worthwhile as possible.

What's the problem? 
I have always been an I Do, then You Do kind of guy. Specifically, when teaching students a new process, I start by modelling an ex¡ample myself using the Silent Teacher (Tip 63) and discussing self¡explanation prompts (Tip 64). Then I challenge my students to try a similar problem on their own -what I refer to as Your Turn. 
Most of the time this has served me well. But there have been enough occasions when my students' understanding was not where I hoped it would be during the practice phase of a learning episode to suggest I might need to modify this approach. 
Many readers will be familiar with the I Do, We Do, You Do approach to modelling. That middle step (the We Do) is designed to be the transition between modelling and independent practice. On Ollie Lovell's excellent ERRR podcast, Anita Archer, the originator of the phrase 'I Do, We Do, You Do', explained: 
'Once we have modelled the expert's thinking process, our 
goal is to then step students through the expert's thinking 
process. It is usually too big of a jump from seeing an ex¡
pert perform to performing like an expert oneself. This is 
where We Do comes in.' 
Ollie asked Anita what the biggest mistake a teacher can make when modelling in this way is. Anita quickly replied, 'Skipping the We Do!' 
Gulp. 
Indeed, when I reflected on my own practice, it was the more complex, multistep processes that students struggled to grasp from a single modelled example. When expanding a single bracket, it seemed fine to jump from I Do to You Do. But when solving a pair of simultaneous equations, or adding fractions involving mixed num¡bers, that jump may be too large, and an interim step is needed. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you regularly do aWe Do? 

ò 
Ifso, what does it look like? 


What's the tip? 
Once we have decided that a We Do is needed, the next question is 'what does it look like?' 
Here we again return to the idea of the means of participation (Chapter 3). When I am modelling using Silent Teacher, my students are silent and focused. During the self-explaining, I may use a com¡bination of Partner Talk and Cold Call. As we will see in Tip 67, the Your Turn part of the process involves independent work. 
During the We Do there is an opportunity to use an even greater breadth of tools to ensure as many students as possible are partic¡ipating, we get a good sense of the group's understanding, and the process is pacey and engaging. 
An example ofWe Do in my classroom 
Let's pick up the prime factorisation example from Tip 64. Following Silent Teacher, self-explaining, and some form of writing up the ex¡ample, I say to students: 
Teacher:  Okay, so let's do an example together. I only want you to respond when I tell you and in the way I tell you. The question is: write 60 as a product of prime factors ... (I write this ques¡tion on the board) ... Choral response ¡is 60 in the correct form? ... (wait) ... three, two, one...  
Students:  No!  
Teacher:  No hands, no talking ... why is 60 not in the correct form? ... (wait) ... Tom?  

Tom: Because 60 is not a prime number. Teacher: (wait) ... Good. Split your mini-whiteboards into three columns like this... (teacher demonstrates on their board) ... Okay, so I am going to draw two lines coming from 60. On your mini¡whiteboards, write down three pairs of numbers that I could use at the end of those lines, one pair in each column... (wait) ... hover your boards when you are ready ... (wait) ... okay, three, two, one, show me your boards. Teacher: I can see a few examples of 60 and 1. Discuss with your part¡ner why this is not a good pair of numbers to use... (wait) ... Heena, you used 60 and 1. Do you now know why should we not use 60 and 1? Heena: Because this does not get us anywhere, we still need to deal with 60. Teacher: (wait) ... Good. Okay, here are some pairs of numbers I could use (teacher writes them on the board): 30 and 2 20 and 3 15 and 4 12 and 5. I am going to choose to use 15 and 4. I'm going to choose someone to explain clearly why I can use 15 and 4 ... (wait) ... 
Cal? Cal: I don't know. Teacher: Okay, don't worry, keep listening, I will be back. Holly, why 
can I use 15 and 4? Holly: 15 and 4 are factors. Teacher: Full sentence please. Holly: Because 15 and 4 are both factors of 60. Teacher: (wait) ... And the two factors of 60 we choose must multiply ... Holly: To give 60. Teacher: (wait) ... Cal, why could I also choose 3 and 20? Cal: Because 3 and 20 are factors of60 and they multiply together 
to give 60. Teacher: And why could we not choose 30 and 6? Cal: They are both factors of 60, but they do not multiply to give 60. Teacher: (wait) ... Good, you've got it. Okay, so I am going to write 15 
here and 4 here. Let's start with 15. Choral response: should I put a circle around 15? ... (wait) ... three, two, one... Students: No! Teacher: No hands, no talking. Why? ... (wait) ... Jenny? Jenny: 15 is not a prime number. Teacher: (wait) ... What do you think ofJenny's answer... Leo? Leo: I agree. Teacher: Why? Leo: Because 15 does not have exactly two factors. Teacher: (wait) ... Nice definition. Okay, so in silence on your mini¡
whiteboards, clean the board and then copy what we have done so far. Then see ifyou can finish the factor tree, circling any prime numbers ... (wait) ... hover your boards when you are ready ... (wait) ... okay, three, two, one, show me your boards. 
Teacher:  Okay, listen carefully and don't do anything until I have finished. Swap your mini-whiteboards with the person next to you. First I want you to check their work, and ifyou are happy with it I want you to finish off the question on their boards by writing 60 as a product of prime factors ... Vanessa, what do I want you to do? ...  
Vanessa:  Check each other's work and then if we are happy, finish off the question on their boards.  
Teacher:  Yep... three, two, one, swap boards.  

At this stage, I would walk around the class with the purpose of checking the final answer given on students' boards (see Tip 7 4 for more on observing students' work with purpose). Any issues could be dealt with one-to-one, or if I spot a trend then I can discuss this with the whole class by sharing students' work (Tip 40). 
You may well notice various tips from earlier in the book within this interaction. Maybe sit down with a colleague and see who can spot the most, and consider how you could improve my practice. Who says teachers don't know how to have a good time? 
We Do depends on the group 
It is not just complex, multistep methods that are suited to the inclusion of a We Do. What seems relatively straightforward to one student may be complex to another. Something that appears as a single step to one student, such as multiplying the term outside a bracket by the first term inside the bracket, is actually several steps to a student who has not yet automated knowledge such as: 
ò 
What is a term? 

ò 
Which operation do I use? 

ò 
Will the answer be positive or negative? 

ò 
How do I combine numbers and letters? 


Of course, this is the very reason we need to deal with relevant prior knowledge before we embark upon a worked example, as discussed in Chapter 7. But even then, our knowledge of our students may suggest that an interim step -the We Do -is needed to firm up their knowledge and confidence before the independent practice starts. 
Where do you write the We Do? 
Eagle-eyed readers may have noticed I only have three columns on my board set up for examples: 'Worked Example', 'Thinking' and 'Your Turn'. So, where does the We Do go? One option is to add an extra column, but I find this does not leave enough room for longer examples. My favoured option is to do the We Do in the Your Turn column and then simply rub it out afterwards. 
And, just like with the Worked Example and Your Turn, I try to line up each line ofworking out with the relevant self-explanation prompt to encourage transfer. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider varying the means of participation for aWe Do to provide an engaging, fast-paced experience that bridges the gap between modelling and independent practice.

Tip 67 -Three errors to avoid with the Your Turn questions 

What's the problem? 
I have made a few errors with the first piece ofindependent practice my students take on following a worked example -what I call the Your Turn: 
1. 
Jumped into the Your Turn too quickly. 

2. 
Changed the question too much from the example so students could not see the link. 

3. 
Made the question too similar, so students didn't have to think. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you determine what thefirst question of independent practice looks like? 

ò 
What challenges do you face? 


What's the tip? 
Here is how I now deal with each of these issues. 
1. Jumped into the Your Turn too quickly 
This can be tackled by the inclusion of a We Do as described in Tip 
66. The We Do can bridge the gap between modelling and practice, and be presented in a fast-paced, engaging way. 
2. Changed the question too much from the example so students could not see the link 
To resolve this issue we need to remember that the purpose of the Your Turn is not intended to stretch students. It is there to ensure students can replicate what they have observed the teacher doing during the worked example. It is the first step on a long road to un¡derstanding, and if we try to change too much -increase the diffi¡culty, change the context, etc. -then we risk students hurtling along this path and potentially falling off. 
3. Made the question too similar, so students didn't have to think This issue is perhaps the most challenging. While we don't want to push the students too hard at this early stage of their experience with an idea, we also want the process to be useful. If the Your Turn is -in the case of maths -simply a case of changing the numbers 
but doing everything else the same, then an opportunity to think and notice has been squandered. 
So, here is what I do. I keep the context, style and difficulty ofthe question exactly the same as the example (and We Do ifwe have done one). But I change the order the intended object oflearning is presented in. 
To see what I mean, let's consider the pair of equations from Tip 58: 
WORKED EX,4tv1PL..E

If you remember, the reason I had varied these examples in this way was that the intended object oflearning was how the structure of the equation impacts the order of the inverse operations we need to perform on each side of the equations in order to isolate the variable. 
Here are the Your Turn questions I would give: 
WORKED EXA-fvlPL-E

Notice that the difficulty is very similar, and there is no additional context. But also notice how changing the order of the Your Turn now means students have to think hard about the intended object oflearning. Take the first Your Turn question. If students just plug in the new numbers but use the same order of operations as the first worked example, then they will be wrong. It is a sign they are not thinking or noticing what I want them to think and notice. 
Changing the order in this way will not always be possible. Also, students are canny -ifyou always swap the order then students may realise that they just do things the other way around for the Your Turn. But I think it is worth considering if it is possible to keep students thinking hard about the intended object oflearning when they do that first piece ofindependent practice. 
Answering and assessing the Your Turn 
My students attempt the Your Turn in silence and on their own. Usually, they answer it on mini-whiteboards. The exception is if a complex or accurate diagram or graph is needed, in which case I print the Your Turn out, but still ask students to write their final an¡swers on their mini-whiteboards so I can see them more easily as I walk around the room and ask students to share their work later on. 
While students are working, I walk around the room. I know in advance exactly what I am looking for (Tip 7 4) -this may be the final answer or how students are setting their work out. I go to my strongest students first as they are likely to have made more progress and hence give me more information to consider. 
When the majority of students are finished (I can tell as they are hovering their mini-whiteboards), I ask them to show me their answers. If there is a spread of answers, I do not confirm the right answer at this stage. Instead, I ask them to swap work with their partners and check if their answers and layout are the same. If they are different, students can try to figure out who is correct. Ifthey are the same, students are asked to decide the best way to explain how to do this question. 
Following the paired discussion, I like to Warm Call (ask certain students based on their work I have seen when walking around the room or when they showed their mini-whiteboards) a student whose work I think is worth sharing. This may be because it is correct and well set out or because there is an interesting error that is important to address with the rest of the class. I can also borrow their mini-whiteboard and take a photo, place it under the visu¡aliser or hold it up to make class discussion easier. We discussed sharing students' work in Tip 40. 
Your Turn always ends with me explaining the correct answer and asking if any students are still confused, before we move on to the next phase of the lesson.

THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Think carefully about the challenge and purpose of the first independent practice you give students.

Tip 68 -Reflect after a worked example

What's the problem? 
In the past, I have gone straight from the Your Turn part of a worked example (Tip 67) into some independent practice. But just like the leap from the worked example to Your Turn is often too great and needs the We Do as a bridge, I think the same is true with the leap from Your Turn to further practice. 
Often, students who appear to have been following everything that has happened, and who can successfully complete the closely related problems you have given them as part of Your Turn, all of a sudden struggle when faced with problems that increase in diffi¡culty or require them to do something slightly different. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do your students sometimes struggle between the Your Turn part of aworked example and inde¡pendent practice? 

ò 
What do you do to ease that transition? 


What's the tip? 
A second explanation can really help. This is the final part ofthe Wood-Trees-Wood approach advocated by Adam Boxer. You have explained the concept (Tip 56) and taken a deep dive into the nitty¡gritty of a worked example, and now it is the time to zoom back out again and reflect on what has happened. This does not need to be a lengthy process, but this opportunity to reflect might just connect a few dots and enable students to be much more successful with the practice that follows. 
Here are a few ideas to help with this second explanation. 
1. Go through the process as a whole, quickly 
You will have gone through the worked example slowly, so students could focus on each of the individual steps. Now is an opportunity to revisit the process from start to finish as a single flow, so stu¡dents can see how it all fits together. You might want to do this with the worked example, but I find it works particularly well ifyou can revisit the concrete example you used in the explanation of the con¡cept. Now we have this new tool up our sleeves, we can solve the problem much more efficiently. This helps fuse the concrete and the abstract, and connect the familiar and unfamiliar. 
2. Show and alternate methods or representations 
One of the beauties of maths is that there is often more than one way to solve a problem. You have focused on one method or repre¡
sentation during your worked example, but now is an opportunity to show students an alternate method or a different representation. 
So, if teaching to halve, you might show them different ways ques¡tions could be posed: 
ò What is half of 208? ò 208 + 2 
ò 208 
2 
ò ~ X 208 
ò 208 X 0.5 
Or, following a worked example to calculate the highest common factor using prime factor decomposition and Venn diagrams, you might take the opportunity to show students some alternate meth¡ods,2 such as: 
ò 
Listing the factors 

ò 
Successive division 

ò 
Table method 

ò 
Euclidean algorithm by subtraction 

ò 
Using a calculator. 


The key here is to focus students' attention on the similarities and the differences of each method. Using the alternate method or rep¡resentation to solve the same problem as the worked example, and challenging students to reflect on what is the same and what is dif¡ferent works well here. Students can then attempt to try the same approach but with the Your Turn question. Finally, they can be challenged to generate examples where each approach is the most efficient. 
3. Ifappropriate, explain the 'why' 
Now students are more familiar with the procedure, it may be appropriate to explain why it works. This could be a formal proof or a reasoned generalisation. My usual caveat applies -if the 'why' is significantly more difficult than carrying out the procedure, we may want to wait until students have built up more fluency and confi¡dence. The 'why' in maths is an important and special moment, and needs to be carefully planned and timed. 
4. Do a final check for understanding 
Students are about to launch into independent practice, so now is a good time for a final check of understanding. My regular go-to was the classic 'does anybody have any questions?' This was invariably met by a wall of silence that I took to be a signal for understanding. 
Now I realise the need for a more reliable check. Better alternatives here are: 
ò 
'Tell me what you understand about... ' 

ò 
'Ask me a question about... ' 


Students could respond to these on mini-whiteboards, followed by a paired discussion, followed by Cold Calling some pairs. The holy trinity strikes again. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Plan your second explanation as abridge between guided practice and independent practice.

What's the problem? 
At each point in a lesson, we want our students to give as much attention to the content of that lesson as possible to give them the best chance of understanding and learning. During the modelling phase, students may need extra support in this area. The concept that we are teaching is likely to be new to them. There is a lot of information they need to take in. It may not be clear to students where they are supposed to focus their attention. And, of course, students' attention is limited. 
Techniques discussed in this chapter, such as using carefully related examples (Tip 58) and self-explanation prompts (Tip 64), or splitting the watching from the listening (Tip 63), should help in this regard. But there is something even simpler that teachers can do. And that is to beware the seductive, redundant details that often creep their way onto PowerPoints, whiteboards and worksheets that have the potential to divert students' limited attention away from the lesson content towards something that will not help them in any way. 
In one of the best-named research papers I have come across ¡'Keep it coherent: A meta-analysis ofthe seductive details effect' ¡researchers Sundararajan and Adesope found that including seduc¡tive details (defined as 'interesting and irrelevant information') in learning material can hinder learning. 
What on earth might these seductive details be? 
Well, the obvious ones are images or GIFs of interesting but irrele¡vant things that we might be tempted to include in a PowerPoint to engage or amuse our learners. The researchers explain that 'even if seductive details positively influenced students' interest and emo¡tions (which we don't know), student learning measured by tests immediately after that intervention was hurt'. I would speculate that the same might apply to the 'amusing' contexts, often includ¡ing the names of students in the class, that I would often include in questions to try to spice things up a bit: 'Amy buys 53 Sam Fender stickers ... ' 
But there are others. 
ò 
Emails or SIMS notices popping up on the screen (often accom¡panied by an annoying bing} midway through an explanation. 

ò 
Jake Gordon described how when teachers use OneNote or go through an exam paper in PDF format, there are often annoying toolbars at the top of the screen, or menus along the side. 

ò 
And then there are subtle ones like PowerPoint slides that use a template that always shows things like the school crest, lesson objectives, key words, and the date. Or whiteboards that always have these things visible throughout the lesson. 


Not only do all these things take up valuable space that could be used for writing bigger and clearer, but they are also redundant details the students need to actively ignore. Do we really want stu¡dents thinking about or effortfully ignoring the date and title when solving a percentage problem? 
Lesson objectives are a regular source of contention between teach¡ers and school leadership teams. For what it is worth, when they are written in a way that students can actually understand, they can be useful in order to signpost where the lesson is going, or reflect upon where it has been. But I see little point in asking students to copy them into books unless we have a really good plan of how they are going to use those objectives to improve their learning. However, that is a separate point. The question for now is this: do we really want lesson objectives visible all the time? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you think of examples of seductive details you might have visible during your teaching? 

ò 
Which ones couldyou remove? 


What's the tip? 
A general principle I try to stick to when evaluating my learning materials is, 'What is visible is what I want students to focus on and think about.' 
And the converse: 'If it is not relevant, remove it.' 
So, the irrelevant images and GIFs have gone. I would be lying if I said I have removed all the 'amusing' contexts, as a few sneak in there when I know they might give the students a boost. As Sarah Cottingham points out, we should treat research as a compass, not a prescription, and you know your students better than anyone. But I certainly use them less than I used to. 
I keep my PowerPoint slides or my whiteboard as clear as possible so what is visible is relevant. When going through an exam paper in PDF format, I snip the PDF up and paste the questions into Pow¡erPoint slides so I can remove the redundant toolbars and menus. I close down my email programme during lessons to avoid any un¡necessary interruptions. 
And with lesson objectives and key words? Well, here is the irony. If they are visible all the time, on every slide or always visible on the whiteboard, then they become irrelevant. Whereas ifwe use them sparingly when they are relevant, students pay more atten¡tion to them. So, I now mention lesson objectives at the start of the lesson to show students the journey we are going on. And then I hide them. But then, when we have solved a problem later on in the lesson, I can reveal them and match the problem up to the relevant learning objective to show students the progress they are making. Doing this at the end ofthe lesson works well also -matching up some of the problems we have tackled to each of the objectives ¡and is a far more effective plenary than 'summarise what you have learned this lesson in a tweet' that I clung onto for a few years. I do the same with key words -only showing them when they are relevant. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Look over your learning materials for interesting but irrelevant details and consider removing them.

CHAPTER9 
STUDENT PRACTICE 
How can we help periods ofindependent practice be as effective as possible? This chapter starts with some general tips to help all practice sessions run as smoothly as possible. Then we discuss dif¡ferent types of student practice, including what interleaving really is, Intelligent Practice and no-number questions. Finally, we take a look at what we can do while our students are practising, above and beyond the casual meandering around the class I used to do.

Tip 70 -Eight ideas to improve student practice time

What's the problem? 
Here are a few scenarios I have experienced when giving students practice: 
1. 
Students complete the practice with their partner or group, and while they get the questions correct, neither I nor they have any idea what they have understood individually. 

2. 
I give my students a set of instructions. The room is quiet. But then, as soon as I tell students to start the practice, a few hands go up. As I go over to answer those questions, the volume in the room creeps up and the focus dwindles. 

3. 
My students are completing their practice in their books. As I circulate the room, I find it hard to check their work without disturbing them by asking them to turn over the page or move their arm. Likewise, the paired discussion is a bit ragged with students struggling to find matching answers to compare. 

4. 
Quite a few students are getting stuck as they complete the practice. I am doing my best to get around them all, but some students who have been waiting a while start to lose focus and behaviour becomes an issue. Even those who are quietly sitting there with their hands raised are not learning a great deal in that moment. 

5. 
My students have all been working on a set ofpractice questions in their books. I now want to assess their understanding, so I Cold Call Tom and ask, 'What did you get for question 1, Tom?' Tom tells me, and I wish I had a way of knowing what everyone else got. 

6. 
My students have completed 10 minutes ofpractice. Now it is time to go through the answers. It turns out many of them got question 1 wrong, and as a result, they also got most of the other questions wrong. 

7. 
I have a new class and I set them 20 minutes of independent practice. I spend most of that time telling individual students to 

keep focusing, stop messing around, get back to work, and so on. 

8. 
My students are working silently and diligently through the practice questions. So I say, 'Well done everyone, this is great!' or 'Remember what to do ifyou are stuck on a question' or '10 minutes left everybody, keep working hard.' The volume level starts to creep up. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to any of these? 

ò
How can we improve things? 


What's the tip? 
Student practice comes in many guises. But no matter what it looks like, there are a few things we can do to give student practice the best chance ofbeing as productive as possible. 
Here are eight ideas. 
1. Always start practice with a period ofsilent, individual work 
There is a classroom management reason for this that we will discuss next. But here I want to focus on the purpose ofpractice. We want our students to develop confidence, fluency, and understand¡ing, but also have that practice serve as a check for understanding. If students complete all their practice collaboratively, we are left requiring an additional check for understanding to determine what they have understood individually. 
Back in Tip 15, we discussed the 4-2 approach -four minutes of silent, independent work, followed by two minutes of paired dis¡cussion. I think this strikes the right balance. Four minutes is long enough to see what you can do on your own (but not too long ifyou get stuck or make a mistake that feeds though), and two minutes is long enough for you to have a discussion with your partner where you can check each other's work and try to sort out any problems (but not so long that the discussion veers off task). 
2. Wait for Golden Silence 
This is another idea from science teacher, Adam Boxer. When Adam sets his students off on a task, he refuses to take any questions for the first minute or so. If students are stuck, they don't raise their hands or ask anyone, they just try to figure it out. If their pen isn't working, they don't have a sheet, or they have forgotten what to do, they keep quiet about it. Why? Because Adam is waiting for Golden Silence: 'If you give the students a bit more time to work in silence, they will be in that place of absorption in the task. They will become engrossed and focussed, and less liable to distrac¡tion. At that point, the silence is strong and robust, and you can give it a little push and it won't buckle. I call this kind of silence Golden Silence -when students are quiet and en¡grossed, and small distractions won't throw them.' 
Once you sense your students are absorbed in a task, you can then quietly go over to students who have a question and deal with it without breaking the focus that exists in the room. 
In a video entitled 'Setting Students up to Succeed', Adam takes this approach one stage further by utilising what he calls 3-30-30: 
'When students settle into an independent task, the teacher should go to Pastore's Perch (a position in your room where all students will be in your field ofview-often one of the corners) and just stand and watch the class for three minutes -being seen looking and using least invasive intervention as required. Even if a student's hand goes up straight away, tell the student you will be with them in a few minutes -don't go to them before the end of the three minutes -they will probably unstick themselves! Follow¡ing the three minutes, start circulating the room. Interact with individual students who need support for 30 sees and then stop and scan the room again for 30 seconds, before engaging with other students for 30 seconds again. This intermittent scanning, with you "being seen looking" stops students from drifting off task, as they know you are still monitoring the whole class.' 
3. Try to standardise the format ofthe practice If all students are answering the practice questions using the same format or medium, it makes your life easier to check their work as you are always looking for the same thing, and it improves paired discussion because students can compare much easier. A work¡sheet works well for this, or you might have a template for a high¡
value activity structure that you regularly use, like my Venn dia¡grams from Tip 6. 
And our good friend the mini-whiteboard can help here. If students are completing the practice questions in their books, you can still ask them to copy their final answers (minus the working out) as they go, nice and big on their mini-whiteboards. This means as you circulate the room you can quickly see their answers and compare to your own without having to disturb them. And ifyou do spot a problem, you can quietly say, 'Tom, sorry to disturb you, but please can you just show me your working out to question 3 in your book?' Likewise, having the final answers written nice and clear on mini¡whiteboards improves paired discussions as students can focus straight in on questions where their final answers are different. 
4. Teach students what to do when they are stuck 
This is from behaviour expert, Tom Bennett. If students' default reaction when stuck is to raise their hand and wait for you to come over, not much learning is happening and behavioural issues may arise. Establishing a routine for what students should do when they are stuck using some of the tips from Chapter 3 will help with this. 
Three options are: 
1. 
If you are stuck, place your planner on your desk so I can see, and then carry on with the next question. 

2. 
If you are stuck, describe clearly where you are stuck on your mini-whiteboard. 

3. 
If you are stuck, ask your partner and see ifyou can figure it out between you. 


5. Use mini-whiteboards to support book work 
Book work is a fantastic tool of mass participation. The problem is it is often followed up by a check for understanding of a small sample -usually asking one student what they got. Sure, you could then ask another student, but that only increases the sample size by one. Or say something like, 'Did anybody get anything different?' but that relies on a certain amount of confidence, honesty and effort on the part ofyour students. 
It is far better to say, 'Okay, please can you look back in your book to question 1 and copy your final answer nice and big on your mini¡whiteboards. Hover your boards when ready ... Okay, from the back, three, two, one, show me... ' 
Now you have a much more reliable check for understanding, and you can focus on the answers that you deem most important. 
6. Consider giving access to answers 
No matter how good our checks for understanding are as we circulate the room, we are not going to be able to see everybody's answers to every question when they answer them. Likewise, even if we adopt the 4-2 approach and have strategic paired discussions, both students in each pair may well make the same mistake. 
If the practice is all on the same topic, then a mistake with an early question is likely to lead to similar mistakes in later questions. This problem is further exacerbated if students are engaging in what I call Intelligent Practice (Tip 72), where all the questions are related to each other. As we know, practice does not make perfect; practice makes permanent. 
For this reason, I always give students access to the answers when they are doing their practice and encourage them to check their work regularly as they go. 
One way of doing this is to write or reveal the answers on the board in a staggered fashion throughout the practice period. So, after a few minutes, show the answer to question 1, and then a few min¡utes later, question 2 will appear. The first time I do this I will say: 
'The answer to question 2 is now on the board. Please 
check it against your answer. If your answer is correct, 
carry on. Ifyour answer is not correct, see if you can figure 
out why. If not, ask your partner when the paired discus¡
sion time arrives. If you are still stuck after that, raise your 
hand.' 
I want this to become a routine, so each time I simply need to say, 'The answer to question 2 is on the board, you know what to do.' 
Maths teacher Femi Adeniran goes further and provides all the an¡swers to practice work on the worksheet or permanently displayed on the board. This enables his students to check their work at the exact point they are ready and allows Femi to focus all his time and attention on his students. 
The most common objection to providing answers is that students will cheat and just copy them down. And, of course, some students will do this. Over the longer term, we need to take steps to develop the culture in our classrooms where students realise classroom practice is not high-stakes -it is, quite literally, practice, maybe for a subsequent Low-Stakes Quiz (Tip 87)-and thus copying answers will lead to trouble later. 
But in the short term, simply revealing the final answer and insist¡ing students show all their working out provides extra reassurance that students are engaging with the practice material as intended. 
7. Use small chunks ofpractice at the start In Tip 19 we discussed how the best tool for the long term might not be the best tool for now. Of course, we want to get to the point where our students can remain focused for prolonged periods of time -for one thing, they are going to need to do that in exams. But it takes time to get students to that stage. Femi Adeniran discusses how he always starts his students offwith short bursts of practice -no more than a few minutes -for the first few topics. This keeps them focused, helps build an all-important foundation of success, and -when they reflect at the end of the lesson -shows them just 
how much work they can do in a lesson. Gradually this allows Femi to increase the duration of practice periods. 
8. Resist the urge to interrupt when students are silent 
I am terrible at this. As soon as the class is silent, I am off: 'It is great to see everyone is working so hard... ' or 'Don't forget to ... ' 
I think it is due to some deep-rooted fear that if I am not talking, then I am not teaching. But silent, focused work is often hard-won and thus sacred, and we should do all we can to preserve it. Keeping quiet ourselves is the best way. 
ª 

THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider key actions you can take to ensure the time for practice is as productive as possible.

Tip 71 -How to harness the hidden power of interleaving

Interleaving

What's the problem? 
In 2021, I attended a session at the researchED National Conference by Paul Rowlandson entitled 'Getting mixed up with interleaving: A deep dive into its research.' I spent the bulk of the session with my jaw on the floor. 
I first came across the concept of interleaving during my mid-career crisis in 2017, when it dawned on me that reading educational re¡search might be a good idea. And yet, despite four years of reading and experimenting, I realised after Paul's session that I had some¡how missed something crucial. 
I take a crumb ofcomfort from the fact that I suspect I am not alone. The term 'interleaving' gets used a lot in classrooms to refer to practice that does not tap into one of the very mechanisms that can make interleaving so effective for retention. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What is your understanding ofinterleaving? 

ò 
How do you use interleaving throughout your teaching? 


What's the tip? 
Let's look at what interleaving actually is, why it works, and how we can use it to enhance our students' practice. 
What is interleaving? 
Interleaving is all about mixing things up. Often interleaved practice is presented as the opposite ofmass or blocked practice: 
Massed Practice 
A 
A 
A 
A 

InterleavedPractice 

Imagine a 12-question worksheet that contains four questions on each of the three topics (A, B and C} that students have been taught. 
In the massed version of the worksheet, students practise the topics in turn. The interleaved version mixes things up. 
Does interleaving work? 
There is good evidence that simply mixing up practice (at least in maths) in this way leads to greater retention. 
In their 2020 paper -'A randomized controlled trial ofinterleaved mathematics practice' -Rohrer and colleagues gave two groups of students practice assignments on four areas of maths: direct proportion, linear inequalities, simplifying expressions and circle problems:

The practice the Blocked Practice group received was themed -so, an assignment all about direct proportion, followed by one on linear inequalities. This is common practice in schools -end-of-topic as¡signments, weekly homework, etc. that just focus on one area. The Interleaved Practice group had mixed assignments that contained questions from all four topic areas. 
Forty-three days later both groups sat the same exam. Notice that this exam was blocked, with the first four questions coming from topic C, the next four from topic B and so on. 
The results? The Blocked Practice group had a median score of 37.6, whereas the Interleaved Practice group averaged 60.7. 
Why does interleaving work? 
Interleaving is one of the Bjorks' 'desirable difficulties'. It is much more challenging to complete an interleaved assignment versus a blocked one. But that greater challenge aids retention via three ex¡planatory mechanisms: 
1. 
Practice retrieval. 

2. 
Attention attenuation. 

3. 
Discriminative contrast. 


Let's take a look at each explanatory mechanism in turn.

1. Practice retrieval 
All good practice causes students to retrieve knowledge. But inter¡leaved practice provides more opportunities to load that knowledge. In the first diagram in this section you will see that once a student first practises concept A, they practise three other things before they practise A again. Now, that separation may only be a matter of minutes, but that gap provides another opportunity for students to reload the memory ofA, and thus strengthen that memory in the process. 
2. Attention attenuation 
With poorly designed blocked practice, students can cruise through on autopilot, applying the same algorithm again and again. Once you have done one question, you have done them all, and you can essentially stop paying attention. But with interleaved practice, that is not the case. Interleaving breaks the algorithmic routine. You are constantly switching from topic A, to topic C, to topic B, and then back to C. Autopilot won't cut it-you need to be on the ball and paying attention. Learning happens when students think hard, and such constant attention is certainly effortful. 
3. Discriminative contrast 
Simply mixing up topics as presented in the Rohrer study taps into those first two explanatory mechanisms and is what I thought interleaving was all about. Hence, I introduced mixed-topic home¡work and Low-Stakes Quizzes into our department, but with little thought as to what I was mixing together. But it is the third mecha¡nism where the power of interleaving really lies. 
Discriminative contrast is all about comparing two questions and asking yourself 'what is the same, and what is different?' It is that reflection that can reveal key insights into the deep structure of a concept, thus helping you understand it better. 
Now, ifwe look back at the examples in the Rohrer study, we do not find many instances of this. Let's imagine students have just an¡swered this question: 
C Simplify the expression. 4(-2x -1) -3(-5x -2) == -8x -4 + 15x + 6 == 7x+ 2 
And are about to try this question: D A circle has an area of 254.34 square cm Find its radius, in cm. Use 7t == 3.14 
A== 1t r2 
254.34 == 3.14 r2 3.14 == 3.14 
r2
81 == 9 == r 
Before they dive in, they ask themselves 'what is the same, and what is different?' Well, not a great deal is the same, and there are plenty of differences: 
ò 
One asks you to simplify; the other to find the radius. 

ò 
One involves x; the other has pi. 

ò 
One has brackets; the other describes a circle. 

ò 
One has units; the other has none. 


The point is these questions are too different. It is hard to pinpoint exactly what feature of each question tells us what approach we need to use to solve it. No meaningful, useful comparison can be made. This is the case with most mixed-topic practice I see that gets labelled as interleaved practice. 
But, if we can design mixed-topic practice that allows for retrieval, attention attenuation and discriminative contrast, we may expect even more positive results. 
Discriminative contrast between topics -SSDD problems Please have a go at this set of questions: 



If the perimeter 
Ifthe area ofthis 
triangle is 96cm2 
equals 24cm, what is 
what is the vertical 
the value of a? 
height? 
7 
12 cm 
10-a 
What is the size of What is the area of angle p? this triangle? 

14 cm 
These four questions are from four different areas of mathematics, so straight away we have practice retrieval and attention attenua¡tion. But notice they all have a surface feature in common -they all contain an isosceles triangle. 
Now the question of what is the same and what is different certainly is worth asking. 
Let's compare the top-left question with the bottom-right question. 
ò 
What is the same? They both contain an isosceles triangle, the base is given, and they both mention area. 

ò 
What is different? In one we have the vertical height and in the other, we have the slanted height. 


It is these differences amidst a background of sameness that makes the comparison worthwhile. Hence, as students figure out (or are taught) that the bottom-right question requires Pythagoras' theo¡rem, whereas the top-left one just needs the area of a triangle for¡mula, their attention is drawn to why. It must be the differences and not the similarities. If there were no similarities, then it would be much harder for students to know what the critical features to focus on are. 
I call such sets of questions SSDD problems -standing for Same Surface (features), Different Deep (structures). Questions like these can tap into the discriminative contrast explanatory mechanism of interleaving, while also making use ofthe other two mechanisms. I house all the SSDD problems I have created (and been sent in from teachers all over the world) here: ssddproblems.com. 
Discriminative contrast within a topic 
Poorly designed blocked practice does not allow for discriminative contrast because all the questions are essentially the same. But consider this set of questions from one of my all-time favourite re¡source writers Helen Konstantine: 

Please spend some time working through them to fully appreciate what is going on. 
Let's compare the top-left question with the question to the right of it. 
ò 
What is different? One is about a line segment, the other is about a square. 

ò 
What is the same? They both require Pythagoras' theorem to solve them! 


Here things are flipped on their heads. The deep structure is what all the questions have in common (apart from the red herring... did you spot it?) against mixed-up surface features. Comparisons between questions are once again useful, but in a different way. If the deep structures are the same, what is it about each question that tells us they both require Pythagoras' theorem to solve them? What are the clues? How can this help us build a mental model of the family of problems that Pythagoras' theorem is useful for? This is peak discriminative contrast. 
Best ofboth worlds? 
The key difference between mixed-topic practice and true in¡terleaved practice is whether there is a discrimination between categories or not. As we have seen, those discriminations can be between a similar surface with different deep structures, or similar deep structures with different surface features. Both are important for students to experience. 
But can we design a set of questions where students experience both types of discriminative contrast? Well, it turns out we can. 
In the 2014 paper 'The benefit ofinterleaved mathematics practice is not limited to superficially similar kinds ofproblems', Rohrer and colleagues offer this set of problems, that I would like you to work through: 
A. A bug flies 48m east and then 14m north. How far is the bug from where it started? 
B. A bug flies 48m east and then 14m west. How far is the bug from where it started? 
C. Find the length of the line segment with endpoints (1, 1) and (5, 4). 
D. Find the slope of the line that passes through the points (1, 1) and (5, 4). 
If students compare A to B or C to D then we have two pairs of SSDD problems. But if we compare A to C then we have two different surface features that have the same deep feature. Discriminative contrast at its finest! Imagine if you had practice questions like this for each topic you taught. 
Now, if I told my wife that I plan to create yet another website to house this sort of problem, I would be a dead man. But if anyone else fancies doing it, be my guest. You just need a snappy name for them. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider shaping your practice to harness the discriminative contrast mechanism of interleaving.

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip71

What's the problem? 
Consider this set of practice questions: 
1. There are 5 red pens, 3 blue pens and 2 green pens in a box. 
Gary takes a pen from the box at random and gives the pen to his friend. Gary then takes another pen at random from the box. Work out the probability that both pens are the same colour. 
2. 
Carolyn has 20 biscuits in a tin. She has: 12 plain biscuits, 5 chocolate biscuits and 3 ginger biscuits. Carolyn takes two biscuits from the tin at random. Work out the probability that the two biscuits were not the 

same type. 

3. 
There are three different types of sandwiches on a shelf. There are: 4 egg sandwiches, 5 cheese sandwiches and 2 ham sandwiches. Erin takes 2 of these sandwiches at random. Work out the probability that she takes 2 different types of 

sandwiches. 

4. 
Here are seven tiles.

2 
2 
2 
3 
3 
Jim takes a tile at random. He does not replace the tile. Jim then takes a second tile at random. Calculate the probability that both the tiles Jim takes have 
the number 1 on them. 
If students work through these they will hopefully develop fluency and confidence in calculating probabilities. But here are two things this practice won't give students: 
1. 
A deeper understanding ofprobability. With practice like this, once you have got a sense ofhow the procedure works you can -to acer¡tain extent -apply it without engaging deeply with the concept. 

2. 
An easy way to differentiate. I like to give my students the same task, partly for logistical reasons (I don't want to become a glorified leaflet hander-outer in lessons, nor wrack up a monstrous pho¡tocopying bill), but mainly because attempting to diagnose in the moment when each ofyour 30 students are really ready to move on to a different task is a perilous proposition. 


All ofthis is not to say that practice like the one above does not have a place. But I think we also need something else.

What does Intelligent Practice mean to you? 


What's the tip? 
Consider this set of questions, and please have a go at them:

Notice that the questions are related. Once again we return to a key aspect ofvariation theory: we make novel meanings ofour own through the experience ofdifferences against a background ofsameness. This is what I call In¡telligent Practice -practice that allows your students to notice things. 
But sequences of questions are not enough. We need students to behave in a specific way to get the most out of them. I call that be¡haviour reflect, expect, check, explain. So, let's look at question 4 above. 
Reflect 
How is question 4 the same as question 3 and how is it different? 
Reflect draws students' attention to the critical change. A student might notice that the number of red counters has doubled and the number ofyellow counters has doubled. 
Expect 
Based on what you noticed, is there any prediction you can make about how the an¡swers to question 4 will relate to the answers to question 3? 
Expect encourages students to move away from the procedure and to think instead about the concept. What impact will doubling the number of red and the number of yellow counters have? Maybe the probability of picking a red and a yellow will stay the same? But the probability of picking two reds will increase? And as for one red and one yellow? ... God only knows. 
Check 
Now work out the answer using the method you have been taught. 
Here students apply the procedure and thus gain valuable practice. 
Explain 
Compare your predictions to the actual answers. Ifthey are different, can you figure out why? Ifthey are the same, how would you explain this to somebody who didn't un¡derstand it yet? 
Students now have an opportunity to think deeply. They may be surprised by the answer, and thus the challenge is to figure out what is going on. Or the answer may be exactly what they expected, and so the challenge is to think of a way of explaining this to some¡one who is struggling. Either way, the answer is not a signal to stop thinking, but in fact to start thinking harder. 
I have written an entire book on this form ofpractice called Reflect, Expect, Check, Explain. Even ifyou don't enjoy the subject matter, the book makes a brilliant doorstop. Here I just want to draw your at¡tention to this form of practice, and also offer a tip I have learned from seeing this put into action in many schools over the last three years... 
What do the students write down? 
This is the big question. You want to ensure that your students are engaging in mathematical thinking and also provide practice of mathematical communication, but to require them to write out their full reflection, expectation and explanation for each question is likely to mean they hardly get through any questions. 
Having worked with hundreds of students on Intelligent Practice exercises, I have concluded that the most successful strategy is: 
ò 
Ask students to do their check in full for each question, showing all their working out, so you can be sure they are practising the procedure. 

ò 
Ask them to do their reflections and explanations in their heads, or jot down thoughts on mini-whiteboards so they don't get bogged down with too much writing. 

ò 
Then, when students have finished, or after a period of time, say to them, 'Okay, now I want you to choose the relationship be¡tween questions that you found the most interesting and write up how you would explain it to someone who doesn't under¡stand it yet. You can use words, diagrams, further examples, whatever you think will explain what you noticed most clearly.' 


I find by reducing the amount students need to write, and by giving them the choice of the relationship they delve into, the responses are of a far higher quality than ifwe ask students to do this for every question. 
Other forms ofIntelligent Practice 
1. Method selection 
Method selection is so important in maths. Students may have mastered each individual procedure, but can they correctly select when to apply each one? Carefully varied sequences help in this sense because only certain things change between each question, 
so students' attention is drawn to what has changed and the impact it has on the method required. 
Here is a set of Intelligent Practice questions that could be used at the end of a unit of work on percentages to ensure students can se¡lect the correct method to use:

No-number Practice 
questions 
What's the problem? 
Have you ever taught students who struggle with those wordy, con¡textual exam questions like this one: 
300 passengers go on a coach trip. Each coach takes 50 passengers. Each passenger pays ú25. 
The table shows the costs for the coach company. 
Cost for each coach  
Pay for driver  ú90  
Fuel  70pper mile  

Each coach travels 200 1niles. Work out the total profit the company makes fro1n this trip. [6 m arks] 
Of course you have! 
Students often do not know where to start. Some are overwhelmed by the amount of information and just down tools. Others imme¡diately grab onto any two numbers they see and apply whatever operation first comes into their heads. They get in a massive mud¡dle, complain that this is a maths lesson not an English lesson, and declare they give up and hate life. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do your studentsstrugglewith these typesof questions? 

ò 
How do you help them? 


What's the tip? 
Step forward Craig Latimir. He suggests that we first present our students with a no-number version ofthe question like this: 
llllpassengers go on a coach trip. 
Each coach takes ª passengers. Each passenger pays úª The table shows the costs for the coach company. 
Cost for each coach  
Pay for driver  úª  
Fuel  òppermile  

Each coach travelsllllmiles. Work out the total profit the company makes fro1n this trip. 
[6 marks] 
26 
By removing the numbers in a question, we are removing a poten¡tial distraction. Without the numbers present, students are both forced and free to look at the underlying structure ofthe question. This enables them to form a strategy to solve the problem. When the numbers are eventually revealed, it is simply a case of slotting them in. 
How does this play out in the classroom? 
Craig suggests starting with the numbers version. Students are likely to struggle, but this provides the headache necessary (Tip 54) to make them buy into the no-numbers approach. 
Then remove the numbers and give students two minutes to plot out a strategy to solve the no-number version of a question in the form of: 'Step 1-Work out the total money received by multiplying the number ofpassengers by the price they pay.' Our good friend the mini-whiteboard works well for this. 
Then a further two minutes for students to compare their strategy with their partner. What is the same and what is different? Can they agree on an approach that would solve the problem? 
Finally, give students two minutes to solve the problem with the numbers revealed. 
Of course, at any stage, we are free to stop the class and intervene. Circulating the room during the plotting and paired discussions will give us an idea of how much support we need to give. 
Another example? 
Here is one for you to have a go with. 
The cost of a holiday is ú1111 Rana pays a deposit followed by monthly payments) in the ratio 
deposit: total of the monthly payments =1:1 
She makes ªequal monthly payments. Work out her 111onthly pay1nent. 
[4 marks] 
27 
Can you plot a solution to this problem? 
Exam strategy 
Of course, when students face questions like these in an exam, the numbers are present. But the hope is that students can learn to ignore the numbers initially and instead think about structure. That should stop students from becoming overwhelmed by these types of questions or just diving into any old operations.
