What's the problem? 
One area of teaching I never realised I was terrible at was what I did when my students were practising. I think my actions could be best described as a 'meander'. I would stroll around the classroom, ca¡sually glancing at my students' work with no real purpose in mind. Occasionally I would get lucky and spot a mistake or an interesting answer. But ultimately, I never really discovered all that much. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What do you do when your students are practising? 

ò 
What do you do when your students are practising? 


What's the tip? 
I needed to replace my meandering with observing my students' work with a purpose. Here are nine ideas to help with that. 
1. Decide what you are looking for in advance 
If students are working through a set of, say, 10 practice questions, then it is impossible to look at the answers of every student for 
every question. So, instead, choose one question that you are par¡ticularly interested in. 
ò 
It may be the first question of an exercise so you can check stu¡dents are on track. 

ò 
Or there may be a twist in one of the questions that you want to make sure students have spotted. 

ò 
Or it may not be the answer you are looking for, but something else about the work. Are students showing their working out? Are they writing in full sentences? Have they included units? Have they rounded their answer? 


The key point is to choose just one focus for each circulation while students are practising. When you have observed that in one stu¡dent, move on to the next student instead oflingering over their work. Much better to observe one thing well, than lots of things not at all. And after one round of circulating, you can always pick an¡other focus for the next. If nothing else, it is good for the step count. 
Now you know what you are looking for, the following are some actions you can take to give yourself the best chance of spotting it. 
2. Ask students to write their final answers on their mini-whiteboards as they go 
This tip comes up time and again, but for very good reasons. If a final answer to a question is the thing you are looking for as you circulate, then it is far easier to see it if students have copied the answer nice and big on their mini-whiteboards, rather than having to strain your eyes to read their tiny writing in the books, or inter¡rupt them to flick back a page to find it. 
3. Standardise the format ofthe practice 
Another classic tip returns! If students are setting out their work in the same way, either on a worksheet, a template, or a specific for¡mat in their books, then it is much easier for you to quickly find the thing you are looking for. 
4. Carry your answers around with you 
Have your answers, plus working out, to hand as you circulate. That makes it much easier for you to compare your answer to theirs, es¡pecially ifyour attention gets diverted by a question or an incident during the circulation. 
5. Carry common errors with you 
Back in Tip 46, we talked about planning for error -that is, taking steps before the lesson to predict where students may go wrong and writing that down. So, along with your answers, have these errors with you as you circulate the room. This makes it much easier to identify where a student may have gone wrong than ifyou have to stop and try to diagnose on the spot. 
6. Tally up common errors as you circulate 
This is a lovely idea I saw in a school I visited. We have already discussed the problem with taking mental notes in Tip 18. As we circulate, we may notice errors in our students' work. Trying to keep these errors in our heads so we can deal with them when we return to the front ofthe class is challenging with the numerous things that vie for our attention in a classroom. Instead, every time you see an error, place a tally mark next to it on your sheet ofpaper (or quickly copy it down ifit was an error that you had not predicted). This allows you to determine which errors are significant enough to warrant a whole-class discussion, versus those errors that are best suited to quick conversations with individual students. 
7. Observe the work ofyour strongest students first 
They are the students who are likely to have made the most progress and hence provide you with the most information. If they are struggling with a question, then the chances are that most of the class is also struggling, so you can do some whole-class intervention. 
8. Don't be afraid to stop the practice 
Ifyour purposeful observation has identified an issue, and your tallying suggests it is widespread, then don't be afraid to call a halt to the practice, get all eyes on you at the front of the classroom, and intervene. The alternatives are either to let their errors go unchecked -practice does not make perfect, practice makes perma ¡nent -or to run around the classroom from student to student like a headless chicken having the same conversations again and again. It is much more efficient to stop everyone for a few minutes and resolve the issue. You can get everyone to write their final answer on their mini-whiteboards and show you so you have an even better sense ofwhole-class understanding, and then go from there, either asking students with different answers to explain their reasoning, engaging Partner Talk, or offering a clear explanation yourself. 
9. When talking to a student, step back 
A last one from Adam Boxer. As you circulate, you will want to stop and ask students questions to dig deeper into their understanding. Or students will ask you questions. There is a danger that these interactions take you out of the game, and more easily distracted students may seize upon this opportunity. Instead, take a step back so you are still focusing on the student you are talking to, but you have a wider field ofvision and a larger presence to stay an active figure in the class. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Take steps to make your circulating during student practice as purposeful and productive as possible.

Tip 75 -Occasionally let students do work in someone else's book

What's the problem? 
Let's end this chapter with a weird one. 
A mistake I made early on in my career was to go overboard with peer assessment. It was the trendy way to wrap up a test or period ofindividual practice: 'Okay, swap books, purple pens out, and get ready to mark.' 
The result was never satisfying. I'd estimate the students dedicated at most 30% of their attention to their peers' books that they were supposed to be marking, and everything else to thinking about what was currently going on in their own books. This was made worse if they swapped books with their neighbour and could see the crosses mounting up: 'Hey, that's a 6, not an 8!' 
I fully subscribe to Dylan Wiliam's notion that the best person to mark work is the person whose work it is. This gives them immediate feedback, and also allows them to benefit from the Hy¡percorrection Effect -the surprise of a wrong answer making the correct answer more memorable in the future (we will discuss the Hypercorrection Effect more in Tip 85). 
However ... 
I think there are benefits to occasionally asking students to do a period of practice in someone else's book. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What is your experience with peerassessment? 

ò
Do you ever ask students to do work in someone else's book? 


What's the tip? 
Three benefits of occasionally asking students to do a period of practice in someone else's book are: 
1. 
Students tend to write neater and take more care over their answers if they are writing in someone else's book. They know their work will be in that book forever, and so tend to put that extra bit of effort in. 

2. 
When students get their book back, they get to experience a dif¡ferent way of thinking or a different way of setting work out. 

3. 
The subsequent peer assessment that you may do is likely to be more successful as students now have their own book with them, and so they may be less concerned with what their part¡ner is up to. Books have a weird ownership thing attached to them. 


I am by no means advising doing this all the time, but for some practice activities, swapping books before practice begins might be worth a go. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider occasionally asking students to do work in someone else's book so they put more effort in, experience adifferent way of thinking, and engage in more effective peer assessment.

CHAPTER 10 
MEMORY AND RETRIEVAL 
Of all the areas of research that have found their way into the class¡room over the last few years, research into memory and retrieval is top of the list. But while teachers may understand the importance of things like retrieval practice and spacing, do our students? In this chapter, we look at how we can help our students understand some of the key research findings in order to improve their engagement in class and the effectiveness of their study. We then discuss some general principles of memory that have powerful classroom appli¡cations, including the role of cues, confidence scores, different types of retrieval questions and how to make corrections as effective as possible. We end with a deep dive into two key retrieval opportuni¡ties that often become lethal mutations -Low-Stakes Quizzes and the Do Now.

Do Now 
Retrieval opportunities 
Before we dive into the tips, I want to spend a few minutes consid¡ering the various retrieval opportunities we may provide for our students, as we will constantly refer to them throughout this chap¡ter. By 'retrieval opportunities', I specifically mean those moments where we challenge our students to remember something other than the content they are studying at that very moment. It may be something they have studied earlier that lesson, yesterday, last topic, or it may be something from a few years back. 
Here are six of the most common retrieval opportunities I see in the schools I visit. 
1. Starter/Do Now 
Teachers often use the start of the lesson to ask students questions about things they have learned in the past -the last lesson, last topic, last term, last year framework is popular here. Or teachers may choose content specifically related to the upcoming lesson -in other words, a relevant prior knowledge check. 
2. Woven into the lesson 
Teachers may weave content from the past into the current topic being studied. In maths, this often involves revisiting high-value concepts such as negative number operations, fractions and deci¡mals by including them in the practice of the new idea. 
3. Plenary/Exit Ticket 
The end of the lesson is a popular time to retrieve what students have learned that lesson, but it may also be an opportunity to revisit ideas learned prior to this in the form of a delayed Exit Ticket. 
4. Low-Stakes Quiz 
A popular form of retrieval opportunity is a regular, low-stakes, mixed-topic quiz on a range ofideas students have encountered in the past. 
5. Homework Homework is unique as a retrieval opportunity in the sense that it takes place outside oflesson time. Homework may be topic themed 
or include a revision section to challenge students to remember ideas from further back in time. 
6. Summative assessment 
Finally, most schools have regular summative assessments with various stakes attached. They may be end-of-topic tests, end-of¡term tests, end-of-year tests, or high-stakes end-of-course exams. 
We will delve deep into each of these retrieval opportunities throughout this chapter. But before we move on, please spend a few moments considering: 
1. 
Which of these retrieval opportunities form part ofyour teach¡ing? 

2. 
Which do you feel have room for improvement in terms of their effectiveness? 


Tip 76 -Show your students the Forgetting Curve 

Tip 76 Forgetting Curve I  Tip 77 Storage & retrieval  '  r  Tip 78 Working memory I  Tip 81 All content I  Tip82 Aim for80%  Tip 83 Vary the questions I  
Get students on board ò I I I Tip79 Tip 80 Long -term Familiaritymemory  I Tip84 Prompts & cues  General principles Tip85 Confidence scores  I Tip 86 Corrections  Tip 87 Low-Stakes Quiz..  
Tip89 Trello  Tip 88 Do Now  

What's the problem? 
I have a theory: I think many students take revisiting things they have learned in the past less seriously than learning new ideas. 
They may choose to not engage fully in a Do Now retrieval starter, saving their mental energy for when the lesson 'begins properly'. They may copy their mates' mixed-topic homework, or leave out questions in a Low-Stakes Quiz, only answering the ones that come easily to them. 
We know from decades of research (the Bjorks remain my go-to source on this) that one ofthe best ways to ensure students do not forget something is to provide opportunities for them to retrieve that knowledge several times over a given period of time. Each act ofretrieval leads to a boost in the storage and retrieval strength of that knowledge (Tip 77), making it more deeply embedded and more accessible next time they need it. 
But if students are not going to put in the required effort when challenged to retrieve something they have learned in the past, then all our efforts to provide these crucial retrieval opportunities will be 
. .
1n vain. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Have you experienced your students not taking retrieval as seriously as the core part ofalesson? 

ò
Have you taken any steps to address this? 


What's the tip? 
One of the most effective ways I have found to encourage my stu¡dents to take retrieval opportunities more seriously is to show them a diagram of the famous Ebbinghaus Forgetting Curve. 
A word of caution. There are loads ofversions of this curve out there, with a whole assortment of numbers on the axes. The original study upon which the curve is based was conducted by Her¡mann Ebbinghaus in the 1880s with a sample size of one (Ebbing¡haus himself!). So, while I am happy to share the general shape with my students, I avoid any statements such as, 'After one day, you have forgotten 50% ofwhat you learned.' 
Here is the diagram I use: 
First learned Retrieved 
i ~ i 
Memory retention (%) 

Elapsedtime 
There is a copy of this diagram in the resources page for this tip. 
I accompany the diagram with dialogue along the lines of: 
'Okay, so I have some good news and some bad news about 
how our memories work. 
'First, the bad news. As soon as you learn something new, you start to forget it pretty quickly. Look at how steep this first curve is. So, even though you might have nailed it at the time, I am afraid that it will become harder and harder to remember it with each passing minute. This is perfectly normal. It is our brain's way of deciding what is worth remembering between all the things it encounters every second ofevery day. And ifyou have just thought about something once, then your brain does not have a lot to go off. 
'But here is the good news! If you try hard to retrieve that memory again in the future, you slow down this rate of for¡getting. Why? Because you are telling your brain that this is important-"I need to remember this!" And each time you try hard to retrieve that memory in the future, that annoy¡ing curve gets flatter and flatter until eventually it is lodged in your brain for a long, long time. 
'So, every time I ask you to do a Do Now, homework or a Low-Stakes Quiz that contains something you have learned before, please try super hard to remember how to do it. Be¡cause each time you do, you are using the way your mem¡ory works to your advantage.' 
Now, of course, you will need to tweak the language to suit your 
students, but hopefully, the general principle rings true. If we want 
our students to take retrieving prior knowledge as seriously as 
learning new material, then we may well need to convince them 
why they should bother. And a clear, simple visual aid, backed by 
a carefully thought-out explanation, might just make the difference. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider showing your students the model of the Forgetting Curve to help them take retrieval opportuni¡ties more seriously.

Tip 77 -Show your students the path to high storage and retrieval strength

Do Now 
What's the problem? 
Showing our students a model of the Forgetting Curve (Tip 76) may help them understand why they forget things. But while providing opportunities to retrieve knowledge over and over again over a pro¡longed time period will help with some of the things students need to remember, it won't work for everything. Because ifthey cannot remember it in the first place, then what should they do? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Have you experienced students getting frustrated in this way? 

ò
Have you taken any steps to help? 


What's the tip? 
This is where it is useful if our students understand the difference between the storage strength and retrieval strength of memories, which can then show us the path to remembering. 
Step 1: The distinction between storage strength and retrieval strength 
Storage strength is how deeply a memory is embedded. Retrieval strength is how accessible that memory is. Every time you access a memory, you enjoy a boost in both storage and retrieval strength. 
A good way to illustrate the difference between these two strengths of memory is with a diagram I first saw maths teacher Kris Boulton use many years ago: 
-= 
...... 
O'I 
C: 
~ 
...... 
V, 
<l.J O'I 
~ 
0
...... 
I.I") 
Retrieval strength 
To demonstrate the different combinations of storage and retrieval strength to our students, we need some concrete examples, from both inside and outside the classroom. Here are some that I have used. 
A: High storage strength, low retrieval strength 
This is knowledge that students have learned really well in the past, but may not have thought about for a long time, hence they strug¡gle to retrieve it. In the classroom, it may be a topic they studied a few years ago. Outside ofthe classroom, it may be a person from the past whose name they definitely know but they cannot quite remember it at this moment -someone from their primary school, their reception class teacher, or a character from a film. This is the most frustrating type ofknowledge. You know it is in there some¡where, but you can't quite find it. 
B: High storage strength, high retrieval strength 
This is the dream. This is knowledge that is deeply embedded and easily retrievable. In the classroom, it might be something students regularly encounter. If you use Call and Response, then it might be the second part to a statement such as, 'The interior angles of a triangle add up to... '. Outside of the classroom, it might be their password to log on to their social media accounts, facts about their favourite sports team, or their mobile number. 
C: Low storage strength, high retrieval strength 
This is the most dangerous type ofknowledge because students can recall it at the time, but if they do not revisit it again in the future, it will drift back to group D. In the classroom, this is something stu¡dents have just been taught. They are performing, but they have not yet learned. A good way to demonstrate this is to give students a five-digit number to look at, take it off the screen, and then ask stu¡dents to tell their partner what the five-digit number was. Continue the lesson as normal, and then ask them to recall the number again later on. 
D: Low storage strength, low retrieval strength 
This is the worst ofboth worlds. The reason students cannot retrieve this kind of knowledge is that it was never really present in the first place. In the classroom, this might be something the students studied last year, but they didn't really concentrate at the time, and have never revisited it since. A nice way to demonstrate this is to ask students to look at a five-digit number at the end of a lesson with no context or further instructions. Then ask them next lesson what that number was. With no effort to encode it, and no further retrieval opportunities, that knowledge is inaccessible. 
Challenging students to think of their own examples for each cate¡gory is useful here to make the concepts even more concrete. 
Step 2: The path to high storage and retrieval strength 
With a better understanding of the difference between storage and retrieval strength, now comes the big question: 
How can we ensure as much ofwhat we learn in the class¡
room fits into group B? 
But the path to group B will differ depending on where that knowl¡edge is: 
ò 
From A to B: No matter how long students stare at a question involving knowledge in group B, they probably won't be able to remember it -or at least it will take a long time. Instead, a review will help. Looking at a worked example, being given the first line of the solution, or seeing a visual cue (Tip 56) may be enough to access the memory and get the ball rolling. The key message here is that ifyou can't remember, do a quick review, and then remember to retest again in the future. 

ò 
From Cto B: Reviews and cues won't help here. Several instances of effortful retrieval are needed to make sure students' brains realise that this is something worth hanging on to. Hence, this journey requires retrieval opportunities spaced out over time. Only then are we able to distinguish performance from learning and start to build up the storage strength to give that memory some staying power. Sustained effort will be required. 

ò 
From D to B: This requires the most effort. It probably requires a reteach or a restudy, followed by spaced practice over a pro¡longed period of time. But at least students know what they need to do. 


Once students understand these paths to group B, and the steps required to get them there, they might be in a position to diagnose their individual needs for different topics they encounter, and be more likely to engage in the required reviews or effortful retrieval practice over the course of the year. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider helping your students understand the difference betweenstorage and retrieval strength so they better understand the steps required to rememberthings

Tip 78 -Show your students the limits of working memory

Do Now 
What's the problem? 
We know working memory is limited. We know that for our stu¡dents to have the best chance of understanding what we are trying to teach them, they need to dedicate as much attention as possible to that key idea. We know, therefore, that anything else that catches their attention is going to reduce the attention they can dedicate to thinking about that big idea. 
But we also know that many students will not pay us a blind bit of notice when we ask them to concentrate. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do your students understand the limits of their working memory and thus the need to concentrate? 

ò 
What do you do to help them understand this? 


What's the tip? 
One of my favourite demonstrations comes in two parts. 
Part I: Ask your students to remember nine things We know most people's working memory capacity is between five and nine unrelated items. So, to enable students to feel the strain on their working memories, project a list like the following on the board, one item at a time, so each is visible for a second before it disappears. Ask students to try to remember the list without talking or writing anything down: 
3, Anna, Q, Pink, Book, Similar, F, Germany, Dog 
Then, after you have removed the final item from the board, count to five and ask students to write down as many items from the list as possible, in the correct order. 
Reveal the answers and ask students to raise their hands if they remembered all nine. 
Part 2: Ask your students to remember nine things... with distractions 
Now the fun begins. Time for another list, again with each item revealed one at a time, but this time we need to disrupt our stu¡dents' concentration. 
ò 
I start by having lots of distractions on my slide -images, words, GIFs, etc. 

ò 
I then project the first two items, one after another, that stu¡dents need to remember: '8, Tom'. 

ò 
Then I ask students to turn to their partners and tell them the best thing they have watched in the last six months. 

ò 
Next, three more items: 'V, blue, table', all appearing then disap¡pearing after each other. 

ò 
Then I play some music -something with plenty of lyrics. Oasis, 'Rock 'n' Roll Star', ifyou are interested -might as well give them a musical education while we are at it. 

ò 
Two more items: 'Quiet, H'. 

ò 
Next, I challenge students to try to spell the word 'elephant' backwards. 

ò 
Last two items: 'Bee, Cow'. 

ò 
Then a story by me about how much I love satsumas. 

ò 
And finally, I ask students to write out the nine items. 


Of course, what is happening here is students' limited working memory capacity is being filled with things to attend to that are re¡dundant -they are of no help in remembering the list ofitems, and indeed they are a hindrance as they steal students' limited supply of attention. 
When you ask students to write down the list now, you can guaran¡tee fewer will be successful. 
You can use this as a springboard to discuss the importance of concentrating, or even broaden this out to suggest that doing home¡work or revising with the TV on, singing along to Spotify, or working with a friend with whom you like to chat about other stuff, are all unlikely to be the conditions most suited to learning. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Instead of telling students why concentrating is important, consider demonstrating thelimits of their working memories.

Tip 79 -Show your students how long¡term memory helps thinking 

Tip 76 Forgetting Curve I  Tip 77 Storage & retrievaI  Tip 78 Working memory I  Tip 81 Al l content I  Tip82 Aim fo r80o/o  Tip 83 Vary the questions I  
Get students on board ò I I I Tip 79 Tip80Long-term Familiaritymemory  I Tip 84 Prompts & cues  General principles Tip 85 Confidence scores  I Tip86 Co rrections  Tip 87 Low-Stakes Quiz  
Tlp89 Trello  Tip88 Do Now  

What's the problem? 
Another thing teachers understand is that knowledge helps think¡ing. Our working memories have a finite capacity, but our long-term memories do not. So, ifwe can pull knowledge from our long-term memory into our working memory, then it can ease the burden of the information we are trying to process. Moreover, we can then do more sophisticated things with that information. 
But, once again, it is one thing teachers knowing this... 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do your students understand the importance of knowledge to aid their thinking? 

ò 
What do you do to help them understand this? 


What's the tip? 
There are two parts to this demonstration, and both are super quick. 
1. How knowledge in long-term memory frees up working memory capacity 
Ask students to remember the set ofletters you are about to flash on the screen. Make them appear all at the same time for about one second before removing them: 
ADEJFPOBD 
Then ask students to write them down. 
Almost all students will fail. 
Now repeat the challenge but this time with these letters: 
CROCODILE 
Most students can remember these letters. The question to pose to students is 'why?' And the answer is that the second set of letters has meaning. They are not nine items that need remembering; they are one. Because students have knowledge of CROCODILE, those nine letters instead take up the capacity of one item, and so are much more easily processed. This is chunking. 
2. How knowledge in long-term memory allows more sophisticated thinking 
Next show students these six letters all at once for no more than one second: 
ODHCMB 
With the letters no longer visible, challenge students to write down the next letter in the alphabet for each -so, if a letter visible was an A, they would write down a B. 
Most students struggle with this. 
Next repeat the exercise with these six letters: 
CYPRUS 
More students are successful. Why? Because once again knowledge in long-term memory has helped us chunk those six letters into one item, and this time we have used the working memory capacity that this has freed up to do something sophisticated with that knowl¡edge -something that was significantly more challenging than with knowledge that is not secure. 
Knowing things makes thinking, learning new things and problem solving easier. 
I have also used these demonstrations to respond to the classic protest: 'Why do I have to learn this? I can just Google it.' The point is that to formulate a question to pose to a search engine and to then interpret the response, requires knowledge. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Show students the importance of knowledgein long-term memory to support their thinking.

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip79

Tip 80 -Show your students that being familiar with something is not the same as knowing it

Do Now 
~ 

What's the problem? 
This is a big one. 
Student: Sir, you will be dead proud of me. I did three hours of maths revision last night! 
Teacher:  That is brilliant news, well done! What did you do?  
Student:  I watched a YouTube video, and read over my notes.  
Teacher:  Oh ...  

Dunlosky's 'Strengthening the student toolbox' ranks the most ef¡fective ways students can study. Right at the top of the list is practice testing. Intuitively, we know this. The best way to learn maths is to do maths, and I am sure that is true in other subjects. Much lower down Dunlosky's list is rereading -where students review material they have encountered before. The reason this is not as effective is that students are not actively trying to retrieve knowledge from their long-term memories and hence get the boost in storage and retrieval strength that we discussed in Tip 77. But it sure feels nice. More specifically, reviewing feels familiar. As we read over those notes or watch that video, we convince ourselves that we know it. Perhaps we see certain cues that ring a bell -familiar phrases and steps -or perhaps we recall the positive feeling of understanding the subject matter at the time and confuse that with actually re¡membering how to do something. Either way, we are seduced by that familiarity. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have youexperienced students being seduced by familiarity? 

ò 
What do you do to combat this? 


What's the tip? 
I have two favourite demonstrations. 
1. The Google challenge 
Write the letters GOOGLE in the colours used by the official Google logo, and then create five other versions using slightly different colour combinations (I would love to show you an image of this, but alas Google are pretty adamant no one should mess with their colour combinations). Label the logos A, B, C, D, E and F. 
With your six logos on a single image, ask students to decide ¡without telling anyone else -which they think is the correct colour arrangement. When you are ready, ask anyone who thinks it is A to raise their hand. Then B, then C and so on. 
I guarantee there will be a wide spread of responses. 
2. The Apple challenge 
Give students a blank piece of paper or a mini-whiteboard, and ensure no Apple products are in view (easier said than done these days). Then say to students, 'Draw a sketch of the Apple logo.' Try this yourself now. 
When students have finished, ask: 
ò 
'Hands up ifyou put the bite on the right? How about the left?' 

ò 
'Hands up ifyou had more than one leaf on top?' 

ò 
'Hands up who drew one leaf? Two? Three?' 


I will leave you to look up how close your attempt is to Apple's apple. 
The point ofboth ofthese quick demonstrations is that even though students have seen both logos thousands of times and thus are incredibly familiar with them, because they have not actively en¡gaged with them, they do not know them as well as they may think. Whereas, if students were asked to select the correct Google logo or sketch the Apple logo every few months, they would complete these two challenges with ease. 
So what instead? 
These two demonstrations should help students feel the need for more effective revision strategies. So now you could show them the table from the Dunlosky paper: 
Effectiveness of Techniques Reviewed  
Technique  Extent and Conditions of Effectiveness  
Practice testing  Very effective under awide array of situations  
Distributed practice  Very effective under awide array of situations  
Interleaved practice  Promising for math and concept learning, but needs more research  
Elaborative interrogation  Promising, but needs more research  
Self-explanation  Promising, but needs more research  
Rereading  Distributed rereading can be helpful, but time could be better spent using anotherstrategy  
Highlighting and underlining  Not particularly helpful, but can be used as afirst step toward further study  
Summarization  Helpful only withtraining on how to summarize  
Keyword mnemonic  Somewhat helpful forlearning languages,but benefits are short-lived  
Imagery for text  Benefits limited to imagery-friendly text, and needs more research  

Source: Dunlosky (2013). 
Then show students what effective practice testing looks like. 
My favourite video on this is called 'How to Study Effectively for School or College [Top 6 Science-Based Study Skills]' -linked to in the resources -which is a collaboration between The Learning Sci¡entists and Memorize Academy. The video describes six effective study strategies: 
1.Retrievalpractice 
2. 
Spaced practice 

3. 
Dual coding 

4. 
Interleaving 

5. 
Concrete examples 

6. 
Elaboration. 


The video is great, but something even better lies in the video de¡scription: a free poster of the six strategies, and practical examples ofhow each can be used across different subjects. 
Of course, you could start by showing students this. But I think my two daft demonstrations help students feel the need for something more effective, and thus make them more receptive. It's the headache¡aspirin approach (Tip 54) again. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Use ademonstration to show students the problem with familiarity, and use this to suggest more effec¡tive study habits.

Tip 81 -Ensure you provide retrieval opportunities for all content

Do Now 
What's the problem? 
At the start of this chapter, we considered six key retrieval opportu¡nities. As a reminder, they were: 
1. 
Starter/Do Now 

2. 
Woven into the lesson 

3. 
Plenary/Exit Ticket 

4. 
Low-Stakes Quiz 

5. 
Homework 


6. Summative assessment. Here are three questions I would like you to consider: 
1. How do you decide what topics to include in your retrieval opportunities? 
ò 
Let's say you are doing a Do Now. How do you decide what questions to ask? 

ò 
Or how about in your weekly Low-Stakes Quizzes? 

ò 
Or a revision section ofyour homework? 


2. When do your students retrieve the knowledge that is hard to quiz? 
In maths this will include topics where students need equipment: 
ò 
Transformations, where tracing paper and grids are needed? 

ò 
Loci and construction, where a compass is needed? 

ò 
Measuring angles and scale drawings, requiring protractors and rulers? 


3. When do your students retrieve complex skills? 
ò 
Tricky exam questions? 

ò 
Questions wrapped up in a context? 

ò 
Questions that weave together different areas of mathemat¡ics? 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
What are your responsesto these three questions? 

ò
Ask a colleaguefrom yourdepartment-how do their responses differ? 


What's the tip? 
The reason I ask these questions is that I do not think they are con¡sidered enough. 
1. How do you decide what topics to include in your retrieval opportunities? 
Unless we are systematic in the scheduling of our retrieval oppor¡tunities, then there is a real danger that some concepts will get underserved or left out entirely. As we will discuss in Tip 88, many teachers use their Do Now to focus on the knowledge required for the upcoming lesson. But what about Low-Stakes Quizzes and homework? Are you confident that you will be able to provide re¡trieval opportunities for all the content you teach your students? 
2. When do your students retrieve the knowledge that is hard to quiz? 
When I see retrieval questions in Do Nows, Low-Stakes Quizzes and homework, they are invariably the 'easy to quiz' topics. That does not mean they are easy to answer, but that no equipment is needed to answer them. So, while I often see fraction operations in Do Nows, I rarely see rotations. I see a lot of equations to solve in Low-Stakes Quizzes, but few angle bisectors to construct. It is clear why this is the case -sorting out equipment for students who forget to bring it is a pain, and as teachers we experience quite enough of that. But it is also a problem, because ifwe have taught our students something and we want them to remember it, then we need to pro¡vide retrieval opportunities. 
3. When do your students retrieve complex skills? We know students struggle with tricky exam questions, contextual questions with no obvious cue, and questions that involve more than one concept. The only way for our students to get better at them is to practise them over a prolonged period, not just crammed into the final few weeks before an exam. And yet the majority of questions I see in retrieval opportunities are more straightforward 
and procedural in nature. We will consider different types of re¡trieval questions in Tip 83. 
So what do we do? 
One solution to this is to schedule retrieval opportunities more sys¡tematically. Here is one way that worked well in a school I worked with: 
1. 
Over the course of the year, create a list of the things you teach your students that you want them to remember. One per lesson is good. List these as objectives along the lines of 'calculate the area of a circle'. 

2. 
Number each of these objectives, so you end up with a list like this: 

1. 
Calculate the area of a circle. 

2. 
Calculate the circumference of a circle. 

3. 
Work backwards to find the circumference/area. 

4. 
Plot a straight line from a table ofvalues. 

5. 
Sketch a straight line from an equation. 

6. 
Work out the equation of a straight line from a sketch. 



3. 
Each time you include a question from your list as part of a retrieval opportunity (Do Now, homework, Low-Stakes Quiz, etc.), place a mark next to it: P if the question you use is proce¡dural in nature, Cifit is complex in nature. 

4. 
After a while, look for objectives that do not have any letters next to them, and ensure you provide a question in a retrieval opportunity soon. 

5. 
Look for objectives that you have only retrieved procedurally, and ask a more complex question on them. 


There are lots of possible variations to this: 
ò 
Have the list on Excel and get all fancy with conditional format¡ting. 

ò 
Schedule in questions at the start of term so you know you won't miss anything. 

ò 
Delete the letter if students struggled with the question and you had to reteach. 

ò 
If the question involves more than one objective, mark all objec¡tives with a letter. 


Just like everything, it is about finding a way that works for you, both in terms ofworkload and student buy-in. But I think the gen¡eral idea ofbeing more systematic with the content of our retrieval opportunities so nothing gets missed out is a good one. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider the most practical, manageable way to ensure your students experience retrieval opportunities for everything you teach.

What's the problem? 
There are two things I regularly see in retrieval opportunities in schools. 
1. 
Retrieval opportunities that are too easy for the class. Loads of students get 100%. This is great for confidence, but perhaps not so ideal for learning. Learning happens when students think hard, and if students are coasting through our Do Nows, Low-Stakes Quizzes and homework without too much difficulty, then it is a sign that we need to ramp up the challenge. Likewise, students who get 100% have done all their thinking during the retrieval. Ifwe are going to spend some time reviewing the solutions to our retrieval opportunities and doing corrections (Tip 86), we want to ensure that all our students have something challeng¡ing to do. 

2. 
Retrieval opportunities that are too hard for the class. And the flip side: if students are scoring less than 50%, then it is doing nobody any favours. Confidence will suffer, and there will be too much for students to correct in the reviewing stage. We want retrieval to be challenging, but we need a bedrock of success as well. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you decide upon the difficulty of the content in your retrieval opportunities? 

ò 
How accurateareyou in what actually happens? 


What's the tip? 
A good rule of thumb that I use is I want the mean score in any retrieval opportunity across the class to be around 80%. This is the magic figure Rosenshine uses in his seventh principle: 'Obtain a high success rate.' 
This is easier said than done. We may quiz students on something that seemed really secure when they learned it, only to find that they struggle. Likewise, we may include a question on a topic they struggled with, only to find that their understanding is better than we hoped. 80% is not an exact figure, but a benchmark to aim for. 
Of course, a mean score of 80% means some students will probably score 100%, and other students will score much lower. In Tip 86 we discuss how the review process will accommodate both ofthese students, ensuring the former will still be challenged, and the latter will not be put off. And in Tip 83 I will suggest some ways that we can vary the challenge of our retrieval questions to edge us closer to that 80% figure from either direction. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Aim for amean score of 80% with each retrieval opportunity.

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip82

What's the problem? 
Think about the types of questions you ask your students during your retrieval opportunities. What do they look like? 
Actually, let's get a bit more specific. If you teach maths, imagine you had taught your students how to share in a ratio and you wanted to provide a retrieval opportunity-perhaps a Do Now, a Low-Stakes Quiz or homework-to see what they have remembered and under¡stood. What kinds of questions would you ask? Non-maths teach¡ers, do this for an upcoming topic. 
If you are anything like me, those questions would tend to be proce¡dural in nature. 'Share ú80 in the ratio 2:3' -that sort of thing. 
Is this a problem? 
I think so, for two reasons. 
1. Depth ofunderstanding 
If the only time students delve deep into a topic with challenging questions is when they are learning that topic, then they never have an opportunity to retrieve that deeper level of understanding, and hence may forget it. 
2. The message 
If our retrieval opportunities consist solely of procedural, recall¡style questions, then what message do we convey to our students? Probably that such a surface level of understanding is all they need. This could affect the type of revision they do, swimming around the surface of a topic instead of diving deep.

school trip

The ratio of pupils to stafffor a school trip is 25:3. How can the actual numbers be adjusted so that the ratio is 15:1? 
Many teachers will use this variety of questions in their teaching, but how many of them do you use in your retrieval opportunities? 
When to use each type of question As we discussed in Tip 82, a mean score of 80% is a good figure to aim for when designing the content of our retrieval opportunities, 
whether they be our Do Nows, Low-Stakes Quizzes or homework. But it is not just the subject content of our retrieval opportunities that determines the difficulty, but the nature ofthe questions them¡selves. A relatively straightforward question on a challenging topic may actually be more accessible to students than a challenging question on a topic that students have achieved some degree offlu¡ency in. Having a variety of question types at our disposal allows us to be more creative when aiming for 80%. 
I think a sensible approach is to vary the types of questions we ask in each of our retrieval opportunities. So, a four-question Do Now might consist of two procedural questions on topics that are either relatively new to students or that they have struggled with. But then for the final two questions on topics that students are more secure on, you could choose some of the other question types described above. Same for Low-Stakes Quizzes and homework. This keeps the challenge level high, and also sends the important message that retrieval is not all about remembering disconnected rules and pro¡cedures, but about securing a deeper understanding of concepts. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider varying the types of questions you ask in your retrieval opportunities to maintain challenge and show students that retrieval is not all about facts and procedures.

Tip 84 -Consider providing prompts and cues during retrieval opportunities

What's the problem? 
We know that students learn when they think hard. The best type of retrieval for boosting storage and retrieval strength of knowledge is that which has no cues -a question and a blank piece ofpaper. Students have to use knowledge in their long-term memories to for¡mulate a solution. That is all they will have with them in the exam, so that was all I used to provide them with during retrieval opportu¡nities -Do Nows, Low-Stakes Quizzes and homework. 
But there is a powerful counter-argument to this: there is no point in doing retrieval if students cannot remember anything. 
A student staring at a blank piece of paper for 10 minutes is likely to learn very little. A nudge in the right direction may be exactly what they need to make the retrieval opportunity a worthwhile learning 
.
experience. But what should that nudge look like? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you provide prompts and cues to support students during retrieval opportunities? 

ò 
Ifso, what do they look like? 


What's the tip? 
It was retrieval expert Kate Jones that really got me thinking about prompts and cues. 
Her first tip is entitled 'Sometimes it is better to review than re¡trieve'. Kate makes the point that if retrieval strength for knowledge is particularly low, then a student may simply not be able to recall it. This can happen if a topic has not been revisited for a long period of time. Of course, the better solution is to ensure we constantly provide retrieval opportunities for everything we teach students (Tip 81), but circumstances may prevent this (after a holiday, an illness, a student joining a class, etc.). In these cases, independent retrieval may not be possible, but a full reteach may not be needed. A prompt like the ones we will discuss below might be enough to trigger that memory so students can complete the task they have been set. 
Kate's second tip is 'Make use of the encoding specificity principle'. My wife is sick of me going on about this. It is the idea that if you provide a cue during the teaching of an idea -for example, an image, a sound or a phrase -then providing that cue alongside a question on that topic during a subsequent retrieval opportunity might be enough to help students remember what to do. 
So, what might these prompts and cues look like? Here are 10 ideas. 
1. Allow notes 
You could allow students to have their notes with them while they work through the retrieval opportunity. As well as providing prompts and cues, this may also encourage students to take neater, more useful notes during worked examples (Tip 65). 
2. Allow knowledge organisers 
A good knowledge organiser -one that is quizzable -may support students in the same way as notes do. It may just help them re¡member a key fact or strategy that will allow them to make a start on the question. 
3. Provide worked examples Providing a complete worked example may help students be more successful in solving a related problem. This can be improved by adding well-written self-explanation prompts (Tip 64) that allow 
students to think deeper about the worked example and how the steps could transfer across to related problems. 
Jake Gordon takes this to a new level. When students complete a retrieval Do Now, Jake places a complete worked example related to each question on the reverse ofthe sheet. The idea is that students first attempt each question, but ifthey get stuck, they can turn over the paper, study the related worked example, and then try the question again. I love this. The same could be used for Low-Stakes Quizzes and homework. 
4. Provide a series ofbackwards-faded worked examples 
In backwards-faded worked examples, students are required to try to find a solution to the last step on problem one, the last two steps on problem two, and so on. In other words, as students' under¡standing and confidence grow, they are required to do more steps independently. A series ofthese worked examples could be pro¡vided, allowing students to build up to solving the question in the retrieval opportunity independently. 
5. Provide self-explanation prompts If you used self-explanation prompts during your worked example 
(Tip 64), then simply providing these without an accompanying worked example might be support enough. 
6. Provide answers without working out 
If the questions require several steps, you could provide students with the final answer and challenge them to fill in the working out. As well as providing a prompt to shape their thinking, this approach is particularly useful for less confident students who may know how to answer a question but refuse to commit to paper unless they are 100% sure they are correct. 
7. Peer collaboration You could allow students to work together during retrieval oppor¡tunities to provide each other with cues and prompts. The obvious danger is the illusion ofcollective wisdom -if a pair successfully com¡pletes a retrieval opportunity, how do they and I know they could have done so independently? So, if allowing collaboration during retrieval opportunities, I would suggest doing something like the 4-2 
approach -four minutes of silent, independent work, followed by two minutes of focused collaboration (Tip 15). 
8. Tell students the topics in advance 
Telling students the topics that will be on a Do Now or a Low-Stakes Quiz allows them to engage in focused revision. If students could be quizzed on the whole of a subject, they have no opportunity to prepare. 
9. Same quiz, different questions 
Continuing on this theme, several schools I work with use the same topics for a series of retrieval opportunities -a Do Now or a Low¡Stakes Quiz -but change the questions each time. Again, this al¡lows students to have an opportunity to do some preparation, and also allows them to see the progress they are making. The downside is that it reduces the number of different topics you can quiz stu¡dents on over the course of a year. 
10. Teacher support 
I have saved the most obvious one for last. If we circulate the room while students are working on the retrieval opportunity, we can 
answer any questions they may have, provide a hint ifwe see they are struggling, or push them back on the right path if we have seen they are going wrong. Of course, we need to be careful that we do not become a permanent crutch for our students, always on hand to help whether they really need it or not. 
Here are four things to bear in mind with these prompts and cues. 
1. Prompts and cues should not be provided on a plate 
If students can retrieve successfully without support, then they should be allowed to. Having the prompts and cues only visible by turning over a page or requesting them is important. 
2. Be aware ofunintended prompts and cues 
Classroom displays are the worst offenders here. If a question on your Low-Stakes Quiz is about prime numbers, and on the wall next to a child is a lovely, colourful poster that displays the prime numbers from 1 to 100, then the student gets a helpful nudge even though they may not have needed it. 
3. Retrieval should still be effortful 
With all these prompts, it is important that the students should still have to put in the effort to make use of them. A good analogy is someone attempting a bench press in a gym. They have stacked the weights, they are trying to lift, but they can't quite make it. Here a mate -called a 'spotter' -is needed. They give that extra support in the final push to get that barbell up, but they do not do the heavy lifting. We are the spotters, providing enough support to get our students going, but then they must do the hard work in terms of retrieval. 
4. Eventually we remove the prompts and cues 
Our ultimate aim, of course, is that students can remember things without this support. Each time our students successfully retrieve something, the next time that topic appears in a retrieval opportu¡nity we should look to reduce the support available. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Ifstudents are struggling to remember, consider providing promptsand cues to support students' retrieval.

What's the problem? 
When going through the answers to a retrieval opportunity-a Do Now, Low-Stakes Quiz or homework -there are two problems I reg¡ularly encounter: 
1. 
Students are more concerned about their scores than correcting their wrong answers. 

2. 
Those students who do want to engage in the correction process do not know where to start, so just begin with the first question they do wrong. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to these problems? 

ò
Have you ever tried asking students to assign confidence scores? 


What's the tip? 
I am not going to lie: I am obsessed with this next tip. I have been experimenting with it for the last five years, and it was the subject of the first research paper I co-authored with Colin Foster, Alison Clark-Wilson, and my Diagnostic Questions co-founder, Simon Woodhead. 
The Hypercorrection Effect 
The relationship between students' confidence and their under¡standing is fascinating. Back in Tip 21 we discussed how students' perception oftheir understanding does not necessarily match their actual understanding. But here I want to focus on the role confi¡dence can play in alleviating misunderstanding. 
Let's imagine Jenny feels really confident about question 7 on a Low-Stakes Quiz, and not confident at all about question 8. Jenny gets both questions wrong. Which question is she best focusing her attention on first during the review process? 
The answer from our research is clear: question 7. This is all due to the Hypercorrection Effect, which states that errors endorsed with higher confidence are more likely to be corrected on a final test than errors endorsed with lower confidence. If Jenny thinks hard about ques¡tion 7 and receives feedback that she understands (usually in the form of a correct worked solution), she will be more likely to get a similar question correct in the future than if she expended the same effort on question 8. 
The mechanisms of the Hypercorrection Effect are not fully under¡stood. I put it down to a cognitive shock. I have seen students make these high confidence errors, and when they realise, they seem to sit up, pay attention, and then work with a steely determination to get to the bottom ofit. The same does not happen with errors stu¡dents expect to make. 
The Hypercorrection Effect is powerful, but how do we harness it in the classroom? 
Quite simply, we ask students to assign confidence scores to their answers before they know what the correct answer is. Here are eight ideas for making this work well. 
1. Do this for a variety ofwork 
You can ask students to assign confidence scores for a Do Now, Low-Stakes Quiz, homework or classwork. Anything where students do not yet know the correct answer. This helps it become part of students' routines. Ben Gordon did this for a mock exam he gave his students. Usually, students just look at their final score, their mates' final score, and then put the paper away. But Ben asked his students to allocate confidence scores to each answer before they handed their work in. When he gave the papers back, he tasked his students with finding and trying to correct their three highest confi¡dence errors. 
2. Choose your confidence scale 
There is nothing in the research to suggest one confidence scale is better than any other. I use Oto 10, but I have seen other schools use 1 to 5, or even a series of faces, from truly fed up to absolutely delighted. 
3. Tell students why you are asking them to do this 
This is always an important step in the launch of a new initiative. Discuss the Hypercorrection Effect if appropriate. The good thing about assigning confidence scores is it is not a lot of extra effort for students, so you are unlikely to get many protests or moans. 
4. Have students assign confidence scores after their work, not as they go 
The obvious thing to do is to ask students to assign confidence scores after each question when it is freshest in their minds. But I ask them to do it at the end of the retrieval opportunity. Making it a separate event elevates its importance. But there is another reason. I can think of no phrase that has less of an impact on students than 'check your work'. In one ear, out the other. But asking them to go back and assign confidence scores when they have finished forces them to review their work as they remind themselves how they felt about that question. Often students will pick up on little mistakes they made as they do this. 
5. When students review their mistakes, tell them to start with their highest con¡fidence error 
This is the big one. Starting to think hard about the highest con¡fidence error gives students the biggest bang for their buck as it allows the Hypercorrection Effect to kick in. As we will see in Tip 87, this is why I project full written solutions on the board for Low¡Stakes Quizzes and Do Nows -instead of modelling each question one at a time -because I want students to focus their attention on the question they need when they have the most attention and time available. And this will be different for different pupils. The highest 
confidence error is where students need to start, followed by the next highest confidence error, and so on. 
6. Be clear on your messaging to students who have made lots oferrors 
Confidence scores provide a structure to the reviewing and correct¡ing process that is often lacking, and this is important for students who are struggling. What do you say to the student who has scored 2/10 on their Low-Stakes Quiz and is feeling overwhelmed with the work they now have to do? Well, with confidence scores we can say, 'Look, let's get your highest confidence errors sorted first. Don't worry about everything else for now.' 
7. Improving the accuracy ofstudents' confidence scores Some students do not think hard about their confidence scores. I have seen lots of students just stick down a 5/10 to hedge their bets. 
This then makes the review process less effective as the student may not know which errors truly were the high confidence ones. 
One way to deal with this is to ban the use of 5/10 so students have to at least commit to being fairly confident or fairly unconfident. Another option is to introduce confidence-based marking. Here students' scores per question are determined by their confidence score. So, if a student gives a confidence score of 7 /10 for a question, they score seven points if correct, but lose seven points if wrong. This certainly provides an incentive to be more accurate when assigning confidence scores. Beware though: this can lead to negative overall scores which may affect morale. Also, calculating these final scores may prove more cognitively demanding than completing the re¡trieval opportunity itself. 
8. Ensure corrective feedback is clear 
This last tip is obvious, but should not be underrated. For the Hyper¡correction Effect to work, students need to know not only that they are wrong, but why they are wrong. So, however we choose to go through the solutions to a retrieval opportunity, we need to ensure our explanation ofthe correct method is clear, and that students are willing to ask us if they still do not understand. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider asking students to assign confidence scores to any work where they do not yet know the correct answer.

~ 
What's the problem? 
Picture the scene. You have set your students a task to complete -an exercise in class, a Low-Stakes Quiz, homework -anything where they do not yet know the correct answers. You then go through the answers, either verbally or by projecting them up on 
the board. Your students mark their own work, and then you tell them to correct anything they have got wrong. All pretty standard stuff. But here are two questions for you: 
1. 
Where do students do their corrections? 

2. 
What do you hope they will do with those corrections in the future? 


The usual answers I get from teachers -and these are the exact same answers I would have given for about 12 years -are: 
1. 
My students do their corrections on their work, whether this be a sheet ofpaper or in their exercise books. 

2. 
My students will use their corrections to revise or improve their work in the future. 


There are two issues with this. 
First, many of the corrections students make have little meaning to them. There is nothing magical that happens in the act offrantically scrawling down the correct answer to a question if the answer makes little sense. Moreover, if students have made lots of mis¡takes, and hence have many corrections to copy down, there is a decent chance they will make some errors with copying, which de¡feats the whole object of corrections. 
Second, corrections on the original piece of work are in a format that makes revising or learning from them in the future really tricky. 
We know from papers such as Dunlosky's 'Strengthening the stu¡dent toolbox' that rereading is, in the majority of cases, a far inferior learning strategy to testing. And yet, rereading is all students are able to do when corrections surround their work. If we want our students to test themselves to see if they can now do something they had previously got wrong -or indeed, if our students want to test themselves -then they would have to find the piece ofwork that contains that mistake (easier said than done ifit is a loose piece of paper or a random page in an exercise book), and then somehow try to avoid seeing the corrections and correct answer when looking at the question. 
The more I think about it, and the more I speak to students about what they actually do with their corrections (most say they never look at them again), the more I am convinced that we need to find a better way. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Are you happy with your correction process? 

ò 
What couldyou do to improve it?


I I 
What's the tip? 
This is something I learned years ago from Australian teacher, author and fellow podcaster Ollie Lovell when I interviewed him on my Mr Barton Maths podcast. Ollie described a process he uses when giving his senior students back their assignments, and a light bulb went off in my head. For the last few years I have been trialling this out with students of different ages around the country, and it seems to work well. 
The key is to make student corrections have meaning and be quizzable. 
I mainly use this approach on mixed-topic Low-Stakes Quizzes in mathematics. But I see no reason why it would not work for differ¡ent types of tasks. 
After my students have marked their work, I ask them to begin the correction process. They start by finding their highest confidence error (Tip 85). With the highest confidence error identified, students begin creating their first review card: 
Front of card: 
ò 
Write the topic the question is from -I can help my students with this by labelling questions with the relevant topic. 

ò 
Write the reference to where students can go for further help or practice -I label each question with this as well. 

ò 
Copy out the question -ifit is a long one or requires a diagram, I will consider printing it out for students so they can cut and stick. I choose the option that will take the least time. 


Back of card: 
ò 
A brief description ofthe mistake they made -students may spot this themselves or it can come from a discussion with their partner. 

ò 
The correct solution -I always project the full written solutions on the board, but if students are still struggling to make sense of it, they can ask me or their partner. I encourage students to annotate the solution to increase its meaning. 


AS card works best for maths (white or pastel colours ifwe are really getting specific). 
In the end, students have a card that looks like this (front ofthe card on the left, back of the card on the right): 
Topic: Adding Fractions Ref: clip 27 
2 
+
3 5 
(Smistai) 
I added tl-ie numerators and denominators' Tfie denominators need to be trie same' 11 
~  
15 is tfie  
.1.. +  _I  lowest aommon  
3  5  mumpte  
2 '1 5  l'/ 3  
~  10  3 k"  

=15+15 
13 
=¡15 ~ Don't add t rie denominators! 
If there is time, students then move on to their second highest con¡fidence error and create a card for that. I find that most students can complete three cards in a 10-minute period. Of course, they could complete a lot more corrections by copying them down frantically from the board, but we are going for quality over quantity here. 
After doing this for a while, students end up with a stack of cards that have the potential to be a highly effective learning resource for the following two reasons. 
1. They are personalised to the student 
Everyone's stack of cards will be different, containing questions specific to that student. The labelling of the topic on each card helps the students know where they need to focus their revision when they use other resources, such as websites, revision guides or videos, and the reference can direct them to somewhere specific (see 'More support and practice?' below). 
2. The cards are quizzable 
This is the big one. At any time -the end of a lesson, during tutor time, etc. -students can get out their cards. They start with the top card, look at the question, and on a second piece of paper try to work out the answer. They can then turn over the card and check. If they get the question correct, then they put the card at the bottom ofthe pile. If they get it wrong, they study the worked solution, seek help if it doesn't make sense, and return the card midway through the deck so it bubbles up to the top quicker. 
Once students are in the flow of this, I also encourage them to reflect on their original mistake, and see ifthey can understand why they made it. 
More support and practice? 
The reference on the front of the card could be a page number of a revision guide or textbook, it could refer to an online programme the school uses, such as DrFrostMaths, Sparx or MathsWatch, or it could be a shortened URL that takes students to a YouTube clip. If students attempt the question on the card in the future and either want some more support because they are stuck, or some more practice to develop fluency, they've got it in one place. 
Where are the revision cards kept? 
I don't let my students take home their revision cards in case they lose them. I keep them either in plastic wallets or envelopes. This changes nearer to an exam, when I allow students to take them home. Some students choose to take photos of the front and back of their cards and upload the images to digital flash card apps such as Quizlet. That way they always have a copy of their cards on them. 
Like everything, not all students will buy into this. But I am pleas¡antly surprised just how many students do. And as well as helping with the specific errors identified on the cards, it also shows stu¡dents the power of testing themselves as a general revision strategy versus rereading. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider having students create review cards instead of doing corrections on the source material. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip86
