Tip 87 -Twenty-one ideas to improve your Low-Stakes Quizzes 

Tip 76 Forgetting Curve I  Tip 77 Storage & retrievaI  Tip 78 Working memory I  Tip 81 Al l content I  Tlp82 Aim for80%  Tip 83 Vary the questions I  
Get students on board ò I I I Tip 79 Tip80Long-term Familiaritymemory  I Tip84 Prompts & cues  General principles Tip 85 Confidence scores  I Tip86 Corrections  Tip 87 Low-Stakes Qu iz  
Tip89 Trello  Tip88 Do Now  

What's the problem? 
At the start ofthis chapter, we discussed how lethal mutations have become associated with a focus on retrieval practice in schools. One area where this is particularly visible is the Low-Stakes Quiz. 
This is a regular review of previously learned content where the stakes are significantly lower than a summative assessment, such as an end-of-term or end-of-year test. This sounds like a great idea. We have a mountain of research to support the power of regular retrieval opportunities, and the low-stakes nature should help alle¡viate some of the anxiety that students may feel with higher-stakes summative assessments. 
But not all Low-Stakes Quizzes are created equally. And ifwe are not careful, they can become something that eats into lesson time, creates a load of additional work for the teacher and -and this is the kicker -has very little positive impact on student learning. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you do Low-Stakes Quizzes with your students? 

ò 
Ifso, what are their key features? 


What's the tip? 
I have been experimenting with Low-Stakes Quizzes in mathemat¡ics across many different settings and contexts over the last five years. While there is definitely no single format that is going to 
work for everyone, there are certain features that raise the probabil¡ity that a Low-Stakes Quiz will be effective and sustainable. 
Here are 21 ideas. 
1. Call them quizzes, not tests 
The word 'test' has negative connotations for many students. They may associate it with feelings of anxiety, embarrassment or failure, or worry that it will lead to set changes or parents being informed. Rebranding what we are doing as a Low-Stakes Quiz may help with this. It also provides an opportunity to develop a shared language. However we decide to run our Low-Stakes Quizzes, ifwe call them this then everybody -colleagues and students -knows what we are referring to. 
2. Make the quizzes mixed-topic 
One of the suggestions from Carpenter et al. in their 2012 paper is 'teachers should give exams and quizzes that are cumulative'. This is very different to the themed end-of-unit tests I dished out for most of my early career. Here are four reasons why having mixed¡topic Low-Stakes Quizzes is a good idea: 
1. 
They allow students to benefit from the spacing effect. Instead ofbeing challenged to retrieve a topic once, students get to retrieve it several times over a prolonged period, getting a boost in stor¡age and retrieval strength each time. 

2. 
Topic-specific quizzes may lead students to believe that cramming is an effective revision strategy. If a quiz will be on one specific topic, and you hammer that topic the night before, you will probably do pretty well on it. If students repeat this strategy on all topic-specific tests throughout the year, they are likely to come unstuck when they encounter a less predictable test -for example, a high-stakes GCSE exam. 

3. 
Mixed-topic quizzes allow students to practise switching. All high-stakes tests in maths are mixed-topic. Answering a question on percentages, then switching to a question on sequences, and then a question on angle relationships can be challenging. Having regular opportunities to practise such switching will be beneficial. 


4. Students and teachers are at less risk ofbeing followed by performance. If 
students are taught ideas in nice tidy blocks, and then assessed on those ideas at the end of those nice tidy blocks, they will probably do pretty well on them. If this continues throughout the year, we may conclude that students understand our sub¡ject well -after all, that is what the data shows. But we know learning happens over the long term, and the only way to see if something has stuck is to provide several opportunities for stu¡dents to retrieve it. Mixed-topic quizzes allow for this. 
3. Quiz the things that are hard to quiz 
In Tip 81 we discussed the need to ensure we provide retrieval opportunities for all content we want our students to remember. Low-Stakes Quizzes are better suited to those harder-to-quiz topics that require students to have equipment, such as for measuring angles (protractor), constructions (compass) or straight line graphs (grids and rulers), than a Do Now, which usually lasts less time, or homework where students might not have the equipment to hand. You could rip off the plaster and choose one week to get a load of your tricky-to-quiz questions out of the way, instead of spreading the pain. 
4. Think carefully about the topics you include 
Referring again to Tip 81, we discussed keeping track of the topics included in retrieval opportunities to ensure nothing gets left out for too long. When compiling Low-Stakes Quizzes it is a good idea to in¡clude questions on: 
1. 
Topics students have recently encountered. We know the rate of forgetting is at its highest immediately after something has been taught. 

2. 
Topics students struggled with on previous retrieval opportu¡nities, but which you have since retaught. Quizzing students on these topics is important to see if that intervention was successful. 

3. 
Topics students were successful with on a previous retrieval opportunity, but which they have not encountered for a long time. This ensures retrieval strength does not dip so low that students cannot retrieve it at all. 

4. 
Topics that students have been successful on recently, and now you want to develop that understanding further. 


5. Vary the types ofquestions you ask Most of the Low-Stakes Quizzes I see -and have administered -are dominated by factual recall or procedural-type questions. These are, of course, important, but they are certainly not the apex of un¡derstanding. If that is the only type of retrieval practice we give our 
students, their understanding will likely remain shallow, and we convey the message that this type of knowledge is all we require. 
In Tip 83 we looked at 20 different types of questions we could include in our retrieval opportunities. In a 10-question Low-Stakes Quiz, here is how we might choose to allocate them: 
ò 
Questions 1 to 5: procedural style questions on content that is relatively new to students, that they have struggled with be¡fore, or that they have not encountered for a long time. 

ò 
Questions 6 to 10: more challenging question types on content that students have retrieved successfully recently. Going deeper into the wrong answers to a diagnostic question, gen¡erating examples and non-examples, and completing an SSDD problem are three of my favourite more challenging question types. 


6. Aim for a mean score of 80% 
In Tip 82 we discussed the importance of not making retrieval opportunities too easy or too hard. Aiming for a mean score of 80% in your Low-Stakes Quizzes is a sensible approach. This will mean using your knowledge ofyour students to select both the topics and the types of questions you ask accordingly. 
7. Tell students (and colleagues) why you are doing Low-Stakes Quizzes This was a massive error I made. I got all excited about Low-Stakes Quizzes, and dived in headfirst. My students did not know what had hit them. They assumed this was some high-stakes test and as such 
got all worried. Sharing with students the reasons behind any sig¡nificant change in practice is always a good idea. 
But so too is sharing the reasoning with colleagues. Before anyone knew it, it was a departmental policy that every class would have a Low-Stakes Quiz every week. Because I had not taken the time to explain the rationale to colleagues, they did not take them as seri¡ously as I did. As a result, they would happen later in the week and towards the end of the lesson, when there was not enough time left to do them properly and students were tired. A far better approach that I see in schools is where one or two colleagues try out different formats with one or two of their classes until they find a way that works well. They then present this to the staff, sharing what works really well, along with the mistakes they have made and the lessons learned along the way. Then build a departmental policy from there. 
8. Run the quizzes on the same day and at the same time ofthe lesson each week 
Chapter 2 was all about establishing and maintaining routines. I want Low-Stakes Quizzes to become a routine. As such I will run them the same way each week. Part of this involves having a set day for Low-Stakes Quizzes for a given class so they get used to it and know what to expect. Likewise, starting them at the same time each lesson helps firm up this routine. I like to start them at the be¡ginning of the lesson so I don't get caught up in something else and run out of time, and so I can dedicate time if needed to immediate reteaching. But it is whatever works best for you and your students. 
9. Where possible, print the quizzes out 
In maths, there is always the temptation to project questions up on the board. It is quick and saves time and money in the photocopy¡ing queue. But for Low-Stakes Quizzes, printing out is a good idea for these reasons: 
1. 
Students are more focused as they don't have to keep looking up to see the next question. 

2. 
Students at the back of the class are not disadvantaged. 

3. 
You can ask a wider variety of questions, especially those that involve diagrams that the students need to write upon. 


10. Students should answer on their own and in silence 
I want to know what the students know. I want the students to know what they know. The best way to achieve this is to start the quizzes out in silence so students can work independently, at their own pace, in a calm, focused environment. 
11. Carefully consider prompts and cues 
In an ideal world, students would be able to work through our Low¡Stakes Quizzes without any support. But that is not always the case, and students are likely to learn very little ifthey are just staring at a question, clueless about how to start. 
In Tip 84 we discussed 10 different types ofprompts and cues you might provide for your students. Of these, a related worked exam¡ple for each question on the back of the Low-Stakes Quiz sheets works really well. It is just the right level of support to get students going, but it still means the student does the heavy lifting in terms of thinking. Having it on the other side of the page also means that students have an opportunity to think before accessing the help. 
12. Have a flexible time limit 
Time limits in teaching are always flexible. When I say to students 'five minutes more on this task', that could mean anything from 30 seconds to half an hour. So what follows below are my rough tim¡ings. As each Low-Stakes Quiz plays out, I will circulate the room and adjust these timings accordingly. 
ò 
Working on the questions: 15 minutes 

ò 
Confidence scores: 2 minutes 

ò 
Marking: 2 minutes 

ò 
Review cards: 10 minutes 

ò 
AfL check: 1 minute 


ò Total: 30 minutes I recommend one Low-Stakes Quiz per week, per class. Over the course of the next few tips, we will dig into what these 
later steps of the process entail. 
13. Have students assign confidence scores before the answers 
This was the big idea from Tip 85, and it works really well with Low¡Stakes Quizzes. You will notice in my timings that assigning confi¡dence scores is a separate event that happens after students have completed all they can on the quiz. This elevates its importance in the eyes of students, and provides a check-your-work-via-stealth as students review their answers to assign their scores. 
14. Don't model solutions; project up full written solutions 
A controversial one here. My approach for many years was to go through how you do question 1, then how you do question 2, and so on. Often I would involve the students and get them to take us through their approach. I no longer do this for two reasons: 
1. 
It takes ages. 

2. 
It is not the best use of student time. 


Let's delve into that second reason. Imagine Charlie has got ques¡tions 9 and 10 wrong, but everything else is right. Is it really in Charlie's best interests to make him sit through the answers to the first eight questions? Sure, he may benefit from seeing how you and other students approach the solution, but not as much as he would benefit from spending extra time thinking about questions 9 and 10. 
Or Katie who has got nine out of the 10 questions wrong. Surely the best thing for her is to see how to do all of the questions? I am not so sure. I cannot dedicate the time Katie needs as I go through each question, so Katie is likely to be rushed, overwhelmed and demor¡alised. Instead, I want Katie to focus her attention on one or two of these questions and ensure she really understands how to do them. These questions will be her highest confidence errors. 
So, now I project up full written solutions, complete with annota¡tions. These remain on the board throughout the marking process and the creation of review cards so students can refer to them in their subsequent student discussions. Students can therefore focus their attention and spend their time on the questions that they need help with, instead of waiting for me to speed up or hoping I slow down. 
Students may still have some questions, but they have access to help when creating their review cards via paired discussions and me if needed. And if my final AfL check reveals that one question is particularly troublesome, then I can go through this in detail. 
15. Include the topic and references to support and practice 
Alongside each worked solution I write down the topic that the question belongs to, and reference any additional support or prac¡tice available. The topic allows students to apply labels to areas of maths that they need further support with. The references -these could be pages of a textbook or revision guide, or clip numbers on DrFrostMaths, MathsWatch or Sparx -direct students to where they can go for that support. This information will then go onto the re¡view cards students are about to create. 
16. Students mark their own work with ticks and crosses 
There is an argument for teachers to mark Low-Stakes Quizzes. Some students may prefer it, and it may help them take the quizzes more seriously. If the point ofyour Low-Stakes Quiz is to have an in-depth knowledge ofwhat students understand, then you proba¡bly need to mark it. 
But I let my students mark their own quizzes. I want them to see this as a tool of learning, not a tool of assessment. There is no in¡centive to cheat as I will not be taking the marks in. I want students to be able to identify what they can do and what they cannot in as little time as possible, so they can then dedicate time to learning from their mistakes. Sure, some students will take advantage of this and do very little, but you should be able to pick up on this as you circulate the room ... and some students will find ways to beat any system. 
17. Students create review cards, starting with the highest confidence error 
We discussed review cards in Tip 86. I give students 10 minutes to create as many as possible, with a target of three. They start with their highest confidence error, copying the question, topic and re¡vision reference on one side, and their description of their mistake and the fully worked solution on the other. 
If they struggle when creating the revision cards, I encourage them to speak to their partner, and if that fails, they can ask me for help. These cards are then saved to produce a personalised, quizzable re¡
. . 
v1s1on resource. 
18. Promote the message 'helping others is the hardest job in the classroom' 
But what about the student who has just one card to create, or even no cards? The temptation might be to come up with some extension material, but then who is going to support the student with it and mark their attempt? No, instead I revert back to Sammy Kempner's mantra that 'helping others is the hardest job in the classroom' (Tip 16). I tell students that if they have created their review cards and don't have any questions, then the real work begins. Their job is to help students around them understand anything they are confused with and support them in creating their review cards. If I make a point of quizzing a student who is struggling on a question, and ei¡ther holding to account or praising their partner depending on their answer, then there is an extra incentive to do a good job here. 
19. Do a super-quick Assessment for Learning (AfL) check 
While I will not be taking their scores in -more on that in a minute -I still want to get a sense ofwhere my students are struggling. So, I may say, 'Are there any questions that anyone is still struggling with?' or 'Hands up ifyou are struggling with question 10.' 
The problem with these approaches is that nobody wants to be the first to say they are struggling. That is why I like the suggestion of Tom Sherrington that we discussed in Tip 31: start with the students who got 8 out of 10. If you say, 'Hands-up ifyou got 8/10' and then say, 'Wow, that is a good score, well done. Which questions did you get wrong?' they are happy to share. You may then find when you ask if anybody else got these questions wrong, students are more forth¡coming and you get a better check for understanding. 
20. Plan when you will reteach 
Following the AfL check, you can then decide what you want to do about it. Are you going to try to fix any problems there and then in the lesson? Or are you going to make a note -not a mental one -to come back to this topic at a later date? 
Because here is the thing. If students really struggle with a question, then the chances are they are going to need more than a model solution to understand it. So, don't include a similar question on a subsequent retrieval opportunity until you have had an opportunity to reteach it. This does not mean spending a full lesson on it. But it may mean another explanation, another worked example, and another Your Turn. Then include a related question on a retrieval opportunity-a Do Now, Low-Stakes Quiz or homework-within the next few days to see if things are moving in the right direction. 
21. Resist the urge to record the scores 
Data, data, data! I know it is tempting to collect students' scores, enter them into a spreadsheet, and let conditional formatting go to work. Before you know it, you have a beautifully traffic-lighted piece of statistical artwork in front ofyou. But here is the big ques¡tion: what are you going to do with it? If it is going to inform your actions in the classroom, then there is a case for it. But couldn't you have got that in the way described above by simply eliciting from students where they are struggling? That takes seconds, versus the time taken to effectively produce a Question Level Analysis each week. 
If you are going to average out the scores, then what does an aver¡age score of, say, 76% actually mean? And ifyou are going to track student progress, then because these are mixed-topic quizzes with different topics covered each week, we should not expect students to score higher and higher each week because the content is forever changing. 
No, I am convinced that the best thing to do at the end of a Low¡Stakes Quiz is to just walk away. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Evaluate how successful your Low-Stakes Quizzes are, and ifthere is potential to use some ofthese ideas to improve them. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip87

3.

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time?

Tip 88 
Trello 
Do Now 
What's the problem? 
In Tip 87 we discussed one potential retrieval lethal mutation in the form of the Low-Stakes Quiz. Now let us dive headfirst into another one... the Do Now. 
Many schools mandate that lessons should start in a specific way, often called the Do Now. This is designed to settle the students into a frame of mind ready to work hard. But many Do Nows are also expected to do other things, such as provide retrieval opportunities, assess prerequisite knowledge, assess student effort in homework, or prime students for the upcoming topic. 
Needless to say, some Do Nows are more effective than others. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò What does the start of your lesson look like? 
ò How effective is it in doing what you want it to do? 
What's the tip? 
I am obsessed with the start of lessons. They set the tone for what follows. I like watching what the teacher does and what the stu¡dents do, and then discussing the purpose and enactment with the teacher afterwards. I have seen hundreds of starts of lessons over the last few years, and learned a lot. Here are 15 ideas for ensuring the start of the lesson -the Do Now -is as effective as possible. 
1. Decide on the purpose ofyour Do Now 
Here are seven different purposes I have seen for Do Nows: 
1. 
Act as a settler. You want to provide a calm, focused start to the lesson. Perhaps students arrive at different times because the lesson is after lunch, after PE, or because some students some¡how get lost on the way. You have things you need to do -take the register, collect homework, have a word with a student, wait for the computer to spring to life -and while you are doing this you want your students to have something to be getting on with. 

2. 
Act as a primer. You want students to be thinking about the content of the upcoming lesson. Perhaps you provide a visual hook and ask students to write down what they notice or what questions they have. Perhaps you write down a key word or phrase and challenge students to write down everything that they know about it. Perhaps you ask them a question, such as 'where have you heard the word "average" in the real world?' 

3. 
Act as a check ofrelevant prior knowledge. We discussed the importance of addressing relevant prior knowledge in Chapter 7. This ensures students have a solid foundation upon which to build a new idea. Perhaps the Do Now is the place you choose to as¡sess this prior knowledge, either via a series of open-response questions that students complete in their books or on mini¡whiteboards, or multiple-choice diagnostic questions that stu¡dents use a voting system to respond to. 

4. 
Act as a retrieval opportunity. All through this chapter, we have discussed the importance of providing retrieval opportunities for content students have learned in the past, and the Do Now could be one place for that. Again, the questions could be open response or diagnostic, and the topics could be chosen from any time period. 

5. 
Act as a direct response to the last lesson. Your Do Now might be based on the last lesson's checks for understanding or Exit Tickets (Tip 29). If students have struggled, you might have a correct worked example on the board for them to study, alongside a re¡lated Your Turn question for them to try. 

6. 
Act as a response to homework. You could use the Do Now to address difficulties students had with their homework. You could select 


the worst three answered questions, provide a worked example 
for each, and then set students the challenge of completing 
three related problems, first independently, and then in pairs. 
We will discuss the importance ofhomework feeding into 
lessons in Tip 90. 
7. Act as a support for homework. This is from Adam Boxer, and I really like it. Adam sets his students homework that they can mark themselves (he uses an online flash card system called Carousel Learning). Students know in advance that the next lesson's Do Now will be on a selection of questions from that homework. If students do badly at the Do Now, they have no excuse as they knew exactly what they were going to be tested on. Hence, the Do Now supports Adam's homework policy. 
Many Do Nows attempt to serve multiple purposes. For example, a classic Do Now structure in maths is this: 

Do Now 

Last topic
Last lesson 
I 
"I 
I 
: I 8cm 
Name the four different types of 
I
I
Find the area of 
I 
transformation. 
I I
this triangle I

factors. 
This is a settler in the sense that it should allow students to work on it in a calm, focused way. It is also a retrieval opportunity with questions on topics students have encountered in the past. And it is also a relevant prior knowledge check as the question from the last lesson is likely to be relevant for the new idea students are learning today. 
The danger with multipurpose Do Nows is that they become a jack of all trades, but a master of none. Looking at the example above, it is unlikely that one question will be enough to assess relevant prior knowledge. So the Do Now might be improved by replacing the first question with another question from an unrelated topic, and then having a separate prior knowledge check (maybe involving diagnos¡tic questions) after this. 
The purpose of the Do Now may change across a week. At the start of a new topic, the Do Now may be a prior knowledge check. But if lesson two is a continuation of the idea from lesson one, then the Do Now may be a retrieval starter. And iflesson three is after lunch and you know the time interval between your first student arriving and your last one arriving could be five minutes or longer, then you might decide to do more of a settler activity because you need ev¡eryone to be present and focused for whatever comes next. 
Before we move on, take a few moments to consider what the pur¡pose ofyour Do Now is, and if it is serving its purpose. 
2. Ensure your students know the purpose ofyour Do Now As I mentioned above, I always watch how students behave at the start of the lesson. A trend I see regularly is that students put far less effort into the Do Now than they do when the lesson actually 'starts' in their eyes -i.e. when the teacher introduces a new idea. I often ask a student who is gazing out of the window during a Do Now or copying down the title at a snail's pace why they are not 
working harder, and the response is always a version of 'it's just the starter'. 
A theme throughout this book has been to ensure students know why we are asking them to do things. The first few tips in this chap¡ter were dedicated to demonstrations to achieve exactly that for retrieval opportunities. The same is true for the Do Now. If students think that this is just some kind of optional warm-up for them to at¡tempt while the teacher gets ready to start the lesson properly, then they will put little effort into it. And worse still, that lack of effort may be hidden from us as they quickly copy the answers down off the board when we go through them. 
If we want students to put in the necessary effort to learn, then we need to explain why we are doing what we are doing. If the ques¡tions are to assess relevant prior knowledge, then tell students. If they are to tap into the magic power of retrieval, then tell them that. 
3. Ensure the format ofyour Do Now means students can start without any guid¡ance from you 
You shouldn't have to say anything in order for students to start your Do Now. I like to stand by the door and greet students as they enter the classroom. While I am doing this, I want the students who have sat down to be able to get cracking, and not to have a relax and then be able to say, 'I didn't know what to do.' 
Standardising the format of the Do Now works well here. Having a single format helps -maybe four questions -as does being consis¡tent with where and how students answer -full working in the back ofbooks, all done in silence. This enables students to spend less time and attention on the structure of the Do Now, and more on the content. 
4. Make the first question ofthe Do Now an easy one that doesn't require equipment 
One thing you don't want when you are trying to get a focused, orderly start to your lesson is a sea ofhands going up. This makes Adam Boxer's notion of Golden Silence that we discussed in Tip 70 almost impossible to achieve. So, choose an easy question to start with. In fact, ordering the questions so the difficulty ramps up throughout the Do Now is a good idea. This also means that all students should feel successful at the very start ofthe lesson, which gets them into a positive frame of mind for the challenges that lie ahead. 
Making sure the first question does not require equipment such as a calculator, ruler or protractor is also a good idea. If students get set¡tled, copy down the title, look at question one, and then realise they need to dive into their bags for their calculators, it creates hustle and bustle at the point ofthe lesson before Golden Silence has been established. Likewise, if some students do not have this equipment, then an early disruption could easily spread. 
5. Don't disrupt the start ofthe Do Now It is hard enough getting our students to focus, without us messing things up. I avoid any non-work conversations at the start of the lesson. A simple 'Hello Tom' at the door, and then on to the next student. Likewise, if there are any equipment issues, I will simply give the spare equipment out (or better still, have it on my desk so students can get it themselves) and then deal with this later, as opposed to asking 'why haven't you got your pen?' at this stage and losing the focused atmosphere I am trying to create. Same with 
missing homework or behaviour issues -they are best dealt with later. 
6. Decide on the means ofparticipation 
I think the most sensible way for your students to start the Do Now is silent, independent work. This creates the calm, focused start to the lesson, and allows you and them to know exactly what they can do. But where are students going to write the answers to their Do Now? If questions require working out, the exercise books make a lot of sense. I ask students to complete the Do Now in the backs of their exercise books. This makes them easier to find, tends to mean students write them a bit neater, and ifit is a retrieval Do Now, it means it does not disrupt the flow of the topic-themed work in the fronts of students' books. 
This silent work can then be followed up by a paired discussion, or you can dive straight into the answers. The paired discussion will be more productive if students do not yet know whether they are right or wrong, but the validity of any subsequent check for under¡standing is diminished if students have an opportunity to change their answers following the paired discussion. I will leave the choice to you! 
7. Think about whether students really need to copy down each question Copying questions takes time, so that time needs to be justified. Sure, questions beautifully copied out complete with answers look miles better in books than just answers, but if that means students 
only answer two questions versus four, I know what I will choose any day. Likewise, there are few sights more deceptively alluring for a teacher than a room of students with their heads down, silently writing in their books during a Do Now. It is only when you realise that they are busily writing down questions and not actually engag¡ing in any productive thinking that the problem becomes apparent. 
It all comes back to something we have discussed several times already -what will students use their exercise books for? Will they really look back at all their Do Nows and revise from them? If not, then consider just asking students to write down working out and the answer. 
8. Circulate the room 
Once students are settled, resist the temptation to crack on with admin. Instead, circulate the room. As we discussed in Tip 7 4, it is a good idea to have a purpose for your circulation. What are you look¡ing for? Is it the answer to question 1? Or that everyone is setting their work out correctly? Or showing working out? Decide on it, and look for it. 
It is a good idea to begin by looking at the work ofyour strongest students first. They are likely to have made the most progress, and hence give you the most information to feedback on. If they are struggling, the chances are the rest of the class are struggling, so you might need to stop the class and intervene. 
Circulating the room also helps you get a sense of how long stu¡dents need for the Do Now. When I watch lessons, I often see Do Nows cut short when barely any students have attempted the final couple of questions, or dragged out to death with the majority of the class ready to move on. Seeing where your students are actually up to is the only way to get an accurate sense ofhow long students re¡ally need. 
Finally, circulating helps you see whether students really are engag¡ing in the Do Now, or if they are copying down the title, date and lesson objectives with the precision of a skilled calligrapher. 
9. Consider providing cues 
Students unable to make a start on a Do Now learn very little, so it is worth providing a nudge in the right direction. My favourite cue for the Do Now is Jake Gordon's tip that we discussed in Tip 84. Jake gives his students six questions for his Do Now on a single sheet of paper: On the back of this piece ofpaper, he provides a worked example to a related question: 
Evaluate 9.2 ¡0.615  How many minutes are there from 4: 10am to 6:23am?  Find the value ofx 122░ 58░ X  
Find the coordinates of the circle 1----1i-------.-:i-1-1----1----1 . l . ) . 1 ' ,____,____,____ t-ò-t-----<--< - Find the HCF of 160 and 150: 160 = 25 X 5 150 =2X 3X 52  Circle all the ratios equivalent to 3:2 1: ~ 6:5 9:6 8:12 30:20 12:8

The idea is that students try each question, but if they get stuck they can flip over the paper, study the example, and then try the question again. The cue is not handed to them on a plate, and the students still need to do the heavy lifting. 
10. Decide what students do ifthey get stuck 
ò 
Are they allowed to ask you for help? 

ò 
Can they ask their partner? 

ò 
Can they use their notes? 

ò 
Do you ask them to write what they know? 


There is no correct answer, but it needs considering. 
11. Decide how you will do a check for understanding 
If students have answered the Do Now in exercise books, the check for understanding is tricky. What tends to happen is teachers revert to Cold Calling-'Amber, what did you get for question 1?' This seems inefficient seeing as all students have participated by writing their answers in their exercise books. 
Instead, I like to ask students to copy their final answer to question 1 nice and big on their mini-whiteboards. I can then ask the whole class to show me their answers at the same time. This gives me much more accurate information about what my students under¡stand, and I can go from there, calling upon students who have wrong or interesting answers, and asking them to share their 
.
reasoning. 
12. Decide how you will go through the answers 
There are lots of options here: 
ò 
The teacher goes through each answer one at a time. 

ò 
The teacher projects full written solutions on the board. 

ò 
The teacher asks one student to explain. 

ò 
The teacher asks more than one student to explain. 


There are pros and cons to each approach. The check for under¡standing helps me determine which approach I will choose: 
ò 
If the mini-whiteboards show the vast majority of students are correct, I may just confirm the correct answer or quickly write up the working out. 

ò 
If the check for understanding reveals that around half the class know the correct answer and half do not, I will instigate a paired discussion, then call upon students to share their think¡ing, then explain the answer myself. 

ò 
If I can see most students do not know, I will make a physical note to revisit this topic again. One worked example is unlikely to be enough to resolve this issue -explanation, examples and practice may be needed. 

ò 
If the Do Now is an assessment of relevant prior knowledge for the core content of the lesson, I have no choice but to deal with any misunderstandings there and then. 


13. Design your Do Nows so students feel successful 
We have already discussed the importance of having a more acces¡sible first question. But the same should be true for most of the Do Now. Again, having the 80% mean score in mind when designing our Do Nows is useful. The purpose of the Do Now is not to catch students out; it is to make them feel successful and that what we are doing in the class is working. A tricky start where students have struggled is hard to recover from. The challenge can come later in the lesson. 
14. Vary the types ofquestions you ask 
We discussed different types of questions in retrieval opportunities in Tip 83. Most Do Nows I see consist of procedural questions. Thinking about our desire to make students feel successful, that might be the best idea. But again, think ofwhat this communicates to students about the depth ofunderstanding they need for topics they have learned in the past. In a four-or five-question Do Now, consider using a different type of question for the final question. Challenging students to generate their own examples and non¡examples, including an interwoven question or asking for alternate methods to solve a problem are three of my favourite types of ques¡tions to include in a Do Now. 
15. Consider just cracking on where the last lesson left off 
Back in Chapter 6, we discussed the idea ofplanning sequences before lessons, and being dictated to by the needs of our students and not the sound of the bell. So, if the last lesson ended with stu¡dents mid-activity, then there is nothing wrong at all with the start of today's lesson simply being a continuation of that activity. This satisfies the requirement for a Do Now to be a settler and can lead to a calm, focused start to the lesson requiring little teacher interven¡tion. You just need to consider whether this meets your needs and challenges ... and satisfies the powers that be. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider carefully if your Do Now serves its intended purpose, and if not, then look to make some improvements.

Tip 88 
Trello 
Do Now 
What's the problem? 
Let's close this chapter with something a bit different. 
Jon Mumford is a language teacher. Like most teachers, Jon ensures his students have access to all the resources and revision mate¡rials they could ever need to help them prepare for their exams. Previously, Jon spent many hours adding these resources -past pa¡pers, quizzes, videos, vocab lists, links, etc. -to his school's virtual learning environment (VLE) in the hope his students would make use of them. But when Jon asked the school's ICT officer for the data (something I had never thought to do), he found that very few students were visiting the languages page -indeed, students were hardly using the school's VLE at all. 
When Jon spoke to his students about their revision, many admitted they didn't know where to start. They found the collection oflinks and files on the VLE both generic and overwhelming, and so were essentially making up their revision as they went along. Not ideal. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you support studentswith their revision? 

ò 
How do you know if they find it useful? 


What's the tip? 
So, Jon decided to experiment with an application called Trello. 
Trello is an online to-do list with some nice extra features. It is more commonly used by productivity gurus to manage large projects. My wife and I used Trello to help plan our wedding -and we are still to¡gether Oust about). 
Jon set up a Trello board for his GCSE French class. He created three columns: red, amber and green. He then created a card for each of the key topics in GCSE French: opinions, free-time activities, travel and tourism, etc. This is what the Trello board looks like: 
R,d

Each card contains resources to support students in that topic. In Jon's case, most cards contain: 
ò

Trello allows you to add any link or attach any type of file to a card. 
Once set up (and this took a while!) Jon can create a copy of the Trello board for each ofhis students and invite them to join it. Jon also becomes a collaborator on each of the boards he creates. 
Now, I am often wary of introducing another piece of technology to my students. It is somewhere else that they need to visit or yet another password they need to remember. The result can be overwhelming and negate any of the benefits. Likewise, it can be a hassle for me as their teacher to keep on top of something else alongside all the other plates I am currently spinning. 
But, having listened to Jon and done some experimenting myself, I am convinced Trello is worth considering, for the following five reasons. 
1. Trello is free 
For the way Jon uses Trello, it does not cost a penny either for the teacher or the student. 
2. Trello helps students stay organised This is the big one. The whole course is mapped out for students. All the resources they need are there. Because you can add links or attach any files to a Trello card, students just need to visit this one site. You can attach PDFs of past papers, links to YouTube videos, whatever you like. Students can even create a card that contains their login details for any websites you link to. And students can add their own links and attachments for any additional resources 
they find useful, making the Trello board dynamic and person¡alised. 
3. Trello stops students just revising the things they are good at It feels great to watch a video and take a quiz on a subject you know really well. And as the hours tick by and the 10/l0s wrack up, students convince themselves that they are in great shape for their exams. But what about the topics they struggle with? Trello can help with this. All cards begin in the red column. The idea is that as students revise them, they move to amber and then eventually 
green. There needs to be clear criteria for this. So, you might say to your students: 
'Ifyou score between 50% and 80% on a quiz, move the card to amber, and ifyou score 80%+, move it to green. Your challenge is to move every card across to the green column, and then see ifyou can keep them there!' 
4. Trello works on any system 
There is a desktop app, an Android app, and an iOS app, all ofwhich are free. So, students can work on Trello in a computer room lesson, on a tablet in class, or on their phones on the bus home. Depending on your school's mobile phone policy, you may allow students to spend time on their Trello boards during tutor time, in the last 10 minutes of a lesson, or just before a Low-Stakes Quiz. 
5. Trello helps students be accountable When Jon sets up the boards for his students, they invite him to be a collaborator. That way, Jon can oversee his students' progress. He can ask them what topic they are currently working on, where they are struggling, or use it to support students in creating a revision schedule. Students can also invite their parents to view their board 
so they have an easy-to-digest overview of what their children are working on. 
Of course, this tip is not just for use in the final few weeks in the build-up to exams. Jon introduces his students to Trello at the start of the year. Then they can see what lies in front of them, and start to build effective study habits from the outset. This feeds in with maths teacher Julia Smith's tip: 'Revision should start from day one of a course, not at the end.' 
As Jon explains, the initial set-up takes time, but once it is in place, the potential rewards are significant.

THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Trello may help you support your students with structured, easy-to-access revision.

CHAPTER 11 
HOMEWORK, MARKING AND FEEDBACK 
Homework, marking and feedback used to be the bane of my teach¡ing life. Students did not put as much effort into their homework as I wanted them to, marking and giving written feedback took ages, and students largely ignored it. In this chapter, I share what I do now. How can we improve homework generally, and how can we make it feed into lessons? Then written feedback -how can we im¡prove its effectiveness or replace it with whole-class feedback? This chapter should save you time and help your students learn more. 
Chapter overview

What's the problem? 
Homework is different to the retrieval opportunities we have looked at so far -in particular, Low-Stakes Quizzes (Tip 87) and Do Nows (Tip 88) -in that it takes place outside of the classroom. This means it does not take up valuable lesson time and students can work at their own pace. Homework has great potential to enhance students' learning. 
But as most of us will attest, the effectiveness of homework is mixed across students and classes. I am going to put my hands up and say that homework was one of the least effective aspects of my teaching. Some students either didn't do their homework or copied their homework, and a regular battle ensued over the year, involving calls to parents, break-time detentions, and so on. But those students were the minority. A more serious issue was the norm that emerged in my classes: putting minimal effort into homework is enough. A large number of students would whizz through homework in five minutes, doing sparse working out and leaving out questions they couldn't do, or could not be bothered to do. But these students did just enough. They knew I would reason that something is bet¡ter than nothing and instead focus my attention (and wrath) on the students who had done nothing. Learning happens when stu¡dents think hard, and my students did not think hard during their homework. 
Why did this happen? 
The mistake I made for many years was treating homework and classwork as separate. The only role homework played in my lessons was the couple of minutes where I handed back students' books, students (maybe) took a look at their scores and my com¡ments (more on comments later this chapter), and I had words with students who had not done as I asked. Then we cracked on with the lesson. And -as is increasingly the case in maths -if the homework 
I set was online and automatically marked, then it would receive even less classroom time. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How seriously do your students take their homework? 

ò 
How does homework feed into what happens in your classroom? 


What's the tip? 
There are some really simple things we can do to help our students take homework more seriously that we have discussed already in this book: 
ò 
We can show students the Forgetting Curve so they understand the importance ofretrieval (Tip 76). 

ò 
We can ask them to add confidence scores to their answers (Tip 


~¡
ò We can celebrate students who have done their homework in an attempt to create the norm that in this class we do our home¡work (Tip 7). 
All of these will help. But the single most important factor to in¡crease the chance ofstudents taking homework seriously is to ensure it feeds into lessons. Without this, we will be fighting a losing battle. 
This is science teacher Adam Boxer's fifth tip -'To make homework more effective, integrate it with classwork' -and is stated in the EEF review ofhomework: 'In the most effective examples homework was an integral part of learning, rather than an add-on.' The point is that if students see homework playing a key role in lessons, they are more likely to realise that the effort they put into it is important. 
But what does this actually look like? Here are seven ideas. 
1. Address homework at the start ofthe lesson 
There are lots of ideas below, and in theory, they can take place at any stage of a lesson. But I find the start the most effective. It means I am likely to dedicate sufficient time to addressing the homework before we get engrossed in this lesson's focus, and it can become a really useful routine that students get used to: the lesson after we have completed our homework starts with a review of that homework. 
2. Use class time to look at homework questions that caused problems 
This is the simplest thing to do. Choose, say, the three worst an¡swered questions in a homework and start the lesson by reviewing them. The review could involve an explanation, modelling and prac¡tice. It could be a completed worked example with a related problem for students to try next to it. It could be a photo ofthe work of a student who got the question correct, a discussion in a pair about the solution, and then a follow-up question to try. Whatever you decide, the point is that using valuable classroom time to address problems in homework helps students realise that homework is important. 
3. Use class time to improve a piece ofhomework 
We have discussed sharing a student's work with the rest of the class in Tip 40. When looking at students' homework, take photos of any piece ofwork that is nearly correct but could be improved. This might be because the working out is not complete, units are miss¡ing, they have not rounded, they have made a daft mistake early on in the solution, the final solution is not clear -anything important that you want to make students aware of. Then start the lesson by showcasing this work, praising the good points, then challenging students -independently at first, then in pairs -to discuss how it could be improved. 
4. Use class time to showcase excellence 
The best way to get homework up to the standards you expect is to provide examples of those standards. This may be in terms of cor¡rectness, neatness, completeness, or effort -whatever is important to you. By showing examples of students who have met this stan¡dard and making explicit why this work has been chosen, it is much clearer to students what they need to do than ifwe try to explain it with words, and much more powerful than if it comes from us. This may also provide an incentive to students to make their homework good enough to 'go on the board'. 
5. Consider not giving books back until the review process has happened Imagine you want to discuss question 3 from this week's home¡work. You give the books back. Sam can see he has got question 3 correct. Where is his incentive to listen? Harriet has got question 3 wrong-maybe this provides an incentive to listen hard, or maybe it makes her feel like a failure so she switches off. Delaying return¡ing homework until the review process is complete provides more 
students with an incentive to pay attention. The same is true when reviewing tests. 
6. Set the Do Now or Low-Stakes Quiz on homework topics 
Announce to students that the next lesson will begin with a quiz on the topics that are part of the homework. You can go even further and say you will choose the exact questions from the homework to make up this quiz. Provided students have an opportunity to seek help if they get stuck on the homework (more on this in Tip 91), then a poor performance on the test is a clear signal that sufficient effort was not put into the homework. Knowing that you will be quizzed on the content ofyour homework provides a good incentive to put effort into that homework. 
7. Treat online homework just as seriously as you treat written homework This is a huge one for maths teachers. More and more these days, half or even all of a student's maths homework is set online. This is brilliant for teacher workload, and the advances these platforms have made in terms of adaptive learning and personalised support mean that if students put the effort in, the improvements in their learning are likely to be more than if they work through a paper¡based homework. But -and it is a massive but -if students realise that this is a case of 'set it and forget it', then the incentive to put the effort in diminishes. Each of the ideas above can -and should ¡
be used for all homework whether we are physically marking it or not. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Ifyou want students to take homework seriously, ensure it plays an integral role in your lessons. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip90

Tip 91-Eight ideas to improve homework 

What's the problem? 
Okay, so following the advice in Tip 90, your students are taking homework more seriously. Great! Now, the next thing is to ensure that homework is as effective as possible. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
What does your homework look like? 

ò 
How effective is your homework? 


What's the tip? 
Here are eight ideas for making homework as effective as possible. 
1. Rebrand homework as 'checking for understanding' 
In Tip 87, we discussed rebranding weekly tests as Low-Stakes Quizzes to shift students' perception of them away from anxiety¡inducing high-stakes assessments. A similar rebranding is needed with homework. If you ask students why they think we set them homework, most will reply with something along the lines of 'so you can give us a mark'. In other words, homework is seen as some¡thing for the teacher and not the student. This can lead to students not doing homework, copying, or only attempting the questions they know they can do. In Building Thinking Classrooms in Mathematics, Peter Liljedahl suggests we rebrand homework as 'check your understanding questions' and explain to our students that the pur¡pose ofhomework is to provide an opportunity, in a safe space, to see if they can apply what they learned in class. This helps students realise that homework is for them, and should lead to increased participation and effort. 
2. Have something students can do ifthey are stuck on their homework 
If homework is going to be where students check their understand¡ing, what do they do if that understanding is not there? 
Let's say a student leaves out a question. You ask them why. They tell you it's because they didn't know how to do it. Where do you go from there? You suspect they may just not have been bothered to think about it. But without a system in place, this is likely to keep happening again and again. And if students only attempt the ques¡tions they know they can do, there will be lots of maths that they will not feel the benefits of retrieval for. 
The key is to make it clear what students should do ifthey are stuck on a question in their homework. Online homework systems can help here, where students can often access a video or a worked ex¡ample to help with a specific question. Ensure your students -and their parents -know this, and that the expectation is that they take up this extra support. 
For written homework in maths, directing them to other sources such as Corbettmaths or their textbooks will help. Take the time to put the support references on the homework-QR codes work well here. Or provide the answers (without the working out) to each question so students can check their answers. Or have a break time each week where students can come and see you to ask for help. Re¡move all barriers to the support so students have no excuse. 
Finally, if a student has accessed the support and is still stuck, there are still a few things they can do. Ask them to write exactly what bit of the question is confusing them, to tell you what they do know about the subject, or to write a related question that they can an¡swer. This will at least get students to engage with the material and give you a clearer insight into their difficulties. Anything is better than a blank page. 
3. Ensure all homework has questions for topics students have learned previously Homework is a retrieval opportunity. It makes sense to include questions on the most current idea students have been learning¡we know that the rate of forgetting is at its steepest immediately after learning something new. But it also makes sense to include questions on topics students have learned in the past. Otherwise, the retrieval strength of that knowledge falls so low that students essentially forget it (Tip 77). In addition, students can work through homework at their own pace, without the time pressures that may 
be associated with classwork. This makes it a perfect place to have students think hard about things they learned in the past. 
But I never used to do that. In my first school, all homework was topic specific. In my second school, I co-wrote the homework... and it was topic specific. It was only when I had my mid-career crisis around 2017 that I realised mixed-topic homework was a good idea. So, we included -and still do include -a revision section on all homework. 
The topics in this revision section could be topics students have not revisited in a while, or topics students struggled with in a recent retrieval opportunity-a Do Now or a Low-Stakes Quiz -which you have subsequently revisited and now want to see what students learned. You could track the appearance of each topic as discussed in Tip 81. Likewise, following our discussion in Tip 83, we can vary the types of questions we ask in the homework, asking different types of questions both for variety and to test students' deeper un¡derstanding of topics they seem procedurally fluent in. 
One word ofwarning -performance will dip. Spacing topics in this way is one of Bjork's 'desirable difficulties'. Mixed-topic homework is likely to be harder than topic-specific homework on the thing students have just learned. We had lots ofpush-back from students and parents complaining their scores had gone down. In the end, we found having a separate revision score and topic score for each homework helped. And, like everything else, explaining clearly to students what you are doing and why is likely to mitigate negative reactions. 
4. Ensure all homework has questions that are relevant prior knowledge for the next topic 
In Chapter 7 we discussed the importance of assessing relevant prior knowledge before teaching a new idea. In that chapter, I de¡scribed how this could be done in class at the start of a new unit ofwork. Another option is to assess relevant prior knowledge in homework. This has the distinct advantage that precious class time can be spent focusing on the areas ofweakness the homework has identified, as opposed to trying to identify those areas. 
Taking this idea with the idea above, an approximate homework composition could be: 
ò 
Questions on topics encountered previously: 30% 

ò 
Questions on the topic that has just been taught: 50% 

ò 
Questions on relevant prior knowledge for upcoming topic: 20%. 


This same approach could also be applied to end-of-topic tests. 
Maths teacher Jo Morgan suggests that ifyou start a new topic next lesson and don't have time to mark the homework or test in its entirety, then spend time looking over the answers to the relevant prior knowledge questions to get a sense of where your students are at, and return to marking the rest of the answers when you have more time. 
5. Challenge students to create a diagnostic question As discussed above, varying the types of questions you ask within homework is a good idea. But here is my favourite homework ac¡
tivity of all time: challenge students to create their own diagnostic question on the topic they have been studying. 
Here is what they need to do: 
1. 
Write a question on the topic we have just learned. 

2. 
Work out the correct answer. 

3. 
Choose three plausible wrong answers. 

4. 
Write what mistake a student choosing each of these wrong an¡swers is making. 


A template like this is useful (available on the resources page): 
Diagnostic question -Template Topic: 
Write your question here: 
Choose 4answers to go here. Think very carefully about the wrong answers. Wrong answers should be designed to tease out specific misconceptions. 

Now for each wrong answer, explain why you chose it.What specific mistake has astudent made ifthey choose each wrong answer? 
This homework gives you a real sense ofthe understanding of your students. Better still, you can use some ofthe questions stu¡dents write in class, first by challenging the class to answer them, and then to see if they can understand the choices of the wrong answers. 
A word of caution: only consider setting this as a homework after students have experienced answering and discussing diagnostic questions verbally in class regularly for at least two weeks. This consistent exposure is needed for students to understand the struc¡ture of a diagnostic question and the purpose ofthe wrong answers. Most important of all, students need to have experienced explaining wrong answers verbally, and listening to the explanations of their peers, before embarking upon writing them up individually. 
6. Collect in books open at the homework 
A quick one, but an important one. When setting homework that students need to write in books, make sure they hand their books in with the page open at the homework. I reckon it saves about 10 seconds per student versus searching through the book trying to find the homework, which is five minutes per class of 30, which is two and a half hours per class over the course of a year. 
7. Consider having a separate homework book This is related to the idea of a student worked-examples book discussed in Tip 61. Students could have a homework book inde¡
pendent of their classwork book. Here are three reasons why this is worth considering: 
1. 
When you are taking them to mark, they are significantly thin¡ner and lighter, and contain only homework content, thus sav¡ing you time and your back. 

2. 
Thinking back to one of the ideas in Tip 90, a separate home¡work book also makes it easier to discuss the homework with¡out students first seeing their work -you can do whole-class feedback and students can write in their exercise books before you give back their homework books. 

3. 
Students may put in greater effort to be neater and more complete with their work ifit is a separate entity and not sur¡rounded by scruffy classwork. 


8. Ask students to assign confidence scores to their answers 
We discussed the power of confidence scores in Tip 85. Asking students to assign confidence scores next to their answers before they hand their homework in means that when you hand their homework back you can challenge students to find their three high¡est confidence errors and try to correct them. This gives a structure and purpose to the review process, and taps into the power of the Hypercorrection Effect. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Once your studentstake homework seriously, take practical steps to ensure thathomework is as effective as it can be.

Tip 92 ¡Two things to check if homework or test scores are a surprise  
Tip 90 Feed into lessons ' ,  Tip 93 Silly mistakes I  l  Tip 94 Detectives I

What's the problem? 
When I used to mark homework and tests, I was regularly surprised by how poorly some students performed: 'Oh wow, I thought Calum understood more than that' or 'Jenny, surely you know how to do this?' 
This was the norm. But given that an unexpected poor performance often led to time-consuming written feedback by me, followed by a lengthy classroom intervention, I started to think about whether there was anything I could do to address the situation. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Are you often surprised by the poor homework or test performance of your students? 

ò 
Ifso, what can be done about it? 


What's the tip? 
If homework and test scores are a surprise, we need to review what we are doing in the classroom. 
Let's keep things simple and assume there are two types of ques¡tions students could be asked on a test or homework: questions on a topic they have studied recently, and questions on topics that they studied in the past. What does poor performance on each tell us? 
Poor performance on topics students have studied recently 
If students are struggling with the current topic, this suggests our checks for understanding in the classroom have not been good enough. Maybe we didn't provide enough opportunities to check for understanding. Maybe we only check surface-level understanding by asking procedural or recall questions. Or maybe our checks for understanding did not elicit mass participation so we assumed all students understood, when in reality only one or two did. If this is the case, there are plenty of ideas for checking for understanding in Chapter 4, and responding to that check in Chapter 5. 
Poor performance on topics students have studied in the past 
Poor performance on topics students encountered a while ago suggests we need to review our retrieval practice schedule. How long is it since students last thought about that topic? Has retrieval strength sunk so low that it has been essentially forgotten? Can you provide more retrieval opportunities in class via Low-Stakes Quizzes or Do Nows? Or can you provide some cues or support if students are stuck on their homework to kick-start the retrieval process? Ideas on doing all of this can be found in Chapter 10. 
Of course, students will always surprise us to some extent with what they know and what they don't. But, on the whole, home¡work and test performance should be fairly predictable ifyou are regularly checking for understanding and providing retrieval oppor¡tunities, which should mean you need to spend less time writing comments and intervening. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Analyse your students' homework and test performance to see if it can be improved by achange in classroom practice. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip92 
ª

2.

3.

4.

Tip 93 -Be careful how you respond to 'silly' mistakes

What's the problem? 
A question requires students to calculate the area of a compound shape involving a square and a semicircle. Here is a student's answer: 
@ 5 X 5 =25Gm2
}.svm

« semi Girvle Areo, = 1rr2 --1T.X2.52
