Toto,/ o,reo, =25 + 9.317 =34.3 (ldp) 
What feedback would you give? 
Ifyou are anything like me, you might write 'UNITS' followed by 27 exclamation marks. 
Forgetting units is an example of one of the most frustrating mis¡takes a student can make -a silly one. These silly mistakes -surely the result of carelessness -lead to marks tumbling in exams and maths teachers around the country going grey prematurely. Here are some other examples: 
ò 
Forgetting to round. 

ò 
Forgetting to finish the question. 

ò 
Forgetting to check the answer is sensible. 


Despite the number of times I tell my students to watch out for these silly mistakes, they keep making them. 
But are mistakes like these really the result of carelessness, and is the best feedback really to remind students to stop making them? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Have you experienced students making mistakes like these? 

ò 
How do you respond to them? 


What's the tip? 
Have another look at the list of so-called silly mistakes: 
ò 
Forgetting to include the units. 

ò 
Forgetting to round. 

ò 
Forgetting to finish the question. 


ò Forgetting to check the answer is sensible. What do they each have in common? 
In each instance the mistake comes at the end of the process -right at the point when the student is most 'cognitively knackered' (not a technical term). So the mistake may not be a result of carelessness; it may be because students simply have no more attention left to dedicate to finishing things off correctly. As a result, simply telling a student to stop making such mistakes will be as effective as telling a collapsed athlete that they need to do one more lap of the track. They simply have nothing left to give. 
So, what are we to do instead? 
Here are three ideas. 
1. Give students a second opportunity to check their work 
Having a code that you can quickly add to a student's work to indicate that there is a mistake somewhere -my old maths teacher used to sketch a pair of glasses -puts the onus back on them to try to find that mistake. This is the idea behind Dylan Wiliam's ap¡proach to feedback to make it more like detective work (Tip 94). 
If the mistake is due to carelessness, this gives them a second chance to correct it. If it is as a result of cognitive overload, then this gives students a chance to review their work at a point when they have more attention to dedicate to it. And ifthe student cannot identify the mistake, then that is a signal that you need to do some¡thing different ... 
2. Isolate and practise that final step 
Each of the mistakes on our list above could be categorised as deci¡sions. Decisions are often made automatically by experts -'of course it is cm2' -and hence do not receive as much attention when mod¡
elling as do visible steps in a procedure. But these decisions may not be as obvious to our students. If these decisions require attention, and if they appear towards the end of a procedure and students do not have much attention to give, we see the kinds of mistakes shared above. 
We discussed an approach to dealing with decisions in Tip 57. The key is to isolate them and practise them separately before introduc¡ing them as part of a multistep procedure. This helps students make decisions in the way we do -without requiring significant attention. This is always a good idea, but especially if students keep making specific mistakes and are not able to identify the issue themselves. 
Here is a sequence of questions I would ask students to help de¡velop fluency in identifying units when circles are involved: 
Question Description Units 
I 
1. 
Acircle has aradius of 1Orn, what is the area? 

2. 
Acircle has aradius of 10cm, what is the area? 



3. Acircle has aradius of 10cm, what is the circumference? 
4. 
Acircle has adiameter of 10cm, what is the circumference? 

5. 
Acircle has acircumference of 10cm, what is the diameter? 

6. 
Acircle has an area of 10cm2, what is the diameter? 

7. 
Acircle has an area of 10cm2, what is the circumference? 



8. Acircle has an circumference of 10cm, what is the area? And here is a reminder of the steps I use: 
Paired 
( Reveal 
Reveal next 
Think and u Explain 
. 
discussion 
answer and 
~ 
example 
vote answer
(if needed) 
pause
1 j 
' 
I 


Have a look at Tip 57 for more information about running such se¡quences of questions. 
3. Teach students to write the final step first 
If students continue to make these sorts of mistakes, a strategy you could try is to encourage them to write that last step first. So: 
ò Forgetting to include the units -write the units at the bottom of the page. 
ò 
Forgetting to round -writing '(to ldp)', or whatever the required de¡gree of accuracy is, at the bottom of the page. 

ò 
Forgetting to finish the question -writing 'the area ofthe total shape is', or whatever the question is asking, at the bottom of the page. 

ò 
Forgetting to check the answer is sensible -making an estimate of the size of the final answer, and writing this at the bottom of the page, ready to be crossed out when they work out the actual answer. 


ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Ifstudents are making mistakes with the final stepof an answer, it might not be due to carelessness, and that means we will need to act differently. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip93 
ª 
OVER TO YOU 
1. 
How does this tip fit into your current practice? Ido exactly that I do something like that Ido nothing like that 

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3. 
When are you going to try this idea for the first time? Please give the exact date and time. 

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
For many years I have been frustrated by the time and effort that I put into marking and giving feedback on homework and tests, versus the time and effort my students put into responding to that marking and feedback. 
Here is a typical scenario: I spend Sunday afternoon working my way through a pile of 30 books to mark a homework assignment. 
Book 1 gets a lot of my attention -I mark diligently and respond to any errors with lengthy, carefully considered written feedback, complete with bespoke follow-up tasks for the student to complete. Thirty minutes later, when I am on book 4, my patience is wearing thin -the ticks and crosses get sloppier, the written feedback gets shorter and contains clear traces of my frustration (an exclamation mark at the end of every phrase being a clear giveaway). Three hours later when I finally get to that last book and I see another mistake, I want to scream, cover the work in a big, red cross, and scrawl in capital letters, 'ARE YOU TRYING TO WIND ME UP!!!' 
And breathe. 
I would feel slightly less animosity towards the time I spent marking and giving feedback if it had a significant impact on my students' learning. But whenever I gave my students their home¡work or test back, they tended to do two things: first, look at their score, and second, look at the score oftheir mate. And my carefully considered feedback, written with blood, sweat and tears? ... A cur¡sory glance at best. 
The equation was all wrong. The time and effort I was putting in nowhere near matched what my students were getting out ofit. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How much time do you spend giving written feedback to homework and tests? 

ò 
Is that time well spent? 


What's the tip? 
Dylan Wiliam offers a solution: 
'Rather than thinking about feedback as information, think about feedback as detective work. The idea is that the feed¡back should cause a puzzle or a challenge for the students to engage in.' 
The general approach 
ò 
I go through the pile of 30 books or tests, but instead of placing ticks and crosses on the students' work, I simply write their scores, say 7 /10. 

ò 
As I do this for each student, I also make a note of any questions that appear troublesome across a number ofbooks so I can en¡gage in whole-class feedback later on (Tip 96). 


What then happens in the classroom depends on whether the work I am marking is topic-specific (e.g. the topic-specific section of homework or an end-of-unit test), or mixed-topic (e.g. the revision section of homework or a mock exam). 
Topic-specific work 
There will be different challenges based on students' performance: 
ò 
A score of100%: 'Which question do you think most students got wrong? Write an explanation that would help someone under¡stand how to answer it. Then see ifyour partner needs help.' 

ò 
A score between 60% and 100%: 'Find your mistakes and correct them.' 

ò 
A score between 30% and 60%: 'I have corrected one ofyour mistakes [I will write the correction in the student's book]. Find the other mistakes and correct them.' 

ò 
Less than 30%: 'Study the Example-Problem Pair on the board, then try the Your Turn.' 


Notice more directive feedback is needed if students struggle, whereas less directive feedback can be used for those with less to correct. 
I find this process works best by giving my students, say, five min¡utes to do as much as they can independently, before then working with their partner to compare answers and support each other. I can then project the full set of correct answers on the board, ad¡dress the significant issues I identified during the marking with the whole class, and pick up any lingering individual issues later in the lesson. 
Mixed-topic work 
Here, a worked example for one question will not help the student understand another question on a different topic. In this case, I still give students a few minutes to look back over their work and see if they can identify any errors, but then the emphasis shifts to collab¡oration. Can students work together to identify all their mistakes and hence produce the perfect, corrected piece ofwork? If this is a mock exam, giving them a blank exam paper to complete together works really well for this. 
As a general rule, I prefer collaboration to happen in pairs, but in this case, the advantage of larger groups is that students have more source material to compare. As with all group work, you want to en¡sure there are not one or two students dominating and a load of free riders. Using Sammy Kempner's approach to group work (Tip 16} to ensure all members of the group are responsible for each other is useful here, as well as reinforcing the mantra that helping others is the hardest job in the classroom. 
Whether I use the feedback as detective work approach for topic-specific or mixed-topic work, it is much less work for me, much more work for my students, and the gamification of the marking process leads to an engaging, productive activity. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Turn feedback into detective work to save you time and ensure all students are thinking hard. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip94 
ª 
OVER TO YOU 
1. 
How does this tip fit into your current practice? Ido exactly that I do something like that Ido nothing like that 

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3. 
When are you going to try this idea for the first time? Please give the exact date and time. 

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
If you've been teaching as long as I have, you might remember the dark days of the 'verbal feedback given' stamp, where every conver¡
sation you had with a child needed to be evidenced. Good God, that was a low point. 
Verbal feedback is quicker to give than written feedback and, according to the EEF report on feedback, can be just as effective. But there is often the need for evidence, and thus the transient nature ofverbal feedback makes it unsuitable for many schools' feedback policies. 
Annoying! 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you give verbal feedback? 

ò 
Have you been able to capture that verbal feedback? 


What's the tip? 
Step forward language teacher, Jon Mumford. Like many teachers, Jon was frustrated by the amount of time his feedback was taking to write, and then the subsequent lack of effect it was having as his students struggled to engage with it. So, for the past few years he has been experimenting with recording verbal feedback using a free service called Qwiqr, available at qwiqr.education. 
Here is how it works: 
ò 
Jon prints off a series ofindividual QR codes from the Qwiqr website. 

ò 
He sticks one QR code on each piece of student work. 

ò 
Jon reads the work of the first student. 

ò 
Jon then scans their QR code using the Qwiqr app, and records his audio feedback by speaking into his phone. 

ò 
The audio feedback saves automatically, or he can rerecord if needed. 

ò 
Then, when the student receives the work back, they scan the QR code with a smartphone or tablet and immediately hear the personalised verbal feedback they have been given. 


Now, this may sound a bit gimmicky and a bit of a hassle. But here are eight reasons why Jon is convinced it is a good idea: 
1. Time saved 
Jon reckons he can record audio feedback this way in about a quarter of the time it would take to write the feedback. The Qwiqr website claims the figure is close to seven times faster. 
2. Student engagement 
Jon describes how his students look forward to receiving feedback because they know they are going to get a personalised message from their teacher -when does that happen with written feedback? 
3. The ability to relisten Verbal feedback given to a student in a lesson is transient. But recorded verbal feedback is not. Students can listen as many times as they need until they understand what the teacher is saying, 
and then listen back again when they have made the necessary improvements. 
4. It is more personalised 
When doing written feedback, I find myselfwriting the same things over and over again. But because recording audio feedback is so much quicker, you can add in comments bespoke to the student. Even simply repeating the student's name over the course of the recording conveys the message that this feedback is especially for them. 
5. You can check students have listened to the feedback Do we really know if students have read our written feedback or not? With the audio feedback, Jon likes to include a code word. Something daft, like 'pineapple'. Then, next time he sees a student, Jon can ask what their code word was as a way to check they have listened properly. This also adds to the personalisation mentioned 
above, and it is a nice bit of fun that you would struggle to get with written feedback. 
6. It is evidenced 
This might be the clincher. Jon describes how SLT love this. They see these weird QR code things in exercise books, and when they ask students about them, they are proudly told that 'this is where sir records feedback to us'. SLT can then listen and be suitably impressed. 
7. It can be used for motivation Jon also describes how he likes to stick a QR code on the front ofhis students' books. Here he records a welcome message at the start of the year, but crucially he can update this message any time he takes the books in and scans the code again. Maybe the student has produced some really good work consistently, and Jon wants to send them a personal congratulatory message. Maybe standards have been slipping a bit, and Jon wants to remind them ofwhat he 
expects and what he knows they can do. All can be done quickly and personally. 
8. Parents can listen 
Parents can use their phones to scan the QR code and listen to the feedback. This is often more accessible than trying to decipher the 
feedback we write in books. It helps the parent feel better connected to the teacher and the work. 
Listening to Jon, I was sold that this is a great idea for subjects like languages where written feedback is used to respond to extended writing. But what about something like maths? I think it can work well, especially ifit is combined with Tip 94: 'Turn feedback into de¡tective work.' Imagine the intrigue when a student gets their work back, scans the QR code, and hears you announce that they have made three mistakes, the first one ofwhich is in question 2, and their job is to sort that out and then find and correct the rest. 
Jon was clear to point out that he wouldn't use audio feedback for every piece ofwork. But including this approach as part ofyour feedback routine might provide the variety necessary to bump up engagement and effectiveness. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider recording verbal feedback as part of your feedback repertoire.

Tip 96 -Twelve ideas to improve whole-class feedback

Tip 91

Whole-class homework 
feedback feedback 

What's the problem? 
We have spent the last four tips thinking about ways to improve the individualised feedback we put on homework and tests: 
ò 
Two things to check if homework or test scores are a surprise (Tip 92). 

ò 
Be careful how you respond to 'silly' mistakes (Tip 93). 

ò 
Turn feedback into detective work (Tip 94). 

ò 
Consider using verbal feedback (Tip 95). 


But several schools I work with have abandoned individualised feedback altogether, especially for homework. They are not con¡vinced that the opportunity cost is worth it -the time teachers spend giving such feedback could be time spent planning better lessons. 
And it seems many of my guests agree. When assessment expert Daisy Christodoulou came onto the Tips for Teachers podcast, her third tip was 'don't do written comments'. Primary expert Clare Sealy's fourth tip was 'no feedback, more teaching'. Dylan Wiliam described how when we give students a score and written feedback, the only thing they look at is the score. US maths teacher Michael Pershan suggested that the best feedback is a learning activity, and it's much better than written feedback on the page. 
And so we have seen the shift to whole-class feedback. Here, instead of writing individual comments on a piece ofwork, the teacher looks for trends, and then addresses these with the whole class. 
This is certainly a welcome change in terms ofworkload, but I feel there is a danger that whole-class feedback becomes yet another lethal mutation along with Do Nows and Low-Stakes Quizzes. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do you engage in whole-class feedback? 

ò 
Ifso, what does your process look like? 


What's the tip? 
Here are 12 ideas for improving whole-class feedback, whether you are responding to homework or a test. 
1. Have a quick flick through all pieces ofwork first 
A mistake I used to make was starting my marking and feedback process on the book or test at the top of the pile. As previously dis¡cussed, the issue here is you end up writing lots about a problem you believe is specific to one child, only to find out seven books or tests down the line that it is a whole-class issue. 
So, when preparing for whole-class feedback, I like to start by hav¡ing a very quick look-no more than 30 seconds -at each student's work, with the solutions or mark scheme beside me. This will allow me to get a sense of problem areas -for example, ifI spot a question that most have left blank, or a common wrong answer. 
2. Have a more in-depth look at three pieces ofwork 
An alternative to this is to choose three pieces ofwork: one ofyour strongest students, one of your weakest students, and one ofyour students in the middle in terms of achievement. Then take a longer look at their work -perhaps five minutes on each. While this will not give you the breadth of an overview that a flick through all books will give you, the extra time may allow you to notice impor¡tant things. For example, ifyour strongest student is struggling on a question, then it is likely that all students will be. If your weakest student has nailed a question, then you can probably ignore it in terms ofwhole-class feedback. 
3. Look through students' work with a purpose Now I have got a sense ofwhat to expect, it is time to take a closer look at each piece ofwork. I spend no more than a minute on each student, because my initial flick or deep dive has alerted me to what I am looking for, and I have the answers beside me. Once I see a number of students are getting a question correct, I will stop looking at that. Likewise, once I see a number of students getting a question wrong and I have diagnosed why, I will stop looking at that. Observ¡
ing with a purpose saves time and helps me identify the most im¡portant trends. 
If I need to mark the work, this is when I will do it. I will simply use ticks or crosses, or write the number of marks for a multistep ques¡tion. No comments, and no indication ofwhere or why the student went wrong. 
4. Have a blank copy ofthe test or homework with you 
In my early days of whole-class feedback, I used to make notes of trends I saw on a piece of paper. I find it far more effective these days to do the same on a blank copy of the test or homework that students have answered. So, if question 9 is an issue, I will write my observations as to why, filling in the answer space with examples of where students have gone wrong and common answers. 
Using this format makes it much easier to write my observations and it is then easier to access them. Best of all, I then have a work¡able document that I can bring into the classroom to support the whole-class feedback that follows. 
5. Categorise issues into two columns This is an idea I have adapted from the wonderful Adam Boxer. I grab a piece of paper and split it into two columns -one labelled 
'Reteach' and the other labelled 'Retrieve'. I look over my annotated test paper or homework and start to populate the columns. 
In the 'Reteach' column I choose three -never more than three ¡topics where a quick going over will not be enough. These are topics that most students have struggled with and will require class time to provide an explanation, worked example, and further practice. I will also plan when I am going to do this. Will I do one of these the next lesson I see students, or will I schedule them for a specific date in the future? 
In the 'Retrieve' column, I place questions that were troublesome for students, but some understanding was there. These are ques¡tions for which a brief discussion, worked example and follow-up question might be enough to get students back on track. Five is the maximum number here. I will then make a note in my retrieval schedule (Tip 81) to ensure they appear soon in either a Do Now, a Low-Stakes Quiz or homework. 
I find these two categories give me a clear focus for what the whole¡class feedback should look like. 
6. Take pictures ofstudents' work 
Real examples of students' work make great stimuli for discussion. For questions in both columns -'Reteach' and 'Retrieve' -I will take pictures of students' work that I want to share with the rest of the class. These may be examples of common misconceptions or mis¡takes, alternative methods, interesting responses (perhaps correct answers that would have cost students marks in an exam because something was left out), and examples of excellence. I will use all of these when beginning whole-class feedback. 
7. Prepare follow-up questions As we will see, my approach in class is to discuss, do a worked example, and then set a follow-up question. It is these follow-up 
questions that I will prepare in advance. As we discussed in Tip 67, these are no harder or easier than the original; they are an oppor¡tunity to see if students can transfer the approach from solving one question to another. 
8. Don't give the work back yet 
Giving work back first was a huge mistake I made for many years. First, you have the time it takes for students to settle back down after they have seen their scores and tried to find out the scores of their mates. Then you need to deal with the usual protests: 'But sir, I got this right and you marked it wrong!' 
But the main issue with giving work back before whole-class feedback is it risks destroying the incentive to listen. We discussed this with homework in Tip 90, and it is just as important if a test is involved. Imagine you are going to give whole-class feedback on question 6, and Isaac can see he has got that question correct. Is Isaac really going to listen to what you have to say? Likewise, imag¡ine Thomas can see he has got question 6 wrong -along with a load of other questions. How is Thomas likely to be feeling? Will he have the motivation to pick himself back up and concentrate? 
Holding fire on giving the work back keeps students concentrating during the whole-class feedback. 
9. Discuss . model . follow-up question 
When giving whole-class feedback to one of the questions in my 'Retrieval' column, my process is: 
1. 
Discuss the problem. Showcasing students' work at this stage can be effective. 

2. 
Model the solution. I split my board in two, and model the solution to the original question using Silent Teacher (Tip 63) on the left¡hand side. 

3. 
Set a follow-up question. On the right-hand side ofthe board, I set stu¡dents the follow-up question that I have prepared in advance. I ask students to answer this on mini-whiteboards so I can do a whole-class check for understanding and then intervene fur¡ther if needed. 


For questions in my 'Reteach' column, the explanation will be longer and there may be several worked examples and more follow¡up practice. 
10. Don't worry about the students who can do it 
The single biggest objection to whole-class feedback concerns differentiation -what about the students who have got the question correct? There is a simple answer to this: don't worry about it. Here are five reasons why: 
1. 
By not giving students their work back straight away, it is more likely that they will be listening hard to how you explain it, and may pick up on something they had missed. 

2. 
Students have an opportunity to see if they really do understand the follow-up question. It is hard to infer understanding from one correct answer. 

3. 
In addition to a follow-up question, you can include a stretch question, pushing students' understanding of the concept further. 

4. 
As Clare Sealy explains, overlearning is a good thing. It is an opportunity to reinforce or shore up their understanding. And as Clare put it, it's not going to kill them to listen to this again. 

5. 
If we can generate the classroom culture that Sammy Kempner describes, whereby your first job is to try to understand some¡thing yourself, and then your next job is to help those around you understand it (Tip 16), the incentive to engage with the ex¡planation and example is ever-present. 


11. Consider using review cards 
After I have addressed the questions I want to, I will give the tests or homework back. Now is a great opportunity to challenge students to create a review card (Tip 86) on each of the questions we went through that they got wrong. I find this more effective than correct¡ing work in books, although if there is a requirement for evidence in books then this is the time to do that. And for students who have got those questions correct, their attention shifts to helping stu¡dents who are struggling. Remember the mantra: helping someone else is the hardest job in the classroom. 
12. End with detective work 
During whole-class feedback, I will go through up to five questions in this way. But what about the rest of the questions? That is where we can use Tip 94. Put the students into pairs or groups and give them a blank copy of the homework or test between them. Their challenge is to use their answers, plus what we have done in class, to see if they can create a perfect version of the test. If we use Sammy Kempner's approach to group work (Tip 16), we ensure that all students are accountable for the understanding of their fellow members, and as such, this should prove a worthwhile learning 
.
experience. 
Whole-class feedback in action 
I saw a brilliant example ofwhole-class feedback in action, utilising many of these ideas, in Ercall Wood Academy. Students had com¡pleted an assessment that the teacher had marked. The teacher had identified five questions that students had struggled with. In¡stead of giving the assessments back, the teacher gave out a copy of the first troublesome question on a slip of green paper. Students stuck this into their books. Then the teacher modelled how to solve the question, while the students watched and listened. When the teacher had finished, students were asked to copy down the written solution, but also to annotate and personalise the solution for the benefit oftheir forgetful future selves. The students were then given three follow-up questions. The first was just like the troublesome question, but with the numbers changed. But the next two were more challenging, pushing the concept on. Students solved these questions in their books, and also copied their final answers onto mini-whiteboards so the teacher could engage in whole-class as¡sessment and intervene ifneeded. This process was repeated for the remaining troublesome questions. Here is an example ofwhat this looked like in a student's book: 

' . 
'\ j IJ 
fetdb.cl( 
To repeat a point made above -this process was beneficial for students who had struggled with the question in the assessment as they received support, but also beneficial for students who had got the question correct on the assessment as they got to think more deeply about the process and have their understanding stretched. Lovely stuff! 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Whole-class feedback can save time and be more effective than personalised feedback, butsteps are needed to ensure students get as much out of itas possible.

CHAPTER 12 
IMPROVING AS A TEACHER 
The end is near! In this final chapter, we look at some general prin¡ciples to help you improve as a teacher, including how to learn best from others, the best way to try something new, and some practical suggestions to improve the delivery of CPD. And to wrap things up, some lovely bonus tips from my Tips for Teachers podcast guests that I couldn't fit elsewhere in the book.

What's the problem? 
This one is for the leadership teams within a department. 
What I am about to say next may well do myself out of a load of business, but never mind... 
When I am asked to work with a maths department over a series of days, I always start with a phone call with the head of department and line manager to get a sense of where the department is at and what they think I might be able to help with. Challenge, engage¡ment and retention are the usual suspects. 
When I then visit the school, I ask ifI can spend the morning watch¡ing as many different teachers as possible, just for 10 to 15 minutes each time. I take loads ofnotes and pictures. Then, in the afternoon, I meet with the head of department to discuss what I have seen. Without fail, I am able to identify outstanding examples of the exact areas that the head of department had identified as needing development. And, without fail, this is a surprise to the head of department. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
òAsa
head ofdepartmentoraresponsibility holder, are you aware ofthe expertise in your team? 

ò
How do you share this expertise? 


What's the tip? 
It is great to see examples of excellent practice, because then when I meet with all the stafffor CPD at the end of school, I can say, 'I have seen some incredible examples of engagement today. For example, here is a picture ofToni using mini-whiteboards in her percentages lesson. Toni, please can you talk us through this... ' I can then build upon this with a few of my own ideas as the session progresses. And colleagues are more receptive because they have seen evidence that it can be done by one of them in their own context. 
But the key point is that expertise and outstanding practice are almost always present within a department. The tricky bit is to find them and then share them. 
Finding the expertise 
Finding expertise is the responsibility ofthe departmental lead¡ership team. Not enough heads of departments get into as many lessons of their colleagues as they need. The reason given for this is always the same: 'I don't have time.' Either their teaching schedule is too full or -and this is more likely -their time is taken up with loads of other non-teaching activities, such as data meetings. 
If you are a head of department (or a responsibility holder), I have found the best approach is to ask the school's leadership team for cover support for 30 minutes for one ofyour classes at least once a month. Leave them work and clear instructions. Then, spend those 30 minutes dipping into at least four lessons. 
Next, ensure you visit those lessons with a clear focus ofwhat you are looking for. Treat this like you treat observing students' work, which we discussed in Tip 7 4. Knowing what you are looking for ¡whether it be how they check for understanding or how students are challenged -allows you to focus straight in on that and come away with a useful insight, as opposed to a vague observation where you see everything but learn nothing. 
Sharing the expertise 
The next challenge is how you share that good practice. Can you organise for staffto drop in for 10 minutes across a series oflessons so they can see it in action themselves? 
If not, then departmental meetings are the perfect opportunity. Let's say that a colleague is particularly strong on explanations. You could ask the colleague to place an iPad or a phone at the front of their desk and film their explanation, giving them full editing rights. Then, show the footage in your departmental meeting and chal¡lenge colleagues to: 
ò 
Think of something they like 

ò 
Think of something they notice 

ò 
Think of a question they want to ask. 


This is better than the teacher trying to describe their practice. And it is infinitely more useful than the usual deluge of admin tasks that swamp many departmental meetings I attend. 
Try to find examples of excellence from as many different col¡leagues as possible. Every teacher has something to offer -some¡thing others can learn from -even ifthey themselves have a lot to learn in other areas. The best departments I visit are ones that have the ethos that we all have something to learn from each other. The challenge still remains to find that and share it, but that is a much easier and more enjoyable challenge than trying to solve everything yourself (or having to put up with me for a day). 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
If you have areas of development in your practice, always start by trying to find the solution within.

What's the problem? 
Sometimes teaching can be a lonely profession. You just cannot get control ofthis student or this class. Your students just do not understand how to factorise quadratic expressions no matter how many times you explain it. They seem to understand things in the lesson, but then in tests they keep messing up. You feel like a com¡plete failure. 
I spent much ofmy first year ofteaching feeling frustrated and close to tears. Then, when I moved schools after six years, I reverted back to square one, only with extra frustration and extra tears thrown on top. 
Looking back, I wish I had taken more opportunities to learn from others. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Do youever feel this way? 

ò
Who do you learn from in these moments? 


What's the tip? 
Here are five different people you can learn things from. 
1. Your students 
Primary teacher Emma Turner explained how one of her favourite questions to ask her students at the end of a lesson is 'what was the most useful thing I did today?' 
Now, of course, we need to be aware of the dangers of relying on self-report and student preference, but I like the emphasis on 'use¡ful' instead of 'favourite'. 
Emma is specifically interested in the part of the lesson where students felt they understood. Was it the first example or the sec¡ond? Was it when Emma drew a diagram or when she asked Lucy? Was it when she asked students to make notes or discuss with their partners? Was it the analogy she used or when she rephrased something? 
Emma went on to say that she always learns something from her students' responses. Moreover, she finds that year-on-year, even when teaching the same topic, different classes will give her differ¡ent feedback. This allows Emma to refine her teaching to be more responsive to the needs of her students in front of her, instead of falling into the trap that I have done many times in thinking, 'This is the best way to explain this, so this is what I am going to do.' 
Emma also finds that doing this regularly makes her students more open to sharing where they are struggling because they see their teacher is also eager to learn and improve. This is another compo¡nent of the culture of error we discussed in Tip 32. 
In terms of practicalities, Emma asks 'what was the most useful thing I did today?' at the end of the lesson, followed by a brief narra¡tion of the lesson -'Was it when I showed you the diagram?', 'Was it when you discussed it with your partner?', etc. Emma then gets her students to vote for their choice, but this could equally be done via mini-whiteboards or Post-it notes. 
The key is that your students may pick up on something that you could do better that could be invisible to you. It helps us to be aware of the messy relationship between what we say and what our stu¡dents actually learn, and to take steps to combat that. 
2. Colleagues in your school 
Hopefully, your head of department has read Tip 97, and as such, there is already a system in place to learn from your colleagues. If not, you will have to do it yourself. Identify a colleague you feel you have something to learn from. Someone who is teaching a parallel class to you or someone who had success in their results last year is a good starting point. Or -ifyou are new to the school -ask your head of department or an experienced member of staffwho the people to learn from are and why. 
Then have a conversation with that person. Tell them why you want to watch them. What will you be looking for? Then, if they are up for it, give them the power to decide when you come to watch them. You want them to feel as comfortable as possible. And offer to return the favour if they feel there is anything they can learn from you. 
Then, when you visit the lesson, treat it like we discussed observing students' work (Tip 74). Have a focus. Direct as much attention to the thing you want to learn as you can. If it is checking for under¡standing, watch how they check for understanding, and ignore ev¡erything else. 
3. Colleagues in other schools Visiting other schools is something I was fortunate to do early in my career when I was appointed as an Advanced Skills Teacher and something I am able to do now. You learn so much from seeing a different environment, a different way of doing things, and different challenges and opportunities. But I rarely see this happen for class¡room teachers. As ever, time is the biggest barrier, but also a sense 
of awkwardness -can I really email the school down the road and ask to visit? 
I discussed this with experienced headteacher Sonia Thompson, and she was a huge advocate ofteachers visiting other schools. Sonia described how, as a headteacher, she would respond favourably to a teacher sending a nice email asking to visit her school, with a genuine reason why-you want to see how they facil¡itate group work because you have heard great things, or you want to see how they start their lessons offbecause it is an area you wish to develop. Offer a reciprocal visit to your classroom to boost your chances of a positive reply. 
Your head of department may already have contacts in other schools that make this easier to facilitate. Ask them and then see if you can get cover on one ofyour quieter teaching days. A good argument to use here is that it will be miles cheaper and a lot more valuable than heading off to some expensive course. Offer to feed¡back on your findings so others benefit too. 
4. Others in the wider world 
I am talking here about conferences, podcasts, Twitter, TeachMeets and blogs. There have never been so many people willing to share so much to help teachers get better. But I return to Tip 1 that started this book -have a focus to the help you seek. Go looking for some¡thing that is either related to an area ofyour teaching you have identified that you wish to develop or that will allow you to improve something you already do. Don't try to change too much at once, and avoid FOMO! 
5. Your former self Let's get a bit meta. Any time you teach a lesson, save it. And not just the presentation or the worksheet. If it is on a Power Point, OneNote or equivalent, save all your scribblings. If you can, make notes underneath slides or in comment boxes about things that went well or didn't go well, key decisions you made, or anything else you consider significant. This is annoying to do at the end of a 
lesson or at the end of the day, but your future self will be eternally grateful. 
Then, when you come to teach that topic again, start by looking at what you did last time. What went well that you can do again? What can you do better this time? 
This will save you time and also provide a concrete example of the development ofyour teaching. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Identify people you can learn from, and take steps to ensure you get the most out of the experience.

Tip 99 -Revisit education books and podcast episodes

and podcasts 

What's the problem? 
I love Twitter. I get a load of lovely ideas from it (and a bit of abuse 
too). But by far and away my favourite forms of non-face-to-face CPD are education books and podcasts. 
The problem is, there are loads of them. Hundreds ofbooks you could read and thousands of episodes you could listen to. 
Where on earth do you start? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
How do you choose which education books to read? 

ò 
How do you choose which podcast episodes to listen to? 


What's the tip? 
At the risk of sounding like a broken record, using the advice from Tip 1 is helpful here. Find a book or an episode description that ad¡dresses an area ofyour teaching that you have identified needs de¡veloping or that builds upon something you already do. 
But I want to make an additional suggestion: revisit education books and podcast episodes. 
Back in Tip 43, we discussed the notion of doing less, but better. I think the same principle can be applied to the books we read and the pod¡casts we listen to. 
Think of a book you read or an episode of a podcast that you learned a lot from at least 12 months ago. Have you gone back to it since? I never do, because there is so much new content to con¡sume. But if that book or episode had an impact on your teaching back then, then there are two things that are likely to happen ifyou revisit it. 
The first is that you will have an opportunity to reflect on the ideas shared and whether you implemented them as suggested. You will no doubt have adapted things to suit the needs ofyour students, and it will be an interesting exercise to consider why. 
The second is more likely-you will learn something new. You are a different person now than you were when you read that book or listened to that episode. The things we read and listen to speak to our condition. Our experiences allow us to find new insights that we missed the first time around because we were simply not in a posi¡tion to appreciate or understand them. 
I know I sound like a grumpy old man who refuses to listen to these new bands and keeps playing 'Definitely Maybe' on repeat, but I think it is sensible to occasionally revisit something that I know is good to see what else I can learn, than to always take a chance on something new. 

Here are examples of three things I have revisited in these last few months that I have gained new insights from: 
1. 
Doug Lemov-Teach like a Champion 3.0 -especially the techniques about ratio. 

2. 
Andrew Martin -'Load reduction instruction, motivation and engagement' -ERRR podcast by Ollie Lovell. 

3. 
Robert and Elizabeth Bjork -'Memory, forgetting, testing, desir¡able difficulties' -the Mr Barton Maths podcast. 


ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Before trying the next new thing, consider revisiting abook or podcast episode that you learned some¡thing from.

What's the problem? 
Hopefully, this book has left you eager to try out some new tips and techniques. You have adhered to the advice presented in Tip 1 to focus on an area you wish to develop or build upon something you already do. 
You have a new idea you want to try. So what next? 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
How do you prepare to use anew idea for the first time? 

ò
What questions do you ask yourself? 


What's the tip? 
Here are a few ideas to consider when trying something new. 
1. Can I improve something I am already doing? 
'Do less, but better' is back again. Before looking to make wholesale changes to your teaching, identify practices you already do and look for ways to improve them. This should take less time and effort, and give you a platform of success upon which to make further changes in the future. 
2. What is the MVP? 
Time for a bit ofbusiness lingo. MVP is the minimum viable product. When businesses are trying out a new product, they do not go all¡in and build the finished version. Instead, they create products with enough features to validate whether continuing is a good idea, and 
to give them ideas ofwhat to change. The creation of an MVP re¡quires fewer costs in terms of time and effort. 
Adopt a similar approach to the changes you make in your teaching practices. Think what the quickest, easiest way is for you to test out this new idea. Examples of MVPs include: 
ò 
Planning just one diagnostic question, learner-generated exam¡ple or We Do instead of planning many. 

ò 
Trying something out with one class instead ofplanning to roll it out immediately to all classes. 

ò 
Trying a technique in a class where you have been free the period before so you can get prepared, instead of rolling it out for every class you teach. 


3. How will I know ifthe idea works? 
How are you going to know if the idea has been a success or not? The more objective the measure, the better. For example: 
ò 
If you are looking to boost your participation ratio by using tools of mass participation, record the number of times you see re¡sponses from all students. 

ò 
If you are looking to develop a routine, time how long it takes students to carry it out. 

ò 
If you are looking to improve how well you observe students' work while they practise, keep a count of the things you notice that you are able to act upon. 


Record video or audio ofyour lesson if possible or practical. If not, record in a book or on a mini-whiteboard. Ideally, you will have one ofyour colleagues support you in this so you can concentrate on teaching. Offer to return the favour. 
4. What will I have to stop doing? This is the question that gets asked the least, and yet is so impor¡
tant. Trying something new may mean you have to no longer do something else. This plays out in two ways: 
1. 
A new idea in the classroom may mean you have less lesson time to do something else. Is that a sacrifice worth making? 

2. 
Planning a new idea may mean you have less planning time to work on something else. Is that a sacrifice worth making? 


ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Before diving into a new idea, ask yourself these four questions to givethe idea the best chance of being effective.

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip100

1.

2. 
What will you need to change to make this tip work for you? 

3. 
When are you going to try this idea for the first time? Please give the exact date and time. 

4. 
What worked well, and what will you do differently next time? 



What's the problem? 
I think teaching is unique in terms of the number of close interac¡tions you have with people in any given day that can make or break you. Some of those are unpredictable, hormone-fuelled specimens, prone to fits of rage one minute, and then declaring you are their favourite person the next. And some of them are students. 
The way teaching used to get me down can be illustrated by these two examples: 
1. 
When I taught a five-lesson day, four of those lessons could go like a dream. But if the fifth lesson didn't quite go according to plan -my students didn't get my explanation or one of them behaved poorly -that would cloud my evaluation ofhow the day went. A colleague or my wife would ask how my day went. 'Terrible,' would come the reply. 

2. 
I used to have a tricky Year 10 class, and they were particularly tricky on a Thursday afternoon. I used to dread that lesson. I felt nothing but relief as soon as it was over, but no sooner had Friday morning come around, the countdown to that lesson would begin again. Six days, five days ... It would ruin my work¡ing day, my evenings and my weekends. I was wishing my life away and failing to enjoy the moments in between. 


ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Can you relate to this? 

ò
What do you do to help you stay positive? 


What's the tip? 
One of English teacher Jamie Thom's tips was to 'tackle the nega¡tivity radio'. I love that phrase. It really does capture the sound of those thoughts that would swim around my head, blocking every¡thing else out. The happiness expert Mo Gawdat also has an evoca¡tive way of describing the constant replaying of negative thoughts and the worry about what is to come -he calls it 'unhappiness on demand'. 
Jamie shared some practical strategies for dealing with this destruc¡tive behaviour that was nicely complemented by another guest: maths teacher, Craig Latimir. Here are their ideas, plus a few of my own. I really hope they help. 
1. Think about the favourite part ofyour day 
Lots ofthese happiness practices sound -at the very least -a bit awkward. Can I interest anyone in standing in front of the mirror each morning and chanting 'I am great' 25 times? But one I have found really useful is the practice of ending each day by thinking about the favourite part of my day, no matter how small and seem¡ingly inconsequential it is. It ensures the day ends on a positive note. 
Jamie describes how he does this with his son before he goes to bed, and I have started doing the same with my three-year-old, Isaac. Verbalising it seems to help, as does sharing in the joy of someone else. Isaac's usually involve when Spider-Man and Hulk battled 
Venom with his Ooshies (don't ask), which I think would be the favourite part ofmost of my days as well. 
2. Be your best selffor five minutes 
I am a nightmare when I come home from a bad day. I have been going over all the bad stuff in my head during the drive home (or during the trip down the stairs), as the negativity radio blares out loud. Then I see my wife and pour everything onto her. And in no time at all, she is down in the dumps too. I have no doubt that my wife came to fear the sound of my key in the lock or my footsteps on the landing as she knew her day was about to get a whole lot worse. 
Craig Latimir has a solution to this. He says that when we arrive home or see our loved ones for the first time, we should strive to be our very best selves for five minutes. So, for a mere 300 seconds we should try to be happy, positive, grateful, loving, inquisitive: all the things we wish we could be. 
This cannot help but feel good. I always feel better at the end of the five minutes and any outpourings (because ifyou cannot let off steam to your loved ones, who can you?) have been tempered. 
3. Don't countdown to the holidays A controversial one. Teaching is unique in that every year is punc¡tuated by six holidays that all your colleagues take at the same 
time. Teachers work hard, and so the temptation is to countdown the days until the next one -only 12 sleeps until Easter! 
The problem with this approach is twofold. First, you are wishing your life away. The title of Oliver Burkeman's influential anti-pro¡ductivity book is Four Thousand Weeks, as that is a rough approximation of the number ofweeks an average person lives. And yet I often started a new term with the pronouncement 'only six weeks to go'. I was literally wishing my life away. Indeed, one of the reasons I took a break from full-time classroom teaching was I felt the years passing by too quickly, with past years rolling together in my mind, indistinguishable from each other. Second, it stops you from enjoy¡ing the here and now. We proceed through our precious days on au¡topilot in a bid to get to our cherished future. 
4. Find joy in small things 
If we are not to countdown to the holidays, what can we do? Teach¡ing can be a brutal, unrelenting job, and it is easy to let the unpleas¡ant, frustrating aspects dominate. Instead, Craig Latimir advises us to try to find joy in the small things. These could be obvious ones, like a coffee at break time with a friend or teaching a classroom of students who are genuinely a pleasure to be around. But Craig also offered some examples of things to be savoured that I would not have considered: 
ò 
A pristine, newly cleaned whiteboard. 

ò 
A tidy classroom at the end of the day. 

ò 
Meeting a class for the first time. 

ò 
Writing with a chisel-tip pen. 


ò Doing a mini-whiteboard routine and everyone gets it right. What are your examples of this? 
5. Put the good days in your pocket When you have a good day, remember it. Remember why it was 
good and how it made you feel. Ultramarathon runner David Gog¡gins describes this as the 'cookie jar method': 
ò 
Imagine you have an empty cookie jar into which you place all of the challenges that you have overcome or the accomplish¡ments that you have achieved -these are the cookies. 

ò 
Whenever you are facing an obstacle or hardship, refer back to your cookie jar to remind yourself of everything that you have been through and how resilient you are. 

ò 
This cookie jar serves as a mental reserve to dig into and serves as a reference point for your ability to make it through what¡ever you're encountering. 


I also like the way Joyce describes this in Richard Osman's The Thurs¡day Murder Club series of novels: 
'In life, you have to learn to count the good days. You have to tuck them in your pocket and carry them around with you. So I'm putting today in my pocket and I'm off to bed.' 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Find ways to deal with the negativefeelingsthat inevitably come with a careerin teaching.

What's the problem? 
I was in two minds about whether to include this tip, as I know it really isn't any of my business. So feel free to shake your head dis¡missively and skip to the next tip. 
There is a recruitment and retention crisis in teaching, especially for certain subjects, including maths. As a result, schools will do a lot to try to keep or lure in decent maths teachers. Time and time again I see teachers with three, two, or sometimes even just one year of experience, being given additional responsibilities -head ofkey stage 3, second in department, or even head ofdepartment -to keep them at a school or tempt them to move somewhere else. 
This is good news for the teacher concerned in the short term as they get a pay increase. But I am not convinced it is good for them in the long term. 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò 
Ifyou are new to teaching, after how many years would you look for paid responsibility? 

ò 
Ifyou have some paid responsibility, what effect did it have on your teaching? 


What's the tip? 
Many ofthese positions of responsibility come with no extra time, or the additional workload associated with them eclipses any extra non-contact time teachers may be given. As a result, the teacher in question has less time to dedicate to planning lessons. 
Now, ifyou have been teaching for, say, 10 years, this is not as big a problem. You have a wealth of experience that you can fall back on. You can put together a decent lesson pretty quickly. Heck, you could probably walk into a room, be told the topic of today's lesson, and do a good job right there and then. 
But with one or two years' experience? I think back to what I was like as a teacher back then. All I had to worry about was planning lessons and marking books, and still I struggled. If someone had taken any time away from me, it would have tipped me over the edge. I had not developed the experience or expertise in my class¡room practice such that I could afford to give it anything but my very best. 
Now, of course, there will be many teachers who are far better than me. There will also be less experienced teachers who teach in de¡partments with high-quality, centrally planned resources that save teachers planning time and enable them to do a better job in the classroom. 
But I just think all teachers need a foundation of teaching experi¡ence behind them. No matter what role within a school you end up doing, it all comes down to your ability to teach students. And you want to give yourself every possible chance to get good at that while you can, while it is your sole focus. 
I know how tempting it is when you see others gain promotions and I know people have different needs when it comes to salaries, so please ignore what I am saying ifyour circumstances make this a necessity. But I just wanted to make the point that I have seen too many good teachers get promoted too early, only for their teaching never to develop to its potential, and as a result, both they and their students suffered. 
Lecture over.

THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Consider focusing solely on your teaching in the early stages of your career.

What's the problem? 
If I'm ever to write another book, it will be entitled How I Wish I'd Delivered CPD. Because, while my teaching has improved since my mid-career crisis where I learned about cognitive science and its applications in the classroom, the lessons did not transfer to when I delivered workshops. 
Maybe you have experienced this too. Think back to the last CPD (continuous professional development) session you attended... 
ª 
WHAT DO YOU THINK? 
ò
Would it havemadean effective lesson? 

ò
If not, whatfeatures were missing? 


What's the tip? 
Here are some ideas I have tried to incorporate into my CPD after learning from people far more effective than me. Hopefully, these will be useful ifyou deliver CPD as part ofyour current role, or ifyou plan to deliver some in the future. 
1. Deliver CPD like you would teach a lesson 
This is the big one. We should use what we know about teaching students effectively and apply it to how we support our adult colleagues. After all -as Craig Latimir pointed out -many of the cognitive science studies into memory and cognitive load were con¡ducted on university students, and hence their findings are likely to apply even more to adults than to younger children. 
So, ifyou would not read through a dozen Power Point slides while colleagues sit passively in groups, then don't do it during CPD. 
Instead, ensure the key components of good teaching are present in your CPD: 
ò 
Set out the big picture -why are we doing this? 

ò 
Be enthusiastic -ifyou are not passionate about what you are talking about, no one else will be. 

ò 
Assess prior knowledge -allow colleagues to reflect where they are currently at, jotting things down on mini-whiteboards, then sharing with their partner and you. 

ò 
Break complex ideas down -what are the key steps? Deal with these individually. 

ò 
Model -show colleagues exactly what it looks like. 

ò 
Include non-examples -show colleagues what it does not look like. 

ò 
Reduce extraneous cognitive load -don't have redundant things on your slides, don't read text out loud before colleagues have had a chance to read it themselves, and integrate images and text together so attention is not split. 

ò 
Scaffold -offer more support at first, then gradually reduce it as colleagues get more confident. 

ò 
Practice -allow colleagues a chance to try these techniques out there and then. If role play is a step too far, then challenge them to write a script or create a storyboard ofhow it might play out in a lesson. 

ò 
Showcase excellence -ifyou see or hear something great from colleagues, share it visually or audibly. 


2. Allow adequate wait time before discussion 
Good CPD presents colleagues with an idea, and then gives them an opportunity to discuss that idea with their colleagues, either in pairs or in groups. But almost every CPD session I have ever attended (or delivered!) falls into the trap we discussed back in Tip 22 -colleagues are not given enough time to think on their own first. Instead, it is always 'here is an idea, now discuss it with your partner'. Colleagues then enter those discussions trying to simulta¡neously decide what they think and consider the thoughts of their colleagues. Those discussions invariably start with silence, followed by a few umms, and by the time they spring to life, the presenter is ready to move on. 
Once again, we need to treat CPD like we would treat a lesson. Give colleagues an opportunity to think about an idea themselves, in si¡lence. Encourage them to make notes on paper or on a mini-white¡board, so they don't have to hold all their thoughts in the working memory. They can then enter into a discussion better prepared to share and to listen. 
3. Check for understanding Checking for understanding is the cornerstone of any lesson. It 
allows us to distinguish 'they learned it' from 'I taught it'. And yet I rarely see it happen during CPD. 
Throughout the CPD session, use tools of mass participation to see where colleagues are at (and provide an incentive for certain colleagues to participate at all). Cold Call them following paired dis¡cussions. Don't just encourage them to ask questions, but ask them to ask you a question. Do all the things you would do in your class¡room to allow you to get a sense ofyour students' understanding. 
4. Don't be afraid that you are patronising 
When reflecting on my own delivery of CPD, I think the key reason I didn't deliver it like a lesson with all these components present is that I didn't want to patronise my fellow colleagues. After all, they are experts. 
But are they? They may be expert explainers, markers and question writers, but what about when it comes to the subject ofyour CPD? We know people need support in the specific area they are a novice in, and teachers are no different. 
And if colleagues do know all the things you are saying? Well, great! I respond exactly as I would when that situation arises in the class¡room: remind colleagues that overlearning is a good thing and that the hardest job in the classroom is to help others, so see what sup¡port they can offer. 
5. Pitch the subject content ofyour CPD outside ofyour colleagues' domain of expertise 
When I deliver CPD, I want my colleagues to first play the role of students. This allows me to model how I would act as a teacher, and it also allows my colleagues to get a sense ofhow their students would feel so they can adapt the technique accordingly using their knowledge of their class. But this is difficult to do because of our subject knowledge. 
Let's take my CPD on using diagnostic questions as an example. I want to model how I use diagnostic questions in the classroom, collecting in responses from students and then responding ac¡cordingly. The mistake I have made when working with secondary maths colleagues is to ask the kind of diagnostic question I would ask secondary maths students -one involving multiplying fractions, for example. It then becomes much more difficult for my colleagues to play the role of students and generate an authentic classroom ex¡perience because they all know the correct answer. 
To combat this I have started choosing content outside the domain of expertise of my audience. This may mean using A-level content if working with a group of maths teachers who primarily teach 11 to 16, or choosing a completely different subject matter. General knowledge is perfect for this. Consider this question: 
A family inherited ú15,000 from a long-lost aunt. They are 
weighing up whether to buy a new car or go on a trip to 
Australia for a month. They decide to go to Australia. The 
opportunity cost of this decision is: 
A. the cost of going to Australia. 
B. the cost of a buying a new car. 
C. the benefits of going to Australia. 
D. the benefits of having a new car. 
Some colleagues will know the answer, and some will not, which is exactly what would happen in the classroom. This creates the au¡thentic classroom experience that I need. 
6. Give teachers time to play the role ofthe teacher 
This may be a maths-specific one, but I have attended lots of prob¡lem-solving CPD over the years that I have absolutely loved. I loved it because I got to spend an hour doing challenging maths. The problem was I never had the opportunity to consider how I would support my students in getting the same positive experiences out of the content as I had. So, while it is important to enable colleagues to play the role of students during CPD, it is just as important to give them the opportunity to put their teaching hat back on and con¡sider what they would need to change to make this work for their students. 
7. Show what you do; don't guess what others might do 
As I described in the introduction of this book, I decided to try to describe as clearly as I could how I apply each of these tips in my classroom, instead of trying to guess how you might apply them in your classroom. The reason for that decision is simple -no one knows your classroom, students, challenges and constraints as well as you do. I approach CPD the same way. I try to convey as clearly as I can what I do, why I do it, what can go wrong and how I act, and then I throw it back over to the audience: 'Now you have a really clear understanding of my process, what will this look like for you? What will you need to change to make it work?' I think this is a much more useful approach than CPD that attempts to offer exam¡ples from loads of other subjects, especially when the presenter is not an expert in those subjects. 
8. Ensure CPD saves on planning time 
Teachers are busy people, and CPD -especially if it is after school -can really impinge on their limited free time. So, try to make it as immediately worthwhile as possible. I do this by starting the CPD session by asking teachers to think of a lesson they will be teaching soon that they haven't planned yet. I do the same for a topic I will be teaching soon. 
Let's say the CPD session is on responsive teaching. I show teachers how I would write a question on my topic -expanding brackets ¡that checks students' understanding. Then I give colleagues an op¡portunity to do the same for their topic. 
Next, I model how I would respond in a variety of scenarios: all stu¡dents get the question correct, most do, half do, or almost nobody does. Then I challenge colleagues to think and write down how they would respond in different scenarios for their questions. Sufficient time needs to be allocated for this. 
The beauty of this approach is that by the end of the session all col¡leagues have got something they can use -in this case, a resource and a strategy. Crucially, not something they can use next year, but something they can use next lesson. You have saved them time and removed a significant barrier to putting the lessons they learned into action. 
9. Ask colleagues to pick just one thing to try 
Ifyour CPD session has been crammed full of gold, there is a danger colleagues will want to try it all. There are two problems with this. First, colleagues are unlikely to be able to dedicate sufficient time and attention to planning each idea to give them the best chance ofbeing effective -'do less, but better' again. Second, with so many variables changing, it is hard to attribute the good and bad points of the subsequent lesson to any specific idea. 
So, end the CPD session by asking colleagues to pick just one idea that they are going to work on first. And advise them not to try any¡thing else until they have that one well and truly embedded. 
10. Get colleagues to commit to when they will try the new idea 
Here is our 'implementation intention' from Tip 2 that we have been using throughout this book. Colleagues saying they will try this next term almost certainly will not happen. Next week probably will not happen. Wednesday, period 3 with my Year 10s is much more likely to happen. Get colleagues to commit to a specific time to put their chosen idea into practice. 
11. Suggest colleagues incorporate a cue 
Let's say the CPD was on using diagnostic questions, and a col¡league has decided that the one thing they want to focus on is using diagnostic questions as a hinge question midway through a lesson to determine what to do next. They plan their question in theses¡sion, and decide to use it for the first time tomorrow in period 5. But that is 24 hours away, and because this is new, if the teacher gets distracted in the lessons, they may forget to try it out. 
Teacher educator Harry Fletcher-Wood suggests that teachers add a cue to their lessons to remind them to try this new idea. This could be something as simple as a star on one ofyour slides, a note on your desk, or a timer on your phone that you set to go off at roughly the correct time. Anything that reminds you ofwhat you intended to do. 
12. Add accountability 
We are much more likely to follow through on our plans ifwe know someone will be checking up on us. I have found the most effective way to do this is to ask colleagues to pair up, and at the end of the CPD session tell their partner: 
1. 
What they are going to try 

2. 
When they are going to try it 

3. 
When they will let their partner know how it went. 


The final stage ofthis could be over a cup of tea at lunch or five minutes at the end of the day. The important point is that you now have an extra incentive to try out the idea. 
Of course, an even better accountability measure is to arrange for your partner to come and watch you in action the first time you try out the idea. This has the added advantage that they can give you a more objective appraisal of how it went than you might be able to muster in the hustle and bustle of a lesson. 
13. Observe and note misconceptions 
When I work with a school, I always try to plan at least one follow¡up visit. This enables me to watch teachers implement the ideas from my previous CPD session and pick up on what is working and what is not. Often I see examples ofbrilliant adaptation -teachers who have taken the core element ofthe idea and changed it to make it work for their unique circumstances. But I also see misconcep¡tions -examples where the teacher is doing something that looks a bit like what we talked about, but misses a key feature. Using mini¡whiteboards to answer questions but never sharing those answers with the rest of the class is an example of this. 
Only by observing teachers putting the CPD into action can we have any idea whether it has been effective. Again, this is exactly what we would do when teaching -assess understanding at a later date to separate performance in the moment from long-term learning. 
14. Plan a follow-up session 
If we have been able to observe the CPD in action, then the follow¡up session is an ideal opportunity to respond to what we have seen. We can share examples of good practice, but also address those misconceptions head-on, exactly as we would do with our students. 
If we have been unable to observe colleagues in the interim period, a follow-up session is still a good idea to give colleagues an incen¡tive to have tried the idea out, and an opportunity to reflect and share how it went. 
With one-off CPD sessions, you end up being like the naive teacher, driving home smiling at the end of the day thinking, 'Yep, they un¡derstood that, job done.' 
15. Ask the CPD organiser how they will know ifthe CPD is effective 
Sometimes it is not possible to organise a follow-up session. If you are delivering CPD in your school, perhaps there is no more INSET capacity. If you have been booked externally, maybe the event is just a one-off. 
In these cases, it is worth asking the event organiser what plans they will put in place to ensure the CPD has been effective. You may need to offer some suggestions. So, if I have been booked to visit a school, I will suggest that they allow time for colleagues to observe each other, and organise a follow-up session (perhaps in depart¡ments) so colleagues can reflect on what they tried. Just something that provides both the opportunity and the incentive to build lasting change. 
16. Consider CPD via stealth 
This is a sneaky one, but it can be effective. Let's say you want to show your colleagues the effectiveness ofmini-whiteboards as a tool of mass participation, but for whatever reason, having this as the stated aim of the session is not a good idea. I experienced this myselfworking in a maths department that had several teachers who point-blank refused to engage in all ofthe head of depart¡ment's suggestions to use mini-whiteboards more. Me coming in to deliver another mini-whiteboard session would have been doomed from the start. 
So instead, I ran a session on lesson planning. I asked colleagues to write a question that they would expect every one of the students to be able to answer by the end of a unit ofwork on Pythagoras. 
Where did I ask them to write this question? On mini-whiteboards, of course. I asked them to share their question with me, then swap with their partner, and then I was able to Warm Call colleagues and hear the thinking behind their choice of questions. The session con¡tinued as colleagues were challenged to list and prioritise relevant prior knowledge, and so on. Each time the mini-whiteboards were pivotal to enable me to check for understanding, facilitate paired discussions, and enhance whole-class discussions. 
At the end of the session, I asked colleagues to consider what the session would have been like without mini-whiteboards. This then led to me sharing a few tips for effective mini-whiteboard use. I know this sounds like I am making it up, but a few weeks later, the head of department emailed to say she had seen all colleagues using mini-whiteboards in their lessons. 
When people are reluctant to change, often they need to be shown how effective that change can be instead ofbeing told it upfront. 
ª 
THE TIP IN ASENTENCE 
Ensure your CPD sessions are as effective as possible by utilising what we know about how people learn best. 
The resources 

tipsforteachers.co.uk/tip103
